### Preface

This book contains a broad All India Survey of three important programmes of land reform undertaken since independence. The three programme^. covered are the Abolition of Intenr'^iary Tenancy reform and Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings fn rp-spp-rt rk each programme the survey covers policy, legislation, implementation and results.

In the seventies I worked as the Land Reforms Commissioner under the Government of India for about five years from 1970 to 1975 and later on from 1978-79,1 was in charge of the subject of land reform as Additional Secretary in the Ministry of Agriculture. Soon after taking over as Land Reforms Commissioner in October 1970,1 had to write a paper on Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings. To my dismay I found that there did not exist any book dealing with all the aspects of the matter. I had to go through vast literature including numerous internal papers of the Planning Commission and the Ministry of Agriculture to produce a paper on Ceilings. Subsequently, the Ministry of Agriculture published that paper and it proved to be of use to students of land reform as a reference book. That experience prompted me to write a paper on Tenancy Reform and it was published in the Economic and Political Weekly in August, 1975.

Indian land reform is well documented. There are several statewise studies and there are numerous articles, some of which are of high quality. The subject has also been dealt with competently as part of larger studies as in Myrdal’s Asian Drama. Yet, unfortunately, even today there is no comprehensive all India survey covering the three programmes dealt with in this study. My aim in writing this book has been to fill that void in the literature on Indian land reform.

![image 19](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile19.png)

![image 20](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile20.png)

xii Preface

Chapter 1 deals with Agrarian structure at independence and

- Chapter 2 with Abolition of Intermediary interests. While writing
- Chapter 3 on Tenancy Reform and Chapter 4 on Ceiling I have drawn heavily on my previous writings taking care to make the study up-todate. My overall assessment of Indian land reform and my views on the changes needed in land policy are given in the concluding Chapter. I hope the study will be of interest to policy makers and general readers. I also hope it will serve students of land reform as a reference book.


I thank the Lai Bahadur Shastri National Academy and its Director Dr. N.C. Saxena and Deputy Director Shri Harsh Mander for their help in publishing the book. I thank my friend Shri A.R. Bandyopadhay for glancing through the type-script and suggesting some changes. Of course I alone am responsible for the views expressed. I thank all my friends, in particular Dr. P. T. George, Shri Pradip Bhattacharya and Shri Amaijeet Sinha, for their help in getting mimeographed papers and other materials.

It would not have been possible for me to complete this study without the unstinted support of my wife Kalyani Kutty. Apart from assisting with typing in the initial stages, she persuaded me to get over my lethargy and complete the work. I am also thankful to my son Prem and his wife Smita for helping me with their computer.

Bangalore, 31 July, 1996 P. S. Appu

### Introduction

Land Reforms have been on the agenda of mankind since the earliest of times. Starting from the seventeenth century B.C. Imperial China made several unsuccessful attempts to introduce land reforms. In the sixth century B.C. the Athenian statesman Solon tried without success to regulate the accumulation of land in the hands of the rich. Some four centuries later, the Roman statesmen Tiberius Gracchus and his younger brother Gaius Gracchus tried, again without much success, to redistribute land to small holders. All through history, measures of land reform have been controversial, often accompanied by political conflict and violence. Because of the stiff opposition by vested interests most attempts at reform could not produce the expected results. The more successful programmes have been those introduced in revolutionary situations, or in specially compelling circumstances. By far the most important instance of the effective implementation of land reforms in a revolutionary situation is the Chinese land reforms of 1949-52. Two cases of reasonably effective land reforms in compelling circumstances are the land reforms introduced in Japan and Taiwan after the Second World War. The Japanese land reforms were formulated and implemented during the occupation of Japan by the Allies. The principal objective of General Mac Arthur was to destroy the base of Japanese feudalism.There was not much that the Japanese vested interests could do to thwart the reforms. In Taiwan the reforms were carried out after the rout of the Kuomintang and their withdrawal from the mainland. Measures of land reforms were formulated and implemented with urgency to win over the support of the peasantry. Other cases of reform undertaken in norma] circumstances generally

![image 23](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile23.png)

![image 24](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile24.png)

###### xiv Introduction



failed to produce the expected results. Measures of land reforms introduced in India after independence belong to this category.

###### Definition

There is no agreement on what constitutes land reforms. According to the Encyclopaedia Britannica land reform means :

“A specific integrated-action programme to bring about more effective control and use of land for the direct benefit of the agricultural population and for the the direct benefit of the community as a whole. For example, land reform includes, without widespread dissent, the redistribution of agricultural land among existing or new owners, including consolidation or subdivision, development or settlement of holdings; adjustment of rental charges¡compulsory reimbursement of costs incurred by the tenant for improvements on the land he cultivates; increase in the security of tenure; adjustment of policy and procedure in the taxation of agricultural land; and the adjudication and registration of land and water titles.”

Land reforms would also include supporting measures to enable the beneficiaries of the reform to utilise the land properly. When the reforms embrace the entire agrarian structure and include measures for the modernisation of agricultural practices and the improvement of the living conditions of the entire agrarian population the term used is “Agrarian Reforms1*. Thus Agrarian Reforms would also include establishment of co-operatives and other institutions for supply of agricultural credit and other inputs and processing and marketing of agricultural produce, establishment of agro-industries, agricultural extension and the provision of a variety of services for the benefit of the farmers and agricultural workers.

###### Scope of the Present Study

In India right from the First Five Year Plan the measures listed under Land Reforms have been the Abolition of Intermediary Interests, Tenancy Reform, Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings and the Consolidation of Holdings. The present study covers all the above measures except Consolidation of Holdings. In respect of each of these subjects, the study deals with the evolution of policy, legislation, implementation and an assessment of the results.

![image 25](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile25.png)

![image 26](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile26.png)

Introduction XV

###### Objectives of Indian Land Reforms

Indian agriculture had been stagnating for several decades when India became independent. Though primarily an agricultural country, India was not producing enough food to feed the rising population. As a matter of fact, the annual per caput availability of food grains had been falling in the decades preceeding independence. The per caput availability of food grains from 1905 to 1985 is shown in Appendix-1. While the per caput availability was 200.2 kg. per annum, or 549 grammes per day, in 1905, it had come down to 152.2 kg. per annum or 417 grammes per day in 1945. Hence, a quick increase in food production was a matter of paramount importance for the new nation. Accordingly, the Grow More Food Campaign that had been initiated during the Second World War was intensified after independence. In the Grow More Food Campaign emphasis was laid on immediate increase in food production through the extension of area under cultivation, extension of irrigation, use of improved seeds and application of adequate doses of chemical fertilizers. The policymakers did not subscribe to the view that an antiquated agrarian structure was the greatest impediment to increased agricultural production and that its thorough overhaul was an essential precondition for fast agricultural growth. As a matter of fact, the policymakers even feared that some measures of land reform like the imposition of ceilings and the redistribution of surplus land would lead to a fall in the marketable quantity of food grains. Land Reforms were viewed essentially as a social welfare measure for the benefit of the weaker sections of the rural society.

###### Agrarian Structure at Independence

The agrarian structure prevailing at the time of independence had several features that inhibited agricultural growth. These were the existence of rent-receiving parasitic intermediaries between the actual tillers of the soil at the bottom and the government at the top, great inequality in the ownership of land, concentration of agricultural land in the hands of the upper classes who shunned physical labour and took little personal interest in farming, widespread prevalence of insecure tenancies on extortionate terms inhibiting the optimum utilization of the

![image 27](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile27.png)

![image 28](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile28.png)

###### xvi Introduction



tenanted land, a preponderance of miniscule uneconomic holdings and the extreme fragmentation and subdivision of holdings.

In a vast sub-continent of such great diversity as India, exposed as she had been to different strands of political, economic, social and cultural influences in her long and chequered history, it was, indeed, natural to come across a bewildering variety of tenurial conditions. Though a multitude of tenures existed at the time of independence, and tenurial conditions varied a great deal between States and even within the same State between regions—it is possible to classify the numerous tenures under two broad categories—the Zamindari and the Raiyatwari systems. The essence of the Zamindari system was the existence of one or more layers of proprietary rights between the State and the actual holder of the land. In the Raiyatwari system no intermediary existed between the State and the landholder. It was estimated that Zamindari tenures covered about 60 per cent of the area at the time of independence.

Traditionally all Indian rulers, Hindu as well as Muslim, appropriated a share of the produce of the land which was the principal source of revenue for the State. In the years of the decline and disintegration of the Mughal Empire, finding themselves unequal to the task of collecting land revenue, the provincial governors resorted to the device of appointing revenue farmers drawn from the ranks of vanquished potentates, feudal chieftains, marauding freebooters, erstwhile government functionaries, moneylenders, speculators etc. They contracted to pay fixed sums to the government, but extorted as much as they could from the landholders, keeping the difference as their profits. Though these revenue farmers were not proprietors, the British administrators treated them as landlords and entered into firm arrangements with them for the payment of land revenue. Apart from simplifying the work of collecting land revenue from numerous landholders every year, it was hoped that the new arrangement would lead to the creation of a rural elite with a vested interest in the continuance of the British rule. The arrangement did certainly subserve the interests of the British Empire. But it had the unfortunate consequence of reducing the erstwhile landholders who had permanent rights in the land to the status of insecure tenants. Thus the Zamindar was the person whom the government recognized as the proprietor of

![image 29](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile29.png)

![image 30](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile30.png)

Introduction xv ii

an estate on his agreeing to pay a certain sum as land revenue. In certain areas, the amount payable was fixed in perpetuity, while in other areas the amount was revised from time to time. The former were known as Permanently Settled areas and the latter as Temporarily Settled areas. The Temporary Settlement was made with individual Zamindars in most areas and with groups of village proprietors in a few areas. In course of time many Zamindars freed themselves from the trouble of managing their estates and collecting rent from cultivators by leasing out their rights. Thus in some areas several layers of such intermediary rights came to be created between the Zamindar and the actual cultivators. All these intermediaries appropriated a share of the rent paid by the cultivators and passed on the balance to their superior. At the summit was the the government to whom the Zamindar paid land revenue. Some parts of the country, particularly in the South and the West, were free from the system of revenue farming. In these areas the government entered into a direct agreement with numerous landholders for the payment of land revenue. This came to be known as the Raiyatwari System. Some Raiyats were owner-cultivators, but many of them leased out their land or a part of it to tenants, mostly share-croppers. The distinguishing feature of the Raiyatwari system was that the landholders paid land revenue directly to the government and there was no intermediary between the landholders and the government.

Initially the Zamindars were given absolute freedom to deal with their tenants as they pleased. In course of time it was found that the Zamindars were harassing the tenants by resorting to rack-renting and summary ejectment. Millions of tenants lost their rights in the lands they had been cultivating for generations. With a view to affording a measure of protection to tenants, starting with the Bengal Tenancy Act of 1885, laws were enacted to confer security of tenure to certain categories of superior tenants. The Zamindars usually kept a portion of their estates under their own management. Such lands constituted their home farms which they cultivated through tenants-at-will or farm servants. Though the Raiyatwari system came to be described as one of peasant-proprietors, right from the beginning a sizeable percentage of landowners used to lease out their land to tenants paying a fixed rent or sharing the produce. Tenants on the home-farms of the Zamindars and

![image 31](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile31.png)

![image 32](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile32.png)

###### xviii Introduction



all categories of tenants in the Raiyatiwari areas enjoyed no security of tenure. It is estimated that on the eve of independence more than one half of the cultivated land was under tenancy and that the bulk of the tenants enjoyed no security of tenure or fixity of rent.

The agrarian structure was also marked by great inequality in the ownership of land. A small minority of big landowners owned a substantial portion of the agricultural land, while millions of small peasants subjected to numerous abuses and weighed down by perpetual indebtedness, eked out a precarious existence on tiny, fragmented holdings employing primitive farming practices. And at the bottom there existed a vast army of landless agricultural workers whose social and economic status was even worse. A unique feature of the Indian agrarian structure was the close inter-relationship between the Hindu caste hierarchy and the agrarian structure. The big landowners invariably belonged to the so-called upper castes, the cultivators to the middle castes, and the agricultural workers to the Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes and other extremely backward castes. The salient features of the agrarian structure are discussed in Chapter I.

###### Constitutional Position

Under the Indian Constitution Land Reform is a State subject. Land Reform falls under entry 18 of the State List. That entry reads:

“Land, that is to say, rights in or over land, land tenures including the relation of landlord and tenant, and the collection of rents; transfer and alienation of agricultural land; land improvement and agricultural loans; colonization.”

So only the State Legislatures have the power to enact Land Reform laws. Implementation is exclusively the responsibility of the State Governments. The Indian Parliament and the Government of India have no powers under the Constitution to enact and implement land reform laws or give directives to the State Governments. The tenuous Constitutional basis for Government of India’s role in land reforms is entry 20 in the Concurrent List which speaks of Social and Economic Planning. This entry is the sole Constitutional basis for the establishment of the Planning Commission. The role of the Planning Commission in respect of Land Reform has necessarily been of an

![image 33](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile33.png)

![image 34](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile34.png)

Introduction xix

advisory nature. The Planning Commission could only lay down the general principles and guidelines for legislation and implementation. Despite the lack of Constitutional powers, the Government of India, if it so chose, could have played a more active role in respect of Land Reforms because for several years after independence the same political party was in power both at the Centre and in the States, and the Central leadership enjoyed immense prestige and authority.

###### Land Policy in the Five Year Plans

In the Five Year Plans the objectives of the land policy were laid down as, firstly, to remove such motivational and other impediments to increase in agricultural production as arise from the agrarian structure inherited from the past and, secondly, to eliminate all elements of exploitation and social injustice within the agrarian system so as to ensure equality of status and opportunity to all sections of the rural population . The principal measures of land reforms adopted to secure the objectives of the land policy laid down in the Five Year Plans were the Abolition of Intermediary Interests, Tenancy Reform, and Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings. These measures constitute the subject of the present study. Chapter 2 deals with the Abolition of Intermediary Interests, Chapter 3 with Tenancy Reform and Chapter 4 with Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings. In respect of each measure the study covers the evolution of policy, legislation, implementation and review of the results achieved.

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests

As we have seen, the intermediary tenures covered about sixty per cent of the land, mostly in the North and the East. The abuses of the Zamindari system had attracted considerable attention during the freedom struggle. The Zamindars were a rather unpopular lot not only because of the oppression practised by them, but also because they had identified themselves, particularly the big ones among them, as the staunch supporters of the British administration. So it was not only good economics, but also good politics to abolish the intermediary interests. Immediately after independence high priority was accorded to the abolition of intermediary tenures so as to bring the cultivators into a direct relationship with the State. Accordingly, every State passed its

![image 35](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile35.png)

![image 36](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile36.png)

XX Introduction

own legislation for the abolition of intermediary rights on payment of compensation. As a result of the abolition of the intermediary tenures some 25 million erstwhile superior tenants became landowners holding land directly under the government. Large areas of privately owned forests and wastelands also vested in the State. One serious flaw in the programme of Abolition of Intermediary Interests was that no effort was made to limit the size of the home farms of the Zamindars in most States. Another inadequacy was that no protection was given to the tenants-at-will, mostly share-croppers, on the home farms of the exintermediaries. The programme would have yielded far better results if steps had been taken simultaneously to limit the size of the home farms and extend protection to the insecure tenants on those farms. Yet, as pointed out in Chapter 2, the programme was implemented more efficiently than the other programmes introduced in later years. It also led to great changes in the rural power structure.

###### Tenancy Reform

Measures adopted for the reform of tenancy are discussed in Chapter 3. The Congress Governments that had been in power in some States for a short while in the late nineteen thirties had initiated action in a small way for the protection of tenants. After independence, right from the First Five Year Plan, emphasis was laid on the reform of tenancy. Three important guidelines were laid down in the Five Year Plans. First, rent should not exceed one-fifth to one-fourth of the gross produce; second, the tenants should be accorded permanent rights in the lands they cultivated subject to a limited right of of resumption to be granted to the landowners; and third, in respect of non-resumable land the landlord-tenant relationship should be ended by conferring ownership rights on tenants.

While formulating the policy the over-riding consideration was that the measures adopted for the protection of tenants should not hurt the landowners seriously. Considering the character of the political power structure and the fact that the owners who had let out their lands were numerous and that they had considerable political clout, the adoption of such a pro-landlord policy was inevitable. But then, the hard truth is that no effective tenancy reform can be carried out without abridging the rights of landowners and hurting their interests. So the

![image 37](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile37.png)

![image 38](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile38.png)

Introduction xxi

reforms inevitably yielded poor results. In order to protect the interests of landowners every tenancy law contained a provision for the resumption of a part of the tenanted land by landowners for ‘personal cultivation*. In all the laws the term ‘personal cultivation* was loosely defined so as to include cultivation of land without putting in one’s own labour or even personal supervision. The laws also permitted the voluntary surrender of tenancies. The inevitable consequence was numerous surrenders of tenancies under duress, but on paper shown as Voluntary surrenders’. Most of the tenancies in the Raiyalwari areas and under-tenancies in the Zamindari areas were oral and informal crop-sharing arrangements. They were seldom entered in the land records maintained by the village level functionaries of the Revenue Department. Some laws left share-croppers out of the purview of tenancy reform. Even the states where the law recognized sharecroppers as tenants, they could hardly avail of the benefit as it was extremely difficult to prove the existence of tenancy. When all efforts to mend tenancy failed, the Planning Commission advised that tenancies should be ended. The new policy was that leasing out of land should be prohibited and all tenants should be made owners of the land they had been cultivating, on payment of compensation to the landowners. The shift in policy did not, however, yield better results. For the problem of proving the existence of tenancies continued. The main consequence of the new policy was to push tenancies underground.

The evolution of a policy on tenancy reform, the enactment of laws, their implementation and results are discussed in Chapter 3. We have drawn attention to the flaws in the formulation of policy, the defects in legislation and the generally poor implementation. As a result of tenancy reform tenants acquired ownership rights only in about

- 4 per cent of the operated area in the country. Seven States account for 97 per cent of the total number of tenants who became owners. Everywhere tenancy reform led to the largescale ejectment of tenants. Share-croppers derived little benefit from the programme except in West Bengal. Tenancy reform has led to a sharp fall in the area of land under tenancy. From over one half of the operated area at independence, tenancy has come down to an estimated 15 per cent. As tenants acquired ownership rights in only about 4 per cent of the


![image 39](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile39.png)

![image 40](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile40.png)

xxii Introduction

operated area, one of the important effects of the reform was that the rural poor lost access to some 30 per cent of the operated area. And, as already pointed out, tenancy reform has not put an end to the leasing out of land. As long as a large mass of poor peasantry with an insatiable hunger for land and a class of unenterprising landowners co-exist, all attempts to prohibit tenancy are bound to fail. If tenancies are legally permitted, tenants will enjoy better rights in the lands that they now cultivate illegally. They will also find it easier to lease in land.

In many instances tenancy reform has also resulted in landowners leaving their land uncultivated. These owners are unwilling or unable to cultivate their lands. They fear that they would lose the land if they lease it out. So they leave it uncultivated. The lifting of the ban on leasing will improve the access of the rural poor to agricultural land leading to the better utilization of the land owned by indolent owners. It will also confer a measure of security on tenants. The result will be the better utilization of the land, fuller absorption of human labour and increased farm output.

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

Chapter 4 deals with Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings. With a view to reduce disparities in the ownership of land and make some land available for distribution to the landless labourers and peasants with very small holdings, the Second Five Year Plan recommended the imposition of ceilings on agricultural holdings. In accordance with that recommendation, by 1961 all States enacted ceiling laws. The Planning Commission had given only broad guidelines with considerable flexibility. The inevitable consequence was wide variations between the laws of different States in the matter of the level of ceiling, unit of application, exemptions etc. The enactment of the ceiling laws was preceded by a national debate attended with a great deal of publicity. Naturally, all alert landowners took steps in good time to distribute the surplus land among their relations, friends and dependents, and in some cases they made paper transactions showing transfers to fictitious trusts.

Because of the ambivalence in the formulation of policy, the shortcomings and loopholes in the laws and inefficient implementation, the ceiling laws did not yield any worthwhile results. By the late sixties it had become clear that ceiling laws would not achieve anything. Yet,

![image 41](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile41.png)

![image 42](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile42.png)

Introduction xxiii

in the context of the radical postures adopted by the ruling party, land reforms in general, and ceilings in particular, received a great deal of attention during the period 1969-72. After protracted deliberations new guidelines were issued in July 1972. The revised guidelines brought down the levels of ceilings, insisted upon the family being accepted as the unit for the application of ceiling and did away with most of the exemptions. Though the new policy was adopted with a great deal of fanfare and the laws were amended quickly, there was no improvement in the quality of implementation. As a result of the implementation of the old and the amended ceiling laws only less than 2 per cent of the agricultural land could be redistributed. In Chapter 4 we have drawn attention to the half-hearted approach to the formulation of policy, the built-in loopholes in the laws, the generally poor implementation and the reasons for poor performance. We have also discussed the deliberations of the Central Land Reforms Committee in 1971 and 1972 which led to the formulation of the new guidelines.

###### Conclusion

Our findings in respect of the three programmes covered in the study are presented in Chapter 5. The conclusion is that the Abolition of Intermediary Interests was accomplished with reasonable efficiency and that though there were shortcomings some welcome results accrued; but Tenancy Reform and Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings have achieved very little. While listing out the reasons for poor performance, we have identified the lack of political will as the single most important cause for the failure of Indian Land Reforms. We have also given a few specific instances to show the lack of political will.

Finally, we have tried to point out the continuing relevance of land reforms. Though in the context of the objective conditions prevailing in the country, land reforms could not play an effective role in bringing about social justice, we have argued that some measures of land reform are still needed to promote fast agricultural growth. We have made some specific suggestions, the most important among them being that the tenancy laws should be amended to permit the leasing out of land. We have digressed a little to comment on the current programmes of rural development and alleviation of poverty in order to bring out the role of land reform in rendering these programmes more effective. In our view, though Land Reform has not been a success so far it should not be abandoned as a lost cause.

![image 43](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile43.png)

![image 44](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile44.png)

![image 45](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile45.png)

Chapter 1

### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence

In this chapter we shall discuss tenurial conditions before independence. To start with, we shall briefly deal with conditions in ancient and medieval times and then discuss the changes introduced during British rule.

###### Ancient India

The evidence available in ancient texts like the Manusmriti, Kautliya’s Artha Shastra and the Buddhist Jatakas indicate that the system of land tenure prevalent in large parts of the country until the fifth century A.D. was essentially of the Raiyatwari or the NonLandlord-type. In most cases cultivators acquired ownership rights as first clearers of the jungle. According to Manu, the first clearer has the right to the land, just as the hunter who first wounded the deer in the chase. There was no intermediary between the state and the cultivator. Nor did the King claim to be the owner of all the land in his kingdom. He was only entitled to a share of the produce as quid pro quo for his giving protection to his subjects. According to a number of authorities the King’s share was one- sixth of the produce. Manu speaks of three rates, £, J and ,l2 depending on the quality of the soil. According to Kautilya, farmers paid an agricultural tax, in kind, at the rate of onesixth of the produce known as “bhaga”. In times of great financial stringency, the tax could be raised to one-quarter or one-third in the case of grains. An edict of Ashoka mentions the reduction of the King’s

![image 47](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile47.png)

![image 48](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile48.png)

###### 2 Land Reforms in India



share from £ to ^ as a favour shown to the birthplace of the Buddha. During the Gupta period the king’s share continued to be J of the produce. The payment was usually made in kind. A hierarchy of government functionaries stationed at the different levels from the provincial headquarters down to the village was responsible for collecting the state’s share.

Right from ancient times Indian rulers followed the practice of giving grants of land to men of learning, courtiers, officials and men who did some signal service to the crown. Where the grant covered land already occupied, what the king gifted away was his share of the produce and not proprietary rights in the soil. The king was not the proprietor of all land in his kingdom and, therefore, he could not gift away the land owned by individuals. The king could, of course, freely make grants out of his own demesne or wasteland. Even in the case of conquest, the property of the conquered in their houses, land and other goods did not pass on to the conqucror. The ownership of the village and field belonged to the respective owners and not to the conqueror whose right was restricted to the collection of the taxes. The parasitic intermediaries who were to become the bane of the Indian agrarian system in a later period had not yet appeared on the scene. Yet the agrarian structure was by no means egalitarian. Though most holdings were small and could be operated by family labour, share-cropping was widespread. Landowners belonging to the so-called upper castes invariably shunned physical labour. Some holdings were, of course, very large, often exceeding a thousand acres in area. The large holdings were cultivated through slaves and hired labour or were let out to sharecroppers. When the share-cropper provided his own bullocks and seeds, his share was one half of the produce. But the share-croppers who contributed labour only were entitled to only \ or \ of the produce. Thus exploitative crop-sharing arrangements have a hoary past in India. The only saving grace was that in ancient India since the pressure of population was light and uncultivated land was plentiful, an enterprising peasant always found it easy to acquire some land of his own. That probably set some limit to rack-renting and other oppressive practiccs resorted to by land owners.

![image 49](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile49.png)

![image 50](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile50.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 3



Though the land tax on cultivated land was generally fixed at ]b of the gross produce, the state also levied various kinds of cesses. There were numerous local levies on all manner of economic activity in the village, on production and consumption, on trades and occupations and on transport. During the unstable conditions that developed after the decline of the Gupta Empire, the demands of the state became more exacting. Rulers and chieftains who exercised power locally raised the government’s share above the traditional ]6 thand added various cesses of an oppressive nature. Even so, the landholder’s rights in his land remained unaffected. The broad contours of the land system were more or less the same in the North and the South, local variations notwithstanding. This is the broad picture of the ancient Indian land tenures that emerges from Kautilya’s Artha Shastra, Baden Powell’s monumental study, “The Land Systems of British India”, and the scholarly report of the Land Revenue Commission of Bengal.1

###### Medieval Period

The advent of Muslim rule did not lead to any great change in the land system. According to Baden Powell “the original theory of Muslim law was that conquered races were to be offered the option between adopting the creed or death or slavery. But this theory soon gave way to the more practical one, that conquered races, if they submitted and agreed to pay tribute (khiraj), were to be let alone. ‘ Respect Tributaries’ said the Prophet; for they are entitled to the same rights and subject to the same laws as Muslims’. The author of Hidaya, (a text book of Mohammadan law) lays down that if a prince conquers a country, he is at liberty either to divide the land among his soldiers, or to leave it in the possession of the inhabitants, on their agreeing to pay capitation (Jaziya) and land taxes; in the latter case, the right of property remains with the inhabitants.....................The author of the Hidaya also has adopted the same rule as the law of Manu asserts, viz. that land is the property of him who first clears it. All Mohammadan jurists agree that the person who first appropriates and cultivates waste land becomes ipso facto the lord of the soil”.2 Thus according to early doctrine, both Hindu and Muslim, the king was by no means the owner of all land in his kingdom.

![image 51](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile51.png)

![image 52](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile52.png)

###### 4 Land Reforms in India



In the Hindu period, as we have seen, the state claimed usually £ of the produce and in emergencies even [ or During the Muslim period, the state’s claim generally varied between \ and £. In Aurangzeb’s time \ became standard. Until the reign of Sher Shah the state’s share was based on seasonal estimates of crop yields. Sher Shah rendered the demand free from fluctuations by basing it on measurement i.e. on the average yield of one bigha of land under different crops, the land taken being also the average of good, and inferior lands of the locality. The demand, however, continued to be paid in kind, as through the ages.3

Akbar converted the crop rates and produce rates on which Sher Shah had based his system into cash rates and replaced payment in kind by payment in cash. The system of land revenue introduced by Akbar’s revenue minister Todar Mai was, as pointed out by Nilakanta Sastri, “a decided improvement on the arbitrary methods of the preceding years and, in some respects, furnished the model for the Survey Settlement of British India. Three salient features characterized Todar Mai’s system: measurement of land, classification of land and fixation of rates.

‘Todar Mai’s system was ryotwari, the collection being made as far as possible from the individual cultivator, and payment being in cash. However, the old system of dividing the crop was retained by Akbar in Kashmir and Sind. Roughly the share of the state was \ of the average yield of the land................The pressure of land revenue upon the individual cultivator must have been heavy, and he had generally little or no margin of profit upon which to fall back in hard times.

“Two salient features of Akbar’s system were abandoned by his successors. These were payment of official salaries in cash and collection of land revenue as far as possible from individual cultivators. The agricultural population thus came to be left at the mercy of various types of assignees and middlemen and the land suffered in consequence. The standard of assessment was also raised from \ to \

of the produce to meet the lavish expenditure of the court at the close of Shahjahan’s reign. That strained the economic system beyond recovery. Bernier, who resided in the empire at the close of Shahjahan’s reign, reports that the land was tilled by only compulsion and irrigation works

![image 53](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile53.png)

![image 54](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile54.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 5



were badly neglected. This state of affairs resulted in Aurangzeb’s order to his assessors to flog the peasantry into cultivating the fields”.4 According to the Cambridge Economic History of India, one major aim of of the Mughal administration was to secure for the state the bulk of the peasants’ surplus. “The shares of the crops taken varied with crops and localities, but one-half in the less fertile regions, and substantially more in in the more fertile seem to have represented the norm.”5

###### Advent of Intermediaries

During the Hindu period, the land tax was collected by a hierarchy of government functionaries. In Akbar’s time this remained true of the Crown lands or Khas mahal. After Akbar’s death the direct collection of land revenue was abandoned even in the crown lands and was replaced by village assessments made with the headman or with farmers as circumstances might permit. Thus well before the onset of the decline of the Mughal dynasty, all over the empire land revenue ceased to be collected by salaried government officers but by a heterogenous group of intermediaries consisting of ruling chiefs, jagirdars, village headmen and revenue farmers.

From the point of view of land revenue administration the Mughal empire could be divided into three distinct types of areas. First there were the Crown lands or Khas Mahal where, as we have seen, during Akbar’s reign the Raiyatwari system developed by Todar Mai was in operation. The second type consisted of areas under ruling chiefs and the third category included areas held by assignees or jagirdars.

The rulers who had been vanquished in battle or had accepted the overlordship of the Emperor, had been left in possession of their domains subject to the payment of tribute to the Emperor. As pointed out by Baden Powell, a number of these old chiefs “though not able to hold their own were quite strong enough to give trouble, and to reappear and head a rebellion on the appearance of the least opportunity. Hence it was a matter of policy to conciliate them by giving titles and still more by leaving them in all their dignity, and with the power of administering justice locally, provided they would consent to pass on a large share of the land revenue they collected to the Imperial treasury”.6 The chiefs continued to collect land revenue from their cultivators according to their traditional methods.

![image 55](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile55.png)

![image 56](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile56.png)

###### 6 Land Reforms in India



The assignment of the king’s share of the produce to religious institutions, men of learning or persons who did some service to the state was a practice with a hoary ancestry. The Hindu rulers made such gifts to temples, men of learning and courtiers. The practice became more widespread in the Muslim period. The Mughal emperors freely resorted to the practice of assigning the land revenue from specified tracts of territory to courtiers and various civil and military functionaries instead of paying them directly from the state exchequer. The assignment was known as a “Jagir” and the person on whom the jagir had been conferred came to be known as a’jagirdar’

In the words of Baden Powell:

the jagir was originally only a life grant. Hereditary nobles did not exist under the Mughal Empire, the Emperor made and unmade dignities at will. When he wished to confer a dignity, he appointed a person as mansabdar of a certain rank, which was estimated according to the number of horsemen he commanded; the jagir was an appanage to the grant of a mansab, and the revenue was appropriated both for the support of the grantee and the maintenance of his troops, which might be from ten to ten thousand. At first the official forms of appointment were minute and carefully followed out..........Clearly under such grants the jagirdar was not in any sense proprietor of the land. Indeed, he was not allowed to collect more from it than the actual amount assigned according to his grade and the terms of the sanad and had to account for all the surplus. In course of time, however, the precautions and rules fell into abeyance, and the jagirdar was allowed to do much as he pleased; and then too it happened that the grant was not resumed on the death of the holder, as it ought to have been, and soon became hereditary.”7 Besides the jagir grant, which was eventually connected with military or state service, there were several other grants which involved remission of land revenue. And then there were the numerous small grants which were made to petty officers like Kanungoes, headmen, Patwaris, guards and watchmen. These grants were conditional on performing certain services. The essential feature of all these grants was the assignment of the land tax in favour of the grantee. The rights of the cultivators in their lands, however, remained unaffected.

![image 57](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile57.png)

![image 58](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile58.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 7



###### Origin of Zamindars

As we have seen, after Akbar’s death all over the Mughal Empire land revenue came to be collected through ruling chiefs, jagirdars, village headmen and revenue farmers. All these people in course of time came to be known as Zamindars, literally meaning “landholders”. According to Baden Powell:

“there probably never was a time when a Mughal governor or emperor deliberately conceived the plan of creating an official collector of rents, or invented as a title, the word “Zamindar” and making a decree or regulation defining their rights and duties. But persons who had a real estate of some kind or degree over villages and districts, were always, from the earliest times of Mohammadan rule, spoken of generically as “Zamindars”, and if they received a warrant or “sanad” from the ruling power, for any purpose, it would probably speak of them as being (official) Zamindars. If they were people of minor importance, they would be called ‘talukdar’—holder of a portion of land—a ‘dependency’ as the word implies, not a great and independent estate. Persons recognized as ‘Zamindars’ and some of the superior ‘talukdars’ were no doubt allowed to collect themselves, and to pay in direct, the revenue of their territories. The rest of the country was managed solely by State officers who collected through heads of villages from cultivators. The Mughal system, it should always be borne in mind (with the exception of the country held on service grants or by such local magnates as it was politic to recognize), was essentially raiyatwari; it went straight to the cultivator through the headman of each village. The original system then did not countenance farming the revenues; so that chiefs and others (recognized landholders) would not then have been known by any particular name or official status. Probably the degree of actual power which the landholder had in managing his estate, varied with the wealth, respectability and influence of each chief or grantee and especially his nearness to, or remoteness from the centre of control. But it would seem that when the Emperor Farukhsiyar ascended the throne in 1713 A.D., the decline of the Empire had already begun, and decline was always marked by relaxation of control, not only over the outlying provinces, but over the whole administrative machinery, and by the substitution of plans of farming the revenues of convenient tracts. Then it was that besides the

![image 59](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile59.png)

![image 60](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile60.png)

###### 8 Land Reforms in India



Rajas, chiefs and ancient grantees, who had a real hold over the country, and were already spoken of as the Zamindars, other classes of persons were employed as farmers, and the same name and the same designation came to be applied to them also. As a matter of fact we find tax officials possessed of wealth and energy—‘amils, karoris, etc.also bankers and court favorites, receiving the name of Zamindar.”8

Revenue Farming

Revenue fanning which had commenced in a small way soon after the death of Akbar became rampant during the decline of the empire. This is how Baden Powell describes the situation:

“The land-tenures were really directly affected when the Mughal Government began to decline. Then it was that Viceroys like those of Oudh, Bengal and the Dakhan (Hyderabad) threw off their allegiance and became independent kings............

“The independent kingdoms did not have a very prosperous course. Before long decay and corruption began to invade every department of the State. Under such a state of things honesty was hardly to be looked for in the local revenue collectors; and the land revenue fell off. No doubt the Central government- as from time to time it fell into the hands of a more vigorous ruler- made desperate efforts to reassert a proper control over the district collectors, but in vain. The device, to save trouble and secure at least a certain revenue, was to employ local agents over greater or lesser areas of the country, and to contract for the revenues of those areas. At first such agents were carefully appointed, and with much form; lists were made out of the villages in their charge; and they were bound to account for all they collected; except that they were allowed certain lands revenue-free, certain items might be deducted for special charges (as office expenses, alms and police) and a certain share, usually one-tenth of the total revenue, as their own remuneration-denominated Nankar, that whereby they made (kar) their bread (nan).

But as time went on, these agents were less and less controlled, and they soon became mere contractors for fixed total sums; and the local officers had no power whatever over them, and finally disappeared before them. No one in fact knew (or cared) what was actually wrung out of the villagers, so long as the contract sum was

![image 61](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile61.png)

![image 62](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile62.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 9



paid into the treasury. Nor was this a fixed sum. Instead of a careful survey and revaluation of lands, the rough expedient was adopted of adding “cesses” (abwab) to the sum demanded from the agents, and so raising the total. These cesses were called by various names indicating the pretext under which they were levied. The agents of course had in their turn to make good the additional demand from the villages, and took the opportunity of adding a number of further cesses for their private benefit on the strength of the example thus set them”.

The system of revenue farming was rampant in Bengal. It was also adopted in Oudh, the North-West Provinces and the northern districts of Madras. But it never extended to the other parts of Madras, the Deccan, Bombay and the Punjab.

###### Rise of the East India Company

It is interesting to note that the East India Company which came to occupy a dominant position in India as the arbiter of political affairs in the second half of the eighteenth century had a rather humble beginning as a petty revenue farmer in Bengal. In 1698 the East India Company obtained from the Nawab (Subedar) of Bengal permission to purchase the Zamindari rights over the villages of Calcutta, Sutnati and Govindpur at an annual rental of Rs. 1,195/-. The company gave a present of Rs. 16,000/- to the Nawab and paid Rs. 1,300/- to the Zamindar as purchase price. By virtue of these transactions the East India Company acquired the right to collect rent from the raiyats, to dispose of waste lands in any way they liked and to impose petty taxes, duties and fines. In 1757 Nawab Siraj-ud-doula assigned to the company 38 villages lying to the south of Calcutta. At the end of the same year, after the battle of Plassey, Mir Jaffar made over to the company a tract of 822 square miles which came to be known as 24 parganas. The company became the Zamindar of this area on agreeing to pay annually a sum of Rs. 2,22,958 to the Nawab. In 1759 the revenue of 24 Parganas was assigned as a jagir to Robert Clive in return for services rendered to the Mughal Emperor. The grant was confirmed for ten years in 1765 with the provision that after that period the rights would pass to the East India Company free of all revenue charges due to the Emperor. As a price for his elevation as Nawab in 1760 the company secured from Kasim Ali Khan free of revenue a

![image 63](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile63.png)

![image 64](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile64.png)

###### 10 Land Reforms in India



large tract of territory comprising the districts of Burdwan, Midnapore and Chittagong. The grant was confirmed by the Mughal Emperor in 1765. The acquisition of Zamindari rights in these districts along with the earlier acquisitions gave to the East India Company a secure footing in the Indian sub-continent.10

Under the Mughal system the Subedar or Governor was the Commander-in-chief and Chief Administrator of the province. Akbar created the office of Dewan to look after Revenue functions. In course of time the office of Dewan became a very powerful one controlling the finances of the province. In 1765 Clive secured from Emperor Shah Alam the Dewani of Bengal, Bihar and Orissa for the East India Company in return for an annual payment of Rs. 26 lakhs to the Emperor. This meant that the company became the representative of the Emperor and acquired the right to collect revenue from the provinces. The jurisdiction of the Nawab was confined to the political, military and criminal matters. The Nawab received an allowance of Rs. 53 lakhs a year for meeting the expenses of the administration and the maintenance of his position.11

###### Events leading to Permanent Settlement

Having acquired the Dewani of Bengal, Bihar and Orissa, the grasping mercenaries in charge of the affairs of the East India Company proceeded to bleed the country white by pitching the land revenue assessments at the highest possible level. For some years after 1765 the East India Company maintained the old revenue system. But in 1772 the Company decided to make direct collections with the objective of exacting as much as possible. A committee of circuit was constituted to tour the provinces and make settlements by auction to the highest bidders for a period of five years. The new system had disastrous consequences for the old Zamindars as well as the raiyats. As summed up by the Land Revenue Commission of Bengal; “the old Zamindars and farmers had to bid up to a higher revenue than their estates could bear, if they wished to retain possession. Speculators stepped in who overbid some of the existing Zamindars and farmers, and could only realize the stipulated revenue by squeezing the last farthing out of the raiyats. The result was that at the expiry of the period of settlement the estates were left in an exhausted condition.

![image 65](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile65.png)

![image 66](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile66.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 11



Arrears accumulated and decreasing collections of revenue so impressed the authorities in India as well as the directors in England, that steps had to be taken to evolve a more satisfactory system-’*12 After the failure of the five year farming system the East India Company experimented for a while with the system of annual settlement with Zamindars. That also did not fare any better. The investigations and discussions that followed culminated in the Decennial settlement of 1789-93. In 1793 the settlement was declared permanent.

###### Permanent Settlement

The Land Revenue Commission of Bengal succinctly describes the Permanent Settlement thus:

“The Permanent Settlement recognized that the East India Company, in its capacity of sovereign authority, and the Zamindars or farmers through whom the state’s share had been collected, had a joint interest in the share of the produce from every acre of land which was the traditional property of the state. It fixed the revenue ten-eleventh of the assets, which left to the Zamindar one-tenth of the revenue which they paid. In addition the Zamindars were to be given the benefit of any future increase in the value of the state’s share which might result from the extension of cultivation or from other causes, and the state promised not to make any demand for the augmentation of the public assessment in consequence of the improvement of their respective estates. In other words, the state declared that it would for ever afterwards be content with the sum thus assessed as the cash revenue of each estate and the property that it gave to the Zamindars consisted of one tenth of the State’s share, plus the increment that might be derived from the extension of cultivation or other causes. In addition, the Zamindars were declared to be proprietors of the soil, and were deliberately given all property rights which could not be proved to be an encroachment on the prescriptive or customary rights of the tenants. These rights were not defined in the regulations because on the information then available to the authors of Permanent Settlement, it proved impossible to do so. They were content to reserve to the State the right at any time to legislate for the benefit and protection of the

![image 67](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile67.png)

![image 68](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile68.png)

###### 12 Land Reforms in India



tenants, and to instruct the Zamindars to treat them with justice and moderation.

“At the same time the Zamindars were made liable to have their estates sold, if the revenue was not paid by sunset of the latest day fixed for each instalment, and no excuses, such as drought or famine, were to be accepted for non-payment”.13 Thus the Permanent Settlement was so called because the assessments fixed under the settlement Rules of 1789-93 were declared to be unalterable for ever. As pointed out by Baden Powell:

“The settlement Rules of 1789-93 laid down separate principles of assessment for Bengal, for Bihar and for Orissa. In Bengal the actual revenue of the preceding year, or some year nearly preceding was to furnish the standard of assessment. In Bihar the standard was to be the average produce of land in any ordinary year, which would give a fair and equitable assessment. If any land had paid the same revenue for twelve years past, that was to be accepted as the settlement rate”.14

The application of these differing principles, however, led to the same result—that of pitching of land revenue at arbitrarily high levels. The following data compiled by R. Palme Dutt show how unreasonably high was the assessment.

“In Bengal, the land revenue in the last year of the administration of the Mughal agents, in 1764-65, totalled 818,000 Pounds. In the first year of the East India Company’s taking over the financial administration, in 1765-66, it was raised to 1,470,000 Pounds. When the Permanent Settlement was established for Bengal in 1793 the figure was 3,091,000 Pounds”.15

Palme Dutt’s grand uncle, Romesh Chandra Dutt, our first economic historian, had come to the same conclusion about the severity of the land tax. In his words “sometimes the land tax approximated to the whole of the economic rent leaving the cultivators little beyond the wages of their labour and the profits of their agricultural stock”.16

###### The Consequences of Permanent Settlement

The Permanent Settlement had superbly served the short-term fiscal needs of the East India Company and the long term political interests of the British Empire. It, however, ruined millions of actual cultivators and produced disastrous social, economic and political

![image 69](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile69.png)

![image 70](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile70.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 13



consequences for Eastern India, from the baneful effects of which the region has not fully recovered even after four decades of land reform and planned economic development.

The first evil effect of the Permanent Settlement was that it elevated a class of tax-farmers to the exalted position of landlords with absolute proprietary rights in land. The defence put forward on behalf of Lord Cornwallis and his advisers runs like.this. “Their only conception of land holding was embodied in the English landlord with his tenants’*.17 They found that the Zamindar was like a landlord in certain respects, and they had no knowledge of the various rights in land or of long standing local customs and practices. In the context of the unsettled conditions prevailing in the country, Cornwallis could not have done anything else. In other words, it was all an innocent mistake. As Vera Anstey put it:

“At first the complicated Indian system was a closed book to the servants of the company. They began the search for the landlord. It subsequently appeared that in most cases the Zamindars had. not previously been owners of the land at all. At the time they were mistaken for “landlords’* in the English sense”.18

The record is, however, set right by Palme Dutt. “This fairy tale is plain nonsense. A consultation of the documents of the time makes abundantly clear that Lord Cornwallis and the statesmen concerned were perfectly conscious that they were creating a new class of landlords, and of their purpose in doing it.**

“The purpose of the Permanent Settlement was to create a new class of landlords, after the English model as the social buttress of British rule. It was recognized that, with the small numbers of English, holding down a vast population, it was absolutely necessary to establish a social basis for their power through the creation of a social class whose interests, through receiving a subsidiary share in the spoils, would be bound up in the maintenance of English rule. Lord Cornwallis, in the memorandum in which he defended his policy made clear that he was explicitly conscious that he was creating a new class, and establishing rights which bore no relation to the previous rights of the Zamindars; he was, he stated, “convinced that, failing the claim of right of the Zamindars, it would be necessary for the public good to grant a right of property in the soil to them, or to persons of other

![image 71](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile71.png)

![image 72](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile72.png)

###### 14 Land Reforms in India



descriptions.” Lord William Bentick, Governor General of India from 1838 to 1835, in an official speech during his term of office described with exemplary clearness the purpose of Permanent Settlement as a bulwark against revolution:

“If security was wanting against extensive popular tumult or revolution, I should say that the Permanent Settlement, though a failure in many other respects and in most important essentials, has this great advantage at least, of having created a vast body of rich landed proprietors deeply interested in the continuance of the British Dominion and having complete command over the mass of the people.”19 This alliance of British rule with landlordism created largely by its own acts continues today.”20

Another, and decidedly a more important and devastating, consequence of the Permanent Settlement was that millions of raiyats enjoying permanent and heritable rights in their lands were reduced to the status of tenants-at-will. Even Baden Powell concedes:

“Grievous as the failure of Permanent Settlement has been, its failure is not due to the fact that Zamindars were confirmed, or that, in the unavoidable necessity of defining and securing their position in English legal documents, they were called and made, landlords. The evil consisted in this, that their right was not limited with regard to all the older raiyats, leaving new-comers to be in principle—contract tenants.21

Baden Powell does not, of course fail to point out that the rights of the tenants were never intended to be forgotten. But then, the pious intentions of the authors of the Permanent Settlement were of little avail to the tenantry. For, no effective step was taken to regulate rent and protect the rights of the tenants. As Baden Powell himself concedes, the measures contemplated by Government for the protection of tenants “were theoretically inadequate and in the course of actual events they proved absolutely futile.”22

Regulation VII of 1793 had enunciated the principle that no Zamindar would be allowed to infringe on the customary rights of the cultivators. Even at the policy level, the principle was of limited application. For the contemplated protection was to be limited to khudkasht raiyats (resident cultivators). The rights of the large mass of

![image 73](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile73.png)

![image 74](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile74.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 15



paikasht raiyats (non-resident tenants) and under-tenants were completely ignored.

The method by which the framers of Permanent Settlement sought to protect Khudkasht raiyats was by requiring Zamindars to grant pattas to those tenants stating the terms and conditions of the tenancy. The rent was to be fixed on the basis of customary pargana rates which it was not easy to ascertain. For, as Baden Powell points out “reassessments were periodically made or rather, virtual additions to the old rates were covered by the irregular expedient of ‘cesses’ and “benevolences” (abwab etc.)” The pargana rates were by no means uniform. The same village often had several rates. And even in the same village there were different rates for the same type of land depending on who the tenant was. The pargana rates being vague and variable it was easy for the Zamindar to pitch the rent so high that no tenant would come forward to execute a contract and accept the patta. The position was summed up by the Land Revenue Commission of Bengal thus:

“One of the provisions by which it was hoped to assure the customary rights of the khudkasht raiyats was the issue by the Zamindars of pattas stating the rent payable by each raiyat. But the Patta Regulation, as it was called, was a complete failure------

“The Zamindars thought it against their interests to limit their claims; while the raiyats apprehended that they might be evicted at the expiry of the 10 years prescribed by the Patta. To remove the deadlock which ensued it was enacted that if the raiyats refused to receive pattas which were in the proper form, a legal tender of the patta might be made by the Zamindars on a notified date, and when that had been done the Zamindar could recover the rent claimed from the raiyat through the courts, or by distraint.------The effect of this provision was to throw on the raiyats the onus of proving what were the customary paragana rates, and to produce a huge volume of litigation, with which the courts were unable to deal. Some of the raiyats abandoned their holdings and looked for land elsewhere; others began to withhold payment of rent”.23

In its anxiety to secure government revenues the government armed the Zamindars with arbitrary powers of distraint which naturally led to the widespread ejectment of tenants. Until the enactment of the

![image 75](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile75.png)

![image 76](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile76.png)

###### 16 Land Reforms in India



Bengal Tenancy Act of 1885 the tenants remained without effective protection. Thus for almost a century from the date of the Permanent Settlement the raiyats were left to the tender mercies of rapacious Zamindars who had also been armed with draconian powers. In the result, millions of raiyats who had enjoyed permanent and hereditary rights for generations were either rendered landless or reduced to the status of tenants-at-will.

As a corollary of the two consequences of Permanent Settlement discussed above, there followed a third and more far-reaching outcome,- a drastic transformation of the socio-economic character of the rural society. Probably there was no time in recorded history when the Indian rural society was truly egalitarian. Disparities in income and wealth always existed; opulence and dire poverty existed side by side. Even so, in between these two extremes there existed a large mass of sturdy owner-cultivators who enjoyed permanent rights in their land. No doubt from time to time their land was laid waste by conquering hordes if they happened to pass that way and often the farming community was subjected to inhuman treatment by marauders and free booters. But after the depredations had passed, within a few years the fanning community would bounce back to normalcy. By and large, the cultivators remained secure on their land and their only obligation was to pay land tax to whoever was the ruler. What the British did was to obliterate at one stroke all the customary rights of the agricultural class, reduce them to the level of tenants-at-will and hand them over to the tender mercies of a new class of landlords whose ugly characteristics we have seen. Ever new forms of exploitation and tyranny were devised to syphon off all available surplus from the farming community leading to a marked deterioration in the economic condition and social st^us of the raiyats. Class cleavages got accentuated. Soon enough a wide gap developed between the small number of Zamindars at the top and the large mass of impecunious and insecure tenants at the bottom. With the annihilation of the middle rungs of the rural society it became easier for the Zamindars to impose their will on the rural population as a whole and coerce them into submission. Subjected as they were to numerous inequities and indignities day in and day out, the large mass of the rural poor in Eastern India remained submissive and forlorn for a long time, and the rural society became even more inegalitarian than it

![image 77](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile77.png)

![image 78](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile78.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 17



had been in the pre-British days. Efforts at tenancy reform starting with the Bengal Tenancy Act of 1885, the other measures of land reform attempted since independence and the various programmes for rural envelopment designed during the last 45 years have not yet completely neutralized the deadly impact of Permanent Settlement on rural society in parts of Eastern India like Bihar and Eastern Uttar Pradesh. Part of the explanation for the sad state of the rural society in this area highlighted by pervasive violence and inhuman outrages against the weaker sections lies in the events discussed above. In recent years the rural poor of West Bengal have managed to escape from similar hardship thanks to radical political mobilization.

Permanent Settlement had a deleterious effect on the development of an adequate administrative set up as well. Whereas in other parts of India an elaborate administrative structure was set up with paid government functionaries at the village, taluk, sub-divisional and district levels, in the areas covered by Permanent Settlement, until the fifties of the present century, the lowest level of revenue and general administration was the Sub-Division. This happened because historically the organization of the administrative structure was related to the need to assess and collect land revenue. As under Permanent Settlement the land revenue had been fixed for all time and there was a harsh law requiring Zamindars to pay land revenue on the appointed day, it was felt that there was no need to establish an elaborate administrative set up as in the other parts of the country. In the result the system of meticulously prepared and annually revised land records adopted in the areas that came under British rule later on was unknown to areas covered by the Permanent Settlement. The only land records were those prepared at the time of Survey and Settlement, which would be looked into again only at the time of Re visional Survey. As there was no revenue functionary below the Sub-Divisional level, the compilation of agricultural statistics was left to the officer-in-charge of the Police Station who did it on the basis of the utterly unreliable data supplied to him by the illiterate village watchmen. All this has, of course, changed since independence. Even so, in some parts of Eastern India the administrative structure at the lowest level still continues to be weak because of the unwieldy jurisdiction of the functionary.

![image 79](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile79.png)

![image 80](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile80.png)

###### 18 Land Reforms in India



Incidentally, the absence of an elaborate administrative structure also made it easy for the Zamindars to impose their will on the tenantry. The Sub-Divisional headquarters being far away, the Zamindars could do much as they pleased, undisturbed by the prying eyes of government functionaries, some of whom could be conscientious men who took their work seriously and discharged their duties sincerely.

###### Subinfeudation

At the time of the Permanent Settlement and for some years after that land revenue was a heavy burden on the estates. But in course of time, with the expansion of cultivation, several estates began to yield large surpluses. The Zamindars found that they could make considerable profits and also save themselves the trouble of managing the estates by farming out the right to collect rent from the raiyats. As Baden Powell put it:

“These tenures are due partly to the desire of improving the estates by handing them over to the more energetic management which a lessee would give, and partly the effect of prosperity and the desire to save trouble. In either case a time came when the landlords began to create permanent subordinate tenures; by this means they escaped not only the labour and risks attendant upon direct management, but were successful in bringing large tracts of wasteland under cultivation.... Considerable portion of estates have thus been conveyed, in perpetuity, by Zamindars in consideration of a bonus paid down and of a fixed annual rent. This rent is calculated so as to leave the lessee a margin of profit over and above the sum payable to the Zamindar and the revenue payable to the government- a margin which it depends on the lessee’s skill and ability to make more and more considerable”.24 The lessee’s “skill land ability” was, of course, a euphemism for his cunning and ruthlessness in exacting the last paisa from the insecure tenantry.

The commonest tenure of this kind to develop in Bengal initially was known as the Patni-taluk. A Patni-taluk was heritable and transferable, and all the rights of the Zamindar were transferred by the grant. The margin left to the Patnidar was often so considerable that the Patnidar, in his turn, could divest himself of the management of the estate, and content himself with a fixed sum, subletting the actual rental

![image 81](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile81.png)

![image 82](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile82.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 19



to persons called dar-patnidars. And such sub-letting could be in respect of the whole estate or a fractional share of the estate. In course of time, this kind of subletting or subinfeudation continued several degrees lower. As summed up by the Land Revenue Commission of Bengal:

“One of the most serious defects of the system is the excessive amount of subinfeudation it has encouraged. The margin between the land revenue and the economic rent of the land has permitted the creation of a number of intermediate interests between the Zamindar and the actual cultivator which in some districts has reached fantastic proportions. The report of the Simon Commission pointed out that in some cases as many as 50 or more intermediate interests have been created between the Zamindar at the top and the actual cultivator at the bottom. This has resulted in dissipating the responsibility for the best use of the land in the national interest among a host of rent-receivers, all of whom have to be supported by the labour of the cultivator, and none of whom have either the incentive or the power to exercise any control over the use of the land. It is not too much to say that the extent of subinfeudation has become an incubus on the working agricultural population, which finds no justification in the performance of any material service so far as agricultural improvements are concerned, and fails to provide any effective means for the development of the resources of the land, which is the greatest asset of the Province...”25

###### The land revenue systems of other Provinces

The Permanent Settlement covered most parts of Eastern India and North Madras. The Permanent Settlement was so called because the land revenue payable by each estate was fixed for all time. Before we proceed further it would be useful to explain the term “settlement”. As Baden Powell puts it: “The process by which the government officials determine the amount of land revenue payable, is called settlement (of land revenue), and the person or the body whom government recognizes as entitled to be proprietor, subject to the land revenue payment, is said to be “settled with” or to “hold the setdement”26

We have seen the evil consequences of the Permanent Settlement from the point of view of the cultivators and the rural society. Soon enough the East India company also realized that it was not in its

![image 83](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile83.png)

![image 84](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile84.png)

###### 20 Land Reforms in India



interest to fix the land revenue in perpetuity. So when new provinces were annexed the mistake made in Bengal was not repeated. The settlements made in later years, whether they be with various types of landlords or individual raiyats, were all temporary. In other words, the settlements were for fixed periods at the end of which government could enhance the land revenue. The settlements made in the different provinces were of two broad types: Zamindari or Raiyatwari. The Zamindari settlements, again, were of two kinds; Permanent or Temporary. Thus, there were in all three types of settlements; Permanent Zamindari settlement, Temporary Zamindari settlement and Raiyatwari settlement, which was always temporary.

As we have already seen, the Permanent Settlement with the Zamindars covered Bengal, Bihar, East Uttar Pradesh, North Madras and parts of Assam and Orissa. (It may be pointed out that there were temporarily settled Zamindari estates even in these provinces). The rest of Uttar Pradesh, the Punjab and parts of the erstwhile Central Provinces came under temporary Zamindari settlement. In these areas the intermediaries were not always known as Zamindars. In Uttar Pradesh the settlement was made with intermediaries known as Zamindars or Talukdars and in some areas the settlement was made with the village landlord community. In Punjab the settlement was with Zamindars or village communities. When the settlement was made with the Village Community of landlords it was known as a Mahalwari settlement. In Central Provinces the government conferred proprietary rights on the village tax collectors known as Malguzars and the settlement with them was known as Malguzari Settlement.

The Raiyatwari system covered the erstwhile Madras and Bombay Presidencies (Of course, leaving out North Madras) and parts of the then Central Provinces and Berar. The Raiyatwari system was based on the assessment of land revenue on “single fields or holdings, surveyed, numbered and marked out on the ground”.27

###### Temporary Zamindari Settlements

The underlying objective of the Temporary Zamindari Settlement and its consequences were not dissimilar to those of Permanent Settlement. The exalted position accorded to the intermediaries and the fate of the actual cultivators of the soil in Avadh after the advent of

![image 85](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile85.png)
