![image 86](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile86.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 21



British rule demonstrate the correctness of this view. In fact this was also the conclusion of the United Provinces Zamindari Abolition Committee which submitted its report in 1948.

Uttar Pradesh could be demarcated into three distinct areas from the point of view of the system of land revenue administration. These areas were:

- 1. The Permanently settled areas consisting of the districts of Banaras, Jaunpur, Ghazipur, Ballia and parts of Mirzapur and Azamgarh.
- 2. The Ceded and Conquered districts comprising the rest of the Province excluding Avadh.
- 3. The Province of Avadh acquired in 1856. In the words of the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee: “But though these three groups of territories acquired by the


British at different periods display a wide diversity in details of revenue administration and the methods and principles of settlement of revenue, the effects in each case were more or less the same. The early settlements and the imposition of the British Judicial System, with the complexities and intricacies of which the people were unfamiliar and which placed the wealthy and educated in a position of advantage over the poor and ignorant, led everywhere to the disintegration of the village communities, the disappearance of the co-operative spirit and its substitution by perpetual discord and bitter strife between different classes. In the struggle that ensued, the customary rights of cultivators acquired by immemorial usage were completely destroyed....Millions of people were, by these settlements, deprived of rights that they had enjoyed for well over two thousand years, hereditary cultivating proprietors of land were turned into rack-rented tenants-at-will, and conditions were thus created that led to continuous social discord and economic deterioration and the decay of agriculture, the most important of India’s industries and the main occupation and source of livelihood of its people.”28

A cursory look at the vicissitudes in the fortunes of the Talukdars of Avadh lays bare the motives of the British rulers in conferring unlimited powers on the new class of landlords that they created. The Talukdars of Avadh, like the Zamindars of Bengal, had a diverse origin. A handful were representatives of ancient families or recipients

![image 87](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile87.png)

![image 88](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile88.png)

###### 22 Land Reforms in India



of titles from the Nawab of Avadh. But the great majority were tax collectors or revenue farmers variously designated as Chakladars, Nazims or Amils. They waxed strong during the rule of successive weak Nawabs and they even built fortresses and started maintaining numerous retainers. Independent village proprietors and proprietary village communities, which could not, because of their weakness, withstand the oppressive demands of the Talukdars surrendered some of their rights to the Talukdars in exchange for the protection that the latter could extend in those unsettled times. As the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee put it:

“The talukdari estates were further increased by:

- (i) The talukdar’s forcible encroachment on the land of his weaker neighbours;
- (ii) The adoption of fraudulent means,
- (iii) sale deeds obtained by force
- (iv) forced sales by auction for arrears of revenue, and bona fide sales for payment of revenue”.29


Sir William Sleeman, the British Resident in Avadh estimated that by 1850 four- fifths of the Crown (Khalsa) lands had been grabbed by the bigger landlords from their weaker neighbours who had previously held the land directly from the state. And having grabbed the land, the talukdars started by paying to the government less than the revenue payable by the original cultivators. Such was the land system of Avadh when the territory was annexed by Lord Dalhousie. Having been aware of the state of affairs, in a letter dated February 14, 1856 Lord Dalhousie issued the following instructions defining the land policy to be adopted:

“The settlement should be made village by village with the parties actually in possession, but without any recognition, either formal or indirect, of the proprietary right....**

“It must be borne in mind, as a leading principle, that the desire and the intention of the government is to deal with the actual occupants of the soil; that is, with village Zamindar or with the proprietary coparcener, which are believed to exist in Oudh and not to suffer the interposition of middlemen as Talooqdars, farmers of revenue and such '.ike.."30

![image 89](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile89.png)

![image 90](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile90.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 23



Accordingly, in the summary settlement of 1856 only about 60 per cent of the land was settled with talukdars and the rest was settled with persons other than talukdars. Though the talukdars did not fare badly on the whole, in some cases they lost the bulk of the land they had grabbed. That provoked many talukdars to take up arms against the British in the rebellion of 1857. During the rebellion the talukdars regained most of the villages that had been taken away from them. After the rebellion was crushed, the British Government consciously adopted a conciliatory policy towards the talukdars and initiated several steps to build them up as the props of the Imperial power. Of course, Lord Canning who adopted that policy was fully aware of the origins and the role of the talukdars in revenue administration. This is evident from the following observation made by Lord Canning in January 1858:

“When we assumed the Government of Oudh in 1858, the greater part of the province was held by the talookdars who represented it. They have been called “Barons of Oude”. But this term applied to them as a class is misleading. Some had received titles from the Kings of Oude, for services rendered, or by Court favour, some few are the representatives of ancient families; but the majority are men distinguished neither by birth, good service or connection with the soil, who having held office under the native government as Nazims (i.e. governors) or Chukladars (i.e. collectors of government rents) or having farmed the revenues of extensive tracts, had taken advantage of the weakness of the native government and its indifference to all considerations of justice, so long as it received revenue, had abused the authority confided to them by that government, and by means of deeds of sale, sometimes extorted by violence, sometimes obtained by fraud, had become the nominal proprietors and the actual possessors of the villages, or the majority of the villages which formed what they called their talookas or estates.

“Owing to the ascendancy which the men of this class acquired, the weakness of the native government, the venality of the courts, and the absence of justice, the condition of the actual occupants of the soil of the province was one of unparalleled depression. Their rights had ceased to exist or were reduced to a mere shadow: they were completely in the power of the talookdars and were subject to every

![image 91](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile91.png)

![image 92](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile92.png)

###### 24 Land Reforms in India



kind of oppression, tyranny and exaction”.31 It is impossible to imagine greater awareness of the nature of the origins and rights of the talookdars and the rights of the actual cultivators and the gross injustice done to them. Yet, bearing in mind the interests of the Imperial power and the need to create a social base for that power, Lord Canning ordered that a settlement be made with the talukdars. And, as in Bengal, the actual cultivators were left unprotected in spite of the tender concern for them expressed in numerous official minutes and memoranda. In the years that followed, as pointed out by the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee.

“The talukdars won all along the line. The rights of underproprietors were recognized but the rights of occupancy and nonproprietary cultivators were ignored....Thus was finally concluded the vexed question of tenant-right in Avadh to the detriment of the vast masses of the cultivators who were deprived of their long established occupancy rights at customary rates of rent.32 Regarding the rights of tenants in Uttar Pradesh the Committee’s conclusion was:

“The necessity for regulating the relations of landlords and tenants was recognized before the introduction of the Permanent Settlement in 1793. It is an amazing fact that effective protection was not given to tenants until the Congress Government passed the U.P. Tenancy Act in 1939, nearly a hundred and fifty years afterwards”.33

In the Punjab and the districts of Western Uttar Pradesh, the settlement was made with Village Communities of landlords. The Punjab had few big zamindars. In that province settlement was made mostly with joint village proprietors. In essence this was also a zamindari settlement. The only difference was that instead of a single individual being the intermediary, the village community became the intermediary and accepted the responsibility for payment of land revenue to the government. The settlement was invariably with small, compact groups, mostly belonging to the upper castes. The actual tillers of the soil were insecure tenants except that the Punjab had a large percentage of owner cultivators as well.

As pointed out by Baden Powell: “There was this essential difference between the Punjab and the North-Western Provinces (i.e. modem U.P.)—that, while their village systems are much alike, the constitution of agricultural society is

![image 93](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile93.png)

![image 94](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile94.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 25



different; the bulk of the land is not cultivated by tenants. Petty proprietors, Co-sharers in village estates-holding fractional shares or according to some other rule-cultivate their own lands, with their sons and cousins, and often their wives, and where, owing to caste and other circumstances, tenants are employed, those tenants often pay no rent at all-merely share the revenue burden with the 61 proprietary families- or else pay rent in kind”.34

In Central Provinces and Berar the Marathas had introduced a land revenue system making revenue farmers known as Malguzars responsible for the collection of land revenue from landholders and remitting it to the government. The British administration elevated the malguzars to the status of landlords and the settlement was made with them. Hence it came to be known as a Malguzari settlement. Clearly that was also a Zamindari settlement. The only saving grace, as pointed out by the Uttar Pradesh Zamindari Abolition Committee, was that:

“While the growth of tenancy tenure in the Central Provinces and Berar under the British rule followed the pattern common to the other provinces of British India, the rights of cultivators in that province were recognized earlier and more effectively safeguarded than in other provinces partly due to the fact that the Central Provinces was acquired later, and the revenue authorities had acquired some experience and knowledge of the customary rights of the cultivators” 35

###### Raiyatwari Settlements

As in Bengal, so in Madras, the East India Company commenced its highly successful career in empire building in a small way by acquiring Zamindari rights over a few villages comprising Madras and its neighborhood. The Nawab of the Kamatic granted those villages as a jagir to the company in return for the military aid given to the Nawab in his wars. The grants were made piecemeal between 1750 and 1760 and they were confirmed by Emperor Shah Alam in 1763. Two years later the East India Company acquired the area known as the Northern Circars extending from Ganjam to Guntur, the former in modem Orissa, and the latter in Andhra Pradesh. These are the areas of North Madras that came under the Permanent Settlement.

In the other districts of the Madras Presidency that came under British rule subsequently, a number of experiments including

![image 95](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile95.png)

![image 96](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile96.png)

###### 26 Land Reforms in India



Zamindari settlements were tried. All those arrangements having proved unsatisfactory, finally a Raiyatwari settlement was adopted. It may, however, be pointed out that in these districts also a few Zamindari settlements survived here and there.

The essence of the Raiyatwari system was the assessment of each field separately and settlement with numerous landholders. Baden Powell lays emphasis on this vital difference between Zamindari and Raiyatwari settlements:

“In the settlement of Northern India there is something like a bargain struck between the landowner (or the village body) and the government officer. The assessment is announced, of course, by the officer after calculations according to his own rules and methods; but if the landowner objects, he is heard and may be able to sustain his plea for an abatement, so that there is really a ‘settlement’ in that sense.

“But in Madras (and so in Bombay) the system does not involve the preliminary determination of any person to be the ‘proprietor’ and to accept the settlement terms; it goes straight to the land and determines, according to its own rules and principles what that field or survey-unit ought to pay, no matter who holds it..............It is the essential feature of the Raiyatwari settlement that every raiyat is free to hold or to relinquish whatever fields of his holding he likes, or to ask for other available numbers, provided all is done by a certain date..........So it is a feature of the Raiyatwari system, that besides the initial assessment, of the land revenue, there is an annual ‘jamabandi’ or settling up with the cultivator, taking account of any change in his holding as shown in his patta and noting deductions (if the system allows any) from his total payment”.36

The real difference, then, is this. In the Zamindari settlement, the government stood forth as the supreme or ultimate landlord and the Zamindar was made the sub-ordinate or limited proprietor. In the Raiyatwari settlement government was the sole landlord.

We propose to leave untouched the lively controversy illuminated by numerous scholarly minutes recorded by a galaxy of distinguished British administrators of the time about the relative merits of the different systems. Suffice it to say that all the systems had the same goal, - to extort as much as possible in the shape of land revenue. The differences of opinion were largely confined to the exact methods that

![image 97](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile97.png)

![image 98](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile98.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 27



should be adopted to sub-serve that goal. Occasionally, one would, of course, come across an administrator who had qualms of conscience about the inequity of the system. But such dissenting voices were no more than cries in the wilderness. With the blessings and active encouragement of the Court of Directors of the East India Company in London, the administrators of British India through the diverse systems of land revenue devised by them ensured that the impoverished Indian cultivator was left with nothing beyond bare subsistence.

Baden Powell himself considered the early assessments to have been unreasonably high. The government share was pitched at ‘‘half the produce of ‘panja’ or dry land (unirrigated) and three-fifths of ‘nanja’ or ‘wet’ i.e. irrigated land, the three-fifths being reduced when there was not a full water supply. Making the necessary allowances, however, for the deductions of the ‘mera’ or grain share by which the village artisans were remunerated, and for the vicissitudes of the season, the actual shares were one-third for dry and two -fifths for wet lands’*.37 These proportions had been calculated in kind. In other words, in the initial years, the government treated all owner-cultivators as tenants-at-will and extorted from them as much as ruthless landowners have always wrung out of their bataidars or bargardars. The shares originally calculated in kind were subsequently commuted into cash. As little consideration was given to seasonal fluctuations in the years in which the crops were below average, the burden of land revenue became crippling indeed and the fate of the cultivators turned out to be worse than that of ordinary share-croppers.

To start with, the assessment under the Riayatwari system was so burdensome that even a conservative body like the Madras Board of Revenue made the following harsh comment on the working of the system:

“Ignorant of the true resources of the newly acquired countries, as of the precise nature of their landed tenures, we find a small band of foreign conquerors, no sooner obtaining possession of a vast extent of territory, peopled by various nations, differing from each other in language, customs and habits, than they attempt what would be called a Herculean task, or rather a visionary project even in the most civilized countries of Europe, of which every statistical information is possessed and of which the government are one with the people, viz., to fix a land

![image 99](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile99.png)

![image 100](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile100.png)

###### 28 Land Reforms in India



rent, not on each province, district or country, not on each estate or farm, but on every separate field within their dominions.

“In pursuit of this supposed improvement, we find them unintentionally dissolving the ancient ties, the ancient usages which united the republic of each Hindu village, and by a kind of agrarian law newly assessing and parcelling out the lands which from time immemorial had belonged to the village community collectively..... professing to limit their demand to each field, but in fact, by establishing such limit, an unattainable maximum, assessing the Ryot at discretion, and like the Musalman government which preceded them, binding the Ryot by force to the plough, compelling him to till land acknowledged to be over-assessed, dragging him back to it if he absconded, deferring their demand upon him until his crop comes to maturity, then taking from him all that could be obtained, and leaving him nothing but his bullocks and seed grain, nay, perhaps obliged to supply him even with these, in order to renew his melancholy task of cultivating, not for himself, but for them.”38 As pointed out by Palme Dutt:

“This plea of the officers on the spot for a collective settlement and for recognition of “the lands which from time immemorial had belonged to the village community collectively was over-borne. The London court of Directors decided for the Ryotwari system, or , in terms of a document of the time, to “Confer the boon of private property” upon the peasantry; and armed with their instructions. Sir Thomas Munro returned from London to impose this system as a general settlement...”39

As the initial assessments under the Raiyatwari system were arbitrary and excessive, many a raiyat had no option but to give up his land and leave the village. Pragmatic considerations forced the administration to reduce the level of assessment. After a series of experiments spread over several decades, finally in 1864 the government’s share of revenue was fixed at half the duly ascertained net produce. So from one-half of the gross produce in the case of dry land and three-fifths of the gross produce in respect of wet land, government’s share came down to one-half of the net produce. Even the reduced assessment was, of course, a great burden on the peasantry

![image 101](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile101.png)

![image 102](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile102.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 29



and it continued to be so until the sharp rise in agricultural prices several decades later.

The system of land tenure adopted in the erstwhile Bombay Presidency was also Raiyatwari. Though in some matters of detail and principles of assessment it differed from the Madras system, in essence it had all the features of the Raiyatwari system evolved in Madras. As pointed out by Baden Powell:

“The Bombay Raiyatwari system has its points of resemblance to that of Madras, inasmuch as it proceeds on the same general plan, - a survey of the land, a division of it into ‘survey numbers’, i.e. blocks more or less representing individual holdings, and a determination of the government revenue rate to be assessed on each field. It allows the raiyat the same privilege of relinquishing any field he does not wish to hold, by giving his -razinama’, or notice of his desire to relinquish, in proper time, it allows him also to apply for any unoccupied lot that is available. It treats the ‘waste land’ in villages and outside villages, on similar principles. Each system has its ‘jamabandi’ or annual determination of the raiyats’ revenue liability”40

As in the other parts of India that came under the sway of the East India Company, in the Bombay Presidency also the primary objective of the administration was to exact as much as possible from the cultivators. To start with, in Bombay also, as in Madras, the officers of the company fixed the assessment at unrealistically high levels. The results were disastrous. The administration report of 1872-73 lamented:

“From the outset it was found impossible to collect anything approaching to the full revenue. In some districts not one-half could be realized. Things now went rapidly from bad to worse. Every effort, lawful and unlawful, was made to get the utmost out of the wretched peasantry, who were subjected to torture, in some instances cruel and revolting beyond description, if they would not or could not yield what was demanded. Numbers abandoned their homes, and fled into tlie neighbouring native states. Large tracts of land were thrown out of cultivation; and in some districts no more than a third of the cultivable area remained in occupation”.41

But soon enough the administrators hit upon a simple procedure for fixing the assessment at the highest levels practicable. As pointed out by Baden Powell, the Mahrattas who had preceded the British were

![image 103](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile103.png)

![image 104](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile104.png)

###### 30 Land Reforms in India



“stem and exacting*’ financiers. They had perfected the art of collecting the golden egg without killing the goose. So what the British administrators did was to make new assessments on the basis of the earlier assessments made by the Mahrattas. This was how the system worked:

“The Bombay method is avowedly an empirical one. When a track (usually a taluka or subdivision of a district) comes under settlement—its revenue history for the preceding thirty years or more is carefully ascertained and tabulated in figured statements and diagrams. These show in juxtaposition, for each year of the series, the amount and incidence of the assessment; the remissions or arrears; the ease or difficulty with which the revenue was realized; the rainfall and nature of the seasons; the harvest prices; the extension or decrease of cultivation, and how these particulars are influenced by each other; the effect of any public improvements, such as roads, railways, or canals and markets on the tract, or on parts of it, is estimated; the prices for which land is sold, or the rents for which it is let, are ascertained. Upon a consideration of all these data the total settlement-assessment is determined. That amount is then apportioned pretty much in the same way on the different villages, and the total assessment of each village is then distributed over its assessable fields in accordance with the classification which has determined their relative values in point of soil, water-supply and situation’*.42

By following this procedure the British administrators succeeded in pushing the assessment to the highest levels possible. In the result, as in the other provinces, in the Bombay Presidency also land revenue turned out to be an extremely onerous burden on the farming community.

Tenurial conditions varied a great deal between provinces and between different regions in the same province. It is neither practicable nor necessary for the purpose of this study, to present a complete picture of the bewildering diversity in tenurial conditions that obtained in different parts of the country. In the foregoing rapid survey we have briefly described the distinguishing characteristics of the three broad systems of tenures; the Permanently settled Zamindari, the Temporarily

settled Zamindari and the Raiyatwari systems. Between them these

three broad types covered the bulk of British India. Even where there

![image 105](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile105.png)

![image 106](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile106.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 31



were sharp differences in details, the system introduced by the British administrators approximated to one or other of these three broad types. And following the British, the Native States too adopted similar systems with this difference that in some of the backward states the peasantry was subjected to worse inequities than in British India. Most Native States, with the exception of progressive states like Travancore, were also slow in adopting the laws enacted in British India for the protection of tenants with the result that, by and large, the position of the tenants was invariably more precarious in the Native States than in British India.

While for the purpose of broad classification it may be stated that a particular system prevailed in a province or a group or provinces, it should be remembered that in small pockets other systems also existed. Thus in the permanently settled provinces of Bengal and Bihar, as already pointed out, there were some temporarily settled estates. And in these provinces, the system adopted in Government Estates known as Khas Mahal had features of the Raiyatwari settlement. Similarly, there were some Zamindari Estates in the predominantly Raiyatwari provinces of Madras and Bombay. There were also other intermediary tenures possessing all or most of the characteristics of Zamindari tenures; for example, the Janmom tenure of Malabar, the Jamma tenure of Coorg and the Khoti tenure of Konkan. Furthermore, all over the country, including the Presidencies of Madras and Bombay, there were numerous intermediary tenures with a variety of nomenclatures, but all falling under the broad classification of “Inams”. The term “Inam” applies to grants made by both Hindu and Muslim rulers for charitable or religious purposes, maintenance of public utilities, rewarding meritorious service, remunerating public servants etc. As pointed out by Baden Powell:

“All native Governments were in the habit of rewarding favourites, providing for the support of mosques, temples, religious schools, shrines and for alms giving, and for the maintenance of Brahmans or Muhammadan saints etc. by granting the revenue of the land, whether they granted the land or not”.43 The inams were quite rightly treated as intermediary interests when every state adopted legislation after independence for the abolition of intermediary interests.

![image 107](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile107.png)

![image 108](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile108.png)

###### 32 Land Reforms in India



###### Administrative Set-up

In the pre-British period and for several decades after the establishment of British power in India, land revenue was the preeminent source of government revenues. Naturally, one of the most important functions of government was the assessment and collection of land revenue. Throughout British India outside the permanently settled provinces of Bengal and Bihar, an elaborate administrative structure was developed to handle the assessment and collection of land revenue. Notwithstanding the differences in the designations and duties of the functionaries, the basic features of the system were the same in every province of British India. And that set up, except in Bengal and Bihar, penetrated deep into the countryside with the Village Accountant variously designated as Patwari, Talati, Kamam, Adhikari etc. at the bottom in charge of a single village or a group of villages. And in every province, at the district level, there was a District Officer designated as Collector, District Magistrate and Collector, or Deputy Commissioner. The hierarchy included at least two functionaries between the District Officer and the Village Accountant. These were the Sub-divisional Officer/Sub-Col lector in charge of the Sub Division, and below him the Tahsildar/Mamlatdar in charge of the Taluk. Of course, right from the beginning, the administration was charged with many functions besides the assessment and collection of land revenue.

The accurate assessment and efficient collection of land revenue necessitated an elaborate system of land records, collection of agricultural statistics and a plethora of rules and regulations. The District Officer’s responsibilities in the matter of maintenance of law and order were at all times as onerous as his responsibilities regarding land revenue. In the field of law and order also, he was assisted by the sub-divisional officer and often by the Tahsildar as well. And in course of time several new-fonCtions were assigned to the District Officer and his subordinates. By the time the British left India, the organization they had built up with the primary objective of assessing and collecting land revenue had become the general administrative structure of the country. Collection of land revenue was only one among its multifarious functions.

In the permanently settled areas there was no need to assess land revenue from time to time. As the landlords had been charged with the

![image 109](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile109.png)

![image 110](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile110.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 33



obligation to pay land revenue into the treasury before the prescribed date, collection was easy. So the British did not preate any administrative machinery at the taluk and village levels in those areas. In the wake of the abolition of intermediary tenures, these States too had to build up an administrative machinery similar to the one that already existed in the other states.

Despite its inadequacies and defects the administrative structure that the British had built up for the assessment and collection of land revenue proved to be a very useful instrument after independence. The administrative system that was inherited from the British did, indeed, satisfy all the basic requirements of the bureaucracy of a modem democracy. That system had been, of course, devised to serve the interests of the imperial power. With the coming of independence there was a sea change in the role of the bureaucracy and it was charged with a variety of developmental and welfare functions. Conscious efforts should have been made to reorient the bureaucracy to equip it to handle new tasks. It was, indeed, unfortunate that no effective step was taken to rid the bureaucracy of its shortcomings and forge it into an adequate tool.

###### Agrarian conditions during British Rule

As already pointed out, one of the grave consequences of British rule was the severe erosion, and often the extinguishment, of the rights of the peasantry. The government’s primary concern was the appropriation of the largest possible share of the fruits of the soil. Standing forth as the supreme landlord (overlord in the permanently settled areas) the British government in India ruthlessly extorted from the peasantry the lion’s share of the produce of the land. In course of time, the revenue administration came to devote its attention to cadastral survey and settlement, maintenance of elaborate land records and collection of agricultural statistics. But all these activities were calculated to serve the primary purpose of pitching the land revenue assessment as high as possible and collecting it ruthlessly. The land revenue system consistently ignored the rights of the mass of the peasantry in the bottom rungs of the agricultural ladder. And even when some tenancy laws were enacted, starting with the Bengal Tenancy Act of 1885, the unambiguous policy was to do no more for

![image 111](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile111.png)

![image 112](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile112.png)

###### 34 Land Reforms in India



the wretched of the land than what was absolutely necessary to stave off open revolt. The tenancy laws which protected the rights of privileged tenants, mostly of the so-called upper and middle castes, extended little succour to the hapless share-croppers. Strangely enough, the conditions of the share-croppers were even more parlous in the Raiyatwari areas that had evolved a more elaborate system of land records. For the land laws and the land records totally ignored sharecroppers and oral tenants. All over the country the clear policy of the Government was to uphold and preserve the rights of the landlords and the superior tenure holders. And nothing was to be done to weaken the landlord’s grip over the land. Even though it had been realized that the outmoded agrarian structure hampered agricultural production, the administration turned a blind eye to the inequities perpetrated on the tillers of the soil. Thus when the Royal Commission on Agriculture was appointed in 1926 to make recommendations for the improvement of agriculture and to promote the welfare and prosperity of the rural population, the Commission was expressly told that it would not be within the scope of the Commission’s duties to make recommendations regarding the existing system of land ownership and tenancy or of assessment of land revenue and irrigation charges. Until the very end, the British Government stuck to the policy of preserving the iniquitous land system that they had evolved in India.

The one hundred and fifty years of British rule (from the Permanent Settlement of Bengal to Indian independence) had other baneful effects on the Indian agrarian economy. That period witnessed the annihilation of India’s flourishing cottage industry, the increased dependence of a growing population on stagnant agriculture and the further impoverishment of the peasantry as a consequence of the inequities of the land system, oppressive taxation and growing indebtedness. Romesh Dutt who was, by no means, a severe critic of British Imperialism, summed up the situation thus in 1901:

“India in the Eighteenth Century was a great agricultural country, and the products of the Indian loom supplied the markets of Asia and Europe. It is, unfortunately true that the East India Company and the British Parliament, following the selfish commercial policy of a hundred years ago, discouraged Indian manufacturers in the early years of British rule in order to encourage the rising manufactures of

![image 113](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile113.png)

![image 114](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile114.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 35



England. Their fixed policy, pursued during the last decades of the eighteenth century and the first decades of the nineteenth, was to make India subservient to the industries of Great Britain. This policy was pursued with unwavering resolution and with fatal success. Orders were sent out, to force Indian artisans to work in the Company’s factories; Commercial residents were legally vested with extensive powers over villages and communities of Indian weavers; prohibitive tariffs excluded Indian silks and cotton goods from England; English goods were admitted into India free of duty or on payment of a nominal duty.

“The British manufacturer, in the words of the historian H. H. Wilson, “employed the arm of political injustice to keep down and ultimately strangle a competitor with whom he could not have contended on equal terms”; millions of Indian artisans lost their earnings; the population of India lost one great source of their wealth. It is a painful episode in the history of British rule in India; but it is a story which has to be told to explain the economic condition of the Indian people, and their present helpless dependence on agriculture.

“Agriculture is now virtually the only remaining source of national wealth in India, and four-fifths of the Indian people depend on agriculture...............”44

The destruction of the old industry led to a sharp deterioration in the economic condition of the people. As pointed out by Palme Dutt:

“It was during the first three quarters of the nineteenth century that the main ravages of Indian industry took place, destroying formerly populous industrial centres, driving the population into villages and destroying equally the livelihood of millions of artisans in the villages. No statistical record is available, but the census records of recent decades show that this process has even continued and gone further in our time.”45

The Central Banking enquiry committee pointed out in 1931: “The proportion of population living on agriculture is very large and it has been steadily on the increase. The proportion was 61 per cent in 1891. It rose to 66 per cent in 1901 and 73 per cent in 1921.”46 So crippling was the damage done to the Indian agrarian economy that even after four decades of planned economic development, we have not

![image 115](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile115.png)

![image 116](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile116.png)

###### 36 Land Reforms in India



succeeded in bringing down noticeably the proportion of the population dependent on agriculture to the level that existed a century ago.

As a result of the working of the iniquitous land system and the increasing pressure of population, the size of the average unit of cultivation had been getting smaller and smaller. In the context of the over-dependence on agriculture and the utter lack of alternative avenues of occupation, the death of a landholder invariably led to the partitioning of the holding among his heirs. The operation of the laws of inheritance and the transfer of land to non-agricultural classes, mostly money-lenders, were the main reasons for the steady decline in the size of the agricultural holdings. Though accurate data for the whole country are not available, there is no doubt that the size of the average holding was falling. Relying on the investigations conducted by Harold Mann, the then Director of Agriculture of the Bombay Presidency, the Uttar Pradesh Zamindari Abolition Committee observed:

“We have no reliable data regarding the sizes of holdings prior to British rule, but there can be no doubt that they were large enough to keep the cultivator in comfort. Dr. Harold Mann’s survey, “Life and labour in a Deccan Village” conducted in and about 1917 showed that the average holding in the village surveyed by him was 40 acres in 1771 and that this declined to only 17 acres in 1915. Dr. Mann’s general summing up applies no less to our villages than to the Deccan villages he surveyed: ‘It is evident that in the last sixty or seventy years the character of landholding has altogether changed. In the pre-British days and the early days of the British rule the holdings were of a fair size, most frequently more than 9 or 10 acres, while individual holdings of less than 2 acres were hardly known. Now the number of holdings has more than doubled and 81 percent of these holdings are under ten acres in size while no less than 60 percent are less than five acres.”47

The following conclusion of the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee regarding Uttar Pradesh is broadly applicable to the whole country:

“The extremely small average holding in the Province, even if other facts were disregarded, could not but compel a life of inescapable poverty. Destruction of subsidiary occupations and industries, the increase of population, and inadequate development of new lands made

![image 117](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile117.png)

![image 118](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile118.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 37



our holdings smaller till the overwhelming majority became uneconomical and agriculture became unprofitable.”48

We have only fragmentary evidence regarding the average availability of land per cultivator and the size class distribution of holdings. The 1921 census reported the average availability of cultivated land per cultivator in the provinces of British India as follows:49

Si No. Province Cultivated Area per cultivator (in acres)

- 1 Madras 4.9
- 2 United Provinces 2.5
- 3 Assam 3.0
- 4 Bihar and Orissa 3.1
- 5 Bengal 3.1
- 6 Punjab 9.2
- 7 Central Provinces 8.5
- 8 Bombay 12.2


Agricultural holdings were not only getting smaller and smaller, they were also being fragmented further. A holding usually consists of plots of differing fertility and character scattered over different parts of the village. With a view to ensure that every member of the family got a fair share of the different types of land, when a holding was partitioned the different plots were also split up. The practice, known as fragmentation, rendered units of cultivation even smaller. The evil effects of fragmentation as summed up by the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee are:

“Fragmentation of holdings involves considerable wastage of boundary lands and the wasteful boundary disputes and litigation costs. It increases the difficulties of management and results in inadequate supervision. Efficiency of cultivation is considerably reduced as a peasant family cannot manage widely divided plots. There is loss of labour and time in going from one plot to another. During busy seasons, such as the onset of the monsoon, when the peasant has to attend to all the plots at the same time, the preliminary operations so

necessary for good cultivation remain neglected on some plots.

![image 119](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile119.png)

![image 120](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile120.png)

###### 38 Land Reforms in India



Fragmentation also prevents land improvement, such as the construction of wells, as a small holder with widely separated plots does not find it profitable to build a well on any one of them.”50

On his dwindling holding, the Indian peasant laboured hard, often without success, to eke out a living under three crushing burdens- an oppressive land tax, exorbitant rents and the extortions of money lenders. The minority report of the Central Banking Enquiry Committee (1931) made a rough estimate of these burdens. Computing land revenue at Rs. 35 crores, rent at one and one-half times the land revenue and interest on debt at Rs. 100 crores, the total annual burden was estimated at about Rs. 200 crores. This was clearly an underestimate. In the permanently settled areas rent was often four to five times the land revenue. The interest burden was computed at Rs. 100 crores on the assumption that interest at the rate of 11 per cent would have to be paid on the estimated rural debt of Rs. 900 crores. The total debt burden was probably much higher than Rs. 900 crores. In 1935 Dr. P.J. Thomas had estimated the total debt burden to have been Rs. 1500 crores. Two years earlier, the Agricultural Credit Department of the Reserve Bank of India estimated it at Rs. 1800 crores. And the rate of interest was invariably much higher than 11 per cent. Most village money lenders used to charge one anna per rupee per month, which works out to 75% per annum. Incidentally, even after the remarkable growth of banking and co-operative credit during the last five decades, such a high rate of interest is not unknown even today in rural India. Be it as it may, even on the basis of the conservative estimate made by the Central Banking Enquiry Committee, the annual burden on the Indian peasant worked out to twenty rupees per head. Considering that the annual per capita income had been computed at forty rupees this was undoubtedly a crippling burden. In the result, even in a normal year, the agriculturist found it very difficult to pay off his dues. When rains failed, inevitably he sank deeper into debt. He could get off the debt trap only by selling off his land. The Census Report of 1931 reached the conclusion that as a result of such transfers a concentration of land in the hands of no-cultivating owners was taking place.51

According to the design of British Imperialism, India’s economic role was to produce the raw materials required for British industry and

![image 121](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile121.png)

![image 122](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile122.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 39



serve as a captive market for British industrial products. In pursuance of that policy, in Indian agriculture emphasis was laid on the production of agricultural raw materials at the cost of food crops. Relying on the data presented by Wadia and Joshi in “Wealth of India” and Radha Kamal Mukheijee in “Food Planning for four hundred millions” Palme Dutt concluded:

“The proportion of non-food crops has increased in relation to food crops. Between 1892-93 and 1919-20 the area under food crops increased from 187 million acres to 210 million acres, or by 7 per cent and the area under non-food crops increased from 30 million acres to 43 million acres, or by 43 per cent. This process has gone still forward in the recent period. Between the average for the five years 1910-11 to 1914-15 and 1934-35 the area under food crops has increased by 12.4 percent; the area under non-food crops has increased by 54 percent. As compared to an increase of 1.6 million acres under non-food crops during 1934-35 to 1939-40, the area under food crops has actually fallen by 1.5 million acres.”52

The overcrowding of agriculture accompanied by the increasing emphasis on the production of non-food crops led to a deterioration in the nutrition of the people, frequent famines and deepening poverty. In his presidential address to the Sixth Conference of the Indian Society of Agricultural Economics in 1945, Manilal Nanavati summed up the situation thus:

“In 1880 India had a surplus of foodstuffs to the extent of 5 million tons and today we have a deficit of 10 million tons. The consumption of food was then 1 £ lb. per individual and now it is 1 lb. Nearly 30 percent of the population of India is estimated to be suffering from chronic malnutrition.”53

The United nations Economic Survey of Asia and the Far East came to the same conclusion:

“Estimates of the long term trend of agricultural production indicate that aggregate food production declined by nearly five million tons during the period 1893-94 to 1945-46 and that the per capita production of food declined by 32 percent over the same period.”54

Thus during this period not only did food production fail to keep pace with the increase in population, but there was actually a fall in the

![image 123](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile123.png)

![image 124](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile124.png)

###### 40 Land Reforms in India



total production. And Indian agriculture being largely dependent on the erratic monsoon, its failure inevitably led to severe scarcity and even famine. As a matter of fact, one of the ugliest features of British rule was the devastation caused by recurring famines. Writing in the year of Queen Victoria’s Diamond Jubilee (1897) R.C. Dutt noted:

“The present year has been a year of rejoicing all over the British Empire in all parts of the globe, in India it has been the most disastrous year since the country passed under British rule....But the famine of 1897 is only one of a series of such calamities which have visited India almost periodically within the present century. Famines are a thing of the past in Western Europe: in India, every generation, every period of twenty years, has its own tale of distress to tell.........The years 1837, 1857, 1877 and 1897 are sad landmarks not of progress and prosperity, but of desolation and disasters”.55

###### Agrarian Structure on the Eve of Reforms

All through recorded history the Indian agrarian structure was inegalitarian and a substantial share of the income from land accrued to the state and the big landowners. As we have already seen, the land policies adopted by the British, particularly the elevation of the intermediaries to the status of landlords and the extinguishment of the rights of millions of cultivators rendered the agrarian structure even more inegalitarian.

According to the Census of 1931, the last comprehensive Census held before the transfer of power, the distribution of rural population according to occupation was as follows:

Figures in thousands

- 1. Non-cultivating proprietors taking rent in money or kind 32.57
- 2. Estate agents and managers 74
- 3. Cultivating Owners 2,70,06
- 4. Tenant Cultivators 3,41,74
- 5.. Agricultural labourers 3,14,80


The above classification does not throw any light on the relative economic status of the different classes. Though only a minority of

![image 125](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile125.png)

![image 126](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile126.png)

Tennrial Conditions Before Independence 41

landlords were really affluent, a substantial percentage of the rent receivers and cultivating owners (including superior tenants in Zamindari areas) with marketable surplus were reasonably well off. All the rest existed at varying levels of deprivation. In a good year, a sizeable section of small holders, and superior tenants, and a small percentage of share cropping tenants produced enough to meet their consumption requirements. When the rains failed they were hit hard. The bulk of the share-croppers and the entire class of agricultural labourers never could make both ends meet. In a year of famine they died in large numbers. The agricultural labourers largely belonged to the Scheduled castes and Tribes. Numerous social disabilities unheard of in any civilized society, the extremely low level of the wages and the seasonal nature of their employment confined the agricultural labourers perpetually to a sub-human existence. And to cap it all, as pointed out by the Congress Agrarian Reforms Committee, agrestic serfdom was prevalent in large parts of the country where the lower and the depressed classes were numerous.56

Considering the great diversity in tenurial conditions between regions, the non-exclusive nature of the different agricultural classes and the great disparities in the areas of land held by persons with similar rights in land, any attempt to describe the agrarian structure by using terms like landlord, tenant and agricultural labourer must necessarily be unsatisfactory. Daniel Thorner, one of the most perceptive observers of the Indian agrarian system, tried to tackle the problem in an entirely new manner. In the course of five lectures on Land Reform delivered at the Delhi University in 1955 Thorner suggested that the rural society could be classified on the basis of the source of income— whether it was derived from the ownership of property or from labour. On this basis Thorner divided the rural society into three classes—Maliks, Kisans and Mazdurs.

Thorner presented his classification thus:

“Usually, but not necessarily, the malik or proprietor will enjoy a high type of property rijrht in the soil. He may hold directly under the Government, or he may he a superior tenant with rights of occupancy, protected by law. He m:iy hold various lands under more than one tenure. The total amount of land held is such that the income serves to meet the major share of the family’s expenses (or, at least the expenses

![image 127](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile127.png)

![image 128](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile128.png)

42 Land Reforms in India

of those members in the family resident in the village). One or another member of the family may act as manager or supervisor, but none is required to work with his hands in the fields in order to ensure the family’s sustenance.

“Within this group or class of maliks, it is possible to separate out two sub-groups. One consists of large absentee landlords who typically have holdings in more than one village. The second consists of smaller proprietors who reside personally in the village in which they own land, and usually exercise some degree of management and control over the cultivation. It must be emphasized that the distinction here is not one of formal tenure. Either of these two types of proprietors may have the highest type of proprietary rights. Either may be an occupancy tenant. Both have the same basic economic interest in keeping up the level ot rent payable to them by lesser tenants, sub-tenants or share-croppers: and keeping down the level of wages of farm servants and other field labourers. For, it is the maliks, large or small, who are the receivers of rural rent and the employers of rural labour.

“The members of the second class, whom we call kisans or working peasants, have also recognized property interests in the land. They may be small owners, or tenants with varying degrees of security. By and large (but not in every state) their levial and customary rights will be somewhat inferior to those of maliks in the same village. The chief distinguishing feature, however, is the amount of land held. In the ease of the working kisan, the size of the holding is such that it supports only a single family and then only if one or more members . r the family actually perform the field labour. In fact the produce i'm:. the land owned by the kisan may not even provide the entire incon -required by his family, but at least it provides a larger share thar whatever funds he may receive from other agricultural sources, such as doing labour on other people’s lands. Kisans. as defined here, are thos? villagers who live primarily by their own toil on their own lands. They c!o not employ labour, except briefly in ploughing or harvest season nor do they commonly receive rent.

“The third rural class, t!;:*t o f ! b .nircrs or mazdur, comprises *.,f. • •; villagers who gain ibeii livciihoe-.l primarily from working on .«¿r people’s land. Families in this class may indeed have tenancy r: its in the soil, or even properly rights, but the holdings aie so small {' ;t the

![image 129](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile129.png)

![image 130](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile130.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 43



income from cultivating them or from renting them out comes to less than the earnings from field work. Wages may be received in money or kind. If the latter, they may be fixed or in the form of crop share. In practice the lower ranks of share-croppers and tenants-at-will are almost indistinguishable from mazdur.”57

The economic inequalities rooted in the outmoded agrarian structure were aggravated by the social disabilities flowing from the cruel and unjust caste system. The maliks generally belonged to the socalled upper castes of the Hindu caste hierarchy, the kisans to the middle castes and the mazdoors to the very backward castes, Scheduled castes and Tribes. Though exceptions were there, by and large this was the picture all over the country. Daniel Thomer’s observations in this regard were generally valid for the whole country:

“Socially the resident maliks and moneylenders form a small and quite distinctive group within a village. A handful of six or a dozen families, they typically belong to Brahman, Thakur or other high ranking castes, alternatively as in Andhra they may be members of respectable cultivating castes like Kammas, Reddis or Raos. They live in larger houses, wear finer clothes and have a better diet than the rest of the villagers. They may send their children to higher schools, subscribe to newspapers, listen to battery radios, or own bicycles—all luxuries usually beyond the compass of debt-ridden kisans, to say nothing of the landless mazdoor log.

“Between the kisans and the mazdurs, although the community of economic interest may appear very large, there is a steep social barrier. The kisans are drawn primarily from cultivating or artisan castes; the mazdoor log primarily from Harijans, Scheduled, depressed or “backward” classes. Certain types of work locally considered degrading, such as ploughing in Eastern U.F., are reserved for these lowly servitors. The rare Chamar, Mahar, Panchma or untouchable who prospers economically and attempts to secure a foothold for his family by buying land may find insurmountable obstacles in the way of the purchase. For he is up against the deeply entrenched tradition of rural inequality—a tradition which goes back to centuries if not millennia. To a considerable extent, the belief that low castes are bom to labour with their hands, and high castes to enjoy the fruits of other’s lahour, is accepted by the former as well as the latter. The separation between

![image 131](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile131.png)

![image 132](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile132.png)

###### 44 Land Reforms in India



proprietorship and physical cultivation both draws sanction from and serves to reinforce the caste structure of rural society.”58

Thus at independence India had a unique agrarian structure. As pointed out by Thomer:

“It represents a blending of remnants from the pre-British economic order (including, above all, the claim of the State to a share of the produce of the land) together with modem western concepts of private property. The result has been a layering of rights from those of the state as superior landlord (or ultimate owner) down through those of the sub-landlords (penultimate owners) to those of several tiers of tenants. Both the State and the Superior holders exercise the right to draw income from the soil in the form of rents. Wherever possible the tenants also try to subsist by collecting rents from working cultivators with rights inferior to their own.

“The maintenance of this hierarchical structure of interest in land has required, in effect, that quite a substantial proportion of the produce be reserved for persons who perform no agricultural labour. What was left to the actual cultivator, after the claims of various superior right holders were satisfied, might well be subject to collection as unpaid debt by moneylenders. The mechanism for the enforcement of this withdrawal of the great bulk of the product from the primary producers was provided by the new body of written law, the courts, the police, the promulgation of ordinances and so forth. In the end, the working Kisan was left with no surplus to invest in better implements, improved seed or fertilizer, and in any case no real incentive to increase his productivity. Since his tenure was in most cases insecure it was scarcely worth his while to think of undertaking long-term improvements. For the landless mazdoor, there was even less point in any attempt to raise his efficiency.

“The superior right holders, from whom a more progressive approach might have been expected, were interested in agriculture only to the extent that they might continue to draw their incomes from it.

“Typically they found it more profitable to rent out their lands than to manage them personally........... As members of the higher castes they preferred not to think in terms of undertaking “degrading” field work themselves. The primary aim of all classes in the agrarian structure has been not to increase their income by adopting more

![image 133](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile133.png)

![image 134](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile134.png)

###### Tenurial Conditions Before Independence 45



efficient methods, but to rise in social prestige by abstaining in so far as possible from physical labour.

‘This complex of legal, economic and social relations, uniquely typical of the Indian countryside served to produce an effect which I would like to call that of a built-in “depressor”. Through the operation of this multifaceted “depressor”, Indian agriculture continued to be characterized by low capital intensity and antiquated methods. Few of the actual tillers were left with an efficacious interest in modernization, or the prevention of such recognized evils as fragmentation. The pattern of landholding, cultivation, and product-sharing operated to hold down agricultural production.”59

This outmoded agrarian structure was the root cause of the stagnation in Indian agriculture and much of the social and economic injustices that prevailed in the rural society. It was also the prime cause of rural poverty. The half- hearted attempts made since independence to transform that structure are considered in the chapters that follow.
