###### Chapter 2

### Abolition of Intermediary Interests

Among the measures of land reforms introduced since independence the first and foremost was the abolition of intermediary interests in land. In the literature on Indian land reform the term “intermediary interest” connotes the hierarchy of proprietary interests that existed between the State and the actual occupier of the land. The intermediaries constituted one or more layers of the amorphous, parasitic groups that appropriated a substantial share of the produce of the land and harassed the cultivators in diverse ways without making any contribution to the process of production.

###### Intermediaries

The top layer of intermediaries included landlords typically designated as Zamindars, Jagirdars, Talukdars, Malguzars, Janmies, etc. Even this top layer did not constitute a homogenous class. There was considerable diversity in the precise nature of their rights, the size of their estates and the magnitude of their wealth and income. The size of their estate could vary from a few acres to tens of thousands of acres spread over large tracts of territory and the annual income could vary from paltry amounts to millions of rupees. Yet they all had certain common characteristics. The first common feature was that they were all rentiers, receiving rent from the layer of intermediary immediately below them, and in the absence of such a layer, from the occupiers of the land. Secondly, they all held their estates directly under the state with the obligation to pay land revenue to the state. As a hang-over of the feudal practices of the past, there were, of course, some who had

![image 141](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile141.png)

![image 142](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile142.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 49



been exempted from the obligation to pay land revenue. Between this top layer of landlords and the occupier of the land there often existed one or more layers of tenure-holders. As pointed out by Kotosky, the system of receiving rent through tenure-holders was developed to a large extent in North India (Uttar Pradesh, Bihar, Bengal and Assam) and to a smaller extent, in South and Central India.1 In Bengal where subinfeudation was rampant, the Simon Commission found that in some cases as many as fifty or more intermediate interests had been created between the Zamindar at the top and the actual cultivator at the bottom.2 To simplify matters we shall call the top layer of intermediaries “Zamindars”. We shall use the term tenure-holder to signify the plethora of intermediate interests that existed between the Zamindars and the occupier of the land. The term intermediary would include Zamindars as well as all kinds of tenure-holders.

###### Extent

In the last chapter we discussed at some length the origin and growth of intermediary interests in land. We saw how the erstwhile feudal chieftains, revenue farmers, tax collectors and diverse grantees, who had only been entitled to collect land revenue from the actual occupiers of the land, had been vested with proprietary rights in the soil and elevated to the status of landlords. Consequently, millions of cultivators who had enjoyed permanent rights in their land for generations were thrown out of their land or reduced to the status of tenants-at-will.

It was estimated that at the time of independence the Zamindari system covered 57 per cent of the area of private land-holdings in nine provinces of British India, the Raiyatwari system 38 per cent and the Mahalwari system 5 per cent.3 The incidence of the Zamindari system, however, varied greatly between states. While its incidence was very high in the North and the East, it was only 27 per cent in the Madras Presidency and 7 per cent in the Bombay Presidency. Intermediary interests existed to a greater or lesser extent in the Princely States as well. After a detailed study of the available data Kotosky came to the conclusion that taking the country as a whole “the landlords owned 55 to 60 per cent of the total privately owned land.4 This seems to be a reasonable assessment.

![image 143](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile143.png)

![image 144](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile144.png)

###### 50 Land Reforms in India



###### Zamindar’s Profits

The principal source of income of the intermediaries was the difference between the amount of rent they collected from the occupiers of land and the land revenue payable to Government. The proportion of this profit expressed as a per centage of the land revenue varied greatly between provinces, and in the same province between regions and between individual land owners. It will be recalled that in Bengal, according to the terms of the Permanent Settlement, the landlord’s profit amounted to only 11 per cent of the land revenue payable by them. Since land revenue had been permanently fixed and the rental income could be enhanced by various devices, by the time of independence the landlord’s profits had swelled to about five times the land revenue. In some permanently settled districts of Bihar the landlord’s profits were as high as 7.7 times the land revenue. In Madras it was about 4.5 times the land revenue.5 Even in the temporarily settled areas where the land revenue was enhanced periodically, the landlord’s profits were substantial. For example, in Uttar Pradesh the landlord’s profits were more than one and a half times the land revenue.6 Thus the profits that the intermediaries appropriated under the authority of the law varied between one and a half times to seven or eight times the land revenue.

###### Illegal Exactions

The rent legally payable to the intermediaries by itself was a heavy burden on the cultivators. This burden became intolerable during the periods when agricultural prices were depressed. According to one estimate “in the thirties and forties of the present century rent averaged 40-60 per cent of the crop in kind for India as a whole, but sometimes it was as high as 80 per cent of the crop.”7 But then, the obligation to pay rent was only a part of the burden. Over and above the rent, the intermediaries made a variety of exactions generally known as Abwabs (additional levies). These extortions took the shape of collections in cash, in kind and forced labour. The illegal levies were made under various pretexts. The signing or renewal of tenancy was invariably an occasion to extort a year’s or even two year’s rental as a bonus. Religious festivals like Holi, Durga Puja, Diwali etc were occasions for compulsory contributions to the Zamindar. Similarly domestic events

![image 145](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile145.png)

![image 146](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile146.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 51



like birth, marriage and death in the Zamindar’s household called for special levies. Tenants were required to supply fodder for the Zamindar’s cattle and horses and furnish funds for repairs to his mansion, the purchase of an elephant, a motor car etc. If the Zamindar extended hospitality to the Governor or other dignitaries or sent his offspring abroad for education, the burden of that too fell on the impoverished tenantry. A list of illegal exactions that prevailed in Rajasthan, where there was hardly any check on feudal malpractices, may be seen in Appendix II. 1. The U.P. Zamindari Abolition committee found that:

“In some ilaqas (areas) illegal exactions amounted to as many as fifty in number. Ingenious as some of these exactions were, the methods of realizing them were even more ingenious. There were cases when fields sown by tenants were ploughed up again and the crops destroyed and crops ready for reaping were either forcibly taken away by the Zamindar’s men or burnt down. The tenant was sometimes made to stand in the sun with a stick between his legs.”8 Other obnoxious and barbarous methods like false criminal prosecutions, illegal evictions, physical torture and destruction of the houses of the tenants were freely resorted to for collecting the illegal levies.9

Apart from paying illegal levies in cash and kind, the tenants were forced to supply free labour for various purposes. They had to work without wages on the Zamindar’s farm or in his household, work on construction projects, clear wood, take part in tiger and elephant hunts etc.10 Such extreme forms of social slavery prevailed in large parts of the country.

For obvious reasons no accurate estimate is available of the monetary value of the illegal exactions in cash, in kind and through forced labour. After analyzing the available information, admittedly of a fragmentary nature, P.N. Driver concluded that normally such exactions amounted to about twenty-five per cent of the rent and in exceptional cases to one hundred per cent or even more of the rent.11

###### Other Iniquities

Besides the severe economic exploitation described in the preceding paragraphs, the peasantry was also subjected to numerous other iniquities. They enjoyed no security of life and property; the

![image 147](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile147.png)

![image 148](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile148.png)

###### 52 Land Reforms in India



Zamindar could do what he pleased with the person and property of his tenants. In areas where powerful, autocratic Zamindar’s held their sway, even the slightest hesitation on the part of the tenantry in carrying out the whims of their masters invariably led to arson, rape, rapine, mayhem and even murders. A hapless peasantry laid prostrate by extreme social and economic exploitation, weighed down by physical and mental oppression and reduced to sub-human existence, was in no position to offer any organized resistance to tyranny. Their degeneration and supineness were so pronounced that in some remote, backward areas rapacious Zamindars could even enforce the notorious medieval custom of Jus Primae Noctis (the right of the first night.)

###### No Contribution to Agricultural Improvements

Despite the grave iniquities pointed out above, conceivably there might still have been some economic justification for the system if the intermediaries had been making a positive contribution to agricultural production by constructing and maintaining irrigation works, taking initiative in effecting agricultural improvements or providing capital. A fruitful collaboration between landlords and tenants, the former providing leadership, knowledge and capital and the latter contributing labour and diligence had led to significant improvement in agricultural production in the United Kingdom and elsewhere. But contrary to the pious hopes expressed by Lord Cornwallis and other protagonists of the Permanent Settlement, the Zamindars showed no inclination to transform themselves into a dynamic agricultural aristocracy. The sole concern of the parasitic groups of intermediaries was to extort the largest possible share of the produce of the land without making any contribution whatsoever to agricultural production. In fact the system had absolutely no redeeming features.

###### Evolution of a National Policy

###### Pre independence period

The founding of the Indian National Congress in 1885 marked the slow political awakening of Colonial India. For several decades the Congress remained a body dominated by the urban elite generally appreciative of the benefits of British rule, seeking only modest

![image 149](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile149.png)

![image 150](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile150.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 53



political reforms. Given the extremely narrow social base of the Congress it was only natural that in the early years the party evinced no great interest in agrarian problems. The advent of Gandhi, and the subsequent transformation of the Indian National Congress into a mass movement with firm roots in rural India led to great changes in its policies and programmes. The long-drawn-out struggle for independence highlighted by several satyagrahas and country-wide Civil Disobedience movements spread over a period of twenty-five years from 1917 (Champaran satyagraha) to 1942 (the Quit India movement) witnessed social and political mobilisation on an unprecedented scale. Some of those struggles—like the no-tax campaigns of the early nineteen thirties which reached serious proportions in Uttar Pradesh, Gujarat and North Cañara—were organized around the actual grievances of the peasants. They assumed the character of peasant struggles led by prominent congressmen. In the words of Jawaharlal Nehru:

“ The growth of the National movement under the leadership of the Congress, resulted in the peasant masses joining the Congress and looking to it for relief from their many burdens. This increased the power of the Congress greatly and at the same time it gave it a mass outlook. While the leadership remained middle class, this was tempered by pressure from below, and agrarian and social problems occupied the Congress more and more.... The struggle for independence began to mean something much more than political freedom, and a social content was given to it.”12

That was how the Indian National Congress came to be interested in the problems facing the agricultural classes. In the nineteen thirties the burning problems were the intolerable burden of land revenue and rent, the illegal exactions and oppression by the Zamindars and heavy indebtedness. Before we proceed to discuss the evolution of the national policy, it is necessary to emphasise that the policy of the Congress before independence as the vanguard of the freedom struggle, and after independence as the ruling party at the Centre and in the States for most of the time, was conditioned by the character of its leadership and the composition of its rural base. The leadership was almost entirely middle and upper-middle class. Most leaders owned land which they did not cultivate personally. And the solid rural base of

![image 151](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile151.png)

![image 152](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile152.png)

###### 54 Land Reforms in India



the party comprised medium and small land owners and superior tenants. As a matter of fact, barring the Princes and a small number of big Zamindars, the bulk of the men of property in rural India supported the freedom struggle. The bottom layers of the agricultural community comprising insecure tenants, share-croppers, agricultural labourers and rural artisans, who survived under sub-human conditions, played the role of swelling the crowds that thronged public meetings and demonstrations. Such being the ground reality, it was inevitable that yawning gaps would develop between practice and precept in the domain of land policy. So the slogans mouthed at party fora and the brave policy pronouncements on solemn occasions should be taken with a pinch of salt. They were often exercises in equivocation, double-speak and blatant hypocrisy as we shall see in due course.

A conference of Congress and Kisan Sabha workers held at Allahabad on 27 and 28 April, 1935 under the presidentship of Sardar Vallabhbhai Patel was probably the first occasion when the policy of the Indian National Congress on Zamindari abolition was set forth with some degree of clarity. The conference declared its “firm conviction that to improve substantially the condition of peasants and of villages and place them on a firm foundation it is necessary to change entirely the present system of land tenure. So long as there are several conflicting interests in the same soil the condition of villages cannot be greatly improved. The present Zamindari system is from the standpoint of the welfare of villages thoroughly wrong. The system was introduced with the advent of the British Government and it has led to the absolute destruction of village life.”13

The conference went on to assert that: “there is only one fundamental method of improving village life (which will benefit both peasants and Zamindars), namely the introduction of a system of peasant-proprietorship under which the tiller of the soil is himself the owner of it and pays revenue direct to the Government without the intervention of any Zamindar or Talukdar. The Kisans should by law be given the right to pay reasonable compensation to Zamindars in several instalments and to become owners of the land under their cultivation. The Government should also arrange to purchase land from Zamindars for tenants on payment of a reasonable compensation and it should advance loans to tenants for the

![image 153](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile153.png)

![image 154](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile154.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 55



purchase of their holdings at a very low rate of interest, and these loans should be repayable gradually within thirty or forty years. This conference is convinced that an arrangement of this kind can be smoothly and beautifully carried out and that it will benefit all the parties concerned and will lead to an all-round improvement in the conditions of the rural populations.”14

It is remarkable that all the essential elements in independent India’s policy in respect of Zamindari abolition like the anxiety to protect the interests of Zamindars, the accent on “reasonable” compensation etc were incorporated in the resolutions passed by the conference. H.D. Malaviya was right in saying that the Allahabad conference was a landmark in the development of Congress land policy and that it exercised a profound influcncc on the later decisions of the Congress at its formal sessions.15

The 49th session of the Indian National Congress held at Lucknow in 1936 under the presidentship of Jawaharlal Nehru considered the land question. But “in view of the fact that land tenures and revenue systems differed not only from province to province but were different in the same province, the Lucknow Congress decided, before drawing up a comprehensive programme to ask the different provincial committees to submit detailed reports on agrarian conditions in the different provinces.”16

As the provincial committees had not completed their work no comprehensive programme could be adopted at the 50th session of the Congress held at Faizapur. Pending the framing of a comprehensive agrarian programme the session adopted a resolution laying emphasis on the reduction of rent and land revenue, abolition of illegal levies, redemption of debt etc. The resolution was, however, silent on the question of abolition of intermediary interests. Similar was the approach in the election manifesto of 1936. The Congress ministries in office during 1937-39 concentrated on measures for giving protection to tenants and preventing evictions. The Congress election manifesto issued in 1946 contained the first authoritative pronouncement of the party on the abolition of intermediary interests. The manifesto stated that:

“The reform of the land system which is so urgently needed in India involves the removal of intermediaries between the

![image 155](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile155.png)

![image 156](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile156.png)

###### 56 Land Reforms in India



peasant and the State. The rights of such intermediaries should, therefore, be acquired on payment of equitable compensation.”17

###### Policy After Independence

At the first session of the All India Congress Committee held after independence in November 1947 a high level committee was constituted with Jawaharlal Nehru as chairman to draw up an economic programme. That committee which came to be known as the Economic Programme Committee (EPC) made the following recommendation regarding the abolition of intermediary interests:

“All the intermediaries between the tiller and the State should be eliminated and all middle-men should be replaced by non-profit making agencies, such as co-operatives.”18 The 55th session of the Indian National Congress held at Jaipur in December 1948 approved the recommendations of the EPC. Meanwhile on the basis of the elections held in 1946 under the Government of India Act of 1935, the Congress Party had come to power in several states and the Centre. Soon after assuming office the State Governments initiated action to enact legislation for the abolition of intermediary interests. Every Province conducted its own study and then legislated for the abolition of the intermediary interests. No serious effort was made to bring about a measure of uniformity in the scope and content of the laws to be enacted by the different states. As a matter of fact, a conference of State Revenue Ministers convened by the Congress President m December 1947 came to the conclusion that “the system of intermediaries having come into existence in the different states at different times, and there being a great divergence in the condition, background, etc of the landlord system from state to state, they (the states) should be free to deal with the question of abolition of the system in accordance with their peculiar circumstances.”19

The conference, however, felt that there was scope for uniformity in respect of the land policy to be adopted after the abolition of intermediary interests. Accordingly, the President of the Indian National Congress appointed an Agrarian Reform Committee headed by J.C. Kumarappa to examine and make recommendations about agrarian reforms arising out of the abolition of the Zamindari system in

![image 157](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile157.png)

![image 158](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile158.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 57



the light of conditions prevailing in the different provinces. The committee was also asked to consider and report on co-operative farming and methods of improving agricultural production, the position of small holdings, sub-tenants, landless labourers, and generally on improving the conditions of agricultural rural population.20

The Report of the Congress Agrarian Reforms Committee submitted to the President of the Congress Party in July 1949 was a moderately progressive document and it had considerable influence in shaping the land policies adopted since independence. It should, however, be pointed out that the Congress Party did not formally endorse the recommendations of the committee. Furthermore, several worthwhile recommendations of the committee regarding tenancy reform and coiling on agricultural holdings were ignored or watered down in the course of legislation and implementation. Given the power equations in the Indian National Congress such an outcome was, indeed, inevitable.

###### Legislation

Under the Indian Constitution (as also under the Government of India Act of 1935 which had been in force until the adoption of the Constitution), “Land” is a state subject. Entry 18 of List II (the State list) of the Seventh Schedule to the Constitution speaks of: “Land, that is to say rights in or over land, land tenures including the relation of landlord and tenant, and the collection of rents; transfer and alienation of agricultural land; colonization”. Hence only the State legislatures have the competence to enact land reform legislauon in India.

Soon after assuming office in 1946 the Provincial Governments initiated action to enact laws for the abolition of intermediary interests in land. The significant features of the laws enacted by the different States were the extreme dilatoriness of the legislative process, shortcomings in their content, the complex and time consuming procedures laid down for their, implementation and the role of the judicial system in frustrating the implementation of the enacted laws. In Daniel Thomer’s words:-

“The State legislatures, in their turn, have usually begun by referring the matter to a “high power” Commission or Committee of Inquiry. Once properly constituted the committee proceeds in

![image 159](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile159.png)

![image 160](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile160.png)

###### 58 Land Reforms in India



time-honored British fashion to hold hearings, collect and sift evidence, and consider the various alternative views submitted to it In due course, the committee presents its report. At this stage the State Government begins to draft the bill. Subsequently the bill is brought in, presented to the legislature, and referred to a committee for consideration. Only after it is reported out again by the committee, debated and possibly amended, will the land reform proposal be eligible for enactment as law.”21

How slow and time consuming the legislative process was is evident from the fact that some four to nine years elapsed before the different states completed the enactment of the Zamindari Abolition laws. This dilatoriness was in stark contrast to the great urgency and speed with which far reaching land reform laws were enacted elsewhere. At the height of the French Revolution, the National Assembly meeting in the historic tennis court at Versailles on the night of 4 August, 1789, after a short deliberation decreed the abolition of the feudal regime and the tithes. Nearer home, and in our own age, the People’s Republic of China took no more than two years to formulate, adopt and implement radical measures of land reform. And after the Second World War, Japan, prodded by the American army of occupation, completed the enactment and enforcement of thorough going land reform in less than four years. In India the extreme dilatoriness of the legislative process was not so much the result of the anxiety to stick scrupulously to the due process of law as a reflection of the half-hearted attitude of the ruling class to land reform. As if by design, the inordinate delay provided ample opportunities to vested interests to frustrate the implementation of the modest laws enacted by the State legislatures. As succinctly pointed out by Thomer:-

“There is no question but that any length of time which elapses between the first mooting of land reform legislation and the eventual enforcement serves to buttress the position of the land holding interests and to weaken the faith of the peasants on whose behalf the action is presumably being taken. The holders of superior rights take advantage of delays to put through paper partitions of joint family properties, to suborn Village officers into falsifying crucial records, to intimidate tenants or crop-sharers into registering themselves as farm servants or wage labourers, and in every way to impress upon the lesser folk of the

![image 161](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile161.png)

![image 162](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile162.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 59



village that, whatever may happen in the State Capital the powers that regulate the life of the countryside will continue to do so.”22

Without dwelling further on the dilatoriness of the legislative process, we shall proceed to have a look at the content of the State laws. As Uttar Pradesh is the largest State in the Indian Union and since the Uttar Pradesh Zamindari abolition law was often held out as a model worthy of emulation, we may as well start with a brief survey of that law. The Uttar Pradesh Zamindari Abolition Bill presented to the State legislature in June 1949 was based on the recommendations of the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee headed by the then Chief Minister, Gobind Ballabh Pant. After having been passed by the State legislature and after the assent of the President, the Bill became law early in 1951.

The avowed objective of the Zamindari Abolition law was to do away with the rights of the intermediaries and bring the actual cultivators of land into direct relationship with the State. On the eve of the enactment of the U.P. Zamindari Abolition act, the actual cultivators of land in Uttar Pradesh fell under two broad categoriesprincipal tenants of diverse nomenclatures who had stable and substantial rights in land and different types of insecure tenants with only a tenuous hold on the land they cultivated. The principal tenants were largely occupancy tenants, hereditary tenants and ex-proprietary tenants. These categories of principal tenants numbered about 13.5 million and they held some 29.6 million acres of land. They constituted 62.5 per cent of the persons having interest in land and they held 71.6 per cent of the land.23

The insecure tenants were largely tenants on the “Sir” and “KhudKasht” land of the intermediaries. “The term “Sir” refers to the home-farm or land which the landlord or co-sharer holds directly in his own management either cultivating it himself or by his farm servants or personal tenants.”24 “Khud-Kasht” means self-cultivated and it refers to land under the personal cultivation of the intermediary. (“Personal cultivation” of course, includes cultivation through insecure tenants, servants etc.)

As the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee put it: “Sir” and “Khud-Kasht” is home farm of the proprietor, that is land

cultivated by the proprietor with his own stock, or by his servants or by hired labour—

![image 163](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile163.png)

![image 164](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile164.png)

###### 60 Land Reforms in India



“The proprietor enjoys special rights in the land under his personal cultivation i.e., “Sir” and “Khud-Kasht” subject to certain restrictions and maximum limits placed by the Tenancy Act of 1939. The Zamindar has an unrestricted right of letting his Sir, the lessee or tenant does not obtain hereditary rights.”25

The tenancy laws enacted in 1939 and 1947 had accorded limited security of tenure to the tenants of Zamindars who paid land revenue exceeding Rs. 250. Out of 1.1 million acres of Sir and Khud-Kasht land let out to tenants 785,000 acres belonged to small Zamindars and so, even recorded tenants could not claim any rights in these lands. Thus under the law the ex-intermediaries could retain possession over the bulk of their Sir and Khud-Kasht land.

The U.P. Zamindari Abolition Act created four categories of rights in land: Bhumidhars, Sirdars, Asamis and Adhivasis. Bhumidhars were given absolute rights in the land held by them. They could sell, mortgage or otherwise dispose of the land. They could use the land for non-agricultural purposes as well. Their only liability was to pay land revenue to the Government. All ex-intermediaries were granted Bhumidhari rights in their unlet Sir and Khud-Kasht land. Small intermediaries (those paying less than Rs. 250/- as land revenue) were granted Bhumidari rights even in the Sir and Khud-Kasht land they had let out to tenants. Thus some 2 million ex-intermediaries acquired absolute rights over 6.8.million acres of agricultural land.

All the occupancy tenants, tenants at fixed rates, hereditary tenants, ex-proprietary tenants, rent free grantees etc., were granted Sirdari rights in their land. Tenants on the leased out Sir and Khud-Kasht lands of large intermediaries were also accorded Sirdari rights. The number of tenants with Sirdari rights at the beginning of reform was 15 million and they held 73.3 per cent of the cultivated area in Zamindari estates.26 The rights of a Sirdar were inferior to those of a Bhumidhar. A Sirdar was, no doubt, granted permanent and heritable rights in the land (a reiteration of the rights he had already been enjoying). But, unlike a Bhumidhar, a Sirdar could not sell, mortgage or otherwise dispose of his land. A Sirdar could use the land only for agricultural purposes. In certain circumstances a Sirdar could also be ejected from the land.

![image 165](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile165.png)

![image 166](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile166.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 61



The law provided for a Sirdar acquiring Bhumidhari rights by paying to the State a lump sum equal to ten times the rent he had been paying to the ex-intermediary. If he failed to make the payment he would continue as Sirdar with the liability to pay to the Government the same amount that he had been paying as rent to the Zamindar. After acquiring Bhumidhari rights he would pay as land revenue a sum equal to one half of the rent he had been paying to the Zamindar. Thus a Sirdar who had been paying Rs. 10 as rent would have to pay Rs. 100 to get the burden of annual payment scaled down to Rs. 5. That was, indeed a needlessly harsh provision in a law aimed at conferring benefits on the tenantry. Further more, the ex-intermediaries and tenants were treated very unequally even after the abolition of intermediary interests. While the ex-intermediary in his new incarnation as Bhumidhar would retain possession of his Sir and KhudKasht lands on payment of the land revenue assessed formerly on his self-cultivated land, the Sirdar who acquired Bhumidhari rights after paying an unreasonably large amount would have to pay as land revenue one half of the rent he had been paying to the ex-intermediary. As the old rate of rent was invariably more than twice the rate of land revenue, the tenant acquiring Bhumidhari right ended up paying every year land revenue at a rate higher than that applicable to the ex-intermediary for similar land. This was an uncalled for discrimination without any rational justification. The only rationale could be that the ex-intermediaries who invariably belonged to the upper classes should be treated liberally while the state would continue to extract as much as possible from the peasantry.

Asami rights were conferred upon tenants or sub-tenants of grove land, tenant’s mortgagees and non-occupancy tenants of pasture lands, or lands covered by water, or set apart for afforestation, or in the bed of a river, or used for casual or occasional cultivation. The law also provided for Asami rights being conferred in future upon persons to whom land might be leased out by Bhumidhars and Sirdars in accordance with the provisions of the Act.

The tenants-at-will on the Sir and Khud-Kasht lands of small Zamindars and sub-tenants were classified as Adhivasis. They were granted the right to hold their land for five years from the commencement of the Act. If they continued in possession beyond five

![image 167](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile167.png)

![image 168](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile168.png)

###### 62 Land Reforms in India



years they could acquire Bhumidhar rights by paying 15 times the hereditary rates in the case of Sir and 15 times the rent of the Superior tenant in the case of sub-tenants. On the payment being made the rights of the Bhumidhars and the Sirdars would be extinguished and they would be compensated by Government. Thus Adhivasi was envisaged as a transitional tenure. In 1954 the Act was amended to convert all Adhivasis into Sirdars and allow them to acquire Bhumidhari rights on the terms available to Sirdars.

The Act prohibited the fresh leasing out of land by all the new categories of land holders. The only exceptions were cases of minors, unmarried women, widows, physically and mentally handicapped persons, members of the armed forces, persons undergoing imprisonment etc. Though leasing out was prohibited, the law provided for crop sharing arrangements. The explanation to section 156 of the Act says “Any arrangement whereby a person is entitled to a right merely to share in the produce grown on the land in consideration of such person assisting or participating with the tenure holder in the actual performance of agricultural operations is not a lease”. This important provision was, of course, in conformity with the time-honoured policy of securing for the upper classes who shunned physical labour a substantial share of the produce of the land without having to till it themselves.

The Act provided for compensation to be paid to the exintermediaries at the flat rate of eight times their net assets. The gross assets of an estate consisted of all the income that accrued to the intermediary. The net assets would be arrived at after deducting from the gross assets the expenses incurred by the intermediary on payment of land revenue, cost of management at 15% of gross assets and an amount calculated at ex-proprietary rates of rent in respect of the Sir and Khud-Kasht lands that would be retained by the intermediary.

In Uttar Pradesh out of the 2 million ex-intermediaries about 85 per cent paid less that Rs. 25 as land revenue and only 1.5 per cent paid sums exceeding Rs. 250. Some 13.5 per cent paid amounts varying from the Rs. 25 to Rs. 250.27

Following the recommendations of the Zamindari Abolition Committee the Act provided for the payment of graded rehabilitation grants to intermediaries paying less than Rs. 10,000 as land revenue.

![image 169](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile169.png)

![image 170](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile170.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 63



Out of the 2 million intermediaries only 390 persons paid land revenue exceeding Rs. 10,000. Thus all but a handful of intermediaries qualified for rehabilitation grants. The grants varied from 20 times the net assets in the case of those paying less than Rs. 25 as land revenue to a sum equal to the net assets in the case of those paying Rs. 5000 to Rs. 10,000 as land revenue. That the ex-intermediaries were treated generously is evident from the fact that while the compensation payable under the law amounted to Rs. 93.43 crores the Rehabilitation grant amounted to as much as Rs. 70 crores.28 Rajasthan and Madhya Pradesh were the other States that provided for rehabilitation grants. The amount payable in Rajasthan was Rs. 18.87 crores and in Madhya Pradesh it was Rs. 3.54 crores.29

By the end of the First Five Year Plan all the States had enacted laws for the abolition of intermediary interests but for a few minor tenures and inams. The salient features of all these laws were more or less similar to those of the Uttar Pradesh Act. All the laws brought the principal tenants on lands outside the intermediary’s home-farm into a direct relationship with the State. Everywhere the home-farms had invariably been cultivated through tenants-at-will, share croppers, etc. The intermediaries were allowed to retain all those lands though they were by no means “tillers” of the soil. And no limit was put on the area of the home-farm that an intermediary could retain except in West Bengal, Assam, Hyderabad, Ajmer, and Jammu & Kashmir. The West Bengal law put a limit of 25 acres on the area of agricultural land that an ex-intermediary could retain as his home-farm. In addition he could also retain up to 15 acres of non-agricultural land. Besides he was also allowed to retain tea gardens, orchards and fisheries. So the ceiling of 25 acres did not hurt him much. In Assam a ceiling of 133£ acres was fixed for proprietors and 50 acres for tenure holders. In Hyderabad the ceiling varied between 18 acres and 270 acres depending on the class of land. In Ajmer the ceiling was put at 50 standard acres.

Jammu & Kashmir was the only state that did not treat the ex-intermediaries generously. A flat ceiling of 22 J acres was put on the area of agricultural land he could retain. Besides he was also allowed to retain orchards and grazing lands. Unlike in the other state laws, in the Jammu and Kashmir Act “tiller” was defined as a person

![image 171](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile171.png)

![image 172](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile172.png)

###### 64 Land Reforms in India



who actually tilled the land with his own hands. No compensation was paid to the Zamindars and the tenants became owners of the land without having to make any payment. Their only obligation in future would be to pay land revenue to the Government. A ceiling of 20 acres was put on the area of land over which a tenant could acquire ownership.

The laws enacted by all states except Kashmir provided for the payment of compensation to the ex-intermediaries. The rate of compensation varied inversely with the net income of the exintermediary. The compensation fixed under the various laws for intermediaries in the lowest slab varied between 15 to 30 times the annual net income. For those in the highest slab it varied between 12.5 times and twice the net income. The details of the scheme of compensation prescribed under the different laws may be seen in Appendix II.2.30

Though the provisions in the laws of the different states are, by no means, identical it may be pointed out that the essential features of all the laws are similar. As a matter of fact there was a broad measure of uniformity among the state laws on the abolition of intermediary interests which were enacted soon after independence without any guide lines from the Central Government or the Planning Commission. This is in striking contrast to the bewildering variety in the ceiling laws enacted in the fifties and sixties on the basis of the guide lines given by the Planning Commission.

###### Implementation

Implementation was seldom marked by efficiency and speed. The main reasons for slow and defective implementation were frequent intervention by the judiciary, the covert and overt resistance of the vested interests, the non-involvement of the beneficiaries, the inadequacies of the machinery for implementation, the unsatisfactory condition of the land records and the complexities of the laws.

###### Judicial Intervention

In several states as soon as the law was enacted the constitutional validity of the entire act or parts of it was challenged in the High Courts. Implementation had to be postponed until the court pronounced

![image 173](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile173.png)

![image 174](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile174.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 65



its verdict. The court’s verdict was seldom the end of the matter. The High Courts entertained fresh petitions on new grounds and there could also be appeal to the Supreme Court. The fate of the Bihar law highlights the role of the judicial process in postponing implementation.

As early as 1946 the Bihar Assembly passed a resolution for the abolition of intermediary interests. After protracted discussions in the legislature, consultations with the Central Government and the incorporation of amendments suggested by the Central Government, the Bihar Bill received the assent of the Governor General on June 6, 1949. Several landlords filed petitions in the High Court challenging the validity of the Act and the court promptly restrained the Government from implementing the law. While the case was pending, the Government also felt that the law needed amendment. So the Act was repealed and a new Bill was introduced in the State Legislature in 1949. After receiving the President’s assent the Bihar Land Reforms Act was published in 1950. The landlords did not, however, sit idle. They challenged the constitutional validity of the Act and the Patna High Court struck it down. That led to the very first amendment of the Indian Constitution. The First Amendment Act 1951 provided for the non-justiciability of the quantum of compensation for estates taken over by the State and also provided for the validation of the Bihar Act. Promptly the landlords filed a writ petition in the Supreme Court challenging the constitutional validity of the First Amendment. Though the Supreme Court upheld the constitutional validity of the First Amendment, the landlords of Bihar did not lose heart. They filed fresh writ petitions which ultimately resulted in two sub-sections of the Act being declared invalid. When the State Government started implementing the law in 1952, the landlords refused to hand over the rent rolls and other records. As most lands in Bihar were under Permanent Settlement all the records were with the Zamindars. The method of calculating compensation laid down in the law was such that no progress was possible in the absence of the records maintained by the landlords. Furthermore, when notices were served on the landlords directing them to hand over the records, several of them approached the High Court and obtained stay orders. Some particularly clever ones obtained stay orders from the Calcutta High Court where the State

![image 175](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile175.png)

![image 176](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile176.png)

###### 66 Land Reforms in India



Government’s case was likely to be neglected. Many of these cases dragged on for several years and all the while the landlords continued in legal possession of their lands.31 In the other States also judicial intervention led to considerable delay in the implementation of the law.

###### Resistance of ex-intermediaries

Besides making the maximum use of the judicial forum the landlords resorted to other means, invariably illegal, to thwart the implementation of the law. This resistance was the toughest in Rajasthan, the classic land of feudal land relations where the Jagirdari system covered over 80% of the land. H.D.Malaviya says ‘the feudal Jagirdars, incensed at the abolition of their heartless and inhuman exploitation, did their worst to defeat the implementation of the abolition legislation and took recourse to the most lawless activities and, like the barbarians of the middle ages, even burnt and pillaged the villages, so much so that their goondaism and lawless armed activities became a major problem for which the Central Government had to intervene. There have been for example, cases when women have been burnt alive, when villages have been razed to the ground, when trigger happy Jagirdars have freely used their guns to kill the kisans.”32

In the words of Daniel Thomer : “It is in Rajasthan that the landed gentry have offered the most savage resistance to land reform. Practically all of the Jagirdar’s lands had been cultivated for them by inferior tenants. Thorough-going abolition of intermediaries would have driven this class completely out of the rural scene. In order to allow the Jagirdars to continue to draw a living from the soil, the Rajasthan Land Reforms and Resumption of Jagirs Act of 1952 empowered them to resume for “self-cultivation” a certain minimum of land. Thus the Jagirdars were allowed lo clear the tenants off such land as they were entitled to resume. Since older agrarian legislation was practically non-existent, and tenant’s rights practically unheard of the clearing off process presented no real difficulties. Rough methods of persuasion were employed upon peasants who did not agree readily to surrender or abandon their holdings. A number of leases were shortened by the termination of the lives of the tenants. After having presented these unrepentant former Jagirdars with the opportunity of remaining on the agricultural scene by

![image 177](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile177.png)

![image 178](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile178.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 67



carving out Khud-Kasht holdings which they could cultivate for themselves, the Rajasthan legislature went one step further. It has now granted to the holders of Khud-Kasht lands the right to let these lands out to tenants and to authorize the tenants in their turn to sublet.”33

Thomer concluded: “Jagirdari had to be abolished because the Jagirdars were “intermediaries”. But the Jagirdars had to live, so they were enabled to acquire Khud-Kasht lands and thereby to become cultivators. Now, as owner of Khud-Kasht lands, they are to be permitted to have their lands cultivated by tenants, ie. to become once again intermediaries. Thus the full circle has been completed; Rajasthan is back where it started.”34 Though not to the same extent as in Rajasthan, in some of the other States also the landlords resorted to direct action and a variety of subterfuges to thwart the implementation of the law.

###### Non-involvement of the beneficiaries

The potential beneficiaries were not associated with the implementation of the laws for the abolition of intermediary interests. A partial exception was Kashmir where the workers of the National Conference were associated at the village level with the implementation of the law. But that association was not of much avail. Thomer found that in the actual process of implementation the State bureaucracy and the workers of the National Conference “co-operated all too well, with and for each other, at the expense of the actual “tillers”.35

In the decade of the thirties the Indian National Congress and the Kisan Sabha had been active in organizing agrarian struggles. Their main demands were the reduction of land-revenue and rent, security of tenure and the abolition of intermediary interests. The rise in agricultural prices in the wake of the second world war had taken the edge out of the demand for the reduction in rent and land revenue. Given its class character, the Congress could not be expected to take any action hostile to the landed interests. Even the Kisan Sabha was dominated by small landowners and superior tenants. As the superior tenants had already come to enjoy security of tenure thanks to the tenancy laws, they did not have much to gain from the abolition of the intermediary interests. The under-tenants and share croppers were too

![image 179](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile179.png)

![image 180](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile180.png)

###### 68 Land Reforms in India



ill-organized and anyway, the laws themselves did not offer them much. Had they been better organized, they could have resisted the efforts of the landlords to thwart reform. In the event the legal as well as the illegal acts of the landlords to thwart the enforcement of the law went unchallenged.

###### Machinery for implementation

In all the States the enforcement of the laws enacted for the abolition of intermediary interests was entrusted to the Revenue Department. In states where special cells were set-up at the State and District levels for the implementation of the laws, they functioned as a part of the Revenue Department and the personnel were drawn from the Revenue Department. And in every State at the lowest level the work was handled by the village functionaries variously designated as Patwari, Talati, Kamam, Adhikari etc. They were invariably corrupt, inefficient and over-worked and they were also under the sway of the landlords. However defective it might have been, the States covered by the Raiyatwari system and temporary settlement had a well-established revenue hierarchy going down to village level. As we have already seen, even that defective organization was non-existent in the Permanently Settled areas. A new organization had to be set up hastily in Bihar, West Bengal and Orissa. The inadequacy of the administrative machinery led to defective implementation. The Central and State Governments were fully aware of this. The Planning Commission’s Panel on Land Reforms (1955-58) found that one of the main difficulties was “the lack of proper administrative agency and in some cases a certain degree of pro-landlord bias in the agency where it exists".36

A decade earlier the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee had waxed eloquent on the pro-landlord bias of the Patwaris and the endemic corruption.

“There are altogether about 27,000 Patwaris in the province on an average pay, till recently, of about Rs. 18/- a month. This has now been increased, the minimum being Rs. 25/-. In addition to the maintenance of the land records the Patwari performs a number of miscellaneous duties and though occupying a very humble position in the official hierarchy he is, in the eyes of most of the villagers in his

![image 181](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile181.png)

![image 182](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile182.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 69



circle, a very important and powerful official. Errors inadvertently or deliberately made by him in the village affect powerfully the fortunes of the cultivators; a dishonest Patwari has therefore considerable opportunities for extortion. Most of them make good use of the opportunity; of the disputes fought in the revenue courts at a ruinous cost to the cultivator many would never have arisen at all but for the wrong entries in the revenue records. An elaborate system of supervision has been provided, but the errors seem to escape through the filter. The proprietors being the most powerful as well as the wealthiest class in the village the Patwari finds it an advantage to keep on their side as against the cultivators, an alliance that is strengthened by the powers of nomination possessed by the land holders when the post falls vacant.”37

In spite of the awareness at the highest levels both at the Centre and in the States, no step was taken to establish a new machinery or cure the old one of its defects. The unsuitability and inadequacy of the machinery adversely affected the implementation of the laws.

###### Absence of reliable land records

Absence of reliable land records contributed a great deal to the delay and shortcomings in implementation. This is what the first five year plan had to say about the status of land records in the country:

“A revenue administration depends, in the last resort, upon a good system of village records. In States like West Bengal, Bihar, Orissa, Rajasthan and Ajmer there are scarcely any village records. In Hyderabad and certain other areas, over large tracts, there existed a system of village records maintained by Zamindars and Jagirdars through their own petty employees. These records were seldom of adequate quality and could not be fully relied upon. Records of rights and other land records become even more important at a time when rapid changes affecting land have become a normal feature of legislative activity. It would not in fact be too much to say that in some States because of defective revenue records the implementation of reforms already enacted will remain incomplete and may even raise new problems which will come in the way of good administration.”38

In the areas covered by the Permanent Settlement the Government had no organization below the Sub-divisional level to handle land

![image 183](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile183.png)

![image 184](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile184.png)

###### 70 Land Reforms in India



administration. The only records with Government were those that had been prepared at the time of Survey and Settlement and updated during Revisional Survey. As resurvey had not been done for long periods, the records were badly out of date, particularly in the matter of tenancy rights. There was no system of annual verification as in the Raiyatwari and Temporarily settled areas. In the Raiyatwari areas the records were up-to-date but they seldom revealed tenancy rights though leasing out was rampant. Up-to-date and relatively more accurate records existed in the temporarily settled areas like U.P., Punjab etc. The important village records maintained in these areas included the village map, the Khasra or field book, the Khewat or the register of proprietary rights, the Khatauni or register of cultivating rights and the Siyaha which showed all payments in cash and kind made by the cultivator to the landlord. The only drawback was that the records did not faithfully reflect field conditions in respect of under-tenancies and tenancies-at-will, particularly on the Sir and Khud-Kasht lands of the ex-intermediaries. The Patwari often manipulated the entries in the records to suit powerful vested interests. Daniel Thomer who happened to visit Uttar Pradesh soon after the abolition of Zamindari interests observed that:

“The mischief which the patwaris perpetrated was not only great, it was also irreparable. One cannot go to a U.P. village without being told by some peasants about lands on which they should have become Sirdars but which, through the Patwari’s misdeeds, persisted in the hands of the Zamindar-tumed-bhumidhar..........After Zamindari abolition in U.P. then, two- thirds of the peasantry remain as holders of weak or inferior rights.”39

###### Complexity of the laws

A major cause for the tardy and inefficient implementation was the complexity of the various State laws. Only the Zamindari Abolition Act of Jammu & Kashmir had relatively simple and drastic provisions. Under that law a proprietor was allowed to retain only the land he tilled with his own hands, a ceiling of 23.75 acres was put on the area of land he could so retain and the law made no provision for the payment of compensation. All the other laws had indicate provisions particularly in the matter of payment of compensation. The provisions in the various State laws regarding compensation may be seen in Appendix H.2.40

![image 185](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile185.png)

![image 186](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile186.png)

Abolition of Intermediary Interests 71

A glance at Appendix II.2 will show that compensation was fixed as a multiple of net income in all States except Andhra Pradesh and Tamil Nadu where it was fixed as a multiple of the basic annual sum. Net income is arrived at by assessing gross income and deducting from it expenses incurred by the ex-intermediary. In the absence of reliable data, working out the gross income and expenses was no easy matter. And the amount of compensation was invariably on a graded basis, the multiples varying between thirty for the lowest slab of net income and two for the highest depending on the size of the net income. If only the law makers had been a little less anxious to preserve and protect the interests of the landed classes the laws could have been made much simpler. For example, compensation could have been fixed as a multiple of the land revenue. After all, the rent that an intermediary could legally collect from his tenants was supposed to bear a strict relationship to the land revenue payable to the Government. For the Permanently Settled areas where the land revenue had been fixed a century and a half ago, the multiple could have been somewhat more liberal. The only objection to such a simple provision would be that it would not reflect the actual profits of the Zamindars on the eve of Zamindari abolition. But then, considering that a good part of those profits consisted of illegal exactions and collections from public facilities like weekly bazaars, ferries, fisheries etc no great injustice would have been done to the intermediaries by fixing compensation as a multiple of the land revenue. But then the law makers were reluctant to hurt the intermediaries in any manner. As the enacted laws were complicated, implementation became slow and inefficient.

As a result of all these factors the implementation of the Zamindari abolition laws left a great deal to be desired. Even so the enforcement of those laws was far more satisfactory than the implementation of the laws enacted for the protection of tenants and the imposition of land ceilings.

###### Results

The assessments of the laws enacted for the abolition of intermediary interests differ very widely. At one end of the spectrum is the view that those laws did away with exploitative land tenures and ushered in radical changes in the agrarian structure. At the other end

![image 187](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile187.png)

![image 188](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile188.png)

###### 72 Land Reforms in India



there is the view that the laws did not achieve anything worthwhile, rober and objective assessments fall between these two extremes.

After surveying the post-reform scene Myrdal came to the following conclusion:

“Despite the weaknesses of the Indian legislation to abolish intermediaries, the reform should not be lightly dismissed. The former intermediaries may continue as ordinary landlords on a fairly large scale, but their reign as semi-feudal chiefs in rural districts has come to an end. Though most persons who became owners or government tenants had enjoyed some protection under earlier laws, it had not always been effective, and the State Governments are probably less likely to indulge in rack-renting and unjustified ejections than were the old intermediaries. A further effect of these reforms has been to alter the power structure in rural areas. Generally power in the villages is passing from the old absentee land-lord class and its agents to an upper middle class composed of merchants, money lenders and peasant landlords often living in the villages. A basis has been laid for the possible development of Indian agriculture along capitalist lines; those who favour such a development are not disposed to support further changes in the tenure systems. For those, on the other hand, who want to see Indian agriculture develop on more egalitarian lines, the abolition of intermediaries was only the beginning of agrarian reform. Yet it is an important beginning. The elimination of the big feudal landlords by parliamentary means is no small achievement in the first decade of independence, even though weakness and corruption in administration may permit the old rulers to exercise considerable influence for some time to come.”41

Myrdal's overall assessment of the reform is accurate, fair and balanced. Another objective assessment is that of Wolf Ladejinsky, one of the most perceptive observers of the Indian agrarian scene. Making a broad assessment of the reform Ladejinsky observed: “Despite opposition and administrative and technical problems, the Zamindari tenures were virtually abolished. Not all have benefitted equally and not all the 20 million cultivators affected have received permanent, heritable, and transferable rights. Nevertheless the effort was a great step toward a reconstruction of Indian agriculture. The measure succeeded because it was politically popular to abolish an agricultural

![image 189](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile189.png)

![image 190](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile190.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 73



system full of abuses imposed by a foreign power. With the British gone, the government went about drafting the necessary legislation, the zamindars were abolished despite the opposition of the landlords, lack of financial means and insufficient administrative and technical staff. Not every “t” was crossed and not every “i” was dotted, but the job was done.”42

###### Beneficial Results

The most significant beneficial result of the reform was that it put an end to the system of parasitic intermediaries and brought the erstwhile tenants into direct relationship with the State. The Planning Commission’s assessment was: “these reforms have brought more than 20 millions of tenants into direct relationship with the State and improved their social and economic position.” 43

Considering that Uttar Pradesh alone had about 14 million superior tenants with recorded rights and West Bengal over 6 million raiyati interests, even after making due allowance for the large scale evictions that took place, it seems the Planning Commissions figure of 20 million is an under estimate.44 My own assessment is that with the abolition of intermediary interests about 25 million erstwhile tenants were brought into direct relationship with the State. Be it as it may, while it is true that the reform led to an improvement in the social status of the erstwhile tenants the economic benefits were not substantial. As pointed out by the Planning Commission’s Task Force on Agrarian Relations:

“With the abolition of intermediary interests the ownersftip of land became more broad-based and the erstwhile superior tenants acquired a higher social status. It should not, however, be overlooked that as a result of the tenancy laws enacted in the decades prior to independence, the superior tenants had already been enjoying security of tenure and fixity of rent. It is a moot point whether the abolition of intermediary interests conferred any new economic benefits on the tenants.”45

As the former tenants were, in most cases, required to make payments for acquiring ownership rights and they were obliged to pay to the government every year as land revenue an amount equal to the rent they had been paying to the intermediary (only a few laws provided for the scaling down of rent) the economic benefits were

![image 191](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile191.png)

![image 192](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile192.png)

###### 74 Land Reforms in India



marginal. Even so, freedom from the illegal exactions of the Zamindars gave some economic relief to the tenants, particularly in backward areas. The social and political gains were undoubtedly significant. Forced labour, agrestic serfdom and other forms of oppression widely prevalent in large parts of the country came to an end. The former superior tenants, mostly belonging to the so-called upper and middle castes acquired a higher social status. In the decades that followed, political power also shifted to these classes. The Zamindar was no longer the ‘Numero Uno’ of the Indian Village. He was obliged to share power with his erstwhile leading tenants who owned substantial areas of land. The most visible demonstration of this shift in political power is the substantial presence of persons belonging to the upper layers of the so called backward castes in the leadership of the political parties. In the last two decades members of the backward castes even became Chief Ministers in Uttar Pradesh and Bihar. Such a development was inconceivable in the pre-reform era. Undoubtedly, the abolitioi*of intermediary interests paved the way for a remarkable shift in the balance of political power.

Another beneficial result was that on the abolition of intermediary interests large areas of private forest land and wasteland vested in the state governments. As pointed out by the Planning Commission :

“The forest lands vesting in the governments have generally been placed under the management of the forest departments. As regards culturable wastelands, in several cases their management has been vested in the Village Panchayats and in other cases they are being managed by government.”46 Out of the waste land and other types of land that vested in government some 6 million hectares was distributed to beneficiaries.47 The benefits from the distribution of this large area of land were diluted to a considerable extent because in many cases the allottee could not get or retain possession of the land and because they were given little assistance to bring the land under cultivation.

The abolition of intermediary interests also resulted in the rationalisation of tenures and a drastic reduction in their number. For example, Uttar Pradesh had some 40 types of tenures before the abolition of intermediaries. After abolition their number came down to three.

Yet another beneficial result was that the reform led to the penetration of the Land Revenue administration right down to the

![image 193](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile193.png)

![image 194](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile194.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 75



village level in the Permanently settled areas. As we have seen, in those areas, the Sub Divisional Officer was the lowest functionary of the Land Revenue administration. After the abolition of intermediary interests it became necessary to establish an administrative set up at the taluk and village levels as in the Raiyatwari and Temporarily settled areas. Incidently, this also led to an improvement in the collection of agricultural statistics.

###### BanefUl Results and Shortcomings

Though the beneficial results are not to be scoffed at, the fact remains that there have been some unwelcome consequences and shortcomings. The most important evil effect was that the reform led to the large scale ejectment of peasants from the land they had been cultivating for generations. Even in States where the tenancy law had conferred security of tenure on occupancy tenants and other similar categories of tenants, resourceful Zamindars succeeded in ejecting some of them by resorting to legal and illegal methods. And in States like Rajasthan where even superior tenants enjoyed little or no legal protection, large numbers were evicted.

The worst consequence of the reform was the large-scale ejectment of tenants-at-will, under-tenants and share-croppers. As we have seen, the zamindari abolition law of every state permitted the ex-intermediary to retain his home-farm or “personally cultivated” land. The term “personal cultivation” was loosely defined to include cultivation through share-croppers, servants, hired labour etc. Furthermore, most laws permitted the ex-intermediary to resume for personal cultivation part of the home farm by ejecting tenants. As it was up to the intermediary to select the land he wanted to resume, even in States where a limit was fixed on the land he could hold, all tenancies on the home farm were rendered vulnerable.

The most glaring defect of the reform was that it afforded no protection to vast numbers of tenants-at-will and share-croppers. This defect was not the result of any oversight. It was an integral part of the national policy. The avowed aim of the policy was to remove all intermediary interests between the State and the actual cultivator. But at all times the intention was that the term cultivator should be defined to include also those who had not been cultivating the land themselves.

![image 195](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile195.png)

![image 196](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile196.png)

76 Land Reforms in India

After considering the matter carefully, the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee put forward with great clarity the rationale for treating as “cultivators” even those who had not been personally tilling the land. In the Committee’s words:

“In many parts of the province considerations of social status and caste prejudice prohibit high caste men, Brahmins and Kshatriyas. from engaging in certain kinds of manual work, such as driving the plough which is considered fit only for lower caste agriculturists or field labourers. All the same, since times immemorial they have been treated as “cultivators”. If the performance of manual operations were imposed

- as an essential condition, a large number of such men, whose livelihood
- at present depends on land would be excluded from the definition of “cultivators”. Nor can we rule out the employment of servants or hired labourers, for all over the world the employment of casual or permanent labour is necessary for carrying on even small and medium-sized farming. If, therefore, the employment of agricultural labour were ruled out cultivation under the existing conditions would become impossible. It is not possible to define the minimum of manual labour which a man must perform in order to qualify himself for inclusion among cultivators. Nor are we prepared to say, in view of the prevailing conditions, that no one can be a cultivator unless he personally performs manual labour. While, therefore, the ideal is that the cultivator and his family should contribute most of the labour required, a person who hires agricultural labour either permanently or casually for the performance of some manual tasks must still be regarded as a “tiller of the soil”, provided that he finances and supervises agricultural production and takes the risk involved”.48


Daniel Thomer’s comments on the Committee’s views are apt and noteworthy:

“The argument from “times immemorial” we may observe, is rather unfortunate in this instance since the classic ancient text, the laws of Manu, explicitly reserves for Brahmanas and Kshatriyas other functions than cultivation of the soil, which is described as a proper activity of the lower Vaisya order. In any case, catering to the socio-religious pretensions of the higher castes runs counter to the spirit of the Secular State. Thus it is not required that the supervision be done in person on the spot, nor is it even specified that the supervisor be

![image 197](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile197.png)

![image 198](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile198.png)

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests 77



resident in the village. What this formulation provides is a mode of considering the non-cultivating owner as contributing to production and accordingly entitled to a share of it even when, in practice, he leaves to others the functions of planning, of direction, of conducting, and of physically carrying out the entire round of agricultural operations/*49

As the term “personal cultivation” was loosely defined, the Zamindars and superior tenants could continue to hold land they had not been tilling themselves and millions of share-croppers could not acquire any rights in the land. To that extent the slogan of “land to the tiller” remained an empty one. Furthermore, as we have already seen, even recorded tenants on the Sir and Khud-Kasht lands of the Zamindars who paid less than Rs. 250 as land revenue (such Zamindars owned 70 per ccnt of all Sir and Khud-Kasht land) enjoyed no tenancy rights and the law permitted the Zamindars to resume such land. As a result of these factors, even in Uttar Pradesh which enacted one of the better laws, the benefits of the reform did not reach the bottom layer of the peasantry consisting of tenants-at-will, share-croppers and agricultural labour.

If such was their fate in Uttar Pradesh, it was worse in the other States. We have seen how terrible was their lot in Rajasthan where large scale forcible evictions were the order of the day. In West Bengal where share-cropping tenancies had been rampant the law expressly denied tenancy rights to share-croppers known in that state as 4Bargadar'. The Land Revenue Commission of Bengal had considered the question of according tenancy rights to Bargadars. The Commission had found that about one-fifth of the land of Bengal was under the Barga system. After considering the pros and cons, the Commission recommended that Bargadars who supplied plough, cattle and agricultural implements should be treated as tenants. The Commission also recommended that the share of the crop legally recoverable from the Bargadars should be one-third instead of half.50 Yet, the law makers of independent India ignored the recommendation of the Commission and refused to confer any rights on Bargadars. The hapless Bargadars had to wait for another twenty years before limited rights were conferred on them. Thus the West Bengal law, inspite of its having put a ceiling on the area of land that an ex-intermediary could keep as his home-farm, failed to extend any benefit at all to the share-croppers.

![image 199](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile199.png)

![image 200](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile200.png)

###### 78 Land Reforms in India



In Bihar the situation was as bad as in West Bengal or even worse. Visiting Bihar soon after the law was enacted, Daniel Thomer observed:

“As in Uttar Pradesh, the Bihar Act permitted Zamindars to retain their Sir and Khud-Kasht. As a result it is not difficult to come across estates of 500, 700 or even 1000 acres in post-reform Bihar. One also finds after Zamindari abolition a persistence of the older structure of land owner, occupancy raiyat, under raiyat and bataidar (crop-sharer). The under-raiyats have only scant protection against loss of possession while the bataidars are roughly equivalent in their rights to the bargadars of Bengal............. In short, neither Zamindari abolition nor Bhoodan persuasion (although Bihar has been the site of the most intensive Bhoodan efforts in the whole of India) has been able to transform rural Bihar. It remains a stronghold of large land-holders and hierarchical property rights; leasing, sub-leasing and evictions are all common.”51

Things have not changed much in Bihar since Thomer’s visit in the early fifties. Though the hierarchy of interests in land has disappeared, large landowners holding more than 1000 acres and numerous bataidars with no rights on the lands they cultivate continue to co-exist. The numbers of both have of course, declined.

The lot of tenants-at-will and share-croppers in the other states was often worse than that in Uttar Pradesh, but marginally better than that in Bihar. Thus the worst feature of the reform was that it extended no benefit to millions of tenants-at-will and share-croppers. As a matter of fact, there was a marked deterioration in their status. Millions of them were deprived of access to the land they had been cultivating for generations. After surveying the scene Myrdal observed:

“Zamindari abolition thus amounted to a transfer of title to certain lands to numbers of the cultivating castes in the villages and the higher caste families whose income had traditionally been drawn from landownership and who had been granted the rights of privileged tenants by colonial tenancy laws. Generally speaking, sub-tenants, that is under privileged tenants and share-croppers, drew no benefits from these reforms. Nor, in practice, did all members of the “cultivating castes” who were direct tenants under the Zamindars profit. A considerable number of them were socially and economically degraded

![image 201](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile201.png)

![image 202](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile202.png)

Abolition of Intermediary Interests 79

when the Zamindars took over land for personal cultivation. Evictions reduced the status of many of them from that of tenant - to that of share-cropper and agricultural labourer.”52

Another unwelcome feature of all the laws was the large compensation given to ex-intermediaries. As we have seen, the rates of compensation were needlessly liberal in some states. The total amount paid to the ex-intermediaries as compensation amounted to Rs. 670 crores. The payment of this huge amount placed a heavy burden on the state exchequer. The Zamindars did not invest the compensation received in industry or any other worthwhile economic activity. Hence the payment of compensation served no useful social purpose.

Thus the major unwelcome features of the reform were that it permitted the landowners to retain and even regain possession of their so-called home-farms, that except in a few cases, no limit was put on the area of land that a Zamindar could retain as his home-farm, that the term “personal cultivation” was loosely defined, that no rights were conferred on tenants-at-will and share-croppers, that millions of tenants and under-tenants were evicted and that a heavy expenditure was incurred on the payment of compensation. Yet it produced the beneficial result of destroying the social and economic power of the big Zamindars. The upper and the middle layers of the peasantry acquired a higher political and social status. The bottom layer of the peasantry consisting of agricultural labourers and share-croppers received no benefit from the reform. And of course, the reform failed to achieve the oft proclaimed goal of “land of the tiller”. For a substantial area of agricultural land continued to be held by non-cultivating owners who get it tilled by share-croppers or hired labour.

###### Chapter 3

### Tenancy Reform

As we have seen, at the time of independence about sixty per cent of the area was covered by the Zamindari and other intermediary tenures while the remaining part of the country was under the Raiyatwari system. With the abolition of most of the intermediary interests within the first few years of independence, the whole country came under more or less the same kind of tenurial system, local variations notwithstanding.
