“In sharp contrast, in the Malabar district which came under direct British governance, the Namboodiris and the chieftains were able not only to retain their control over land but also to strengthen their hold over it....... More serious than the concentration of private ownership was the denial under the British administration of the customary rights associated with ‘Kanam’ and ‘Verumpattom’ tenures in the earlier traditional society. Security of tenure came to be denied to even those in the upper strata of agrarian society (such as the families who had earlier secured land in ‘Kanam’ tenure) and the bulk of the cultivators (who were previously left alone as long as they carried out the traditional obligations) were reduced to mere tenants-at-will, subject to eviction at any time.”38

![image 273](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile273.png)

![image 274](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile274.png)

###### Tenancy Reform 115



According to a survey conducted by the State Bureau of Economics and Statistics in 1967 the total area under tenancy was 19.21 lakh acres constituting 42.5 per cent of privately owned land.

Kanam, Kuzhikunam and Kanam-Kuzhikunam tenancies accounted for 8.79 lakh acres and Verumpattom for 8.49 lakh acres. All the other types of tenancies put together accounted for 1.93 lakh acres. It is interesting to note that valid documents existed in respect of 98.9 per cent of the Kanam, 90.7 per cent of the Kuzhikunam, 93.4 per cent of the Kanam-Kuzhikunam and 82.2 per cent of the Verumpattom tenancies. Either through mutual agreement or as a result of the orders of the tribunals all but a few of the Kanam, Kuzhikunam and KanamKuzhikunam tenants acquired ownership rights. About 92.2 per cent of the Verumpattom tenants also became owners.39

Appendix m .6 shows that 28.42 lakh tenants acquired ownership rights in 14.50 lakh acres by 1992.

While assessing the performance of the State of Kerala in respect of tenancy reform it is necessary to bear in mind two important points. A substantial part of the agricultural land in Kerala was held by Janmies who, as we have seen, were like the Zamindars. The superior tenants of Janmies who acquired permanent rights were exactly like the superior tenants of Bengal, Bihar or Uttar Pradesh who became owners as a result of the abolition of intermediary interests. The second point is that the data compiled by the Bureau of Economics and Statistics did not cover oral share-cropping tenancies. On the eve of reform informal share-cropping was the dominant form of tenancy. Not only the Janmies, but also the bulk of the Kanam tenants and a substantial per centage of the Verumpattom tenants had sub-let their land to sharecroppers. Though share-croppers were treated as tenants under the law, as almost all such tenancies were oral and informal, as in other States, in Kerala also all share-croppers could not acquire rights in land. A large number of the erstwhile share-croppers lost access to the land they had been cultivating before the reform.

A noteworthy feature of tenancy reform in Kerala was the conferment of ownership rights on hutment dwellers known as Kudikidappukars. Under the law a Kudikidappukaran was defined “as a person who did not have in his possession either as owner or as tenant, land exceeding in extent three cents in a city or major municipality, or

![image 275](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile275.png)

![image 276](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile276.png)

###### 116 Land Reforms in India



five cents in any other municipality or ten cents in any panchayat area or township or who had been permitted with or without an obligation to pay rent, by a person in lawful possession of any land, to have the use or occupation of portion of such land for the purpose of erecting a homestead or had been occupying a hut belonging to the landowner.” All hutment dwellers were accorded permanent rights in ten cents of land around the hut in rural areas, five cents in small towns and three cents in large municipalities and cities. As a result of the enforcement of this provision 2,84,203 persons acquired permanent rights in their homesteads. This measure conferred tangible and appreciable benefits on the landless agricultural labourers who hardly derived any benefit from the other measures of land reform. The main reasons for the better implementation of tenancy reform in Kerala were the organization of agrarian struggles under the leadership of the Communist Party, and the general shift of political power in favour of weaker sections. As a result of the implementation of the measures of tenancy reform, the incidence of tenancy at present is negligible in Kerala. According to the 37th round of the N.S.S. it is as low as 2.05 per cent. While tenancy has practically disappeared there is no evidence of the reform having led to better utilization of the agricultural land. There are numerous instances where the landowners keep their land fallow because they shun physical labour and would not cultivate crops like paddy by hiring labour as it is not profitable to do that. Nor do they lease out the land clandestinely as in some other States because they are likely to lose control over the land.

###### West Bengal

The only State where tenancy reform conferred substantial benefits on share-croppers is West Bengal. Cultivation through share-croppers, locally known as “Bargardars” has been widely prevalent in West Bengal at all times. Under the Barga system the practice was for the bargardar to supply seed, manure, plough and cattle and all the required physical labour. The produce was shared equally between the landowner and the bargardar. The landowner could be a Zamindar, tenure-holder or Raiyat. The Tenancy Act of 1885 extended limited protection to bargardars. Even that limited protection was extinguished through an amendment of the Tenancy Act in 1929. The years 1946-47

![image 277](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile277.png)

![image 278](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile278.png)

###### Tenancy Reform 117



witnessed a powerful agrarian struggle known as the “Te-bhaga movement”, the modest objective of which was to limit the landowner's share to one-third of the produce. That movement led to the enactment of the West Bengal Bargardar’s Act in 1950 according limited protection to bargardars. The West Bengal Estate Acquisition Act, 1953 provided for recording the names of bargardars in the record of rights and the West Bengal Act of 1955 devoted a whole chapter to bargardars. The protracted procedure laid down for recording the names of bargardars required the bargardar to put in a claim and pray for the recording of his name which would be disposed of by the revenue officers after hearing all the interested parties. Until the middle of 1978 no more than about 4 lakh names could be recorded.

As already pointed out, the law was amended in 1977 introducing a new principle of presumption in law that a person lawfully cultivating the land of another person is a bargardar in respect of such land if he is not a member of the landowner’s family. The barga right was made heritable and the bargardar could be evicted only if he failed to cultivate the land personally, failed to tender the landowner’s share of the produce or the landowner required the land bona fide for personal cultivation. It was also stipulated that resumption should be subject to two conditions, firstly that together with the resumed land the landowner’s total holding does not exceed three hectares and secondly, that the resumption should not lead to the area of land held by the bargardar being reduced below one hectare. For purposes of resumption from a baragadar, ‘personal cultivation’ is defined rather rigidly in that it does not include cultivation by labourers. Furthermore, if the landowner fails to bring the land under personal cultivation within two years, the land will be put to sale, the erstwhile bargardar having the first right of refusal. The West Bengal land Reform Rules 1965 were suitably amended to empower Revenue Officers to detect, determine, or incorporate the names of bargardars at any time after holding an enquiry.

Starting from early 1979 the Government of West Bengal mounted a campaign christened “Operation Barga” to detect and inscribe the names of bargardars in the record of rights. Squads of revenue officers moved into villages, held camps with the clear objective of conscientising the peasantry and instilling in them the necessary

![image 279](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile279.png)

![image 280](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile280.png)

###### 118 Land Reforms in India



courage and determination to claim their rights. On the spot enquiries were made openly in the presence of the villagers and the names of bargardars were recorded. The efforts of the bureaucracy were backed by the active support of the workers of the left parties who were ruling the State. The most important factor was, of course, the unambiguous and determined political direction. An equally important factor was the gearing up of the bureaucracy under the leadership of dedicated officers of outstanding ability. The campaign resulted in the recording of the names of 13,25,167 bargardars by June 1985.40 The number has since gone up to 13.9 lakhs. “Operation Barga” has not ushered in the millennium for the poor peasantry, nor has it resulted in all barga cases being detected and recorded. Yet it resulted in substantia] benefits being conferred on the bulk of the share-croppers who occupy the lowest but one rung in the agricultural ladder. And nothing like this has yet been attempted in any other State. If only a similar operation had been mounted in the other States, particularly in Bihar, Orissa and Eastern Uttar Pradesh, substantial benefits would have accrued to hundreds of thousands of hapless share-croppers.

###### Incidence of tenancy at present

We shall now take a quick look at the incidence of tenancy at present in the different States. Though the National Sample Survey data do not reflect fully the incidence of tenancy, they are useful in as much

- as they throw light on the relative incidence of tenancy in the States. According to the 37th round of the N.S.S. the incidence of tenancy in the different States in 1981-82 was as noted below:


The incidence of tenancy is over 10 per cent in the States of Haryana, Punjab, West Bengal, Tamil Nadu, Bihar and Uttar Pradesh. In Orissa it is only marginally lower at 9.92 per cent.

Between September 1998 and May 1990 the probationers of the Indian Administrative Service conducted a survey in a few selected villages of the different States under the supervision of the National Academy of Administration. The results have been compiled in the “Draft Report of the Empirical Study of Tenancy Reform in India” published by the Land Reform Unit of the Lai Bahadur Shastri Academy of Administration, Mussoorie. Though the sample was small and no attempt was made to ascertain the incidence of tenancy in the selected villages, the study is useful as it throws light on the terms of

![image 281](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile281.png)

![image 282](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile282.png)

###### Tenancy Reform 119



Table 1 Area Leased in as a Percentage of the Operated Area

- 1. Haryana 18.22
- 2. Punjab 16.07
- 3. West Bengal 12.34
- 4. Tamil Nadu 10.92
- 5. Bihar 10.27
- 6. Uttar Pradesh 10.24
- 7. Orissa 9.92
- 8. Assam 6.35
- 9. Andhra Pradesh 6.23

lease, security of tenure and the socio-economic condition of the tenants in the post reform period. The study shows that 57.7 per cent of the tenancies that persist are unrecorded and only 43.3 per cent are recorded. The overall per centage of recorded tenants reached 43.3 per cent because of the high per centage of recording in West Bengal and Tamil Nadu. Recorded tenants constituted 89.8 per cent of all tenants in West Bengal and 76.3 per cent in Tamil nadu. In Bihar and Uttar Pradesh there are no recorded tenants, and in Rajasthan only 2 per cent of the tenancies are recorded. One of the main reasons for the higher per centage of recorded tenancies in West Bengal and Tamil Nadu is that both States permit tenancy and so there is no dire need to suppress the fact. The other reasons are the effective drive for recording tenancies during “Operation Barga” in West Bengal and the enactment and enforcement of a law in Tamil Nadu for recording tenancies. If we leave out West Bengal and Tamil Nadu, most of the tenancies that exist are unrecorded. The study shows that share-cropping is the major form

![image 283](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile283.png)

![image 284](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile284.png)

###### 120 Land Reforms in India



of tenancy. Usually the tenant bears the cost of cultivation, but there are also instances where the landlord and tenant share the cost of the inputs. Instances of leasing in of the land of small owners by substantial landowners were noticed in Punjab and Haryana. The overall conclusion of the study is that tenancies, particularly sharecropping arrangements under terms unfavourable to the tenants continue to exist and that their incidence is substantial in some States.41

###### Legalizing Tenancies

Four decades of tenancy reform has not resulted in the attainment of the objectives set out in the Five Year Plans. The incidence of tenancy is still substantial in some regions. The banning of tenancies and imposing restrictions on leasing out has only led to tenancies being pushed underground. As long as a class of landowners who shun physical labour and a vast army of landless agricultural labourers and marginal peasants coexist, any legal ban on tenancy will remain a dead letter. As tenancy is contracted secretly in violation of the law, the tenant’s position always remains precarious and consequently, the tenant has no incentive to cultivate the land efficiently. And in several regions, landowners keep the land fallow or raise only one crop where two could be raised. They do not lease out the land for fear of losing their rights if they let it out illegally.

Apart from the fact that a total ban on the creation of tenancies is unworkable, there are also serious doubts about the advisability of prohibiting leasing out of land. As A.M. Khusro says: “In India in the 1970’s when one expects a great deal of transfer of population from agricultural to urban areas, thanks to the process of industrialization, many small people in the country are destined to leave their land and move out into the cities in search of gainful, high-wage employment. Given the uncertainties of finding a job, if rural people, particularly small men, are forced to sell their land rather than lease them out for the time being, a great loss of welfare can be demonstrated to occur. It should not be an objective of agricultural policy to make it difficult for people to adjust their occupations and to improve their mobilities. Permission to lease out one’s land is an important aspect of the mobility of labour.’*42

![image 285](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile285.png)

![image 286](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile286.png)

###### Tenancy Reform 121



The National Commission on Agriculture recommended a partial modification of the national policy of banning tenancies. The Commission’s view was: “As experience indicates tenancy cannot be totally banned under the present man-1 and ratio until such time as socio-economic development brings about a radical change in man-land ratio, tenancy will have to be permitted in a restricted and strictly regulated form. Leasing out should be permitted only in the case of marginal farmers. The period of lease should rot be less than three years at a time, renewable with mutual consent. The lease should be in writing and the landowners must not be allowed to resume before the expiry of the term. Eviction of a tenant from the land during the lease period should be prohibited and redress of any grievance of the landowner should be through the court of law by means other than eviction. In case of any eviction, the onus of proof should be with the landowner.’*43 The Commission’s recommendations are, more or less, on the lines of the policy laid down in the First Five Year Plan. Even the partial modification suggested by the National Commission on Agriculture has not been accepted by Government. The working group on land reform that considered the question on the eve of the formulation of the Eighth Plan felt that:

“Despite some validity of the proposition outlined above (to permit tenancies), it must be cautioned that restoration of tenancy as a legal institution at this stage of land reforms is likely to produce more harm than good. Landlords will resort freely to the exceptional provisions to induce and force small and marginal farmers to lease out the latter’s land to them. It will be difficult for such farmer lessors to establish their rights on the lands at a later stage. As the experience of the agriculturally rich—the so-called green revolution—areas shows, there is a trend towards concentration of landholdings through the leasing out of land by small holders to big ones. This trend will be reinforced and the eventual loss of land by the small farmer to the big one is likely to be legitimized. The group, therefore, unequivocally holds that there is no warrant for changing the accepted policy of prohibiting leasing of land by landowners who do not cultivate it. The fear that the possession of small plots of land—not sufficient for a living—nevertheless inhibits the owner from venturing out of land for a living, is somewhat exaggerated. In any case, an average farm family has more than one

![image 287](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile287.png)

![image 288](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile288.png)

###### 122 Land Reforms in India



working hand. It is not as if all the working hands of the family could expect to get employment or find sources of living outside the land, if they were not discouraged by the fear of losing their land. The small piece of land that an average farmer’s family holds, can be taken care of by the remaining members of the family, even if one of the working hands were to leave agriculture.”44

The working group’s fear about the danger of big landowners forcibly taking over the land of small owners are exaggerated. After all, even now it is open to the big landowners to expand their holdings up to the ceiling limit by purchasing outright the land of small owners. As a matter of fact the legal restrictions on leasing operate to the disadvantage of the small owners. For one thing, as the leasing is illegal, the rent that he can get is less than the market rent. For another, in some States he runs the risk of losing his land if the tenancy is detected. Where the small owner who is in straitened circumstances leases out land to big farmers, it is the former who should get protection. The simplest way of protecting him is to amend the law to permit the leasing out of land. As far as the poor peasants leasing in the land of big owners are concerned, under the existing state of affairs they acquire no rights at all. As leasing in is illegal, the law takes no notice of such arrangements and therefore, the tenants acquire no rights

- at all. If tenancy is made legal, it will certainly lead to an improvement in the status of the tenants. Landowners who are not in a position to cultivate their land, may also prefer leasing out their land to keeping it fallow, for there will be no risk of their losing their land. The result will be greater accessibility of the rural poor to agricultural land, enhanced employment opportunities and increased output. For all these reasons we are in favour of the tenancy laws being amended to allow leasing out of land.


The leases should, however, be strictly regulated. Only small and marginal farmers and owner-cultivators having holdings below the ceiling limit should be permitted to lease in land. The area owned plus the area leased in should not exceed the ceiling limit.The lease should be for fixed periods of three to five years and should be renewable after the expiry of the stipulated period. The lease should be in writing. One copy of the lease should be lodged with the village panchayat and another with the taluk office. The rent should not exceed the limit laid down in the law and the owner should be required to grant receipts.

![image 289](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile289.png)

![image 290](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile290.png)

Tenancy Reform 123

Even if such an amendment is made, the landowners will still be reluctant to agree to written leases. For, they may entertain the fear that the law may be amended subsequently to confer ownership rights on tenants. So it will be necessary to give a solemn assurance that no such amendment will be made in the future.

In our view, there is nothing retrograde about allowing the leasing out of land. In the context of the existing ground realities it will be a wise and timely step. It will result in the existing clandestine and illegal arrangements being brought out into the open with beneficial results to the rural poor and the agrarian economy.

Chapter 4

### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

Evolution of Policy

As we have seen, the Indian National Congress started taking an active interest in agrarian problems and organizing agrarian struggles after its transformation into a mass organization with a strong rural base. The issues discussed at the political fora in the nineteen thirties related to the protection of tenants and the abolition of intermediary interests. It was, therefore, only natural that the Congress ministries that assumed charge in the Provinces in 1937 and remained in office for about two years gave priority to the enactment of laws for the protection of tenants. Soon after independence, laws were enacted for the abolition of intermediary interests. Not much thought was given in that period to the question of imposing ceilings on agricultural holdings. Yet, considering the characteristics of the Indian agrarian structure and the need for the Congress to win and retain the support of the large mass of the impoverished peasantry, it was inevitable that, in due course, the party would propose limits on the area of land that large owners could keep. Though Gandhi did not formulate any detailed programme for the reform of the agrarian structure and the re-distribution of land, his concept of rural re-construction envisaged a non-exploitative, egalitarian social order. In his famous interview to Louis Fischer in June 1942 Gandhi made the startling observation that in independent India the peasants would take the land they cultivated and that no compensation would be paid to the landlord. He even added that the landlords might co-operate by fleeing from the villages.1

![image 295](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile295.png)

![image 296](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile296.png)

126 Land Reforms in India

Jawaharlal Nehru, the second most influential leader of the Congress stood for an egalitarian social order. The faith of the Indian National Congress in social justice was reiterated in a resolution passed at the Meerat session held in November 1946 a few months before the transfer of power. The resolution stated:

“In the opinion of the Congress, swaraj cannot be real for the masses unless it makes possible the achievement of a society in which democracy extends to the social and economic sphere, and in which there will be no opportunity for privileged classes to exploit the bulk of the people nor for gross inequalities such as exist at present.”2

The resolution on economic objectives, adopted at the next session held in November 1947 soon after the attainment of independence, made it clear that the Congress stood for a democratic society based on social justice and equality. As we have seen, it was at that session that the A.I.C.C. appointed a committee headed by Jawaharlal Nehru to draw up an economic programme. The Economic Programme Committee made far-reaching recommendations. Among other things the Committee recommended the imposition of ceilings on agricultural holdings. The Committee’s specific suggestion was:

“The maximum size of holdings should be fixed. The surplus land over such a maximum should be placed at the disposal of village co-operatives” 3

“At its annual session held at Jaipur in December 1948, the A.I.C.C. “generally approved the report and recommendations of the Economic Programme Committee”.4 The ‘general’ approval would, no doubt, leave enough room for departure from the policy in important matters of detail. Some eighteen months later the Congress Agrarian Reform Committee headed by J.C. Kumarappa submitted its report to the Congress President incorporating comprehensive and far-reaching proposals for agrarian reform. Among other things the Committee gave detailed suggestions for the imposition of ceiling on agricultural holdings. Though the Committee’s recommendations provided some guidance to the Planning Commission in the formulation of policy, it was typical of the manner in which the Congress Party functioned that the A.I.C.C. never passed any resolution formally adopting or modifying the report. It is, of course, doubtful if the A.I.C.C. or the Congress Working Committee would have accepted the report without

![image 297](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile297.png)

![image 298](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile298.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 127



making substantial modifications. For the reality was that several stalwarts among the top leaders of the Congress, and the bulk of the middle level leaders were opposed to progressive measures of reform which would adversely affect men of property who wielded considerable influence in the villages. It would not have been in the best interests of the Congress Party to alienate that class. Those leaders were ever willing to subscribe to vague resolutions calling for social justice or an egalitarian social order out of deference to Jawaharlal Nehru and his friends and to maintain an outward show of unity. But they would have never subscribed to concrete proposals that would have adversely affected the interests of the rural rich. As the Congress practised a large measure of inner party democracy in those days, Jawaharlal Nehru could not have had his way even if he had been willing to make a stand. So, the only practical course possible for the Congress was probably to call for progressive reform in general terms but go slow when it came to adoption of concrete measures. Thus the party could easily accord its “general” approval to the report of the Economic Programme Committee or it could, without any hesitation, take the initiative in including a whole chapter on Directive Principles of State Policy in the Indian Constitution incorporating such lofty concepts as social, economic and political justice and an egalitarian social order. But when concrete proposals for reform adversely affecting vested interests were made, the issue would be neatly bypassed. That was probably why the A.I.C.C. did not bother to discuss and adopt the report of the Congress Agrarian Reform Committee.

Be it as it may, we have enough evidence to show that in the years immediately after independence the Congress would not have unanimously accepted any proposal for the imposition of ceilings. How strong would have been the opposition in the Congress Party to the imposition of ceilings is evident from the report of the Uttar Pradesh Zamindari Abolition Committee. That committee was headed by Gobind Ballabh Pant, the Chief Minister of Uttar Pradesh and one of the top leaders of the Congress. The U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee finalized its report in July 1948, some six months after Jawaharlal Nehru had submitted the Report of the Economic Programme Committee to the Congress President. It is typical of the dichotomy in the attitude of the Congress Party to progressive measures

![image 299](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile299.png)

![image 300](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile300.png)

128 Land Reforms in India

of reform that while the Economic Reform Committee recommended the imposition of Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings, the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee opposed the measure. After weighing the pros and cons of the proposal, the Zamindari Abolition Committee felt that the disadvantages far out-weighed the advantages. Considering that the imposition of ceilings would inflict great hardship on landlords and, therefore, would be opposed by them, would not solve the problems of uneconomic holdings and landlessness and would reduce the marketable surplus of food grains, the Committee advised against the imposition of ceilings. The Committee’s conclusion was:

“On a consideration of all these difficulties we do not think that the results achieved by the redistribution of land would be commensurate with the discontent and hardship resulting from it. We, therefore, recommend that no limit be placed on the maximum area held in cultivation either by a landlord or tenant.”5 Thus, until independence and for some years after that, the policy of the Indian National Congress on the imposition of Ceilings remained vague and nebulous. While pious declarations were made about the need to impose ceilings, on practical considerations concrete proposals were soft-pedalled or turned down.

###### Policy in the Five Year Plans

After independence the Congress became the ruling party at the Centre and in most States. Soon after that the country opted for planned economic development. A high powered Planning Commission headed by the Prime Minister was constituted. Right from the beginning land policy was treated as an integral part of our development plans and so it became the concern of the Planning Commission to lay down the national policy on land reforms. The Indian National Congress, continued to pass ritualistic resolutions calling upon the State Governments to expedite the enactment of land reform laws and their implementation; but the initiative had passed on to the Planning Commission. The following facts support this view.

As we have seen, the Congress Economic Programme Committee headed by Jawaharlal Nehru had recommended the imposition of ccilings on large agricultural holdings. The recommendations of the Committee were approved by the A.I.C.C. in December 1948. The

![image 301](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile301.png)

![image 302](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile302.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 129



Congress Agrarian Reforms Committee which submitted its report in July 1949 had given specific suggestions for the imposition of ceilings. Yet, while formulating the First Five Year Plan, the Planning Commission postponed action for the imposition of ceilings on the ground that reliable data on land holdings were not available. The First Plan merely called upon the States to conduct a census of land holdings during the year 1953. When the A.I.C.C. met at Agra in July 1953 there was no criticism of the inordinate delay in the imposition of ceilings. All that the A.I.C.C. did was to pass the following resolution approving the line adopted by the Planning Commission.

“The State Governments should take immediate steps in regard to the collection of the requisite data and the fixation of ceilings on land holdings, with a view to redistribute the land, as far as possible, among landless workers.” 6

Considering that the Planning Commission was headed by Prime Minister Nehru himself who took a personal interest in economic planning, it was inevitable that the Planning Commission would emerge as the principal forum for the delineation of land policy. So we have to look into the plan documents and the reports of the committees set up by the Planning Commission to get a dear idea of the evolution of the policy on land ceilings after independence.

###### First Five Year Plan

Right from the First Five Year Plan the procedure adopted by the Planning Commission has been to prepare a draft plan incorporating all the policies and programmes for the following five year period, place the draft before the National Development Council and draw up the final plan in the light of the comments of the State Governments and the National Development Council. Usually there were no significant variations between the draft plan and the final plan. In the case of the land policy in the First Plan there were, however, substantial differences between the contents of the draft outline and the final plan.

The Planning Commission worked on the First Plan in 1950-51 and the Draft Outline was published in July 1951. The Country had just emerged out of the perils and trauma of partition. The break-down of civil administration had barely been averted. Overshadowing all these was the chronic shortage of food grains rendered almost intractable by

![image 303](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile303.png)

![image 304](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile304.png)

130 Land Reforms in India

the failure of the monsoon in successive years. Against that background the planners were reluctant to suggest any ceiling on large agricultural holdings which could reduce the marketable surplus of food grains. The proposals made by the Congress Agrarian Reform Committee did not find favour with the Planning Commission. That Committee had recommended that there should be a ceiling on the size of holdings which any one farmer could own and cultivate. The rationale for coming to that conclusion, in the Committee’s words were:

“In the first place, the supply of land, in relation to the number of people seeking it, is so limited that not to put a ceiling on individual holdings would be irrational and unjust. Secondly under the present technique of cultivation, the managerial capacity and financial resources of an average cultivator in India, the optimum size of a holding has to be fairly low. The coiiunillce has, therefore, recommended that the optimum size should be three times the size of the economic holding.”7

The Draft Outline of the First Plan contains a comprehensive analysis of the agrarian problem. Too many people on too little land, the predominance of subsistence farmers with uneconomic holdings incapable of making the necessary investments for improving the land and adopting modem methods of fanning, and widespread under-employment and unemployment. All this led to a stagnant agriculture incapable of feeding a growing population. The preponderance of uneconomic holdings was identified as the greatest handicap to increasing agricultural production. The Planning Commission considered the question of imposing ceilings on large holdings, taking over the surplus land and distributing it so as to raise the area of the uneconomic holdings to the desired size. After pondering over all aspects of the matter the Commission came to the conclusion that the problem of uneconomic holdings could not be solved by the redistribution of surplus land. The Commission thought that too little land would be available, and thal its distribution among the various classes of peasants, namely, the small owners, tenants and landless agricultural labourers would present “numerous practical problems involving basic social conflicts.” The fact that the surplus land would be scattered all over the village in bits and pieces would also present problems. Furthermore, the operation would call for the

![image 305](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile305.png)

![image 306](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile306.png)

Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 131

commitment of administrative and financial resources far beyond the capacity of the State. The over-riding consideration was, of course, the fear that the break up of the large farms would lead to a fall in agricultural production, a risk that the country could ill afford to take while facing severe food shortages. The Commission felt that if ceilings were imposed “on larger farms the production would fall and, for a period at any rate, on other farms also, and it may well be that the decline in production may have a serious effect on the well-being and stability of rural society as a whole’*. Having reached that conclusion, the Commission rejected outright the idea of imposing ceilings on large holdings. Following in the footsteps of the U.P. Zamindari Abolition Committee, the Planning Commission, however, expressed itself in favour of putting a ceiling on future acquisitions.8

The Planning Commission seems to have ignored the ground reality that the so called large farms were not compact areas of contiguous land owned by individual farmers. More often than not the ‘large farm’ was nothing more than an assortment of bits and pieces of land spread over one or more villages, and invariably the bulk of the land was cultivated through under-tenants and share-croppers. In such a situation the imposition of ceilings would not have been as disastrous as the Planning Commission had imagined it to be. Anyway, the Planning Commission took a less categorical stand in the final plan document which was published in December 1952. As in the draft outline, in the final plan also, the emphasis was on increased agricultural production. Again, as in the draft outline, small and uneconomic holdings were considered to be the root cause of the many difficulties in the way of agricultural development. Having made that diagnosis the planners could not have, in good conscience, recommended any ceiling on large holdings. Yet they found it expedient to show some concern for social justice. So taking into account the social objective of reducing disparities in wealth and income the First Five Year Plan suggested that:

“When land is managed directly by substantial owners and there are not tenants in occupation, public interest requires the acceptance of two broad principles.

(I) There should be an absolute limit to the amount of land which any individual may hold. This limit should be fixed by each state, having regard to its own agrarian history and its present problems. The census of land holding and cultivation which it

![image 307](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile307.png)

![image 308](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile308.png)

132 Land Reforms in India

is proposed to hold during 1953, will give the data relevant to this decision.

(II) The cultivation and management of land held by an individual owner should conform to standards of efficiency to be determined by law”.9

The First Plan envisaged the actual operation of the policy in the following manner:

“As a practical approach to the problem of large individual holdings, it would be best to divide substantial farms which are directly managed by their owners into two groups, namely, those which are so efficiently managed that their break up would lead to a fall in production, and which do not meet this test. For the latter category, the

• land management legislation should give to the appropriate authority the right to take over for the purpose of management the entire farm or such portion of it as might be in excess of the limit for resumption for personal cultivation and secondly the right to arrange for the cultivation of lands so taken over. For the cultivation of such lands, preference could be given to co-operative groups and to workers on the lands which pass into the control of the land management authority. The proposals made above would provide for a large measure of redistribution of land belonging to substantial owners. In the legislation a date might be indicated with effect from which the law would be enforced in respect of farms in excess of the prescribed limit. Generally speaking, in order to set up the machinery for land management and to undertake the necessary survey before the law can be enforced effectively, a period of about two to three years might be necessary”.10

The halting, half-hearted proposal made in the First Five year plan could not have led to any significant redistribution of agricultural land. The Planning Commission itself put a spoke in the wheel by dilating upon the difficulties resulting from the absence of reliable data regarding the size of the holdings and suggesting a census of land holdings to be conducted in 1953. Though the Commission did not expressly say so, the States, quite naturally, concluded that the laws for the imposition of ceilings were to be enacted only after the census data became available. And the dilatory manner in which the Central and State Governments went about the business of conducting the proposed census reflects the very low priority given to the matter. Though the

![image 309](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile309.png)

![image 310](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile310.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 133



census was to be conducted in 1953, it was only in January 1954 that the Ministry of Agriculture could send a draft questionnaire to the States for their comments. A conference was convened in Delhi in November 1954 to discuss the matter, taking into account the difficulties expressed by the states. It was decided that the states could confine the census to holdings above 10 acres in area. Strangely enough, the Planning Commission expressed its reluctance to extend financial support to the states for conducting the census. The conference expressed the hope that the census would be completed latest by 1955. As pointed out by Daniel Thomer, the results of the survey would not have been of any great consequence. Thomer summed up the likely outcome thus:

“The final prospect thus includes one case of complete enumeration, a few cases of full return from the Patwari records, several cases of partial returns from the Patwari records ( on holdings of 10 acres or more), three or more cases of sample survey, and at least two cases of doing nothing’’11

And, in the event the results of the special survey ordered by the Planning Commission, or of the 8th Round of the National Sample Survey conducted in 1954, turned out to be of no practical use either in laying down the policy on ceilings or in drafting the laws or in implementing them. For what one needed for those purposes was precise information about individual holdings which a sample survey could not have revealed. The only purpose that the survey served was to postpone the imposition of ceilings by several years. Further more, suggestions in the First Five Year Plan regarding land management legislation, the laying down of standards of cultivation, the setting up a machinery for implementing and applying ceilings only to inefficient farms would have rendered the whole scheme difficult to implement, and would have also led to rampant administrative corruption. The significant contribution of the First Five Year Plan was that it kept alive the debate on land ceiling which the draft outline had sought to wind up. In the event, however, the inordinate delay in the enforcement of ceilings resulting from the Planning Commission’s own formulation proved fatal to the whole programme.

In the matter of the level of ccilings the First Five Year Plan, more or less, adopted the recommendations of the Congress Agrarian Reform

![image 311](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile311.png)

![image 312](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile312.png)

###### 134 Land Reforms in India



Committee. That Committee had evolved three norms regarding sizes of holdings—Basic, Economic and Optimum. The central concept was that of an Economic Holding. The Committee suggested that the Economic Holding should be determined on the basis of the agronomic conditions of different regions. In the Committee’s view an Economic Holding must afford a reasonable standard of living to the farmer and his family, it must provide full employment to a family of normal size and a pair of bullocks and it must have a bearing on other relevant factors peculiar to the agrarian economy of the region. Taking into account the preponderance of small, uneconomic holdings whose rehabilitation was beyond the organizational competence of the State, the Committee evolved the concept of a Basic Holding. It was defined as a holding smaller than an Economic Holding but larger than a holding which is palpably uneconomic on which individual farming would not be possible. The Committee was of the view that the Basic Holding should be the lower limit of the holding on which productive individual farming was possible.

The Committee recommended individual farming supported by multi-purpose co-operative Societies for holdings not smaller than a Basic Holding. The Committee thought that even with all possible support from co-operatives, productive farming was not possible on tiny holdings smaller than a Basic Holding. The Committee recommended Co-operative joint farming for such holdings.

The Committee felt that just as it had recommended a lower limit for individual farming, there should also be an upper limit. The Committee also felt that in order to avoid social injustice there should be a reasonable relationship between the economic holdings and the maximum size of a holding that an individual could be allowed to cultivate. The Committee’s recommendation was that the maximum size of the holding, which it also described as Optimum Size Holding, should be three times the size of the Economic Holding.12

Following the recommendations of the Congress Agrarian Reforms Committee, the First Five Year Plan recommended that the ceiling on agricultural holdings should be fixed at three times the “Economic Holding” which the Plan called “Family Holding”. The relevant portion of the Planning Commissions recommendation may be seen below:

![image 313](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile313.png)

![image 314](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile314.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 135



“As one method of determining a limit, which may often prove applicable in practical work and is here used by way of illustration, it may be useful to apply a rough and ready criterion such as, for instance, a multiple in terms of what may be regarded as a “family holding” in any given area. A family holding may be defined briefly as being equivalent, according to local conditions and under the existing conditions of technique, either to a plough unit or to a work unit for a family of average size working with such assistance as is customary in agricultural operations. Another possible method of indicating a limit may be to propose an average level of money income which the permissible holding may be expected to yield. The limit which may be appropriate has to be determined by each State in the light of its own circumstances but, broadly speaking, following the recommendations of the Congress Agrarian Reform Committee about three times the family holding would appear to be a fair limit for an individual holding.”13

The First Plan did not visualize any large scale redistribution of land by imposing ceiling on large holdings. It was left to the State Governments to fix ceilings within the broad parameters indicated by the Agrarian Reform Committee and, as already pointed out, thanks to the procedure recommended by the Planning Commission nothing worthwhile could be done during the First Five Year Plan.

###### Report of the Panel on Land Reform

In May 1955 the Planning Commission set up a Panel on Land Reforms to review the progress in the implementation of the land policy laid down in the First Plan and suggest further steps to be taken during the Second Plan. The Panel set up four committees to examine and report on four different subjects, namely, Tenancy Reform, Size of Holdings, Problems of Reorganization and Bhoodan. The convener of the committee on the size of holdings was the distinguished economist M.L. Dantwala. The other members of the committee were H.D. Malaviya, Lai Singh, Narsingha Rao, N. Prasada Rao, Radha Kumud Mukherjee and G. Sankaran Nair. There were sharp differences among the members of the committee regarding the level of ceilings, exemptions etc. N. Prasada Rao, Member of Parliament and Communist leader, circulated a note calling for radical redistribution of

![image 315](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile315.png)

![image 316](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile316.png)

###### 136 Land Reforms in India



land and doing away with most exemptions. A diametrically opposite view was held by Sardar Lai Singh, Member of Parliament and a former Director of Agriculture in Punjab. He gave a minute of dissent opposing the imposition of ceilings. The Committee, however, accepted the principle that there should be an absolute limit to the amount of land which any individual might hold and gave the following justification for fixing a ceiling on agricultural holdings:

“The policy of imposition of ceiling would be able to make a contribution towards fulfilling the following objectives:.

- i. Meeting the widespread desire to possess land.
- ii. Reducing glaring inequalities in ownership and use of land,
- iii. Reducing inequalities in agricultural incomes, and
- iv. Enlarging the sphere of self-employment.”14


The Committee also suggested that ceilings should be applied to the other sectors also because the imposition of a ceiling on agricultural lands would, then, meet with less opposition and there would be greater chances of its proving successful.

The Committee considered the question of applying a ceiling only to land under the personal cultivation of the owner, or to the entire land of the owner i.e. personally cultivated as well as leased out land. On the assumption that in respect of leased lands, positive steps would be taken to enable the tenants to become owners, the Committee recommended that the ceiling should apply to owned land under personal cultivation. The Committee also recommended that in determining the area under personal cultivation, all uncultivated but cultivable lands not leased to tenants should be taken into account. Land taken on lease but not liable to resumption should also be taken into account for the purposes of the ceiling. In the event the Committee’s assumption that tenants would become owners within a reasonable period of time proved to be unrealistic.

The Committee deliberated at some length on the level of the ceiling. In the Committee’s own words:

“The level of ceiling is usually indicated in terms of a multiple of the ‘family’ or the ‘economic’ holding. The question whether this basic norm should be determined by reference to acreage or to income was discussed. A view was expressed that the determining criterion should be what is termed as a “plough unit” i.e.,. the area of land which an

![image 317](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile317.png)

![image 318](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile318.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 137



average family could cultivate with a pair of bullocks. We are, however, of the view that family holding should ensure the minimum income necessary for supporting a family, which a plough unit may not always yield, although cases could be visualized in which it could yield substantially more. Some reference to income was, therefore, considered necessary. According to the data on national income, the annual income per earner engaged in “agriculture” amounts to Rs. 500/-. Assuming 2 to 2.5 earners in the family of an agriculturist, the annual income of an average agricultural family should come to Rs. 1200/-. No unit which yielded an income less than the national average to an agricultural family could legitimately be considered as economic. On adopting this as a rough basis, wc suggest that a farm which yields a gross average income of Rs. 1600 or a net income including remuneration for family labour of Rs. 1200, and is not less than a plough unit or its multiples in area, may be considered as a family holding”.15

In passing it may be pointed out that the above report is the only official paper that ever suggested a specific annual income as a criterion for deciding the level of ceiling. The Second Five Year Plan, as we shall see presently, did not accept the proposal as it was rightly considered to be impractical. Though there were differences among the members of the committee regarding the level of the ceiling, the consensus was that the ceiling should be fixed at three times the family holding. Accordingly the Committee recommended:

“The limit should be three family holdings for an average family in which the number of members does not exceed five. One additional family holding should be allowed for each additional member, subject to a maximum of six family holdings.”16

The Committee also considered the question whether in applying the ceiling the aggregate area held by all the members of the family should be taken into account or whether the land held by an individual member should be regarded as constituting a separate holding. The Committee’s view was that: “Family is the real operative limit in land ownership as in land management. We, therefore, recommend that in the fixing of the ceiling, the aggregate area held by all the members of the family should be taken into account. For this purpose members of the family should be deemed to consist of husband, wife and dependent

![image 319](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile319.png)

![image 320](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile320.png)

###### 138 Land Reforms in India



sons and daughters and grandchildren. Land held by married daughters and earning sons should be excluded. Where the property has devolved on two or more heirs after the death of the parent but the property has been held in common by them undivided the share of each heir should be regarded as a separate holding.”17

The other important recommendations of the Committee were about payment of compensation, exemption from ceiling and distribution of surplus land. Regarding compensation the Committee advised that “the amount should in no case be more than 25 per cent of the market value and should not exceed (inclusive of interest charges) the aggregate of the increase in land revenue for 20 years. The increase in land revenue will be due to the fact that the state will be receiving fair rent from the new settlers on the surplus land which will be more than the land revenue paid by the ex-owner. The compensation may be paid in bonds redeemable in about twenty years. After say 20 years payment of fair rent, the recipient should be declared to be the full fledged owner, after which he will be liable to pay no more than the prevailing land revenue.”18

The Committee favoured exemption of plantations of tea, coffee and rubber, existing orchards, bonafide cattle breeding, dairy and wool raising farms and compact, well managed, mechanized farms from the ceiling law. The Committee was however, against exempting sugar cane farms from the ceiling law. The Committee could not arrive at any agreed conclusion regarding the distribution of surplus land and the imposition of a floor below which individual farming should not be permitted. As we shall see presently, the Planning Commission while formulating the Second Five Year Plan did not accept in toto the recommendations of the Panel on Land Reforms. The departures made in the plan tended to make the policy more flexible and to some extent even vague.

###### Second Five Year Plan

According to the Second Five Year Plan the objectives of land reform were two fold:

“Firstly, to remove such impediments on agricultural production as arise from the agrarian structure; and secondly, to create conditions for evolving, as speedily as may be possible, an agrarian economy with high levels of efficiency and productivity.”19

![image 321](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile321.png)

![image 322](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile322.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 139



Again, as in the First Plan, the emphasis was on increased production. The imposition of a ceiling was not recognized as a measure that would promote agricultural production. The sole justification was that it would promote social justice and provide a congenial environment for the development of a co-operative rural economy. This was how the Second Plan put forward the case for ceilings.

“In the conditions of India large disparities in the distribution of wealth and income are inconsistent with economic progress in any sector. This consideration applies with even greater force to land. The area of land available for cultivation is necessarily limited. In the past rights in land were the principal factor which determined both social

status and economic opportunity for different groups in the rural

population. For building up a progressive rural economy, it is essential that disparities in the ownership of land should be greatly reduced. In view of the existing pattern of distribution and size of agricultural holdings redistribution of land in excess of a ceiling may yield relatively limited results. Nevertheless, it is important that some effective steps should be taken in this direction during the Second Five Year Plan period so as to afford opportunities to landless sections of the rural population to gain in social status and to feel a sense of opportunity equally with other sections of the society. Reduction of disparities in the ownership of land is also essential for developing a co-operative rural economy, for, co-operation thrives best in homogeneous groups in which there are no large inequalities.”20

In the Second Five Year Plan the main goal of agrarian policy was increased production of food and raw materials to meet the requirements of a growing population and support rapid industrialization. A ceiling on agricultural holdings was not perceived to play a positive role in promoting agricultural growth, and hence it was not advocated with any great zeal or conviction. Taking into account the need to promote social justice the Second Plan recommended:

“It is proposed that during the Second Plan steps should be taken in each state to impose a ceiling on existing agricultural holdings. The ceilings would apply to owned land (including land under permanent

and heritable rights) held under personal cultivation, the tenants being

enabled to acquire rights of ownership on lines indicated earlier.”21

![image 323](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile323.png)

![image 324](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile324.png)

###### 140 Land Reforms in India



Regarding the question whether the ceiling should apply to holdings of individuals or holdings of families, the Planning Commission did not take any definite stand. After discussing the pros and cons. The Planning Commission left the matter to be decided by the State Governments. The Second Plan made the following suggestions regarding the level of ceiling:

“In determining the level at which the ceiling should apply there is need for some convenient units which could be indicated in a general way and later worked out in detail in different areas. In the First Five Year Plan it was suggested that for this and other purposes multiples of what may be regarded as a “family holding” in any given area may be used. A family holding may be considered from two aspects, namely (a) as an operational unit, and (b) as an area of land which can yield a certain average income. In the First Five Year Plan a “Family holding” was described as an area equivalent, according to local conditions and under existing conditions of technique, either to a plough unit or to a work unit for a family of average size working with such assistance as is customary in agricultural occupations. The income from a given area of land depends on the crops grown, the level of agricultural efficiency and the amount of investment which is made. A given area of land may yield different incomes to different individuals, depending on their skill, capacity and resources. As improved agricultural practices are adopted and agriculture becomes more efficient and diversified, income per unit of land should increase steadily. Thus, it is difficult to correlate a family holding to a given level of money income adjusted to a supposed level of prices. The balance of convenience, therefore, is in favour of each state specifying according to the conditions of different regions, classes of soil, irrigation etc. the area of land which may be declared as a family holding. The practical application of the concept of family holding is not without its difficulties and it might be of value to States if a small group of experts with practical experience of settlement and revenue work could study the subject further.

“In view of the fact that only a small fraction of agricultural holdings can be described as large holdings, it will be convenient to place the ceiling at about three family holdings. If the ceiling is determined with reference to individual holdings, it might not be necessary to take further account of the number of members in an

![image 325](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile325.png)

![image 326](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile326.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 141



individual owner’s family. On the other hand, if the ceiling applied to the area held by a family, it would be necessary to prescribe some method for taking account of the number of members in the family. According to its social conditions and other relevant factors, each state may determine whether the ceiling should apply to individual holdings, or to holdings of families, and especially in the latter event, the basis on which the size of the family should be allowed for in the application of the ceiling. For instance, the committee of the Panel on Land Reforms to which reference has been made above was of the view that where the number of members of a family is larger than five, the ceiling o( the family holding may be raised to a maximum of six family holdings.”22

Thus the Planning Commission rejected as impractical the suggestion to relate the concept of family holding to any particular level of income. The family holding was conceived of purely as an operational unit, and it was suggested that the ceiling for the family should be fixed at about three family holdings. As already pointed out, it was, however, left entirely to the State Governments to decide whether they would have ceilings for individuals or families and in the latter case, what allowance should be made for the size of the family in the application of the ceiling. Regarding exemptions, the Plan added sugarcane farms also to the list recommended by the Committee. The Planning Commission did not make any firm recommendation about the principles to be followed in the payment of compensation. The suggestion of the Panel that compensation should not exceed 25 per cent of the market price of the land was ignored. It was left to the State Governments to take a decision in the light of local conditions. And as could be expected, the result was a lack of uniformity in this matter also in the different State laws.

Regarding the distribution of ceiling-surplus land, the Second Plan recommended that preference should be given to tenants displaced as a result of the resumption of tenanted land by landlords, farmers with uneconomic holdings and landless workers. What should be the inter-se priority among these categories was not spelt out nor was it indicated if there should be any upper limit to the area of land to be allotted to each individual. It was, however, suggested that as far as possible settlements should be on co-operative lines.

![image 327](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile327.png)

![image 328](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile328.png)

###### 142 Land Reforms in India



###### Third and Fourth Plans

The Third Plan simply reiterated what had been stated in the Second Plan. The twin objectives of land reform continued to be the removal of “such impediments to the increase in agricultural production as arise from the agrarian structure inherited from the past and the elimination of all elements of exploitation and social injustice within the agrarian system, to provide security for the tiller of the soil and assure equality of status and opportunity to all sections of the population.”23

In pursuance of the second objective justification was found for imposing a ceiling on agricultural holdings. The Planning Commission knew that not much surplus land would become available for redistribution. The Commission contented itself by entertaining the fond hope that “such re-distribution of land as might be possible would along with other measures which have been taken for resettlement of wastelands, afford a measure of opportunity to the landless section of the population.” 24

The Third Plan gave no new guide lines on ceilings. Though it was found that the States had adopted different principles in enacting the laws, no attempt was made to bring about a measure of uniformity. The view taken was that “once legislation had been enacted amendments should aim primarily at eliminating deficiencies and facilitating implementation rather than in introducing fundamental changes in the principles underlying legislation.” 25

The Planning Commission realized that large-scale transfers tended to whittle down the effect of the ceiling legislation. The Third Plan made the following suggestions:

- 1) “Where legislation does not contain a provision for disregarding transfers, in view of the fact that transfers have taken place on a considerable scale, a suitable date subsequent to which transfers are disregarded may be proposed, if necessary through an amending legislation. This date may be the date of the publication of the ceiling proposals or an earlier date as may be prescribed in view of the local conditions.
- 2) In respect of transfers made after the specified date, distinction may be made between (a) transfers among members of the family, (b) benami transfers and other transfers which have


![image 329](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile329.png)

![image 330](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile330.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 143



- not been made for valuable consideration and through a registered document, and (c) transfers made for valuable consideration through a registered document. Transfers coming under (a) and (b) can be disregarded. Transfers falling under (c) may need to be dealt with differently in view of the fact that the transferee may be small owners or landless persons who may have purchased some land. It may be necessary to protect such transfers, at any rate up to a prescribed limit, say, a family holding.
- 3) There should be a provision for a review of transfers by a competent authority on the lines suggested above.”26 In respect of exemptions the Third Plan reiterated the policy laid down in the Second Plan.


By the time the Fourth Plan came to be finalized, land reform had ceased to be a subject of topical significance. In the Fourth Plan there was only a brief reference to the question of ceilings. The Plan simply noted that the State laws were far from perfect and that even those imperfect laws had not been implemented effectively.

###### State Laws on Ceilings—1950-1970

As we have seen, the Second Five Year Plan had recommended the imposition of a ceiling on agricultural holdings during the plan period. After a review of the progress of land reform, the standing committee of the National Development Council decided in 1957 that the States which had not yet enacted necessary legislation should do so by the end of March 1959 and the laws should be implemented within a period of about three years. The Indian National Congress at its session held at Nagpur in January 1959 passed a resolution urging the States to enact ceiling laws expeditiously. Following these directives all the States enacted ceiling laws before the end of 1961. Jammu & Kashmir and West Bengal had enacted ceiling laws well before the Second Plan. Under a law enacted in 1950 (the Big Landed Estates Abolition Act, 1950) Jammu & Kashmir fixed a uniform ceiling of 22 J acres on individual landholders without providing for any classification of land. A significant feature of the law was that the surplus land was to be

![image 331](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile331.png)

![image 332](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile332.png)

###### 144 Land Reforms in India



taken over without the payment of any compensation to the landowners. On the implementation of the ceiling law about 1.8 lakh hectares of surplus land vested in the State Government In West Bengal a ceiling on the agricultural holdings was imposed in 1955 through an amendment of the West Bengal Estates Acquisition Act, 1953. A uniform ceiling of 25 acres was fixed on individual landholders. The legislation resulted in the vesting of about 3.5 lakh hectares of surplus land in the State. These were the only states where a sizeable area of surplus land vested in the state following the enactment of the first round of ceiling legislation. Apart from other reasons, the fact that the laws were enacted well before the publication of the Second Five Year Plan and the protracted public debate on land ceiling led to the better implementation of these two laws.

As we have already seen, the Second Five Year Plan had left it to the States to decide, in the light of local conditions, whether the ceiling should apply to holdings of individuals or to holdings of families. The Plan simply gave a broad suggestion that the ceiling should be put at three “family holdings.” It was, however, left to the States to fix the area of land that would constitute a family holding in different regions taking into account classes of soil, irrigation etc. Besides the concept of a family holding, another concept that was adopted by several States was that of a “standard acre.” As early as 1954 the Government of India had suggested that an average acre of irrigated land or other land of good quality in a state as a whole or a region thereof, might constitute a “standard acre” and other classes of land given correspondingly higher or lower values. As pointed out by the Planning Commission, “the principal criteria adopted in the States are the yields of principal crops, the rent rates or the assessment rates worked out at the last settlement and the market values of the land. Generally the soil classification at the time of settlement had served as a working basis with such modifications as became necessary in view of the changes which might have occurred since the last settlement.”27

Though the Second Plan had listed the categories of farms that could be exempted from the ceiling, the recommendation was rendered flexible by adding that “in the nature of things these are general suggestions which should be adapted to the needs and conditions of the State.” It was also left to the State Governments to decide the basis on

![image 333](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile333.png)

![image 334](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile334.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 145



which compensation should be paid to the land owners, the manner in which the surplus land should be distributed and the amounts to be recovered from the allottees. The Planning Commission contented itself by giving some broad suggestions. The inevitable result was great variations between the ceiling laws of the different states in respect of unit of application, levels of ceiling, classification of land, exemptions and compensation.

We shall now proceed to discuss briefly the contents of the ceiling laws enacted by the different states during the period 1950-1970. In the discussion we shall be taking into account the amendments made up to the end of 1970. A reference has already been made to the law of Jammu and Kashmir enacted in 1950 which fixed a ceiling of 22 J

acres for a landholder without making any allowance for differences in the quality of the land or the number of members in the family. The West Bengal law enacted in 1955 provided for a uniform ceiling of 25 acres for individual landholders without making any allowance for the quality of land or the size of the family. Later on the law was amended to apply it to the family. The ceiling limit was reduced to five hectares (12.4 acres) of irrigated land or 7 hectares (17.3 acres) of unirrigated land. Irrigated land was defined as land capable of being irrigated by any state canal, or state tube well.

The law of Andhra Pradesh fixed the ceiling for a family of five or fewer members at 4^ family holdings. A family holding was defined to be equivalent to 6 acres of class A land, 8 acres of class B land, 10 acres of class C land, 12 acres of D land, 24 acres of class E land, 36 acres of class F land, 48 acres of class G land or 72 acres of class H land. The classification was made taking into account the availability of irrigation and the nature of the soil as in the settlement records of the region where the land is located. For a family of five or fewer members the actual area varied between a minimum of 27 acres and a maximum of 324 acres depending on the classes of land in the holding. Allowance was made for the size of the family of the landholder by allowing one additional family holding for every member in excess of five without any overall limit.

The Assam law fixed the ceiling at 50 acres for a landholder without making any allowances for the size of the family or the quality of the land. Subsequently the limit was reduced to 25 acres.

![image 335](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile335.png)

![image 336](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile336.png)

###### 146 Land Reforms in India



In Bihar the ceiling for a landholder was fixed at 20 acres of class I land, 30 acres of class II land, 40 acres of class HI land, 50 acres of class IV land or 60 acres of class V land. Land irrigated by public canals was treated as class I land, land irrigated by state tube wells or lift irrigation works was treated as class II land, orchards and land used for horticultural purposes was treated as class III land, diara land was treated as class IV land and class V land consisted of hilly or sandy land, surplus homestead land or other land that would not yield paddy, rabi or cash crops. In the case of undivided Hindu families each co-parcener was entitled to a separate ceiling area. Persons entitled under the personal law to be maintained by the landholder and dependent on him were treated as his family. Where the number of members in the family exceeded four, additional 1 /5th of the ceiling area was allowed for every member in excess of four subject to an outside limit of twice the ceiling area.

Under the Gujarat law different levels of ceiling were fixed for different local areas specified in the Act. For perennially irrigated land the ceiling varied between 19 and 44 acres, for rice land and seasonally irrigated land the ceiling varied between 38 and 88 acres and for dry crop land including land irrigated from non-government sources the ceiling varied between 56 and 132 acres. Perennially irrigated land was defined as land with an assured supply of water for a period of not less than ten months from any tank, canal or bandhara constructed or maintained by the State Government. Seasonally irrigated land was defined as land with assured supply of water for a period of less than ten months but not less than four months during the period from September 15 to the end of February. No allowance was made for the size of the family in the Gujarat law.

In the laws of Punjab and Haryana the ceiling was fixed for the individual landholder without making any allowance for the size of his family. In both the laws ceiling was fixed at 30 standard acres but not exceeding 60 ordinary acres. For displaced persons the ceiling was 50 standard acres not exceeding 100 ordinary acres. A standard acre was defined as an acre of land whose average settlement yield is not less

- than 10 maunds of wheat (or other equivalent produce) and not more
- than 11 maunds of wheat. A valuation of 16 annas was given for the standard acre. Higher or lower valuation was given for each plot on the


![image 337](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile337.png)

![image 338](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile338.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 147



basis of the yield of wheat assumed at the time of the last settlement. Depending on the quality of the soil the ceiling could vary between

- 26^ and 60 acres of land. In Kerala the ceiling for a family of two or more but not more than

five members was fixed at 10 standard acres with the condition that the ceiling area shall not be less than 12 or more than 15 acres. For an adult unmarried person or a family consisting of a sole surviving member the ceiling was fixed at 5 standard acres so however that the ceiling area shall not be less than six and not more than seven and half acres in extent. In the case of a family consisting of more than 5 members, the ceiling of ten standard acre would be increased at the rate of one standard acre for each member in excess of five, so however that the ceiling area shall not be less than 12 and more than 20 acres in extent. In a schedule appended to the act a detailed conversion table was provided for converting various categories of land into standard acre. The term family was defined as husband, wife and their unmarried minor children.

In Maharashtra the ceiling was fixed for the individual landholder, but an allowance was made for the size of his family by allowing an additional 1/6 of the ceiling area for every member in excess of five subject to an outside limit of twice the ceiling area for a family of five . The law fixed the ceiling area for different classes of land in different regions. The limit was fixed after taking into consideration soil classification, climate and rainfall of the area, the average yield of crops, the prevailing prices of commodities, the general economic conditions and agricultural resources of the area. For land perennially irrigated by flood irrigation the ceiling was fixed at 18 acres, for seasonally irrigated lands with irrigation for two seasons the limit was

- 27 acres and for land with irrigation for one season the ceiling was fixed at 48 acres. For dry land the ceiling varied between 66 and 126 acres depending on the region where the land was located. The term family was defined so as to include father, mother, brother, son, grandson, and dependent sister and daughters.


In Karnataka (Mysore) the ceiling was fixed at 27 standard acres for a family of five members. Where the number of members in the family exceeded five, an additional area of six standard acres was

![image 339](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile339.png)

![image 340](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile340.png)

###### 148 Land Reforms in India



allowed for each member in excess of five subject to an outside limit of twice the ceiling area. A standard acre was defined as one acre of first class land or larger areas of inferior land equivalent to one acre of first class land. The ceiling area for a family of five could vary between 27 acres of first class land to 216 acres of class seven land. For a large family the actual area admissible could vary between 54 and 432 acres. The term family was defined so as to include husband/wife dependent children and grandchildren.
