In Madhya Pradesh, as in Bihar, a separate ceiling area was allowed to each co-parcener of a Hindu undivided family. The ceiling was fixed at 25 standard acres. A standard acre was defined as an acre of perennially irrigated land or two acres of seasonally irrigated land or three acres of dry land. Allowance was made for landless dependents at the rate of five standard acres for each such dependent subject to an outside limit of 50 standard acres. Under the law dependents could include son, grandson, unmarried daughter of a son or a grandson, and unmarried sister.

In Orissa the ceiling for each landholder was fixed at 20 acres of class I land 30 acres of class II land, 60 acres of class III land and 80 acres of class IV land. No allowance was made for the size of the family. Land with irrigation for not less than 240 days in the year was classified as class I land, land with assured irrigation for at least 120 days was classified as class II land, other irrigated lands and all other lands on which paddy was grown were classified as class HI land and other lands were classified as class IV lands.

In Rajasthan for a family of five or fewer members the ceiling was fixed at 30 standard acres. Five standard acres were allowed for each member of the family in excess of five subject to an overall ceiling of 60 standard acres for any family. In terms of ordinary acres the ceiling could vary between 22 and 336 acres.

The Tamil Nadu law fixed the ceiling for a family of five or fewer members at 15 standard acres. The term family was defined so as to include a person, his/her wife or husband, his/her minor sons, unmarried daughters, minor grandsons and unmarried grand daughters. Allowance was made for the size of the family by allowing additional land at the rate of five standard acres for each member in excess of five subject to a limit of 30 standard acres to a family. A standard acre was

![image 341](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile341.png)

![image 342](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile342.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 149



defined as an acre of land assessed to land revenue exceeding Rs.10 but not exceeding Rs. 15. Taking into account the productivity of the land

- as reflected by the land revenue, conversion ratios were fixed for different types of land. In actual practice the ceiling for a family of five could vary between 12 and 60 acres depending on the type of land comprising the holding.

In Uttar Pradesh the ceiling was fixed for an individual landholder

- at 40 acres of “fair quality land”. The law defined “fair quality land” as land the hereditary rate whereof was above Rs. 6 per acre. In calculating ceiling area for land other than “fair quality land”, one and one half acre of such land, the hereditary rate whereof was above Rs.4 or less per acre, but did not exceed Rs. 6 per acre, and two acres of such land, the hereditary rate whereof was Rs. 4 or less per acre, was deemed to be equal to one acre of “fair quality land”. Thus for a family of five members the area of land admissible could vary between 40 and 80 acres. Allowance was made for larger families by allowing 8 acres of “fair quality land” for each member in excess of five with an overall limit of 64 acres of such land.


Thus there were wide variations between States in the matter of the level of ceiling, classification of land, unit of application and allowance for the size of the family. Equally bewildering were the variations between the laws of different States in the matter of exemption from the ceiling law . As we have seen, the Second Five Year Plan favoured exemption for plantations of tea, coffee and rubber, existing orchards, bonafide cattle-breeding, dairy and wool raising farms, compact, well managed, mechanized farms and sugar cane farms operated by sugar factories. The State legislatures in their wisdom exempted many more categories of land from the operation of the ceiling law. Some of these exemptions were reasonable and justified while the others were needlessly liberal. It was eminently sensible to exempt land held by Central and State Governments, and local bodies and Bhoodan and Gramdan land. But there was little justification for the exemption granted to private forest land in Kerala, to forest land and land used for grazing cattle or growing trees, in Karnataka, to hill land in Tamil Nadu and to Tank Fisheries in West Bengal. There were several provisions in the laws of the different States that fell between these two extremes. Such were the exemptions to lands awarded for gallantry, lands held by

![image 343](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile343.png)

![image 344](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile344.png)

###### 150 Land Reforms in India



religious, charitable or educational institutions/trusts, other kinds of public trusts, private and public sector industries, co-operative farming societies and other types of co-operative societies. While it was reasonable to allow them to keep the minimum area needed for carrying on their legitimate activities, there was no justification for wholesale exemption granted in some of the laws. No useful purposes will be served by dilating on this subject. All that is necessary for our present purpose is to note that the numerous exemptions reduced drastically the area of surplus land that could be mopped up.

The ceiling laws of the different States adopted widely differing principles for determining the amount of compensation payable for surplus land. The simplest provisions were in the laws of Assam and Madhya Pradesh where compensation was fixed as a multiple of land revenue. In Assam it was fixed at 50 times the land revenue for cultivated lands and 25 times the land revenue for fallow land. The compensation was payable in cash in annual installments not exceeding five. The Madhya Pradesh law fixed the compensation on a graded slab system based on land revenue per acre. It was 50 times the land revenue for land whose land revenue was one rupee or less and it was 20 times the land revenue for land whose land revenue was Rs. 6 per acre. Compensation up to Rs. 1000 was payable in cash and the balance was payable in 19 equal instalments. The ceiling laws of Gujarat and Maharashtra fixed the quantum of compensation in graded multiples of the assessment. (In the land system of the erstwhile Bombay Presidency “assessment rates” meant the historically determined rates worked during Survey/Resurvey and Settlement taking into account the classification of soil, depth of the soil etc. and making allowance for altered circumstances).28 As the assessment rate is the basis for determining the land revenue, the method adopted in Gujarat and Maharashtra was similar to the one adopted in Assam and Madhya Pradesh. In the Gujarat law the compensation was fixed at 200 times the assessment to 80 times the assessment for different classes of dry land. In Maharashtra the compensation was 195 to 55 times the assessment in different local areas for dry crop land. The amount of compensation was enhanced by 25 per cent, 50 per cent and 100 per cent depending upon the intensity of the irrigation. Both laws provided for the payment of compensation in cash or transferable twenty year

![image 345](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile345.png)

![image 346](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile346.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 151



bonds or partly in cash and partly in bonds. The Bihar and Kerala laws provided for fixed amounts of compensation for different classes of land. In Bihar the compensation was Rs. 900, 600, 450 and 300 per acre for class I, II, III, and IV lands respectively. For poorer lands it could be Rs. 150, 75 or 50 per acre. The compensation was payable in cash or in 30 year bonds. The Kerala law fixed the compensation for different types of paddy lands between Rs. 700 and Rs. 2000 per acre in different taluks. For other kinds of land the compensation varied between Rs. 200 to Rs. 3000 per acre. The compensation was payable in cash or in 16 year bonds or partly in cash and partly in bonds. The Uttar Pradesh law fixed the compensation at different rates for Bhumidhars, Sirdars and Asamis. For Bhumidhars it was 80 times the land revenue or 40 times the hereditary rates, whichever was greater, plus 20 times the difference between the hereditary rate and the land revenue. For Sirdars and tenants with permanent and heritable rights the compensation was 20 times the hereditary rate, plus 20 times the difference between the hereditary rate and the land revenue. For Asamis and other tenants without permanent rights the compensation was 5 times the rent. The compensation was payable in cash or in bonds. In Rajasthan compensation was computed at 30 times the rent rate for the first 25 acres of surplus land and at 25 times the rent rate for the remaining land. The compensation was payable in cash or bonds or partly in cash and partly in bonds. Gross annual income from the land was the basis for the calculation of compensation in Andhra Pradesh and fair rent computed at one-fifth of the gross produce was the basis in Punjab and Haryana. In Andhra Pradesh for the first 4\ family holdings of surplus land compensation was 5 times the gross income, for the next 4 j family holdings 4 times the gross income and for surplus land beyond that 3 times the gross income. The gross income was to be determined with reference to the average gross yield of the land during a period of three years immediately prior to the filing of declaration at the averages of the prices prevailing during the period of ten years immediately preceding such date. The compensation was payable in cash or in bonds or partly in cash and partly in bonds. The Punjab and Haryana laws provided for the payment of compensation at 12 times the fair rent for the first 25 standard acres and for the

![image 347](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile347.png)

![image 348](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile348.png)

###### 152 Land Reforms in India



remaining land at 9 times the land revenue or Rs. 200, whichever was less. The law also stipulated that fair rent would be one fifth of the gross produce. The compensation was payable in cash or in bonds or partly in cash and partly in bonds. In Karnataka, Tamil Nadu and West Bengal compensation was fixed on the basis of the net income. The Karnataka law fixed compensation at 10 times the average net annual income. The law also prescribed the manner in which the tribunal constituted for the purpose would calculate the annual net income. Compensation for small areas of surplus land not exceeding one basic holding was payable in cash. For larger areas the compensation was payable in 20 year bonds. Under the Tamil Nadu law the compensation was 12 times the net income for the first Rs. 5000 of the net income, 11 times the net income for the next Rs. 5000 of the net income, 10 times the net income for the next Rs. 5000 of the net income and 9 times the net income for the balance. Compensation was payable in cash or in bonds or in both. Amounts below Rs. 50 were to be paid in cash. In West Bengal compensation was payable on a graded slab system according to the net income. The rate varied from 20 times the net income for the first slab of Rs. 500 to two times for the highest slab of above Rs. 80,000. In the case of religious or charitable institutions the compensation was payable as perpetual annuity equal to the net annual income. Such were the wide variations between the ceiling laws of the different states regarding the payment of compensation for surplus land.

When it was noticed that transfers and partitions were militating against the objectives of the ceiling law, a specific suggestion was made in the Third Plan that the ceiling laws should be amended for nullifying such transactions made after a particular date. The Commission also suggested that retrospective effect should be given to the law from the date on which the ceiling proposals were published or even from an earlier date if local conditions warranted it.

As the mischief had already been done subsequent legislation for nullifying the transactions proved to be of little avail. We shall briefly discuss the manner in which the different states proceeded in the matter. The Orissa Act did not contain any provision for disregarding transfers. There was also no provision for the vesting of the surplus land in the state. The landlords were left free to dispose of the surplus

![image 349](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile349.png)

![image 350](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile350.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 153



land as they liked. Subsequent amendments were of little use. In Rajasthan, though the original Act provided for ignoring transfers after the introduction of the Bill, by a subsequent amendment transfers were permitted. The ceiling laws of Bihar and Madhya Pradesh permitted landowners to effect transfers within specified periods after the enactment of the law. Under the Bihar law a landholder could transfer lands within six months of the commencement of the ceiling law by way of gift to his son, daughter, any children of his son or daughter or to any other person or persons who would have inherited such land or would have been entitled to a share therein had the landholder died intestate. All that the law did was to invalidate other types of transfers and gifts made after the date of introduction of the Bill except transfers made by registered documents for valuable consideration. The Madhya Pradesh law permitted transfers before the expiry of two years from the commencement of the Act by way of sale to joint farming or better farming societies, agricultural labourers, landless persons, displaced persons and small holders. The law invalidated only other transfers and partitions made after the date of publication of the Bill. The ceiling laws of Assam, Kerala, Tamil Nadu, Uttar Pradesh and West Bengal provided for ignoring transfers made after the date of introduction of the Bill or its publication. There was, however, a significant loophole in the Kerala law. The law invalidated only voluntary transfers other than those made by way of partition, on account of natural love and affection, or in favour of a person who was a tenant from before 7 July, 1960 and continued to be a tenant till the date of transfer or in favour of a religious, charitable or educational institution. The exemptions were so numerous as to make the provision totally infructuous. The Andhra and Maharashtra laws provided that transfers made after the enactment of the ceiling law should be ignored. The Gujarat law provided for ignoring transfers effected after 15 January, 1959 the date on which the Indian National Congress passed a resolution at the session held at Nagpur calling for ceiling on agricultural holdings. Thus only a few states acted on the Planning Commission’s belated recommendation to nuliify transfers with retrospective effect. Even where the law was amended to nullify transfers, the provision remained largely unimplemented. Thus transfers, legal as well as illegal, proved to be a potent factor in rendering the ceiling laws ineffective.

![image 351](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile351.png)

![image 352](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile352.png)

###### 154 Land Reforms in India



###### Implementation

After enacting the modest ceiling laws riddled with numerous loopholes, the states proceeded to implement them in an unhurried and perfunctory manner. The high levels of ceiling, numerous exemptions and widespread transfers, both legal and illegal, would have anyway led to a drastic reduction in the area of land that could be declared as surplus. Furthermore, as we have seen, the laws were enacted after a protracted debate spread over several years. So the alert landowners got plenty of time to dispose of the surplus land by resorting to partitions, transfers and a variety of other devices. Orchards were planted overnight and co-operative farms or other exempted categories of farms were established in great hurry. Formation of phoney trusts for religious, educational or other purposes proved to be a major device for the evasion of the law. Even the imperfect laws were so inefficiently implemented that the whole exercise was rendered futile. Apart from the apathy of' the bureaucracy, there were also some practical difficulties. The land records were in a poor shape in several states and this made implementation difficult. Most enactments laid down protracted procedures which slowed down, and even thwarted, their enforcement. Several rich landowners resorted to litigation to frustrate the enforcement of the law. The implementation of the law was left to the Revenue administration which was overburdened with numerous duties. The senior officers were by and large friendly or sympathetic to the large landowners. The lower echelons of the Revenue administration, apart from lacking in integrity were also under the sway of large landowners who were also men who wielded considerable political power. In spite of all these difficulties, implementation would have been less unsatisfactory if the political leadership had accorded high priority to the strict enforcement of the law. We have seen how ambivalent was the attitude of the political leadership to the question of ceiling on agricultural holdings. The indifference continued during implementation as well.

In the circumstances it is not at all surprising that the ceiling laws did not produce any worthwhile results. As of December 1970 only about one million hectares of agricultural land had vested in the State Governments and only about half of it had been distributed. The State wise figures are given in the table which follows:29

![image 353](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile353.png)

![image 354](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile354.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 155



State Area vested Area distributed (in hectares)

- 1. Andhra Pradesh 29,500
- 2. Assam 27,200
- 3. Gujarat 16,500 5,600.
- 4. Haryana 72,900 22,000.
- 5. Jammu and Kashmir 180,000 180,000.
- 6. Madhya Pradesh 30,250 5,000.
- 7. Maharashtra 152,000 46,600.
- 8. Punjab 76,650 24,150.
- 9. Tamil Nadu 10,100 6,250.
- 10. Uttar Pradesh 96,240 48,250.
- 11. West Bengal 346,250 138,840.


Source: A Country Paper prepared for the F.A.O. Special Conference on Agrarian Reform, p. 14, mimeo, Government of India, Ministry of Agriculture, December 1970.

###### The Second Round of Legislation

By the end of the sixties it had become clear that in the conditions obtaining in the country nothing worthwhile was likely to be achieved by the imposition of ceilings on agricultural holdings. The policy makers had ceased to have any interest in the subject. The Third Five Year Plan did not pay much attention to the problem. The Fourth Plan practically ignored the issue. Yet the year 1969 witnessed a sudden revival of interest in land reform in general and in ceilings in particular. Three factors seem to have been responsible for this renewed interest in land reform. The first factor was the land grab movement launched by the Communists. Though the movement itself was no great success, it drew attention to the poor implementation of the ceiling laws. The second factor was the growing agrarian unrest in several areas spearheaded by Naxalite groups. A paper prepared by the Research and Policy Division of the Ministry of Home Affairs in 1969 drew pointed attention to the failure of land reform as the main cause for the widespread agrarian unrest.30 The third and perhaps, the most important factor responsible for the revival of interest in land reform was the split in the Congress Party and the adoption by the ruling group of a set of populist programmes ostensibly intended to favour the

![image 355](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile355.png)

![image 356](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile356.png)

###### 156 Land Reforms in India



weaker sections of the society, a s this new interest in land reform was bom out of partisan electoral calculations and not out of any genuine political convictions, it lasted for only a short period. And as there existed no political will in favour of thorough-going reform nothing much was achieved, though a great deal of noise was made all over the country. We shall now deal with the developments during that period.

###### Chief Ministers’ Conferences, 1969 and 1970

A conference of Chief Ministers was held in New Delhi on 28-29 November, 1969 to discuss measures of land reforms. The agenda notes circulated at the conference gave the rationale for reducing the levels of ceiling and enforcing the laws vigorously. The relevant part of the agenda notes is reproduced below.

“It has been observed that the provisions relating to the level of ceiling and transfers, as they stand at present, will not make available sufficient surplus lands for redistribution. In the Third Five Year Plan it had been recommended that the ceiling laws should be enforced vigorously without waiting for introducing major changes in legislation. Now in the context of the high yielding varieties programme, improved methods of agriculture and intensive cropping, it is worth examining whether the ceiling limits provided in the state laws do not call for a review. In states where the provisions have not so far been enforced, or where substantial progress has so far not been made, suitable amendments could be incorporated immediately with a view to:

- a) leveling down the ceiling limit;
- b) making conditions of transfers more stringent; and
- c) reducing evasions which have been made possible by over liberal exemptions.


“Recently the Supreme Court has upheld the validity of the Orissa Land Reforms (Amendment) Act, 1965, which sought to reduce the ceiling limit which had been imposed earlier. There seems to be no constitutional difficulty in reducing the ceiling limit, particularly in states where the ceiling provisions have not been enforced. With irrigation support, even a plot of 1-2 hectares becomes a viable unit. In view of the heavy concentration of ownership and cultivation holdings and existence of a large section of the rural population as landless

![image 357](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile357.png)

![image 358](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile358.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 157



agricultural workers without any alternative employment, retention of substantial holdings can hardly be justified.

“With a view to making large areas of land available for redistribution, the provision relating to transfers of land and exemption need to be reviewed.”31

In his opening address the Union Minister for Food and Agriculture said:

“Another important factor which has highlighted the urgency of land reforms is the increase in disparities between the rural poor and the rural elite as a consequence of the technological development. This has disturbed the existing agrarian relationship, leading to social tensions and even to violence. The rural poor and the backward classes in the rural society which have all these years tolerated a subordinate position are no longer reconciled to the existing position. There is a clamour for redistribution of land. With irrigation support, when a holding of 3-5 acres has become a viable unit, there is hardly any justification for existence of over-sized holdings specially where there are a large number of landless agricultural labourers, with little prospects of non-farm employment. Although legislation providing for ceiling on land holdings has been enacted in almost all the states, implementation has been only nominal in most of them. In a number of states the provisions have not been enforced at all, or if enforced, no land has yet been taken over and distributed. There are inherent weaknesses in the legislation. The ceiling limits are relatively high. It is not applicable in many cases to the aggregate area of land held by all the members of the family. Provisions relating to transfers and exemptions are too liberal. It seems necessary therefore, that the provisions of the existing ceiling legislation should be reviewed and necessary modifications made to ensure sufficient surplus lands for distribution to landless agriculturists.”32

The Conference did not make any specific recommendation, but noted that in several states the provisions with regard to the level of the ceiling, the transfers and exemptions required a careful review in the light of recent technological developments in agriculture and social requirements.33

Soon after the conference a few states initiated action to amend the ceiling laws. Kerala and Tamil Nadu reduced the levels of ceiling. The

![image 359](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile359.png)

![image 360](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile360.png)

###### 158 Land Reforms in India



Legislative Assembly of Assam passed a Bill for scaling down the ceiling limit from 50 to 25 acres. Maharashtra abolished the exemptions in favour of sugarcane farms, efficiently managed farms and other specialized farms. When West Bengal was under President’s rule an ordinance was promulgated to reduce the level of the ceiling, provide for the classification of land and make the family the unit of application.

Another conference of Chief Ministers was held on 26-27 September, 1970. In the agenda papers circulated for the conference it was pointed out that it would be “difficult to justify in the present context retention of a level of ceiling in respect*of irrigated land exceeding 15 to 25 acres and in respect of other land exceeding 30 to 50 acres. The ceiling should apply to the aggregate area of land held by husband, wife and minor children.’*34 It was also suggested that the provisions regarding exemptions should be reviewed. The Conference decided that the entire range of problems relating to ceiling on agricultural holdings should be referred to the Central Land Reforms Committee on which a few Chief Ministers would be co-opted. The Central Land Reforms Committee was a high powered body established on the suggestion of the 1969 Conference of Chief Ministers. The Minister of Food and Agriculture was its Chairman. The members included the Deputy Chairman of the Planning Commission and the Ministers of Home and Law and Justice. The Committee was charged with the responsibility to review periodically the progress of land reforms in the States. In accordance with the recommendation of the Chief Ministers’ Conference of September 1970, the Chief Ministers of Bihar, Himachal Pradesh, Maharashtra, Punjab and Tamil Nadu were co-opted as members of the Committee. The recommendations of that Committee led to the issue of a new set of guide lines aimed at scaling down the levels of ceiling, doing away with a large number of exemptions and bringing about a large measure of uniformity in the state laws on the ceiling.

###### The Recommendations of the Central Land Reforms Committee

Soon after the Conference of Chief Ministers a new post of Land Reforms Commissioner was created in October 1970 to head the Land Reforms Divisions in the Ministry of Agriculture and the Planning

![image 361](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile361.png)

![image 362](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile362.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 159



Commission and service the Central Land Reforms Committee. I was appointed to that post though I had no previous experience of land reform either at the Centre or in the State. When I mentioned my lack of experience, Tribhuvan Prasad Singh, the then Secretary of Agriculture dismissed it as irrelevant. He assured me that in about three months I should be able to study all the relevant materials and produce a background paper for the consideration of the Committee. I felt over-whelmed when I glanced through the rich literature and the mass of relevant materials in the Planning Commission and the Ministry of Agriculture. By the end of January 1971 I had managed to produce a paper giving a brief account of the evolution of the policy on the ceiling, the salient features of the State laws, the significant variations between the laws and an overview of their implementation. The paper also contained a discussion of the case for and against low ceilings, possible policy alternatives and my specific suggestions regarding levels of ceiling, unit of application, exemptions from ceiling, payment of compensation and distribution of surplus land. Extracts from the paper dealing with the latter topics may be seen in Appendix IV-1. Portions of the paper dealing with the evolution of policy, the contents of the State laws and implementation have been excluded from Appendix IV-1, as these matters have been covered at some length in the present writing. No useful purpose will be served by repeating here the arguments for and against low ceilings. Readers who are interested in the debate may glance through Appendix IV-1.

The Central Land Reforms Committee on which five Chief Ministers had been co-opted met on April 16, 1971 and again on August 3, 1971. The Committee spent considerable time discussing my suggestions regarding levels of ceiling, unit of application and exemptions from ceiling . At the meeting held on August 3, 1971 a consensus was arrived at regarding the unit of application and the range within which ceilings should be fixed. The Committee’s recommendations were:

- i) The ceiling should be applied for the family as a whole, the term family being defined so as to include the husband, wife and minor children;
- ii) Where the number of members in the family exceeds five, additional land may be allowed for each member in excess of


![image 363](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile363.png)

![image 364](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile364.png)

###### 160 Land Reforms in India



- five in such manner that the total area admissible to the family does not exceed twice the ceiling limit for a family;
- iii) the ceiling for a family of five may be fixed within the range of 10 to 18 acres of perennially irrigated land or irrigated land capable of growing two crops. As soil conditions, productivity of the land, the naturd of crops grown etc., vary from state to state, the committee considered it desirable simply to indicate a range within which the ceiling should be ~xed instead of suggesting any rigid ceiling for the whole country;
- iv) for various other categories of land the conversion ratios should be fixed taking into account the availability of water, productivity, soil classification, crops grown etc. The absolute ceiling for a family of five, even in the case of dry lands, should be put at 54 acres. This limit would be relaxable if there is special justification for doing so on account of the nature of the soil, rainfall, chronic drought conditions etc;
- v) exemption in the existing state laws in favour of mechanized farms, well managed farms etc should be withdrawn;
- vi) the exemption in favour of plantations of tea, coffee, cardamom, rubber, etc should be carefully examined in consultation with the ministries concerned and the State Governments. Thereafter these and other types of exemptions should be discussed with the Chief Ministers in order to formulate the national policy.


As recommended by the Central Land Reforms Committee the need for allowing exemption for plantation crops was examined in consultations with the Ministries of Finance, Foreign Trade and the Planning Commission. After obtaining the comments of the State Governments also, a conference of Chief Ministers was held on April 14, 1972 to consider the question of exemptions. The Chief Ministers arrived at the following consensus :

- 1) It was agreed that the exemptions in favour of plantations of tea, coffee, rubber, cardamom and cocoa should continue.
- 2) Lands held by the Bhoodan Yagya Committee, Co-operative Banks, Nationalised Banks, Central or State Governments and


![image 365](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile365.png)

![image 366](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile366.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 161



- local bodies should continue to enjoy exemption. Similarly, land held by industrial or commercial undertakings for non-agricultural purposes should be exempted from the ceiling law.
- 3) In the case of registered co-operative farming societies it was agreed that exemption might be granted with the stipulation that while computing the ceiling area for a member his share in the co-operative society will be taken into account along with his other land.
- 4) Lands held by Agricultural Universities, Agricultural Colleges, Agricultural Schools and Research Institutions should be exempted from the ceiling law.
- 5) There was a good deeil of discussion about exemptions in favour of land owned by religious, educational or charitable trusts, lands granted to members of the armed forces for acts of gallantry and lands covered by orchards. Regarding religious, educational or charitable trusts it was agreed that only genuine trusts of a public nature deserved special treatment. They may be either granted annuity or some other suitable arrangement may be made in order to ensure that the objectives for which the trusts were created are not frustrated. Regarding awards for gallantry it was agreed that grants made since independence should be exempted. The question of continuing to exempt grants made prior to independence would be examined further. About orchards the general consensus was that the ceiling limit of a person who owned orchards whether or not in addition to other land, might be increased by two hectares or the actual area of the land comprised in orchards, whichever was less. Some Chief Ministers thought that there might be a higher relaxation by computing the area under orchards as dry lands. The State Governments which faced serious difficulties in enforcing the ceiling in respect of orchards would individually discuss the matter further with the Government of India.
- 6) It was agreed that all other exemptions should be withdrawn.


![image 367](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile367.png)

![image 368](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile368.png)

###### 162 Land Reforms in India



###### Controversy

On April 17, 1972, the Minister for Agriculture made a statement in Parliament regarding the consensus arrived at the meeting of Chief Ministers regarding exemptions from the ceiling law. Normally after obtaining the approval of the Union Council of Ministers, guide lines would have been issued to the State Governments within three or four weeks. But that was not to be as a great public controversy arose about the question whether land irrigated from private sources should be equated with land irrigated from public sources.

As we have seen, it was at the meeting held on August 3, 1971 that the Central Land Reforms Committee made certain recommendations regarding the unit of application and the level of ceiling. The very next day Fakhruddin Ali Ahmed, the then Minister for Food and Agriculture made a statement in the Lok Sabha listing the recommendations. The same day the Minister of State, A.P. Shinde made a similar statement in the Rajya Sabha. In response to a point raised in the House the Minister of State clarified that while referring to perennially irrigated land or irrigated land capable of growing two crops the Committee had in mind land irrigated from Government sources. Subsequently the same clarification was given in answer to a Lok Sabha question on 15 November, 1971. The matter did not rest there. Several weeks later reports appeared in a section of the press alleging that the Ministry of Agriculture had deliberately distorted the recommendations of the Central Land Reforms Committee. Some reports even identified the minister of State, A.P. Shinde as the person responsible for the distortion. There was a furore in Parliament. The Minister for Food and Agriculture made a statement refuting the allegation. He pointed out that in all the State laws on ceiling, including the West Bengal Land Reforms Act of 1971, the term irrigated land referred only to land irrigated from government sources. This was so because records existed of only land irrigated from government sources. But it did not mean that all other land, including land irrigated from private sources, would be treated as dry land qualifying for a very high ceiling. For clause (iv) of the committee’s recommendation made it clear that for all other categories of land conversion ratios should be fixed taking into account the availability of water, (emphasis added) productivity, soil classification, crops grown etc. The conversion ratio could be less than

![image 369](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile369.png)

![image 370](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile370.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 163



unity, unity or more than unity based on these factors. The Minister’s clarification did not, however, put an end to the controversy.

The Central Land Reforms Committee met on 8-9 May, 1972 to look into the matter afresh and resolve the controversy. After considering all aspects of the matter and going through the verbatim reports of meetings held on 16 April, 1971 and 3 August, 1971 the Committee came to the unanimous conclusion that there was absolutely no basis for the charge that the Ministry of Agriculture had distorted the recommendations of the Committee. The Committee however recommended that the question whether or not private irrigation should be equated with public irrigation for the purpose of the ceiling law should be considered at a meeting of all the Chief Ministers. The

Committee further recommended that a few related issues like a ceiling

on future acquisitions, giving retrospective effect to the ceiling law, the principles to be followed in the distribution of surplus land etc. should also be placed before the conference of Chief Ministers.

###### Recommendations of the Congress Committee

Meanwhile, in the context of the raging controversy in the Parliament and the press, the President of the Indian National Congress appointed a high powered committee to consider all aspects of the ceiling question and make suitable recommendations. The members of the committee were Fakhruddin Ali Ahmed, C. Subramaniam, Mohan Kumaramangalam, H.R. Gokale, V.P. Naik, Bafkatulla Khan, Rajendra Kumari Bajpai, Dev Raj Urs, and K. Karunakaran. The Committee submitted its report in June 1972. The important recommendations of the committee are noted below.

The Congress Committee suggested that the reference to “perennially irrigated land” in the recommendations of the Central Land Reforms Committee should be deleted because it was doubtful whether such lands really existed. (The Central Land Reforms Committee had incorporated the term “perennially irrigated land” because the laws of Gujarat and Maharashtra had such a category and it referred to land with assured supply of water from public sources for a period of not less than ten months in the year.) The Congress Committee further suggested that the important question was not whether water was available to a given plot of land all through the year,

![image 371](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile371.png)

![image 372](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile372.png)

###### 164 Land Reforms in India



but whether there was assured irrigation for growing one crop or two crops. The Committee recommended that the level of ceiling should be fixed in the following manner:

- (i) The best category of land in a state with assured irrigation and capable of yielding at least two crops a year should have a ceiling of 10 acres. This ceiling may increase up to 18 acres taking into account the fertility of the soil and other conditions.
- (ii) In the case of land having assured irrigation for only one crop in a year, the ceiling shall not exceed 27 acres.
- (iii) For all other types of land the ceiling shall not exceed 54 acres. Note : In view of desert conditions on a wider scale in Rajasthan, the State has got some special problems regarding the fixation of a ceiling under this head. The State Government will discuss this with the Ministry of Agriculture and take an appropriate decision,
- (iv) In the case of owners with holdings consisting of different types of land the total holding after converting the irrigated or better category of land into the lowest shall not exceed 54 acres.
- (v) The above ceiling shall be fixed in terms of physical acres and not in terms of so-called “standard acres”.
- (vi) Having considered the arguments for and against a differential ceiling as between lands irrigated from public and private sources, the committee recommended that “where irrigation is provided by private investment and initiative, allowance should be made by giving an advantage of 1:1.15 i.e., if for land irrigated from government source the ceiling is 10 acres the corresponding ceiling for land irrigated from private source should be 11.5 acres”


The Committee also made it clear that under no circumstances, land irrigated from private sources, capable of yielding at least two crops a year should have a ceiling of more than 18 acres.

Regarding the unit of application, the Congress committee recommended that the term family should be defined so as to include a husband, wife and all their children, whether major or minor. In the

![image 373](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile373.png)

![image 374](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile374.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 165



Central Land Reforms Committee’s formulation a family consisted of husband, wife and their minor children.

In the matter of exemptions, the Committee’s view was that no exemption should be allowed for orchards and lands awarded for gallantry.

The recommendations of the Congress Committee that the term family should be defined so as to include all children whether major or minor was not accepted by the Congress Working Committee. At the meeting held on July 22,1972 the working committee decided that the definition of a family for the purpose of the ceiling should be the same as given in the Congress Party’s Election Manifesto of 1972. The Election Manifesto had stated that the ceiling should be applicable for the family as a whole, the term “family” being defined so as to include husband, wife and minor children.

###### Final Recommendations.

A conference of Chief Ministers was held on 23 July, *1972 to consider all aspects of a ceiling on agricultural holdings. The conference considered the report of the Congress Committee and other materials. After discussions spread over several hours the conference made the following recommendations:

- I. Level of Ceiling The best category of land in a State with assured irrigation and


capable of yielding at least two crops a year should have a ceiling within the range of 10 to 18 acres, taking into account the fertility of the soil and other conditions.

Allowance may be made for land irrigated from private sources and capable of growing at least two crops in a year by equating 1.25 acres of such land with one acre of land irrigated from public sources and capable of growing at least two crops in a year. The ceiling for such land irrigated from private sources shall not, however, exceed 18 acres.

The term “irrigation from private sources” shall mean irrigation from a tubewell or lift irrigation from perennial water sources operated by diesel and or electric power.

![image 375](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile375.png)

![image 376](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile376.png)

###### 166 Land Reforms in India



There will be no reclassification of land falling within categories referred to in clauses (ii) and (iii) below for the purpose of the ceiling consequent upon the completion of a private irrigation scheme in future.

- (ii) In the case of land having assured irrigation for only one crop in a year, the ceiling shall not exceed 27 acres.
- (iii) For all other types of land the ceiling shall not exceed 54 acres.


In areas where there is potential for sinking tube-wells the ceiling for dry lands may be kept below 54 acres at the discretion of the State Government.

- (iv) In special cases like desert areas and hilly areas the ceiling for category (iii) may have to be relaxed. The State Governments may discuss specific cases with the Ministry of Agriculture before formulating their ceiling laws.
- (v) In the case of owners with holdings consisting of different types of land, the total folding after converting the better categories of land into the lowest category shall not exceed 54 acres.


###### II. Unit of Application of Ceiling

- (i) The unit of application of ceiling shall be a family of five members, the term ‘family’ being defined so as to include husband, wife and minor children. Where the number of members in the family exceeds five, additional land may be allowed for each member in excess of five in such a manner that the total area admissible to the family does not exceed twice the ceiling limit for a family of five members. The ceiling will apply to the aggregate area held by all the members of the family.
- (ii) Where both the husband and wife hold lands in their own names, the two will have rights in the properties within the ceiling in proportion to the value of the land held by each before the application of ceiling.
- (iii) Every major son will be treated as a separate unit for the purpose of application of ceiling.


![image 377](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile377.png)

![image 378](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile378.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 167



It should be ensured that there is no discrimination between major children governed by different systems of personal laws.

#### in.Retrospective Effect

The amended ceiling laws should have retrospective effect from a date not later than 24 January, 1971. (The date on which the Election Manifesto of the Congress Party was released.)

A specific provision should be made in the ceiling law making it clear that the onus of proving the bonafide nature of any transfer of land made after the date will be on the transferor.

###### IV. Exemptions

The Chief Ministers endorsed the consensus arrived at in their conference held on 14 April, 1972 on the question of exemptions from the ceiling with a few modifications, the final recommendations are reproduced below:

- (i) The exemptions in favour of the plantations of tea, coffee, rubber, cardamon and cocoa should continue;
- (ii) Lands held by the Bhoodan Yagya Committee, co-operative Banks, Central or State Governments and local bodies should continue to enjoy exemption. Similarly land held by industrial or commercial undertakings for non-agricultural purposes should be exempted from the ceiling law;
- (iii) In the case of registered co-operative fanning societies exemption may be granted with the stipulation that while computing the ceiling area for a member his share in the cooperative society will be taken into account along with his other lands;
- (iv) Lands held by Agricultural Universities, Agricultural colleges. Agricultural Schools and Research Institutions should be exempted from the ceiling law;
- (v) State Governments may, at their discretion, grant exemption to the existing religious, charitable and educational trusts of a public nature. The institutions or trusts will not be exempted from the operation of the tenancy laws and all the tillers of the land should be brought into direct relationship with the trusts or institutions to the exclusion of all intermediary


![image 379](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile379.png)

![image 380](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile380.png)

###### 168 Land Reforms in India



- interests. No exemption should be allowed to private trusts of any kind;
- (vi) In the case of existing Gowshalas of a public nature and existing stud farms, the State Governments may take a decision in consultation with the Ministry of Agriculture;
- (vii) No exemption should be allowed in the case of sugarcane farms. However, for the purpose of research and development, sugar factories may be permitted to retain an area not exceeding 100 acres.
- (viii) For the purpose of ceiling the existing orchards may be treated as dry land and no additional land should be allowed to be retained as recommended earlier. Coconut and arecanut plantations will not be treated as orchards. When surplus orchard land vesting in Government is distributed the assignees should be required to maintain the orchards in tact.
- (ix) All other exemptions including that in respect of land given as gallantry award should be withdrawn.


- V. Compensation Compensation payable for the surplus land should be fixed well

below the market value of the property so that it is within the paying .capacity of the new allottees mainly comprising the landless agricultural workers who belong to the Scheduled Castes and Tribes.

- (ii) The compensation may be fixed in graded slabs and preferably in multiples of land revenue payable for the land.
- (iii) The scheme for compensation should be worked out in such a manner that there will be no financial burden on the Central and State Governments.


- VI. Distribution of surplus land While distributing surplus land, priority should be given to the

landless agricultural workers, particularly those belonging to Scheduled Castes and Tribes.

- VII. Amended Ceiling Laws The amended ceiling laws should be enacted by 31 December,


1972.

![image 381](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile381.png)

![image 382](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile382.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 169



- VIII. Inclusion of Amended Laws All the amended laws should be included in the Ninth Schedule to

the Constitution.

- IX. Implementation Implementation will be the responsibility of the State Govern­


ments. They should set up non-official bodies at appropriate levels and set up competent official organization to administer the ceiling legislation. The concurrence of the Central Government will be obtained in respect of any incidental departure from the guide lines necessitated by the special conditions prevailing in any State or Union Territory.

On the basis of the recommendations made by the Chief Ministers the Government of India issued fresh guide lines for the amendment of the ceiling laws and advised the State Governments to complete the legislative process before 12 December, 1972.

###### Comments on the Controversy

As the writer participated in all the meetings of the Central Land Reforms Committee and of the Chief Ministers held in 1971 and 1972 to discuss the ceiling question, it will be appropriate to comment on the controversy about the level of ceiling for land irrigated from public and private sources. Writing about the controversy in the Economic and Political Weekly in September 1972, Ladejinsky observed that it was “the most strident of debates, almost unprecedented in the annals of the political history of India.” Ladejinsky summed up the controversy in the following words:

“The main provisions of the Central Land Reforms Committee’s proposal stipulated that ‘the ceiling for a family of five members may be fixed within the range of 10 to 18 acres of perennially irrigated land or irrigated land capable of growing two crops.’ The Congress Party’s pre-election manifesto incorporated it as its plank. When announced on 3 August, 1971 it was widely assumed that this provision would apply with equal force to privately and non-privately irrigated land. However, in the 1971-72 Annual Report of the Ministry of Agriculture, issued on 19 April, 1972 the relevant part of the original recommendation read: ‘10 to 18 acres of perennially irrigated land or land under assured irrigation from Government sources capable of growing two crops.’

![image 383](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile383.png)

![image 384](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile384.png)

###### 170 Land Reforms in India



The crux of the controversy was in the words ‘from Government sources.’ To many participants and observers of the ceiling proceedings, the insertion of these three words came to mean that, in effect, privately irrigated land is no longer subject to the ceiling. Events proved that the critics correctly sized up the situation. This would mean that Punjab, for example, with 80 per cent or more of the land privately irrigated would escape the ceiling provision and roughly the same would have applied to Haryana, part of Western U.P., Bihar and Andhra Pradesh, and generally to that portion of the owners who have the most and the best privately irrigated land. Hence the hue and cry that the Minister of Agriculture distorted the original formulation of the C.L.R.C.”35

Even such a knowledgeable, astute and perceptive observer of Indian Land Reform as Wolf Ladejinsky was misled into believing that the privately irrigated land in the well endowed Gangetic valley would have escaped the ceiling under the guide lines proposed by the Central Land Reforms Committee in August 1971 read with the subsequent clarification of the Ministry of Agriculture.

The Central Land Reforms Committee’s recommendations were based on the fourth alternative given in my paper on “Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings”. (Appendix IV-1). The relevant part of the fourth alternative is reproduced below to facilitate easy reference. “The broad suggestion is that for States with a very high density of population the ceiling for a family of five should be fixed at 10 to 15 standard acres. In the other States it may be fixed at 15 to 20 standard acres. Land should be classified on the basis of availability of water, productivity, soil classification, crops grown etc and conversion ratios should be fixed for different classes of Land.” (Appendix IV-1, page 43)

The States that had accepted the concept of “standard acre” in their ceiling laws had adopted diverse criteria for defining the term “Standard acre”. As it would be extremely difficult to arrive at a uniform definition of the term “standard acre” for the whole country, the committee decided to accept irrigated double cropped land as the norm. The term “perennially irrigated land” was added to ensure that land with irrigation for crops like sugarcane which stood in the field for about a year would be treated on par with irrigated double cropped land and would not be treated as single cropped land.

![image 385](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile385.png)

![image 386](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile386.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 171



Clause (IV) of the recommendation was that “for various other categories of land, conversion ratios should be fixed taking into account availability of water, productivity, soil classification, crops grown etc.” The term availability of water is wide enough to cover the amount of annual precipitation and its distribution, flow irrigation and ground water resources. As a matter of fact, in some tracts with adequate, well-distributed rainfall there exist considerable areas of double cropped land which do not require any irrigation from public or private sources. Such land is treated on par with land having assured irrigation from government sources.

There are certain practical difficulties in fixing a ceiling on the basis of whether or not water is available from a private source for growing crops. The quantum of ground water, rates of its replenishment, the extent to which it can be optimally exploited and the cost of exploitation vary widely from region to region. There are also diverse irrigation works like tubewells of various sizes, kutcha and pucca wells, ponds etc. Since records do not exist of the exact nature and extent of irrigation from such private works, it would be a difficult administrative task to classify land on the basis of irrigation from private sources. Moreover, as between two individuals who own land of the same class with identical ground water resources, it will be unfair and illogical to fix a lower ceiling for the person who has completed an irrigation scheme by investing his own or borrowed funds and allow a higher ceiling to the other person who has made no investment in irrigation. What is important is whether or not ground water is available and not if it is being exploited. If the original formulation of the Central Land Reforms Committee were honestly adopted, in the Gangetic valley and the deltas of the great rivers, land endowed with plenty of ground water resources would have had a conversion ratio of only 1.2:1 or 1.25:1 as compared to land with assured irrigation from Government sources for growing two crops.

Be it as it may, one concrete benefit flowed from the controversy. It led to the ceiling limit for well-endowed land with irrigation from private sources being laid down with greater precision. The revised guide line laid down that in the case of land irrigated from private sources and capable of growing at least two crops in a year, 1.25 acres of such land should be equated with one acre of land irrigated from

![image 387](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile387.png)

![image 388](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile388.png)

###### 172 Land Reforms in India



public sources and capable of growing two crops. It was also clarified that the term” irrigation from private source” would mean irrigation from a tube well or lift irrigation from a perennial source of water operated by diesel or electric power. Furthermore, it was laid down that in areas where there is potential for sinking tube-wells the ceiling for dry lands may be kept below 54 acres at the discretion of the State Government. In the process of seeking greater clarity, the guide line lost to some extent the flexibility of the old formulation. Thus in the well endowed Gangetic valley or the deltas of the great rivers it became open to the State Government to fix a ceiling of even 54 acres if the land owner had not started exploiting the ground water. Another weakness of the new guide line is that there is no mention of fixing a conversion ratio for various types of land taking into account the availability of water and other factors. Anyway, the controversy came to an end after the issue of the revised guide lines. As pointed out by Ladej insky.

“In fairness, the irrigated ceiling formula as it finally emerged was not an off the cuff decision. Its advantages and dis-advantages and those of other alternatives claimed the attention of the Central Land Reforms Committee, and any other approach would not have been without imperfections of its own.”36

###### Legislation and Implementation

On the basis of the new guide lines the ceiling laws were amended without much delay. The limits laid down in the guide lines and the limits fixed in the amended laws of the different states may be seen in Appendix IV.2. In all states other than Karnataka, Uttar Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh the new levels of the ceiling for different categories of lands are within the range laid down in the guide lines. In Karnataka and Uttar Pradesh the departure from the guide lines is marginal. Higher limits have been fixed for dry land in Rajasthan taking into account the desert condition and in Himachal Pradesh because of the hilly terrain. The guide lines contemplated relaxation of the upper limit for dry land in such special cases. But for these special cases the guide lines were followed by the States while amendi».g the ceiling laws.

![image 389](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile389.png)

![image 390](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile390.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 173



###### The Amended Laws

As we have seen in the earlier laws the level of ceiling varied greatly between the states. For example, in Andhra Pradesh under the old law for a family of five members the admissible area could vary between 27 acres and 324 acres depending on the class of land. Under the amended law, the ceiling for a family of five ranges between 10 and 54 acres. In Rajasthan under the old law the admissible limit for the best type of land was 22 acres and for the poorest land was 336 acres. Under the amended law the range is now 18 acres to 175 acres. The amendments have thus led to a substantial reduction in the levels of ceiling across the board in respect of all types of land. Apart from effecting a reduction in the levels of ceiling, the amended laws have also simplified the classification of land. As against the numerous classes of the past, now there are only three broad classes of land: irrigated double cropped, irrigated single cropped and dry land.

Under the old laws six States had fixed the ceiling taking the family as the unit of application, two States had fixed the ceiling for each member of the landholders family and the other States had fixed the ceiling for individual land holders. The family is now the Unit of application in all the States. The amended laws have done away with a number of exemptions permitted under the old laws. The existing exemptions are in conformity with the guide lines laid down by the Central Land Reforms Committee. The amendments have thus resulted in a large measure of uniformity in the ceiling laws of the different States.

###### Implementation

Soon after the process of amending the ceiling laws in accordance with the revised guidelines had been completed the country underwent the traumatic experience of the emergency declared under Article 352 of the Constitution. The speedy implementation of the ceiling laws and the distribution of the surplus land was included in the Twenty (or was it twenty one?) Point Programme drawn up in great haste immediately after the declaration of emergency. Yet, it seems that the authors of the emergency who acted with fierce determination and ruthlessness in subverting the democratic process and snuffing out civil liberties, could not, or rather, would not, make any significant headway in the

![image 391](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile391.png)

![image 392](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile392.png)

###### 174 Land Reforms in India



implementation of the Ceiling laws or of the other measures of land reform. This is the inescapable conclusion if we consider the areas of land declared surplus, taken over and distributed. According to the data compiled by the Ministry of Agriculture, by the middle of 1986, under the amended ceiling laws 4,572,640 acres of land had been declared surplus, 3,243,409 acres had been taken possession of land 2,279,652 acres had been distributed. These figures compare favourably with 2,881,965 acres declared surplus, 2,553,973 acres taken over and 2,056,860 acres distributed under the old laws.37

Thus by the middle of 1986, under the old and the revised laws, 7,454,605 acres had been declared surplus, 5,788,382 acres had been taken over and 4,336,512 acres had been distributed. However, according to information available with the Ministry of Rural Development as of 9 October, 1992 the total area declared surplus had come down to 7.265 million acres. The fall in the area declared surplus was caused by several declarations being set aside subsequently. As of October 1992 the area of land actually taken over was 6.339 million acres out of which 4.961 million acres were distributed.38

In order to assess the relative performance of the different states, I have worked out the area of surplus land distributed in each state as a per centage of the operational area of the State. The state-wise figures of the operational area, area declared surplus, area taken over, area distributed and area distributed as a per centage of the total operated area may be seen in Appendix IV.3.

The total area of surplus land distributed in eighteen States up to October 1992 constitutes only 1.24 per cent of the operational area of those states. In the States of Uttar Pradesh, Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan, Gujarat, Karnataka, Tamil Nadu, Himachal Pradesh and Tripura the area distributed is less than 1 per cent of the operated area. It is only marginally above 1 per cent in Bihar, Punjab and Orissa. The only States in which over 5 per cent of the operated area has been distributed are Jammu & Kashmir, West Bengal and Assam. In Jammu & Kashmir, 17.77 per cent of the operated area has been distributed. In West Bengal the area distributed constitutes 6.715 per cent of the operated area and in Assam the per centage is 5.9 per cent. Leaving out these three States, the imposition of ceilings has not led to any worthwhile redistribution of agricultural land in the rest of the country.

![image 393](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile393.png)

![image 394](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile394.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 175



Thus the efforts of the Central and State Governments spread over a period of about 35 years to enact, revise and implement the ceiling laws resulted in less than 1.5 per cent of the agricultural land being redistributed. The Government’s own assessment is that:

“Judged by the quantum of land declared surplus, this reform measure has failed to achieve any substantial impact.”39

###### Reasons for Poor Performance

According to the Government of India, the major factors that led to a wide gap between expectations and the achievements of the land ceiling programme were:

- (a) Provision for holding land up to twice the ceiling limit by families with over five members.
- (b) Provision to give separate ceiling for major sons in the family.
- (c) Provision for treating every shareholder of a joint family under applicable personal law as a separate unit for ceiling limits.
- (d) Exemption of tea, coffee, rubber, cardamom and coca plantations and the lands held by religious and charitable institutions beyond ceiling limits.
- (e) Benami and farzi transfers to defeat the ceiling law.
- (f) Delay in the disposal of cases before the administrative and judicial courts.”40


While all these reasons did play a role in reducing the quantum of surplus land that could be mopped up, they were not the most important reasons for the poor results.

In my view the prime reason for the poor performance was the total lack of political will. The halting and ambivalent formulation of the policy, the painfully slow process of legislation and the luke warm and inefficient implementation were all the inevitable consequence of the absence of political will. At no point of time since independence did the Government of India display the requisite political will to enforce effective ceilings on agricultural holdings. The State Governments, barring a few exceptions for limited periods, showed even less political will. Absence of political will was by far the most important cause for the failure of the ceiling laws.

The second most important reason was the long gap of several years that occurred between the declaration of the intention to impose

![image 395](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile395.png)

![image 396](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile396.png)

###### 176 Land Reforms in India



ceilings and the actual enactment of the ceiling laws. Alert land owners got plenty of time to evade the law by resorting to partitions, gifts, creation of trusts, sales for valuable consideration and fictitious sales.

The third important reason was pedantry and the pursuit of theoretically perfect formulations ignoring ground realities. The outstanding example of this weakness was the insistence on adopting the family as the unit for the application of the ceiling law with allowance for members of the family in excess of five . I must confess that in 1970-72 I also made the blunder of accepting without questioning the prevailing conventional wisdom that imposing the ceiling on the family basis would be a step in the right direction. Theoretically a larger area of land could be declared surplus if ceilings were applied to the total area of land owned by a family instead of applying it to an individual landholder. But really there is no basis for this view. In practice, in most families land is recorded in the name of the head of the family. Only in a few families would there be other members owning land. Hence there would have been no great shrinkage in the area of land that could be declared surplus if the ceiling had been imposed on individual landholders. The great advantage in making the individual landholder the unit of application is that since the land records show ownership of individuals, and not of families, the implementation would be easy. Making allowances for larger families and defining “family” so as to include a person, his/her spouse, and minor children led to a considerable shrinkage of the area that could be declared surplus, rendered implementation very difficult, and opened the flood gates for corruption. There existed no record anywhere of the number of members in a landholder’s family or of the age of the members. So it became necessary in every case to ascertain the number of members in the family and the age of each member. The determination of age was vested with great significance because the question whether or not a member would be entitled to a separate ceiling depended entirely on his age on a particular date. The procedure gave condiderable discretion to officers of the Revenue Department inevitably leading to a great deal of corruption. The simplest possible course would have been to fix a ceiling for landholders without making any allowance for the size of the family and without providing for any classification of land as was done in Jammu & Kashmir and originally

![image 397](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile397.png)

![image 398](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile398.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 177



in the laws of West Bengal and Assam. If the ceiling for a land holder had been put at 50 acres or so without any classification of land the results would have been far better. If it was considered absolutely necessary to take into account the great variation in the productivity of different types of land, a simple three fold classification of double-cropped, single-cropped and dry land could have been adopted. A ceiling of 15 acres for double cropped, 25 acres for single cropped and 50 acres of dry land would have been reasonable. Such a simple law without any allowance for the size of the family would have resulted in considerably more area of land being declared surplus. But unfortunately such simple alternatives were lost sight of while working out with meticulous care theoretically perfect and equitable formulations.

While commenting on the implementation, of the first round of ceiling laws, the reasons that led to the poor performance were pointed out. Most of these reasons operated during the implementation of the revised laws as well. Protracted litigation continued to bedevil the implementation of the ceiling laws in the second phase as well. With a view to reduce the time taken in the adjudication of disputes, the Government of India had advised the State Governments to set up Land Tribunals. The States went about the business of setting up tribunals in a leisurely fashion and it seems that even the setting up of the tribunals did not lead to any radical improvement in the situation. The following excerpt from the agenda notes for the Conference of Chief Ministers held on 9 October, 1992 is self-explanatory:

“It has been the common experience that the proceedings before the courts have proved dilatory; the courts have not been in a position to undertake expeditious disposal. The states have been advised to constitute Land Tribunals under Article 323-B of the Constitution. Laws have been passed in the States of Bihar, Kerala, Tamil Nadu and West Bengal for constituting Land Tribunals under article 323-B of the Constitution for disposal of cases relating to land reforms. In Bihar, the provisions of the law providing for constitution of a Land Tribunal have been set/aside by the Patna High Court: but the State Government has gone in appeal to the Supreme Court whose final verdict is awaited. In Karnataka, the State Government has requested the High Court to set up special tribunals to dispose of land reforms cases pending in the

![image 399](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile399.png)

![image 400](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile400.png)

###### 178 Land Reforms in India



High Court or to earmark separate benches for the purpose. The Divisional Commissioners have also been requested to file caveat in the High Court whenever land tribunals deteinmine surplus lands. Where the States do not consider it expedient to constitute the Tribunal, or where it is in the process of being constituted, the High Court may be requested to set up a Special Bench for dealing with the land cases.**41

The importance of the role that litigation played in frustrating implementation is evident from the fact that as of 1992 out of the 7.28 million acres that had been declared as surplus as much as 1.16 million acres (about 16 per cent) were involved in litigation.

As in the case of the first round of ceiling legislation in the second round also, the absence of reliable land records made implementation difficult. The inadequacy of the administrative set up which often colluded with powerful landowners also led to poor implementation.

Another weakness was the general apathy of the potential beneficiaries of the ceiling law. In most areas they were passive, unorganized and inarticulate. And in the few areas where they acquired some degree of militancy under the influence of the Communist parties or the various extremist groups, instead of enlisting their support the administration’s efforts were invariably directed towards suppressing the rural poor.

As a result of all these reasons the implementation of the ceiling laws of the first round as well as of the second round left a great deal to be desired.

###### Results

What did the efforts to impose ceilings on agricultural holdings achieve? As we have seen, the enforcement of ceilings did not lead to any substantial redistribution of agricultural land. The generally poor quality of the surplus land and the lack of financial assistance for bringing the land under cultivation reduced further the benefits that could have accrued to the rural poor. Up to the mid-seventies the beneficiaries received no financial assistance. In the financial year 1975-76, the Government of India drew up a scheme to give an outright grant of Rs. 1000 per hectare to the beneficiaries and made a modest provision of Rs. 25 crores in the budget. Because of the apathy of the State Governments the bulk of the budget allocation remained

![image 401](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile401.png)

![image 402](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile402.png)

###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings 179



unutilized in the initial years. Later on the financial assistance was raised to Rs. 2500 per hectare. The State Governments were also advised to give priority to assignees of surplus land in the selection of beneficiaries under the Integrated Rural Development Programme and other schemes for Rural Development. In spite of all these, a large number of beneficiaries could not bring the land under cultivation because of its very poor quality.

Though the imposition of the ceiling did not lead to any significant redistribution of agricultural land, it probably prevented further concentration of land. The operation of the laws of inheritance as well as the enforcement of ceiling laws led to a fall in the absolute number of operational holdings above 10 hectares and of the absolute area of land covered by such holdings. The number of operational holdings above 10 hectares was about 2.31 million in 1960-61 and 2.17 million in 1980-81. In 1960-61 they constituted 4.75 per cent of the operational holdings but in 1980-81 they accounted for only 2.4 per cent of the total number of operational holdings. (An important cause for the sharp decline in the percentage was, of course, the marked increase in the total number of holdings.) The area operated by holdings above 10 hectares was 40.39 million hectares in 1960-61. It came down to 37.71 million hectares in 1980-81. The large holdings accounted for 28.95 per cent of the total area of the operational holdings in 1960-61, but they accounted for only 23.0 per cent in 1980-81. The imposition of ceilings was partly responsible for these changes. Though the ceiling laws did not lead to any large scale redistribution of land, they had played a role in reducing the concentration of land. And without doubt, but for ceilings there would have been further concentration in the ownership of land. In the context of the significant growth in agricultural production since the late sixties there would have been a scramble for the purchase of agricultural land resulting in greater concentration in the ownership of land but for the existence of ceiling laws.

All in all, the results achieved by the imposition of the ceiling have not been commensurate with the amount of time, money and labour invested in the operation. As I had pointed out in my 1970 paper, the imposition of the ceiling on agricultural holdings in India is a case of inchoate policy, imperfect legislation and inefficient implementation. If only we had been a little more pragmatic and less hypocritical far better results could have been achieved.

![image 403](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile403.png)

![image 404](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile404.png)

###### 180 Land Reforms in India

Chapter 5

### Conclusion

An Overview

At the time of independence the Indian agrarian structure was plagued by a number of weaknesses which inhibited agricultural growth. The more important among the factors that obstructed agricultural growth were the parasitic Zamindari system, insecure tenancies subjected to rack-renting, concentration of land in the hands of indolent land owners, great inequality in the size-class distribution of holdings, preponderance of tiny uneconomic holdings and widespread subdivision and fragmentation of holdings. The land policy adopted since independence ostensibly aimed at curing these weaknesses. Right from the First Five Year Plan, land reform was included as an integral part of economic planning. The objectives of the land policy were set forth in the Third Plan in these words:

“Land reform programmes, which were given a place of special significance both in the first and the second plan have two specific objects. The first is to remove such impediments to increase in agricultural production as arise from the agrarian structure inherited from the past. This should help to create conditions for evolving as speedily as possible an agricultural economy with high levels of efficiency and productivity. The second object which is closely related to the first is to eliminate all elements of exploitation and social injustice within the agrarian system, to provide security for the tiller of the soil and assure equality of status and opportunity, to all sections of the rural population.”1

![image 409](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile409.png)

![image 410](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile410.png)

###### Conclusion 183
