The programmes adopted in pursuance of the above policy were the Abolition of Intermediary Interests, Tenancy Reform, Imposition of Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings in tandem with the re-distribution of surplus land and the Consolidation of Holdings. All these measures except the Consolidation of Holdings have been considered in the present study.

###### Abolition of Intermediary Interests

The intermediaries known as Zamindars, Talukdars, Jagirdars, Malguzars, Janmies etc. were parasitic rentiers who appropriated a substantial share of the produce of the land and harassed the actual cultivators in diverse ways. Soon after assuming office in 1946 the provincial governments initiated action to enact laws for the abolition of intermediary interests. The significant features of all those laws were the slow pace of legislation, inadequacies in the content of the legislation, the time-consuming procedures laid down and the role of the judiciary in frustrating the implementation of the enacted laws. By the end of the First Plan all states had enacted laws for the abolition of intermediary interests. All those laws brought the principal tenants on lands outside the intermediaries’ home farm into direct relationship with the State. The intermediaries were allowed to retain their home farms which were invariably cultivated through tenants-at-will. All the laws except that of Jammu and Kashmir provided for the payment of compensation to intermediaries.

The implementation of the laws was neither speedy nor efficient The main reasons for the slow and inefficient implementation were frequent interventions by the judiciary, the covert and overt resistance mounted by the vested interests, the non-involvement of the beneficiaries, the inadequacies of the machinery for implementation, the unsatisfactory condition of the land records and the complexities of the laws. Yet, the implementation of the laws for the Abolition of Intermediary Interests was far more satisfactory than the implementation of the laws enacted in later years for the Reform of Tenancy and the imposition of Ceilings on Agricultural Holdings.

The most important beneficial result of the reform was that it put an end to the system of parasitic intermediaries and brought some 25 million erstwhile tenants into direct relationship with the state. Though

![image 411](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile411.png)

![image 412](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile412.png)

###### 184 Land Reforms in India



most of those tenants had come to enjoy security of tenure by the time of independence, the protection given by the laws was not always effective. With the abolition of intermediary interests the principal tenants became owners of the land. They were freed from the illegal exactions of the Zamindars. Forced labour, agrestic serfdom and other forms of oppression widely prevalent in large parts of the country came to an end. The erstwhile principal tenants acquired a higher social and political status leading to a shift in the rural power structure. The most visible demonstration of this shift is the emergence of persons belonging to the upper layers of the so-called backward castes in the leadership of the political parties. But for the far-reaching changes ushered in by the abolition of intermediary interests, it is most unlikely that a Laloo Prasad Yadav would have become the Chief Minister of Bihar or a Mulayam Singh Yadav that of Uttar Pradesh. Another beneficial result was that large areas of private forest land and wasteland vested in government. Out of the wasteland and other types of land that vested in government some 6 million acres could be distributed to beneficiaries.

Though the beneficial results were substantial, the reforms had serious unwelcome consequences. The most important evil effect was that it led to the large scale ejectment of poor peasants from the land they had been cultivating for generations. Even in the states where the tenancy law had conferred security of tenure on occupancy tenants, resourceful Zamindars succeeded in ejecting some of them. And in states like Rajasthan where even superior tenants enjoyed little legal protection, large numbers were evicted. Everywhere the reform led to the large scale ejectment of tenants-at-will, under-tenants and sharecroppers. All the laws permitted the intermediaries to retain their home farms or “personally cultivated” land. Except in Jammu and Kashmir and West Bengal no limit was put on the area of land that an intermediary could retain. And most laws permitted them to resume the whole or a part of the home farm for “personal cultivation”. In every law the term “personal cultivation” was loosely defined to include cultivation through share-croppers, servants, hired labour etc. The inevitable consequence was the widespread eviction of tenants-at-will.

** The bottom layer of the rural society consisting of agricultural labourers, share-croppers and other tenants-at-will received little

![image 413](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile413.png)

![image 414](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile414.png)

###### Conclusion 185



benefit from the reform. As a matter of fact, there was only a deterioration in the condition of share-croppers and other tenants-at-will. Millions of them lost access to the land they had been cultivating. Another unwelcome feature of the reforms was the payment of a large sum amounting to Rs. 670 crores as compensation to the ex-intermediaries. That placed a heavy burden on the state governments. Despite these flaws, the implementation of the laws for the abolition of intermediary interests ushered in remarkable changes in the agrarian structure and irreversibly altered the rural power-structure. And all those laws were implemented less inefficiently than the laws enacted in later years for the reform of tenancy and the imposition of ceilings on agricultural holdings.

###### Tenancy Reform

With the abolition of the intermediary interests the bulk of the superior tenants were brought into direct relationship with the state. Sub-letting was, however, widespread in the Zamindari areas a?d most of the sub-leases were oral and terminable at will. The laws enacted for the abolition of intermediary interests conferred no benefits on the tenants-at-will. Though the Raiyatwari system was supposed to have been one of peasant-proprietors, leasing was rampant in the Raiyatwari areas also. Those leases were mostly oral, informal and terminable at will. The measures taken since independence to extend protection to tenants in the Raiyatwari areas and to sub-tenants in the erstwhile Zamindari areas were considered in Chapter 3.

Three important guidelines were laid down in the Five Year Plans for the reform of tenancy. The guidelines were:

Firstly, that the rent should not exceed the level of one-fifth to one-fourth of the gross produce;

Secondly, the tenants should be accorded permanent rights in the lands they cultivate subject to a limited right of resumption to be granted to the land owners, and

Thirdly, that in respect of non-resumable lands, the landlord-tenant relationship should be ended by conferring ownership rights on the tenants.

In all the laws except those of the Punjab, Harayana and the Andhra Area of Andhra Pradesh, fair rent has been fixed within the

![image 415](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile415.png)

![image 416](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile416.png)

###### 186 Land Reforms in India



limits prescribed in the Five Year Plans. Even in the Punjab, Harayana and the Andhra Area the legally fixed fair rent is only marginally higher than the level indicated in the Plans.

But the second objective of giving security of tenure to tenants has not been attained in most states. The legal protection extended to tenants proved to be ineffective because of the shortcomings in the laws and the generally poor implementation of the enacted laws. The effectiveness of a tenancy law would depend upon some important factors like the definition of the term ‘tenant’, the provisions for the resumption of tenanted land, definition of the term “personal cultivation”, provisions for the regulation of “voluntary surrenders”, the status of the land records, the competence and attitude of the administrative machinery responsible for the implementation of the law and the militancy and organizational strength of the beneficiaries. In the laws of Uttar Pradesh and West Bengal, share-croppers were deliberately excluded from the definition of the term “tenant”. Limited protection was extended to share-croppers in West Bengal only after 1970. A limited right of resumption was extended to landowners in most states, and it was left to the landowner to choose the land he would resume for “personal cultivation”. That provision rendered all tenancies insecure and inevitably led to the large scale ejectment of tenants. In every law the term “personal cultivation” was loosely defined. Not even residence near the land was insisted upon. Many a tenant lost his land because he was forced to hand it back to the landowner under the guise of “voluntary surrender” of tenancy. Provision made in later years for the regulation of voluntary surrenders proved to be ineffective. As we have seen, most of the surviving tenancies were oral and informal crop sharing arrangements. No records existed of such oral tenancies. Except in West Bengal in recent years no campaign was mounted to unearth share-cropping tenancies and record them. Even where the rules provided for recording the rights of the share-croppers in the revenue records, usually no entry was made in the official registers as the employees of the Revenue Department were invariably under the sway of the landowners. Finally, as the beneficiaries were socially, economically and politically weak and ill-organized, there was no countervailing pressure favouring the effective enforcement of the law.

![image 417](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile417.png)

![image 418](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile418.png)

###### Conclusion 187



When all attempts to regulate tenancies failed, the Planning Commission recommended that in respect of non-resumable land the landlord-tenant relationship should be ended by conferring rights of ownership on the tenants. As most tenancies were oral and informal, only a small per centage of tenants could prove that they were tenants and become owners. So the new policy also failed to produce satisfactory results. Taking the country as a whole, by 1992 ownership rights had been conferred on (or tenancy rights had been protected of) some 11 million tenants on 14.4 million acres of land. That constituted no more than 4 per cent of the operated area. It is interesting to note that the seven states of Assam, Gujarat, Himachal Pradesh, Karnataka, Kerala, Maharashtra, and West Bengal account for 97 per cent of the beneficiaries. Practically no benefits accrued to the tenants in the other states. Even in those seven states tenancy reform led to the large scale eviction of tenants. Except in West Bengal, share-croppers, by and large remained beyond the pale of tenancy reform every where. Only in West Bengal and Kerala did those in the lowest rungs of the peasant society receive solid benefits on the implementation of tenancy reform. The rights of some 14 lakh share-croppers were protected in West Bengal and in Kerala some 2.84 lakh rural poor, mostly agricultural labourers, became owners of tiny homesteads. As a result of tenancy reform there has been a sharp fall in the area of land under tenancy. From over one half of the operated area on the eve of the reform, the area under tenancy has come down to about 15 per cent of the operated area now. The tenants acquired ownership rights or were made secure in only about 4 per cent of the operated area. Thus the reform led to the rural poor loosing access to some 30 per cent of the operated area.

Tenancy reform has not put an end to absentee ownership of land nor has it led to the disappearance of tenancies. It has only resulted in tenancies being pushed underground. Most of the tenancies that still exist are informal crop-sharing arrangements. In many parts of the country the reform has also resulted in land being left uncultivated. For the landowners fear that they may lose the land if they lease it out. We have argued that the ban on leasing out should be lifted as it will improve the access of the rural poor to agricultural land, augment employment in the agricultural sector and enhance farm output. What we have suggested is a complete reversal of the national policy on tenancy reform.

![image 419](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile419.png)

![image 420](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile420.png)

###### 188 Land Reforms in India



###### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

Another programme which could have brought about radical changes in the agrarian structure is the imposition of ceilings on agricultural holdings. The distinguishing features of the Indian agrarian economy at independence were great inequality in the ownership of land, absentee landlordism, high incidence of tenancy, preponderance of uneconomic holdings and extreme fragmentation and subdivision of holdings. Apart from the commercial plantations of tree crops concentrated in a few areas, there existed few large farms resembling the farms of Europe, North America or Australia The large holdings that existed in India were not compact farming units with a home for the owner and a collection of buildings for storing the crops and sheltering animals. The typical large holding was nothing but an assortment of scattered pieces of land bereft of fixed assets spread over one or more villages. Where the owner of such a large holdings was an absentee landlord he invariably got the land cultivated through tenants-at-will. Even the resident landowners let out the bulk of their land to tenants. In such a situation there was no danger at all of the imposition of ceilings resulting in any “breaking up” of large farms. These large holdings were, in most cases, indifferently cultivated absorbing sub-optimal levels of labour and other inputs. The imposition of ceilings would have simply resulted in large areas of agricultural land passing on to those who had actually been cultivating it. The result would have been the fuller utilization of land and labour leading to enhanced output. As a matter of fact, the farm management studies conducted in the nineteen fifties had shown that output per acre increased with a fall in the size of the holding. There existed adequate economic and social justification for the imposition of ceilings and the re-distribution of surplus land. Yet, overwhelmed by the insoluble prublem of uneconomic holdings, the Planning Commission went about the business of imposing ceilings on agricultural holdings with little enthusiasm and less conviction. The inevitable results were confusion and ambivalence in the delineation of policy, enactment of laws riddled with loopholes and defects and the half hearted and inefficient implementation of those laws.

The enactment of the ceiling laws was preceded by a protracted debate spread over several years. That gave plenty of time to alert

![image 421](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile421.png)

![image 422](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile422.png)

###### Conclusion 189



landowners to dispose of the land that would have been declared surplus. The Planning Commission had only given broad guidelines for enacting ceiling laws. The guide lines were flexible and it was left to the states to settle the details. The result was great diversity between states in respect of the level of ceiling, classification of land, unit of application, allowance for the size of the family, exemption to special categories, treatment of transfers and partitions and the quantum of compensation. The generally high levels of ceilings, numerous exemptions and widespread transfers reduced greatly the area of land that could be declared surplus. All the laws contained built-in loop holes which large owners eagerly availed of to save their land. And the enacted laws were implemented inefficiently. The lack of unambiguous political will, the apathy and collusion of the bureaucracy, the absence of reliable land records, the frustrating role of the judiciary and the lack of organization on the part of the beneficiaries led to the poor implementation of the ceiling laws. The implementation was so poor that by 1970 only about one million hectares of surplus land had vested in government and only about one half of that could be distributed.

Though it became clear by the end of the nineteen sixties that nothing substantial was likely to be achieved by the imposition of ceilings, the year 1969 witnessed a sudden revival of interest in the Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings in the context of the split in the Indian National Congress and the adoption of a set of populist policies by the ruling group. As that renewed interest was not rooted in genuine political convictions, it did not last long. Be that as it may, a high powered committee on which some Chief Ministers were also represented, reviewed all aspects of land ceilings at several sittings in

- 1971 and 1972. The Committee made its final recommendations in July
- 1972. The recommendations were aimed at bringing about a measure of uniformity in the laws. The committee recommended reduction in the levels of ceiling, simplifying the classification of land, adoption of the family as the unit of application and the withdrawal of a number of exemptions. Soon after that all the state laws were amended in accordance with the new policy. The zeal shown in reviewing the policy and amending the laws did not extend to their implementation. The implementation of the new laws was as inefficient as that of the old laws. As a result of the implementation of the old and the new laws, by


![image 423](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile423.png)

![image 424](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile424.png)

###### 190 Land Reforms in India



1992 only about 2 million hectares of surplus land could be distributed to some 4.76 million beneficiaries. Thus the efforts spread over a period of three and a half decades to enforce ceilings and take over surplus land led to the re-distribution of less than 2 per cent of the operated area. If we look into the performance of individual states, we find that the area distributed as a per centage of the total area operated is 17.4 for Jammu and Kashmir, 6.36 in West Bengal and 5 per cent in Assam. In all the other states only less than one per cent of the operated area could be distributed. Obviously, the imposition of ceilings and the re-distribution of surplus land made no impact on the agrarian structure in most states.

There is little doubt that the most important reason for the poor performance was the utter lack of political will. The second most important reason was the long gap of several years between the declaration of the intention to impose ceilings and the actual enactment of the laws. The third important reason was pedantry and the adoption of theoretically perfect formulations ignoring ground realities. The role of the judiciary, inadequacies of the machinery for implementation and the lack of organization on the part of the potential beneficiaries were also factors contributing to poor implementation.

As we have seen, the enforcement of ceilings did not lead to any substantial redistribution of agricultural land. The generally poor quality of the land and the lack of supporting facilities to bring the land under cultivation, reduced further the benefits that could have accrued to the rural poor. Though the ceiling laws did not lead to any significant re-distribution of land, it prevented further concentration of land. On the whole, the results achieved have not been commensurate with the amount of time, money and labour invested in the enactment and enforcement of the ceiling laws. Indeed, the game was not worth the candle.

###### Results of Land Reforms

Thus the programmes of land reform implemented since independence did not lead to any significant re-distribution of land, or, the removal of all the obstacles to increasing agricultural production. Of the three programmes considered in this study, the laws for the abolition of intermediary interests were implemented fairly well. But in

![image 425](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile425.png)

![image 426](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile426.png)

###### Conclusion 191



the case of tenancy reform and ceilings on holdings, the policies adopted were ambivalent and there were large gaps between policy and legislation and between legislation and implementation. We have seen that as result of the implementation of the tenancy laws, tenants became owners of or acquired rights in only about 4 per cent of the operated area. The enforcement of ceilings led to the re-distribution of less than 2 per cent of the operated area. Thus these two measures taken together led to the re-distribution of only about 6 per cent of the operated area. This is insignificant compared to the re-distributive effect of the programmes of land reforms implemented after the Second World War in the People’s Republic qf China, Taiwan, South Korea and Japan. In the People’s Republic of China between the years 1950 and 1952 about 43 per cent of the agricultural land changed lands. As the re-distribution in China was effected in a revolutionary situation we may leave it out for purposes of comparison. But the performance of India suffers badly even when compared to the results of land reform in Taiwan, South Korea and Japan. The extent of re-distribution was 37 per cent in Taiwan,32 per cent in South Korea and 33 per cent in Japan.2

Though the Indian land reform was a failure in the matter of the redistribution of land, it will be a mistake to write it off as a non-event. It did bring about great changes. It did not make the actual tiller the owner of his land in all cases. Yet, it did away with the numerous layers of parasitic intermediaries between the State and the actual cultivator. Now in cases where the owner himself does not cultivate the land, he deals directly with the tenant-at-will. The Zamindari system with all its notorious abuses has disappeared yielding place to a new rural power-structure in which the erstwhile Zamindars share power with their former substantial tenants. The balance of political power has shifted in favour of this new group drawn mostly from the upper and middle castes. The reforms, however, left untouched the bottom layer of the agrarian structure consisting of agricultural labourers, share-croppers and other tenants-at-will and rural artisans. They received little benefit from the reform.

###### Reasons for Poor Performance

We have discussed at some length in chapters 2, 3 and 4 and

![image 427](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile427.png)

![image 428](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile428.png)

###### 192 Land Reforms in India



briefly in this chapter the reasons for the poor results. The Planning Commission’s Task Force on Agrarian Relations (1972-73) identified the lack of political will, absence of pressure from below, inadequacies of the administrative machinery, judicial intervention, the absence of correct and up-to-date land records and the lack of supporting facilities for beneficiaries as the major reasons for the poor performance. When I drafted the report of the Task Force in 19721 had only about two years experience of Indian land reform. After having observed the programmes for a further period of twenty-two years I do not consider it necessary to modify the findings of the Task Force. As the report of the Task Force is a document of considerable importance and its copies are not readily available now, the main report is included as Appendix V for the benefit of students of land reform.

###### Lack of Political Will

I shall not repeat here all the reasons given in the Task Force Report and those discussed in Chapters 2, 3, and 4 .1 should, however, like to make some observations about the lack of political will which has been identified as the prime cause for the poor performance. Though most people familiar with Indian land reform agree that political will has been lacking, there has been the occasional criticism that the Task Force made a sweeping statement, unsupported by solid facts. There are enough materials in the Task Force Report and in Chapters 2, 3, 4 of the present study to support the view that Indian land reform was ham-strung by the lack of political will . For the benefit of sceptics who seek hard evidence I am giving below a few facts within my knowledge to demonstrate the absence of political will in favour of reform. Some of these instances will show the exercise of unambiguous political will to thwart land reforms.

First I shall deal with political will at the level of the Central Government. Prime Minister Jawaharlal Nehru had a broad interest in land reforms. Those who followed him as Prime Minister did not have even that kind of general interest in the subject. In the seventies when I served as the Land Reforms Commissioner at the Centre I came across a few instances in which State Governments run by the ruling party at the centre refused to amend the state laws to bring them in line with the policy laid down by the Central Government. Apart from sending

![image 429](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile429.png)

![image 430](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile430.png)

###### Conclusion 193



demi-official letters signed by the Minister for Agriculture, and occasionally by the Prime Minister, the Government of India took no effective steps to make the State Governments fall in line. And that was a period when the limited autonomy that the states enjoyed under the Indian Constitution had practically been snuffed out and Chief Ministers used to be thrown out of office on the mere suspicion of lack of loyalty to the Prime Minister. If only the Prime Minister had taken a little personal interest, any recalcitrant Chief Minister could have been persuaded to amend the law in accordance with the national policy and implement it. But then, land reform was not a subject in which Prime Minister Indira Gandhi had special interest. Wolf Ladejinsky once told me about his infructuous efforts to get Indira Gandhi interested in land reforms. Ladejinsky was one of the best known international experts on land reforms. He was the chief architect of the Japanese land reform undertaken in the years 1946-48. Ladejinsky had acquired an insight into agrarian conditions in India in the course of his work with the Ford Foundation in the early sixties and with the World Bank during 1964-75. On the initiative of the Prime Minister’s Office, Ladejinsky once met Indira Gandhi to brief her on land reforms. He spent a few minutes trying to explain the crux of the problem. Indira Gandhi showed no interest in the subject and after a few minutes she started looking into her papers. Ladejinsky took the hint, said good-bye and left. If Indira Gandhi had little interest in land reforms, those who succeeded her had even less interest in the subject.

As far as the States are concerned there is plenty of hard evidence to show that political will has been totally absent. There have even been instances when political leaders at the State level showed open hostility to reform. I shall just cite a few instances within my personal knowledge. The Government of Harayana had been reluctant to accept the Central Government’s policy on ceilings. When the Financial Commissioner discussed the matter with me I told him that the Government of India would not agree to any departure from the guidelines. Thereafter the then Chief Minister, Bansi Lai met the Agriculture Minister, Fakhruddin Ali Ahmed. Minister of State, Anna Saheb Shinde and I were also present. Bansi Lai opened the discussion by shouting at me and accusing me of being the evil influence responsible for the Central Government’s adamant attitude on land

![image 431](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile431.png)

![image 432](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile432.png)

###### 194 Land Reforms in India



reform. Though Anna Saheb Shinde tried to point out that I was only reiterating the policy laid down by the Congress Party and the Government of India, it had no effect on Bansi Lai. His outbursts ended only when the suave and gentle Fakhruddin Saheb pointed out that the Ministry of Agriculture had no authority to permit any deviation from the guidelines approved by the Council of Ministers. Though other Chief Ministers were not as outspoken as Bansi Lai, most of them were lukewarm in their attitude towards land reform.

As I have spent about 20 years in Bihar I am personally acquainted with the working of the government there. While I was serving under the Government of India as Land Reforms Commissioner, in the context of the severe drought prevailing in Bihar, I was recalled for six months in the autumn of 1972 and posted as Commissioner of the newly created Kosi Division comprising the districts of Pumea, Saharsa and Begusarai. I joined the post a few weeks before the harvesting of the main paddy crop. I knew that the Kosi Division was an area where share-cropping was rampant and where all manner of inequities used to the perpetrated on share-croppers. At a meeting held on 9 October, 1972,1 instructed the District Officers to take effective steps to uphold the rights of the share-croppers. Accordingly, the District Officers geared up the official machinery to ensure that those who grew the crop would harvest it. The District Officer of Pumea was James Lyngdoh, an able, upright, dedicated and fearless officer. In a couple of cases in which big landlords forcibly harvested crops grown by share-croppers, Lyngdoh and his officers took deterrent action against the landlords* men. As a result of that the harvesting of the paddy crop that year passed off peacefully. But the powerful lobby of landlords started exerting pressure on the State Government to get Lyngdoh transferred. When I came to know that Government was considering Lyngdoh’s transfer, I wrote to the Chief Secretary pointing out that James Lyngdoh had done splendid work and that his transfer would demoralize the administration. Despite my strong protest, Lyngdoh was transferred from Pumea. The correspondence between the Chief Secretary and me may be seen in Appendix V-l. My two letters show that the Government of Bihar displayed unambiguous political will to thwart land reform. As a foot note to this incident I may add that a few weeks later I happened to meet the then Deputy Chief Minister, Daroga

![image 433](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile433.png)

![image 434](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile434.png)

###### Conclusion 195



Prasad Rai, on the Indian Airline’s flight from Patna to Delhi. When I asked him why he, a prominent leader of the so-called backward classes, did not protest against the unjust and uncalled for transfer of Lyngdoh, his response was a sheepish smile. He admitted that the landlords were so powerful that he could do nothing.

After completing my tenure under the Government of India, I returned to Bihar in August 1975 and was posted as Finance Secretary. On one occasion when I happened to drop into Chief Minister Jagannath Mishra’s room, he was having a discussion with the Director General of Police. The Chief Minister told the Director General of Police within my hearing that the bataidars were indulging in lawlessness and that the police did nothing to protect the landlords. Clearly the Chief Minister’s intention was that the police, who had always been generally on the side of landlords, should do a bit more to help the landlords.

A couple of years later I happened to hold the post of Chief Secretary for about nine months in 1977-78. I told Chief Minister Karpoori Thakur that in my view the non-implementation of land reforms was the root cause of Bihar’s all round backwardness and stunted development. The Chief Minister agreed with me that effective steps should be taken to implement land reforms. On my suggestion, A.R. Bandyopadhyay, an officer of outstanding ability familiar with all aspects of land reforms was appointed as Land Reforms Commissioner. Soon after that I left Bihar and moved to Delhi. Later on I came to know that Bandyopadhyay did not get much support from the Chief Minister and that nothing much could be done in the field of land reforms. It was not that Karpoori Thakur was opposed to land reforms. He did not have the requisite authority, ruthlessness and determination to remove the road blocks put up by vested interests. Probably he feared that he would lose his chair if he earned the wrath of the powerful landlords. In the eighties the Government of Bihar had made a half-hearted attempt to draw up a district-wise list of big landlords who owned more than 500 acres of land. That incomplete list contained 84 names. Not much has been done all these years to take over the surplus land of most of these identified landowners.3

These few instances show that none of the political parties that ruled Bihar since independence displayed any will to implement land

![image 435](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile435.png)

![image 436](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile436.png)

###### 196 Land Reforms in India



reforms honestly and efficiently. On the basis of my personal experience of the Central and State Governments, I have no doubt at all that the absence of political will was the prime reason for the poor implementation of land reforms in India.

###### Post-Reform Agrarian Structure

We shall now have a look at the agrarian structure that has emerged after the reform. The broad features of the post-reform structure are the continued dependence of the bulk of the rural population on agriculture and allied occupations for employment and income, the steady decline of the share of agriculture in the gross national product, the persistent fall in the size of the average holding and the increasing marginalisation of holdings, the continuance of inequality in the distribution of agricultural land, the increasing marginalisation of poor peasants, extreme fragmentation and subdivision of holdings in areas where holdings have not been consolidated , a sharp fall in the access of the rural poor to agricultural land and the under-utilization of our land, water and manpower resources. A significant aspect of the Indian economy is that the impressive growth of the manufacturing and services sectors has not led to any marked fall in the per centage of the wOrk force dependent on agriculture and allied occupations. It was 73 in 1921 and twenty five years after independence it was 73.8 in 1971. Recently there has been a marginal decline to about 68 per cent. While the population dependent on agriculture has remained steady at the high level of around 70 per cent, the share of agriculture in the gross national product has been falling steadily. At independence the share of agriculture and allied occupation was about 60 per cent. It came down to 45 per cent in 1971 and further fell to 31 per cent in 1991. The steady increase in population, the continued dependence of the bulk of the work force on agriculture and the decline of the share of agriculture in the gross national product have caused great hardship to the bottom three or four deciles of the rural population. The preponderance of marginal holdings and the small size of the average holding are not the only problems. Except in areas where the consolidation of holdings has been completed, all holdings, whether they are large, small or marginal, are subdivided and fragmented. Consolidation of holdings has been completed only in the Punjab, Haryana, and Uttar Pradesh. So in large

![image 437](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile437.png)

![image 438](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile438.png)

###### Conclusion 197



parts of the country holdings continue to be subdivided and fragmented. That is an obstacle to the optimal utilization of the agricultural land.

Nearly four decades ago Daniel Thomer had attempted to arrange the various states and areas in a series according to the degree of change discernible at the village level. The basis of the classification was the extent to which the old agrarian structure had been affected by the enforcement of land reform laws, by the fear of the coming land reform, or by any other factor. Thomer identified three types of areas: areas of least change or very little change, areas of some perceptible change and areas of greatest change. In Thomer’s view the areas of least change were Assam, Bihar, Himachal Pradesh, Malabar, Madhya Bharat, Mysore, Orissa, Rajasthan, Tamil Nadu, Travancore-Cochin, Vindhya Pradesh and West Bengal. The areas of some perceptible change were Bombay, Delhi, Hyderabad, Madhya Pradesh, Pepsu, Punjab, Saurasthra and Uttar Pradesh. And the areas of the greatest change were Kashmir and Andhra. Thomer made that classification in the initial years of land reform when only the Abolition of Intermediary Interests had been completed. Thomer’s classification is out-dated and it no longer reflects the actual field conditions.4

Thomer himself noticed some important changes in the agrarian structure when he visited India again a decade later. He briefly referred to those changes in his preface to the second edition of Agrarian Prospect in India. He wrote: “As I visited villages in many parts of India in the opening months of 1965 and again in the monsoon seasons of 1966 and 1967,1 came to feel that my earlier characterization of the rural scene had to be seriously qualified. There were new forces at work. The most striking of these, I wrote in four articles in the Calcutta Statesman, 1 to 4 November, 1967, was the distinct emergence of a nationwide class of capitalist producers. Partly these were better off peasants in the villages who had made good, less numerous, perhaps but certainly more striking - in fact, unprecedented - was what I called a sort of gold rush of urban-based “gentlemen farmers” to the country side. Businessmen, professional people, retired officers from the military services, government officials, all suddenly were buying land and taking to intensive investment in agricultural production. What attracted them were the smashing profits to be made from direct cultivation—forty per cent, sixty per cent and sometimes even eighty per cent per year. As added attraction there were the absence of any tax

![image 439](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile439.png)

![image 440](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile440.png)

###### 198 Land Reforms in India



on agricultural income, and the opportunity via agriculture, of transforming black market money into white’*.5

Thomer characterized these changes as a capitalist revolution in “socialist” India. Though isolated cases of such capitalist farming could be observed all over the country, they were concentrated in the Punjab, Haryana, Western Uttar Pradesh, certain other well endowed areas and some villages located near cities and major towns.

Recently V.M. Rao tried to classify states on the basis of the change in the agrarian structure in the wake of the implementation of land reforms.6 Rao identified four types of areas. They are given below.

- 1. ‘Semi-feudal’ areas like Bihar, where, according to scholars like Pradhan Prasad, the barriers to reform inherent in the prevailing structure of society are too strong to be overcome by the scale and intensity of efforts for land reforms made so far.
- 2. Areas (for example Karnataka) with less formidable barriers to land reform and stronger thrust for reforms as compared to semi-feudal areas but having mixed and, on the whole, modest results of reforms.
- 3. Areas (for example West Bengal and Kerala) with strong political mobilization and effective land reforms but where the growth performance has been too weak to complement reforms to bring about enduring structural changes.
- 4. Green revolution areas (eg. Punjab) where capitalist farming is firmly entrenched and where reforms are needed mainly to improve wages, work environment and economic conditions.


Rao’s study is illustrative and not exhaustive. It deals only with the states of Bihar, Karnataka, West Bengal, Kerala, Punjab and Haryana. The other states do not fit neatly into this four fold classification. There is great diversity in the post- reform agrarian structure in the different states and even in different parts of the same state. Considering the vastness of the country, the diversities that obtain and the absence of relevant empirical data for the whole country, it is not possible to classify the different states or areas on the basis of any typology. We do not have adequate materials for even attempting a simple classification on the basis of the type of farming that has emerged, e.g. large scale

![image 441](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile441.png)

![image 442](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile442.png)

###### Conclusion 199



commercial farming, progressive peasant farming, subsistence farming and tenant farming. Apart from the absence of data, another serious problem is that different types of farming co-exist even in compact areas. For example, even in the Punjab where commercial fanning has made great headway there do exist subsistence farming, tenant farming and absentee land ownership. Even in Bihar, the classic land of semi-feudal land relations, there are well managed commercial farms and very efficiently operated medium and small holdings. Additionally, I have some doubts about the usefulness of such classifications.

There have certainly been differences between states in the matter of the implementation of the different programmes of land reform. But if we leave out tenancy reform in Kerala—implemented fairly efficiently—these differences in the degree of efficiency or inefficiency with which land reforms have been implemented are, indeed, of a minor nature. Moreover, land reform has been only one of the several factors that have contributed to the changes in the agrarian structure. So it will not be appropriate to make any classification on the basis of the implementation of land reforms. From the point of view of practical usefulness what we need is a detailed study of each agro-climatic region taking into account the natural endowments and the institutional and other problems, and then working out specific strategies for each region. I have neither the competence nor the resources to undertake such a detailed study covering the entire country. For the present I shall content myself by drawing attention to the problems of one large area of great potential for rapid agricultural growth.

Bihar and Eastern Uttar Pradesh

The area that calls for urgent attention is Eastern Uttar Pradesh and the Bihar plains. The Indo-Gangetic valley is one of the world’s richly endowed agricultural tracts blessed with good alluvial soils, plentiful ground water, large areas under flow irrigation and climatic conditions favourable to the growing of crops all the year round. The resource endowment of the eastern half of the Gangetic valley is in no way inferior to that of the western half. As a matter of fact, as one proceeds from the west to the east, the annual rainfall increases and ground water becomes more plentiful. Yet the spectacular increase in agricultural productivity witnessed in the western half since the late sixties has not

![image 443](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile443.png)

![image 444](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile444.png)

###### 200 Land Reforms in India



spread to the east in full measure. This becomes obvious when we compare agricultural productivity in the districts of Ludhiana and Pumea, the former in the Punjab and the latter in Bihar.

Some relevant data for the decades of the sixties and the seventies are given below.7

Ludhiana Pumea

- 1. Gross croppcd area in the 1960’s (3 year average) in hectares. 401,692 897,584
- 2. Gross cropped area in the 1970’s (3 year average) in hectares 507,666 918,340
- 3. Gross irrigated area in the 1960’s (3 year average) in hectares 230,442 1,349
- 4. Gross irrigated area in the 1970’s (3 year average) in hectares 425,266 98,125
- 5. Fertilizer consumption in 1960’s (3 year average) in metric tons. 4,575 143
- 6. Fertilizer consumption in the 1970’s (3 year average) in metric tons. 41,359 3,255
- 7. Number of diesel and electric pumps in the 1970’s 50,472 3480
- 8. Average output per hectare of 19 crops in the 1960’s (3 year average) in rupees 1,267 934
- 9. Average output per hectare of 19 crops in the 1970’s (3 year average) in rupees 2,102 786


The data show that in the first decade of the so-called “Green Revolution”, while Ludhiana’s output increased by 75 per cent that of Pumea declined. In the seventies the per hectare output in Ludhiana was 2 \ times that in Pumea. G.S.Bhalla and Y.K.Alagh who made a district wise study of Indian agriculture found that between the trienniums 1962-65 and 1970-73 the average annual exponential growth in agricultural output was 7.98 per cent in Ludhiana8. During the same period Pumea witnessed a negative growth of - 2.29 per cent.

Some allowance has to be made for the adverse weather conditions in Pumea in 1971-72 and 1972-73. Even so, the fact remains that there was hardly any improvement in Pumea’s agricultural productivity in the first decade of the Green Revolution. Pumea also

![image 445](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile445.png)

![image 446](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile446.png)

Conclusion 201

witnessed fast agricultural growth in the next two decades. Yet the growth has not been commensurate with the potential. The latest data available for the year 1988 show that while the per acre average output of wheat was 4.38 quintals in Ludhiana it was only 1.28 quintals in Pumea. Similarly in the case of rice, the average output per acre was 3.24 quintals in Ludhiana while it was 1.08 quintals in Pumea.9

A superficial look at the figures may give the impression that the reasons for the higher productivity of Ludhiana are the larger per centage of irrigated area, the more intensive exploitation of ground water and the application of larger quantities of fertilizers. And some casual observers may also offer the glib explanation that the Punjabi farmer is hardworking while his Bihar counterpart is lazy and unenterprising. If one were to probe a little deeper, it will, however, become obvious that the reasons for the under-utilization of the irrigation potential, the low levels of inputs and the lack of initiative and enterprise on the part of the farmer are all rooted in the semi-feudal relations of production that exist in the agriculture of Pumea.

In Ludhiana and the other districts of the western half of the Indo-Gangetic valley most of the land is cultivated by the farmers themselves or under their direct supervision. By and large, the informal tenancies that survive there are the leasing in of the land of small holders by owner-cultivators anxious to augment the size of their farms. Small and marginal farmers also exist, but share-cropping by subsistence farmers is practically non-existent. Fragmented holdings have been consolidated in the Punjab, Haryana and Western U.P. facilitating investments in irrigation, land improvement and the more efficient use of land and water. The situation in Pumea is very different. A substantial part of the land is held by absentee land owners who get their land cultivated through share-croppers or through wage labour under the supervision of a manager or a distant relative. Land reforms have had little impact on the high concentration in the ownership of land in Pumea. There are still rich landlords owning several hundred acres of land located in scattered villages. Even if the landlord lives in one of the villages in which part of his land lies, he is an absentee owner in respect of his land in the other villages. Furthermore, a substantial portion of the land in Pumea is owned by members of the so-called upper castes who do not engage in physical

![image 447](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile447.png)

![image 448](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile448.png)

###### 202 Land Reforms in India



labour even if their holdings are small and they have no other source of income. So, even small holders let out their land to share-croppers. When I studied the conditions in Pumea in 1972-73 I came to the conclusion that about 40 per cent of the land in Pumea was under share- cropping.10

The per centage of land under share- cropping has come down since then, but still it is probably not less than 25 per cent. Though under the law share-croppers are entitled to security of tenure and the share of the landowner is limited to 25 per cent of the gross produce, in practice the protection granted to share-croppers is illusory. Since the tenancies are oral and the share-croppers are weak politically, economically and socially, they seldom succeed in securing the rights to which they are entitled under the law. They have only a tenuous hold on the land they cultivate and invariably they have to hand over to the land owner one half or more of the produce. In such a situation the tenant has no incentive to make any long term investments in the land or cultivate it efficiently by providing higher levels of purchased inputs and applying larger doses of his own laboui. Though theoretically both the landlord and the share-cropper stand to gain by sharing the cost of cultivation and cultivating the land more efficiently, such a practice has not been adopted on any significant scale in Pumea. The landlords do not look upon the land as a productive asset that should be operated efficiently to maximize their income. To them land is essentially a store of value, a hedge against inflation, an instrument for appropriating the large unearned increments accruing from the huge public investments in irrigation and, most importantly, the very foundation of political and economic power and social status. The attitude of the landowners is not as irrational as it appears on first sight. They are reluctant to take any steps that may lead to the strengthening of the economic conditions of the share-croppers. In Pumea and some other districts in the eastern half of the Gangetic valley, share-cropping tenancies have none of the redeeming features noted by writers on the basis of their experience in East Asia. After studying the field conditions in the Kosi area some two decades ago I came to the conclusion that concentration in the ownership of land, absentee land ownership, fragmented holdings and insecure share-cropping tenancies were the principal reasons for the gross underutilization of the irrigation potential. These very factors also

![image 449](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile449.png)

![image 450](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile450.png)

###### Conclusion 203



inhibit the fuller exploitation of the plentiful ground water and the provision of optimum levels of inputs. The causes for Pumea still lagging behind Ludhiana in agricultural productivity are rooted in the antiquated agrarian structure. Some or all of the unwelcome features of the agrarian structure of Purnea are also found in the other districts of the eastern half of the Indo-Gangetic valley and the deltas of the great rivers. If the agrarian structure is suitably reformed and some other essential measures are taken these areas of unrivalled agricultural potential should be able to grow all the food that our growing population needs. Hence reforming the agrarian structure in these areas continues to be a matter of the highest national priority. The package of reforms needed will include the strict enforcement of the ceiling laws and an effective campaign for recording the rights of share-croppers. After these are completed successfully, attention should be paid to the consolidation of holdings.

The above observations are primarily based on my experience in the Kosi Division two decades ago. Though much water has flowed down the Kosi since then and Purnea too has experienced considerable agricultural growth ,some of the institutional obstacles to growth still persist. Ten years ago the Reserve Bank of India appointed a committee headed by S.R. Sen to look into agricultural productivity in Eastern India. That Committee which submitted its report in 1984 came to the conclusion that though Eastern Uttar Pradesh and Bihar had experienced some agricultural growth, it did not match the potential. The Committee found that:

“the persistence of some semi-feudal institutions and forces in the region act as an impediment to investment and dampen the will and capacity for change on the part of farmers in spite of the rich agricultural base.”11

###### New Perspectives and Policy Issues

As we have seen, at independence the Indian agrarian structure suffered from several serious weaknesses that impeded agricultural growth. Agriculture had been stagnating for several decades at low levels of productivity .The prevailing pattern of rights in land and sharing of the produce stood in the way of the adoption of modem farming practices and the application of optimum doses of labour,

![image 451](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile451.png)

![image 452](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile452.png)

###### 204 Land Reforms in India



capital and other inputs. The measures of land reform adopted after independence were aimed at curing the more serious of the maladies that impeded agricultural growth. Those measures were also expected to subserve in a small way the goal of social justice that had inspired the freedom struggle and which was later on incorporated in the Directive Principles of State Policy included in the Indian Constitution.

The period since the mid-sixties has witnessed a sea change in the situation. Indian agriculture is no longer stagnant. In fact it has shown noticeable dynamism during the last three decades. In the closing years of the Second Five Year Plan it became clear that the Community Development Programme was not likely to produce significant results and that the strategy of spreading the available resources thinly all over the country would not lead to fast agricultural growth. That was also the period when persistent droughts had led to severe shortages in food supply. The country had to resort to large scale import of food grains. That was the background against which a new agricultural strategy was evolved. The essence of that strategy was to concentrate all efforts in selected districts with potential for rapid agricultural growth. As luck would have it, the introduction of the new strategy coincided with the emergence of high yielding varieties of wheat seeds. Taking into account the great potential of the new seeds the strategy was further modified laying emphasis on the introduction of those seeds and multiple cropping. The great spurt in food production that followed was hailed as the ‘Green Revolution*. The increase in food production was impressive by any standard. The output of food grains shot up from 89 million tons in 1964-65, which was the best year before the Green Revolution, to 100 million tons in 1969-70. Still more impressive was the increase in the output of wheat, the cereal that responded best to the new package of practices. According to Bhalla and Alagh :

“The growth rate of wheat output has been phenomenal. It grew at a rate of 10.50 per cent per annum over the period 1962-65 to 1970-73. Another very important feature of wheat output has been that although the area under this crop has risen substantially, the contribution made by yield increases has been really phenomenal. Thus, the yield of wheat grew at a rate of 6.29 per cent per annum during the period under study. Surely, this is one of the most notable achievements of Indian agriculture during the last decade12.’*

![image 453](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile453.png)

![image 454](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile454.png)

###### Conclusion 205



In the first decade of the Green Revolution the breakthrough in production was largely confined to the western half of the IndoGangetic Valley. There was, as we have seen, a marked resistance to its spread to the equally well endowed eastern half. That resistance has considerably weakened in recent years. The decade of the eighties witnessed a substantial increase in the production of food grains, particularly of rice. The annual rate of growth in eastern India moved ahead of the average rate of growth for the country as a whole. The full potential is, however, yet to be realized. Even so, Indian agriculture is in a confident mood today. The spectre of famine has vanished, a large buffer stock of food grains has been built up and the country is now in a position to export food grains. There is, however, a dark side to this rosy picture. Because of the failure of land reforms and other programmes for the re-distribution of assets, and the inadequate growth of employment, widespread poverty and unsatisfied hunger co-exist with mounting stocks of food grains. Another unwelcome aspect of the so-called Green Revolution is the widening gap between the large farmers and the small holders and between regions. Though a number of such serious problems still remain, the agrarian scene today is totally different from what it was at independence. It is, therefore, necessary to have a fresh look at our land policy taking into account the great changes that have taken place during the last thirty years.

It is not only the agrarian situation that has changed. Significant changes have taken place in the political, social and economic environment as well. If political will in favour of meaningful land reform was weak at independence and weaker still later on, it is nonexistent today. Land reform has practically disappeared from the agenda of most political parties. This, in my view, is the inevitable consequence of the great changes that have taken place in the social and economic fields. With the of abolition intermediary interests the erstwhile superior tenants belonging mostly to the upper and middle castes acquired a higher social status. Rise in agricultural productivity, higher incomes from cultivation and steep rise in land values have greatly augmented their economic strength. Substantial landowners who wield great authority in rural India are bitterly opposed to ceilings on agricultural holdings. They are able to have their way because no serious efforts have been made to organize the landless and the land

![image 455](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile455.png)

![image 456](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile456.png)

###### 206 Land Reforms in India



poor and enlist their support in favour of reforms. As far as tenancy reform is concerned, there is a certain commonality of interest between all land owners—large, medium, small and even marginal. They are all passionately attached to their land and all of them are opposed to conferring benefits on insecure tenants. In the first round of land reform only the intermediaries were adversely affected. They were few in number and were weak politically. They had also made themselves obnoxious by aligning themselves with the colonial power. So it was easy enough to abolish intermediary interests. And it was done without hurting them much. But injuring the interests of the present class of landowners is an entirely different proposition. No political party that wishes to win elections and come to power can afford to do that. At the time of independence this class of land owners did not wield much political and economic power, and with a modicum of political will the reforms could have been implemented. But now it has become almost impossible to carry out the reforms. As pointed out by P.C. Joshi:

“In the first phase, the main aim was to abolish Zamindari and Jagirdari tenures, or such feudal or semi-feudal rights over land as were primarily acquired through extra-economic and political sanctions during the colonial rule. In this phase, the reforms were intended to curb or eliminate a functionless and parasitic class of landlords which was economically isolated from the production system and morally rootless on account of its identification with colonialism. In the second phase, however, the target of attack is the far broader and pervasive class of proprietors of land, including not only tlie remnants of the former landed gentry reemerging as large farmers but also large sections of the working peasantry having the status of proprietors. The second type of land reforms attack not just the principle and practice of unearned property income (‘noncultivating landlordism’) but they appear also to launch a war against the principle of income earned through enterprise in agriculture. They provoke, therefore, a large section of the peasantry into a state of active or passive resistance by offending their property instinct as well as their commercial drive.”13

P.C. Joshi and M.L. Dantwala are two outstanding academicians with a deep and comprehensive understanding of all aspects of Indian land reforms. Both believe that we have ‘missed the bus’ in the matter of land reforms. While Joshi continues to entertain a certain degree of

![image 457](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile457.png)

![image 458](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile458.png)

###### Conclusion 207



optimism, Dantwala believes that land reform is a lost cause. Dantwala says:

“Years back when enforcement of land ceiling could have been effective, the political leadership consciously permitted its evasion. With the lapse of time, the pressure of population coupled with fraudulent partitions, that opportunity has been missed, inasmuch as the ‘surplus’ land available for redistribution has shrunk. Under the circumstances, while I would still plead for a more egalitarian landownership, I would urge social scientists concerned with the elimination of poverty and affluence in rural areas to shift their emphasis from re-distribution of land to a more equitable access to non-land resources, such as irrigation and inputs like seeds, fertiliser and credit.”14

Apart from the difficulties involved in implementing the reforms, there are also serious doubts about the relevance of such reforms in the new context. There is a general agreement on the need to revise the land policy. But there are sharp differences regarding the lines on which the policy should be recast. One school believes that the agrarian constraints no longer exist in the old form, and that even if they do in some pockets, they can be effectively countered by the adoption of modem technology and necessary institutional support for the provision of credit and other inputs. Some of them argue that the ceiling laws should be abrogated and that all restrictions on the leasing of agricultural land should be removed. V.M. Dandekar was a leading proponent of this view. He puts forward the case for reconsidering the land policy in these words:

‘There was a time when tenancy legislation giving security of tenure and regulating rent, and in some cases, making the tenant the owner of the land, was necessary and justifiable. The purpose was to promote owner-cultivation. Owner cultivation had to be promoted by legislation because, with the traditional agricultural technology, cultivation was not profitable and landowners preferred to lease out their land, rack-renting and otherwise exploiting the tenant through trading and money-lending as allied activities. For the same reason, a non-agriculturist was prohibited to purchase agricultural land. In the conditions of agricultural technology then prevailing, the nexus of absentee ownership, trading, and money-lending had to be broken.

![image 459](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile459.png)

![image 460](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile460.png)

###### 208 Land Reforms in India



There is no denying that the success has been only partial. One reason is that millions of owners and tenants are involved and the arm of the law does not reach them all. More importantly, with the new agricultural technology coming in, things have changed radically. Owner cultivation of a minimum size of land has become viable and it no longer needs legislative support. We have not taken note of this fact and continue with the same old tenancy legislation.

“The second element is ceiling on land holdings. One must admit this has totally failed; that it has been circumvented by various means such as subdivision and ‘benami’ transactions. One need not be surprised. The surprising thing is that it was at all accepted showing how politically weak was the lobby of even owner cultivators in those early days.”15 Dandekar made a strong plea for the unfreezing of the labour market by repealing the ceiling laws and removing the restrictions on the leasing out of land. Dandekar did not come to this conclusion because he was opposed to radical land reforms. As early as 1970 he had observed that in the overpopulated countries radical land reform was an excellent strategy, as North Vietnam had demonstrated, to break through the vicious circle of poverty and overpopulation. Dandeker ruled out that strategy for India because *it takes a determined government, supported by a countrywide political cadre and endowed with leadership with rare political acumen, to take the country onto this path. Governments wanting in these qualities, or what Myrdal refers to as ‘soft’ governments, must work within the institutional framework of private property in land.

The second school of thought believes that there is the need to revise the land policy in the context of the changes that have taken place during the last four decades, but it will be unwise to abrogate the ceiling laws. Steps should, of course, be taken to enforce the existing laws and distribute the ceiling surplus land. No useful purpose will be served by scaling down the levels of ceilings or tinkering with the ceiling laws. As far as tenancy reform is concerned, this school is in favour of removing restrictions on the leasing out of land and allowing clandestine tenancies to come to the surface. Distinguished agricultural economists like M.L. Dantwala, C.H. Hanumantha Rao and V.S. Vyas support these views. According to Hanumantha Rao:

“With population pressure on agricultural land persisting and there being, as yet, not enough evidence of economies of scale in Indian

![image 461](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile461.png)

![image 462](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile462.png)

###### Conclusion 209



agriculture, there is little justification for doing away with the ceiling on the ownership of land at this stage, especially because farmers are able to augment their operational holdings in response to the changing technological and economic factors.

“However, the experience also suggests that freeing the lease market for land may contribute to equity as well as efficiency of resource use. The pure tenants as well as the small and marginal farmers would be able to augment their operational holdings by leasing in area, where there is pressure on land and where wages are low and poverty is high. This may also contribute to the more efficient use of land, labour, and other resources. On the other hand, in areas experiencing technological change and high growth, where population pressure on land is low and wages are high, the marginal farmers may gain by leasing out their area and taking up non-farm employment. Where wages and the rate of mechanisation are high, the medium and large farmers who are leasing in area would be able to make a fuller use of their fixed equipment.” 16

V.S.Vyas is also in favour of retaining the ceiling on holdings but removing the restrictions on the leasing of land. In his view :

The basic approach of land reforms should be to create as many viable holdings as possible, by physical expansion of the land holdings of the small farmers, by encouraging them to lease in land from other small landowners and, by enabling them to have larger value added from their agriculture. Three sets of measures may help to achieve this objective:

- (i) activising the land market, encouraging large-scale purchase of land from the large landholders and sale of that land on easy terms to the potentially viable small farmers.
- (ii) defreezing the lease market, encouraging the non-viablc marginal farmers to lease out their land, without losing their ownership rights, to other small farmers.
- (iii) retaining the ceiling on land holding as well as the provisions discouraging the entry of large companies in agricultural sector as producers, but encouraging integration of the small farmers’ economy with large agro-processing units, preferably with the units in the co-operative sector but also with those in the private sector.17


![image 463](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile463.png)

![image 464](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile464.png)

###### 210 Land Reforms in India



The third point of view is that despite all the changes that have taken place it will not be possible to promote sustained agricultural growth commensurate with social and economic justice unless some minimal institutional reforms are carried out. It is conceded that the opportune moment for radical land reform passed long ago. It is also accepted that the technological breakthrough and the so-called ‘Green Revolution’ have rendered the needed institutional reforms even more difficult. But instead of rendering land reform irrelevant, the adoption of new technology has, as a matter of fact, highlighted the urgent need for institutional changes. P.C. Joshi who is one of the leading proponents of this school of thought says:

‘We have seen how the lion’s share of the gains of this scientific and technological revolution have been appropriated by economically stronger and politically more powerful regions and interest groups in rural India. Obviously the potentialities have remained unrealised. In real life the moral and the technological compulsions have been pulling in opposite directions. The fault does not lie with modern technology but with the constraints inherent in the emerging agrarian structure which discriminates in favour of the already privileged and against the poverty-stricken. It is also clear that the solution of this contradiction lies not in the direction of modem technology. It lies in the direction of removing the institutional constraints. The pressure for expediting the institutional reforms—for transformation of production-relations—is thus inherent in the drive for accelerating and broadbasing the technological transformation of agriculture. Modern technology has made institutional reforms not only more important but also more urgent. Postponing institutional reforms clearly involves prolonging and widening the gap between the potential-actual lag in agriculture.”18

Distinguished economists like Pradhan H. Prasad and V.M. Rao also hold similar views. As a matter of fact P.H. Prasad was the pioneer in identifying (in the context of Bihar) production relations as the principal obstacle to sustained agricultural growth in Bihar.19

In V.M. Rao’s view:

“Growth in Indian agriculture now confronts barriers which cannot be overcome through primarily technocratic strategies which succeeded so admirably in the green revolution areas. Growth in the green revolution areas is now levelling off. It is also realised that it is

![image 465](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile465.png)

![image 466](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile466.png)

###### Conclusion 211



being achieved at a high and increasing cost in terms of scarce inputs like irrigation and fertiliser. Growth in dry land agriculture and in the potentially rich eastern parts of India needs a combination of both technocratic and reformist strategies. The point is that, unlike in the immediate post-independence years, the need for land reforms is now far more pressing—since sustained growth itself depends on themand this may have the effect of strengthening the political will for reform.”20

Rao also thinks that the implementation of the massive povertyalleviation programmes have had a conscientising effect on the development personnel as well as the rural poor and that the rural poor may now be in a better position to influence government policy. Joshi has not spelt out any specific grounds for optimism. He has drawn attention to the need to create a new momentum in favour of land reform. Joshi thinks that:

“a momentum can be created on the land reforms front, not just by streamlining the administrative procedures, by creating new administrative bodies, and by replacing the rule of one section of the political elite by the rule of another section. The challenge of land reforms has to be met ideologically by redefining the whole approach to the property question in general and the question of property in land in particular in the dynamic context of building a new type of society and polity in India. It has to be met politically by mobilising the widest support for this new approach and by releasing social forces wedded to this approach.”21

While I am in complete agreement with the view that some measures of land reform are urgendy needed for fostering rapid agricultural growth, particularly in the eastern half of the Gangetic valley and some other well endowed areas, I do not share the optimism of Rao and Joshi about the emergence of a climate favourable to land reform in the immediate future. I would not, however go to the extent of saying that such a development is impossible. For even in the present context a constituency in favour of purposeful land reform can, perhaps, be created by forging a coalition to subserve the genuine self interest of political parties, the captains of industry and the rural poor particularly the agricultural labourers and share-croppers. The

![image 467](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile467.png)

![image 468](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile468.png)

###### 212 Land Reforms in India



intellectuals could play a significant role as catalysts. As of now such a development is no more than a theoretical possibility.

In this brief discussion I have left out of consideration two extreme points of view, occasionally aired in seminars and workshops on land reforms and in the press. The first of these is that in the context of the liberalisation of the Indian economy all land reform laws should be repealed and that Indian agriculture should be allowed to grow entirely depending on market forces. The second, and entirely contrary, view is that the levels of ceiling should be drastically scaled down and that the laws should be strictly enforced so as to bring about a radical redistribution of land. Both these views ignore the objective conditions prevailing in the country and so they do not, in my opinion, merit any serious consideration.

After having closely observed the formulation of land policy, the enactment of land reform laws and their implementation in the seventies and having seen from a distance the recent developments in Indian agriculture, I have come to the conclusion that it is high time that we made substantial changes in our land policy. My specific suggestions are given in the following paragraphs.

###### Suggestions for revising land policy

Tenancy Reform

The restrictions on the leasing out of land have only led to tenancies being pushed underground. As long as a class of land owners who shun physical labour and a large mass of indigent peasantry co-exist, any legal ban on tenancy will remain a dead letter. And in a situation in which employment opportunities are not expanding commensurate with the size of the work force, the land owners are in such a strong position that they find it easy to push up their share of the produce. In the context of the social and economic conditions that obtain at present it is unrealistic to expect that a ban on tenancies will be effective. In states like Kerala where the rural poor are politically conscious and well organized, many land owners keep their lands fallow or grow only one crop where two could be grown. After considering the pros and cons, I have suggested in Chapter 3 that tenancies should be allowed to be created subject to certain conditions.

![image 469](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile469.png)

![image 470](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile470.png)

###### Conclusion 213



In my view the revival of tenancies would improve the access of the rural poor to agricultural land leading to enhanced employment and output.

I would, however, like to enter a caveat here. In the Bihar plains, eastern Uttar Pradesh and the delta of the Mahanadi instead of straightway legalising tenancy, the first step should be to amend the law as in West Bengal for conferring limited rights on share-croppers. Thereafter an intensive campaign should be mounted for recording the rights of share-croppers on the lines of West Bengal’s Operation Barga.

Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

Despite the dynamism displayed by Indian agriculture and the sustained growth experienced in recent years, the agrarian structure continues to be beset by a host of serious problems. The pressure of population on land continues to rise steadily and there has been no fall in the proportion of the rural population dependent on agriculture. Over two thirds of the rural work force continues to depend on agriculture and allied occupations for employment and income. Even a much faster growth of the manufacturing and services sectors is not likely to lead to any substantial reduction in the work force dependent on agriculture. As pointed out by Hanumantha Rao, there is no clear evidence, as yet, of economies of scale in Indian agriculture. Nor is there any evidence of a positive co-relation between increase in the area of the holding and employment. The size-class distribution of land holdings continues to be skewed and the number of small and marginal farmers is growing year after year. In the context of rising agricultural productivity and soaring land prices there would have been a greater concentration of land in the hands of rich farmers and speculators but for the ceiling laws. If the ceiling laws are relaxed there will be a scramble for the purchase of land by rich farmers and speculators leading to a further rise in land prices. And the speculators may not be interested in putting the land to intensive use. The small and marginal farmers will find it even more difficult to expand their holdings by buying more land. Considering all these factors I am opposed to the repeal of the ceiling laws at the present stage. The question of abrogating those laws can be considered when the rural population dependent on agriculture falls below, say, twenty per cent or so.

![image 471](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile471.png)

![image 472](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile472.png)

###### 214 Land Reforms in India



I am also opposed to any further lowering of the ceiling levels. Apart from the fact that any reduction in the level of ceilings is not feasible politically, it is also doubtful if it will serve any useful purpose.
