In future the state governments should concentrate on the better implementation of the existing ceiling laws and the quick distribution of surplus land. Better results can be obtained if attention is concentrated on the large landowners who have successfully evaded the law so far.

Absentee Landowners

One of the principal aims of our land policy should be to facilitate the transfer of agricultural land from the hands of absentee owners who do not take sufficient interest in cultivating their land. The strict enforcement of the ceiling law will be of some help in achieving this objective. But that alone will not be enough. Another measure one can think of is to lay down minimum standards of cultivation. But any such law will be difficult to enforce. It will also lead to widespread corruption. So it has to be rejected as impractical.

Two measures that will help reduce the incidence of absentee ownership of land are the revival of tenancy and the implementation of a well thought out programme for the purchase of their land and its distribution to the landless and the land poor. If the restrictions on leasing are removed and government make an irrevocable declaration that the tenancy law will not be amended in future to confer ownership rights on tenants, quite a few absentee landowners will agree to lease out their land. Those who are not anxious to hold on to their land will be willing to sell it to government if they can get a good price. Both these measures will lead to a reduction in the incidence of absentee landownership and improve the access of the rural poor to agricultural land.

###### Land Reform and the Alleviation of Poverty

When it was realized that the poor were not getting a fair share of the fruits of economic development and that regional disparities were increasing special schemes were started targeting weaker sections. In the Sixth Five Year Plan emphasis was laid on area planning and special schemes designed to help the weaker sections of the society. It

![image 473](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile473.png)

![image 474](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile474.png)

###### Conclusion 215



was proposed that all the ongoing programmes in the field of rural development should be closely integrated and that steps should be taken to ensure that the benefits flow to the target groups consisting of small and marginal farmers, agricultural labourers and rural artisans. Having come to the conclusion that the purely project approach adopted in the past plans in respect of the various rural development programmes would be inadequate, the Planning Commission suggested the integration of the different programmes like the Small Farmer Development Agency, Drought Prone Area Programme, Command Area Development, Special Live stock Production Programme and the Food For Work Programme. Thus was bom the well known Integrated Rural Development Programme (IRDP). Though comprehensive instructions were issued for integrating all schemes of rural development, in the field integration did not become fully effective. Furthermore, in subsequent years a number of new centrally sponsored schemes were started ignoring the concept of complete integration of all programmes of rural development and poverty alleviation under one umbrella. The more important among the new schemes are Training of Rural Youth for Self-employment, Rural Landless Employment Guarantee Programme, Jawahar Rojgar Yojna, Employment Assurance scheme etc. Subsequently under the Jawahar Rojgar Yojna two subschemes known as Indira Awas Yojana and Million Wells Scheme were introduced. Some of these were hastily formulated in an ad hoc manner in the hope of securing immediate political, or rather, electoral benefits. Though there has been a phenomenal increase in the allocations for rural development, not much has been done in the direction of revamping the machinery for implementation.

###### Strategy for the alleviation of poverty

It is beyond the scope of this study to present a comprehensive survey of our efforts in the fields of rural development and alleviation of poverty. Nor do we propose to consider in detail the present strategy for the alleviation of poverty and the programmes that are being implemented. The subject is briefly dealt with here only with the purpose of highlighting the role of land reform in poverty alleviation.

The twin planks of our strategy for the alleviation of poverty have rightly been the augmentation of employment opportunities and the

![image 475](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile475.png)

![image 476](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile476.png)

###### 216 Land Reforms in India



transfer/creation of productive assets. Though the strategy is the right one, there is considerable scope for improvement in the formulation and implementation of the various programmes.

Employment generation

In the matter of increasing employment opportunities the ad hoc approach highlighted by hastily designed schemes like the Jawahar Rojgar Yojana should be given up. The bulk of the additional employment should be generated in highly productive programmes of soil and water conservation formulated on a watershed basis, social forestry, afforestation, desilting of rivers, canals, reservoirs and ponds, the development of common property resources etc.

Revamping Block Administration

The administration at the block level will have to be thoroughly revamped and upgraded if the schemes are to be drawn up competently and implemented efficiently. The Block Development Officer is utterly inadequate for the task. I should like to repeat here a suggestion that I have been making since 1978. An average Block has at present access to resources of the order of over Rupees one crore in a year and in the next Plan perhaps it will exceed Rupees two crores. If the programmes for rural development and alleviations of poverty are to be formulated and implemented efficiently, a matter of the topmost priority is to find some 6000 able and dedicated persons to work as Block Development officers. In the context of the prevailing value system it is inevitable that the great majority of the bright young men and women of every generation should seek opportunities for gainful employment abroad or within the country itself in low priority jobs in industry or business. Even so, if suitable improvements are made in emoluments and working conditions, it should be possible to find an adequate number of young men and women of high calibre to work as Block Development Officers. In my considered view all fresh recruits to the Indian Administrative service and all other All India and Central services should be required to work as Block Development officers for five years. Probably the right thing will be to post them to Blocks after six months training and decide their allocation to the different services at the end of five years on the basis of their performance in the Blocks..

![image 477](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile477.png)

![image 478](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile478.png)

###### Conclusion 217



Besides making the services of a large number of bright young men and women available for rural development, such a step will also enable those who will eventually man key positions in the different public services to acquire some insight into the real problems of rural India. A second important step could be to offer short term contracts of five years to suitable young men and women who pass out of the Indian Institutes of Management, the Indian Institutes of Technology and other such institutions. After some six months* training they could be posted as Block Development Officers. At the end of five years, those who have done well and are keen to continue to serve in rural India should absorbed in the superior civil service. This suggestion is not unrealistic. Though there has been in a recent years a phenomenal increase in the starting salary and perquisites of the products of these institutions, I know it from my personal experience that a considerable number of them will be willing to opt out of the rat race and come forward to accept challenging assignments in the field of rural development. Of course, their salary and status should be the same as that of fresh recruits to the IAS and sufficient care should be taken to overcome the possible resistance from the existing personnel.

Another pressing requirement is to revamp and augment the machinery for planning and monitoring at the district level. Personnel of the requisite calibre from the relevant disciplines should be drafted for this important work. The introduction of Panchayati Raj will not alter the situations in any manner. If anything, Panchayati Raj will only make the recruitment of personnel of a higher calibre for rural development a matter of even greater urgency. These organs of local government will not be able to discharge their onerous duties unless they are serviced by dedicated personal of outstanding ability.

Redistribution o f land

The second, and equally important element in the strategy for the alleviation of poverty has been the redistribution/creation of productive assets. Land is by far the most important productive asset in rural India. In a situation where the bulk of the rural work force depend upon low productivity agriculture for survival and the prospects of shifting a substantial portion of that work force into other sectors are bleak, in the short term, considering that some three-fifths of that work force is

![image 479](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile479.png)

![image 480](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile480.png)

###### 218 Land Reforms in India



self-employed, the redistribution of agricultural land could have been the most potent weapon for waging a successful war on poverty. The experience of the Peoples’ Republic of China, a country that could appropriately be compared to India in terms of area, population, resource endowment, stage of economic development and incidence of poverty when both embarked upon planned economic development, underlines the role of land reform in the alleviation of poverty. Commenting on the Chinese land reform, Dwight H. Perkins of the Harvard University observed: “It is clear that land reform had a dramatic effect on the distribution of income . Because a major effort was made to ensure that the poorest villagers received a large share of the benefits, and because there was no compensation for landlords, the incomes of the poor rose substantially. The average income of a peasant in the poorest fifth of the rural population rose by 88 per cent, that of the bottom 40 per cent rose by 37 per cent.”22

In China the radical redistribution of land was the most important single factor that led to the abolition of the abject forms of rural poverty within a short period. In India also a radical re-distribution of land could have led to a substantial reduction of poverty. As we have seen all our efforts at redistribution of land have been of no avail. The prevailing objective conditions are unfavourable for attempting any substantial redistribution of land. Though we rule out as impractical the radical redistribution of land, we still consider it necessary to provide the self-employed rural poor with small plots of land to enable them to take up animal husbandry, poultry farming, growing of vegetables or other economic activities. In this context we make the following suggestions.

We have already suggested the revival of tenancy. This will improve the access of the rural poor to agricultural land. Uur own estimate is that this single measure will enable the rural poor to lease in about 10 per cent of the arable land in the country.

Second, the enacted ceiling laws should be implemented efficiently and the ceiling surplus land should be distributed among the rural poor. Of course only land of reasonably good quality that can be brought under the plough should be distributed. That alone will not make adequate land available for redistribution.

![image 481](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile481.png)

![image 482](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile482.png)

###### Conclusion 219



The third suggestion is that good agricultural land owned by absentee landowners, and landowners who are unable or unwilling to cultivate their land properly should be acquired under the Land Acquisition Act. In villages where enough such land may not be available, other agricultural land of good quality should be acquired. All the land acquired by government should be distributed among the landless and the landpoor so that every household of the rural poor will have about 20 cents of land. This will improve their economic and social status. It will also enable them to take up subsidiary occupations. This proposal will, of course place a heavy financial burden on the state exchequer. But by dropping some of the present schemes, and reducing the allotments for some, it should be possible to find the necessary funds within the overall allocation for rural development. Adequate steps will have to be taken to prevent the likely spurt in land prices when government goes in for acquisition of land on a large scale. But in our view all these problems can be tackled if the administration is revamped and geared up for the purpose. In the conditions obtaining in rural India, schemes for helping the rural poor have a reasonable chance of success only if they do not directly hurt the rural rich. The proposal for the large sale acquisition of private agricultural land satisfies that condition.

Access to non land assets

I agree with Dantwala’s suggestion that our efforts at redistribution of land having failed, attention should now shift from redistribution of land to a more equitable access to non-land resources, such as irrigation and inputs like seeds, fertiliser and credit. While we recognize the crucial role of irrigation in protecting crops against the failure of rains, raising agricultural productivity, facilitating multiple cropping and augmenting employment opportunities, we cannot ignore the unpleasant truth, that, more than any other factor, irrigation has been instrumental in aggravating the disparities in rural income and wealth. The country’s water resources constitute a precious social asset which ought to be exploited for the benefit of the society as a whole. When a public irrigation project is constructed, what happens is that the direct benefits from irrigation get distributed in proportion to the area of land owned by the different households within the command area. The

![image 483](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile483.png)

![image 484](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile484.png)

###### 220 Land Reforms in India



distribution of land being highly skewed, normally, more than one-half of the benefits accrue to the top .ten per cent of the rural households, while the bottom two-thirds of the households get only about ten per cent of the benefits. Thus the benefits from the massive investment made in irrigation has been shared in an entirely inequitable manner. The providing of irrigation has led to large increases in agricultural productivity and has pushed up land prices sky high.No step has been taken so far to garner for the public exchequer a portion of the increased agricultural income, or mop up a part of the capital gains. The inequity of the whole thing becomes even more glaring as the prevailing water rates are so low that they do not cover even the working expenses. These baneful effects can be countered to some extent if betterment levies are collected, water rates are raised suitably and priority is accorded to small and marginal farmers in getting irrigation.

Common property resources

A number of recent studies show that the common property resources have been dwindling steadily mainly because of encroachment by vested interests. The rural poor will benefit greatly if the encroachments are removed, they are assured of access to them and cost-effective schemes are executed for the exploitation of the common property resources.

###### Land Reform for Growth

Though the role of land reform in removing obstacles to raising agricultural production had been recognized right from the beginning, the greater emphasis was always on its role in levelling down social and economic disparities. During the last five decades we did make a good deal of noise about land reform. Some efforts were also made. But, as we have seen, the results have not been commensurate with the noise or the efforts. We have now stopped paying even lip service to the socialist or socialistic ideal. So there is no point in continuing to talk about the role of land reform in ushering in an egalitarian social order.

The emphasis should now shift to the role of land reform in fostering agricultural growth and augmenting employment

![image 485](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile485.png)

![image 486](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile486.png)

###### Conclusion 221



opportunities. We have made some concrete suggestions in this regard. The implementation of these suggestions should lead to the better utilization of our resources in land and water, the fuller absorption of human labour, increased agricultural output and a substantial improvement in the income of the rural poor. An improvement in the incomes of the rural poor is a matter of high priority not just for altruistic reasons. Increased incomes mean increased purchasing power. The resulting spurt in the demand for goods of mass consumption will foster industrial growth. And that could pave the way for the success of the new economic policy that depends on the market as the engine of economic growth. We need certain minimal measures of land reform to facilitate the growth of the Indian Economy on the capitalist path of development that we have now chosen.

Appendix II. I

##### List of certain illegal exactions in some Princely States of Rajasthan

- (1) Malba: This was usually realized from the tenants in consideration of the actual miscellaneous expenses of the Thikana officials who were required to visit the villages for collecting the Jagirdar’s share in the produce. The rate of this cess was generally one anna per Kham bigha.
- (2) Nanwa: Nanwa means the account book. A cess varying between 2 and 4 annas per Khata was realized by the Jagirdar for remunerating his Navisanda (recorder) who recorded the names of the cultivators in his account book.
- (3) Korad or Pher : The peasants had to give fodder for the maintenance of horses and other animals kept by the Jagirdar. This was realized both in cash and kind.
- (4) Bai-ji-ka-Hathkharch: This was a sort of contribution towards the marriage of the Thakur’s daughter. It was generally realized from the time a girl was bom to the time of her marriage at the rate of one anna per rupee of the commuted rent.
- (5) Mataji-ki-Bhent: It was realized at the rate of one rupee per village for making presents to the mother of the Jagirdar.
- (6) Jazam Kharch: It was realized to purchase carpets and to meet other miscellaneous contingencies of the Thikana officials at the rate of 6 pies per kham bigha.
- (7) Neota: According to local custom, neota was a voluntary contribution but the Jagirdars used to take it on a compulsory basis from their sub-grantees and tenants. The rates varied between one to two annas per Kham bigha.
- (8) Bagdam or Dhol Danka: This was realized by the Jagirdars on the occasion of any marriage celebrated by the tenant. The rates varied between Rs. 21- and Rs. 3/- per marriage.


![image 493](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile493.png)

![image 494](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile494.png)

###### Appendix 225



- (9) Dhunwa Bhachh or Nalbandi: This was nothing but a hearth or house tax and it was realized at the rate of Rs. 2J- to Rs. 5/- per house.
- (10) Begar (Forced Labour): The Jagirdar used to force free services to his household from Chamars, Malis, Barbers, Carpenters and the like for various domestic jobs.
- (11) Baithiya: The Jagirdars used to take free work of two labourers from every tenant for a day for each agricultural operation.
- (12) Hal-Bethiya: Every tenant was required to send one plough for a day to the Jagirdar’s field without any remuneration.
- (13) Khuntabandi and Pan Charai: The former was realized on pack camels while the latter was realized on sheep and goats as grazing fee. The rate of Khuntabandi varied between Rs. 2 and Rs. 3-80 per camel whereas the ‘pancharai’ was collected at the rate of two annas per head. Besides, the fee on other cattle was realized as Charai at Re. 1/- per animal.
- (14) Napa-Kori: It was realized as a fee for renovation of an old house at the rate of Rs.3/- per 20 sq. cubit.
- (15) Naharbas: Rs. 1-40 was realized per well annually from the tenant.
- (16) Kansa Parosa: The tenants were under an obligation to send at least 25 patals (plateful) to the Jagirdar’s house on the occasion of any marriage or Nukta in the former’s family.
- (17) Kunwarji-ka-kalewa: This cess was realized for meeting the pocket expenses of the sons of the Jagirdar.
- (18) Karaj Kharch: This was realized for meeting the expenditure to be incurred by the Jagirdar on the death of his relatives.
- (19) Bhagwat Fee: It was realized for recitation of Bhagwat at the rate of one rupee per village. In fact, no recitation was held.
- (20) Patda-Mekh: This cess was meant for meeting the incidental expenses on the occasion of any marriage in the Jagirdar’s family.
- (21) Hari: Green fodder was also collected from the cultivators.
- (22) Ghee and Milk: The Jagirdars used to obtain ghee and milk from the tenants during the month of October for celebrating the Shradhs of their forefathers.


![image 495](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile495.png)

![image 496](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile496.png)

226 Land Reforms in India

- (23) Dana: Additional grain was collected from the defaulting tenants for meeting the expenses of lataras (revenue collecting officials) of the Jagirdars.
- (24) Nazar: Rupee one per village was realized for making presents to the Jagirdars on the occasion of Dashera and Holi festivals.
- (25) Mohrana: This was realized from the cultivators for allowing them construction of pucca houses.
- (26) Kamdar-ki-Lag: Cess charged by Kamdar (Jagirdar’s Senior Officer) for his benefit.
- (27) Likhai-ki-Lag: Fee for writing the peasants’ rights and other details.
- (28) Kajawe-ki-Lag (Kiln Cess): Cess for manufacturing bricks for building purposes.
- (29) Sharab-Kadane-ki-Lag: A cess for distilling country liquor.


###### Some strange taxes levied in the former Bikaner State

- (1) Rupota, a tax levied on shops and on the sale of camels and certain goods in the city.
- (2) Afim-ka-Sauda, or licence tax on speculation in the prices of opium. It is levied on each speculator, and varies from Rs. 21- to Rs. 6/-.
- (3) Menh-ka-Sauda, a licence tax on speculation on the probability of rainfall.
- (4) Tankri ghi, raw sugar etc. Zarda Tamaku taxes on the weighing of these articles.
- (5) Kapra-ki-Dallali, levied on cloth merchants in the shape of Brokerage.
- (6) Sona-rupa-ki-chadami, tax on the sale of Gold and Silver.
- (7) Kanodi-ka-laga, licence tax on the making of sweetmeats for festivals.
- (8) Kiraut-lagon-ki-bach, licence tax on craftsmen, such as goldsmiths, iron-mongers, tailors, shoe makers etc.
- (9) Khola, or adoption fee, is paid by the person adopting a son and is generally regulated according to his means but supposed to be limited to Rs. 1,000/- (Exactions by Golas - Rs. 25,000 in the case of one Seth in Sam vat year 1925).


![image 497](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile497.png)

![image 498](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile498.png)

###### Appendix 227



- (10) Chauth Zamin, on sale of private land and buildings, 1/4 was taken up by Raj.
- (11) Gaimal or proceeds of unclaimed property went to Raj.
- (12) Dhuan, house tax for each family.
- (13) Kurar Jhunkera, tax for fodder for each family.
- (14) Neota Bach, tax on presents on occasions of marriage, at Rs. 7 and 7 takas.
- (15) Talibab, cess levied from non-agriculturist classes, at Rs. 2/- on each family and Rs. 4/- per camel.
- (16) Singhoti, tax on sheep and goats, at the rate of Re. 1/- for every 14 heads.
- (17) Chaudrbab at Rs. 11/- from each village chaudhri.
- (18) Kot and Khari Bach, tax occasionally levied for the repairs of the Bikaner Fort.
- (19) Reshkash, Succession fee on Pattas, which is generally equal to the yearly “rakm” payable by the Pattas, but sometimes very much more (Important source of income of the State).
- (20) Nazrana levied on the occasion of change of pattas and often arbitrarily when the chief wants money.
- (21) Rajtilak, a present made to the chief, on the occasion of accession to the ‘Gadi’. It consists of horses, elephants, gold mohurs, etc.
- (22) Solselari, tax levied from Dheds at Rs. 2/- or Rs. 2 1/2 per family.


[Several other taxes or levies of a peculiar nature might be mentioned ] 1

Appendix II. 2

##### Rates of compensation and réhabilitation grants payable in some States

###### Sl.No State Rates 1 Andhra Pradesh

If the basic annual sum is below Rs 1000 compensation is 30 times the annual sum. There is a decreasing rate for higher slabs, and where the basic annual sum exceeds Rs 100.000 compensation is 12^ times the basic annual sum. [The annual sum is about onethird of the Raiyatwari assessment.] Where the net income does not exceed Rs 1000, compensation is 15 times the net income. There is a decreasing rate for higher slabs and where the net income exceeds Rs 300.000 compensation is twice the annual income. If the net income is below Rs 500 compensation is 20 times the net income. There is a decreasing rate for higher slabs. Where the net income exceeds Rs 100,000 compensation is three times the net income. Rates of compensation vary according to the nature of the tenure. In some cases compensation is fixed under the Land Acquisition Act, while in some other cases it is three times the net income and in a few cases it is ten times the land revenue.

###### Appendix 229



- 5 Madhya Pradesh
- 6 Madras
- 7 Orissa
- 8 Rajasthan
- 9 Uttar Pradesh
- 10 West Bengal


Compensation is ten times the net income. In addition Rehabilitation grant is payable at different rates to small and medium intermediaries. As in Andhra Pradesh. Where the net income does not exceed Rs 500, compensation is 15 times the net income. There is a decreasing rate for higher slabs, and where the net income exceeds Rs 15,000, compensation is 3 times the net income. Compensation is at the rate of seven times the net income. In addition rehabilitation grant is payable at different rates. Where the gross income is less than Rs 250 the rehabilitation grant is 11 times the net income and the rate declines with rise in gross income. Where the gross income exceeds Rs 30,000 the rehabilitation grant is twice the net income. Compensation payable is 8 times the net assets. In addition rehabilitation grant is payable at varying rates. Where the land revenue of the estate is less than Rs 25, the grant is 20 times the net assets. The rate decreases with rise in the land revenue payable. Where the land revenue is between Rs 5,000 and Rs 10,000 the grant is equal to the land revenue, and no grant is payable if the land revenue exceeds Rs 10,000. On the first Rs 500 of net income the compensation is 20 times the net income. The rate decreases with rise in net income. Where the net income exceeds Rs. 109,000 the compensation payable for the excess amount is twice that amount.

Appendix III. 1

##### Provisions in the laws of the various States regarding the landlord’s right to resume tenanted land

###### Andhra Area of Andhra Pradesh

A landowner owning less than two-thirds of the ceiling area under his personal cultivation is entitled to resume tenanted land not exceeding one-half of the area held by a tenant provided that after resumption the total extent of land held by the landowner under his personal cultivation does not exceed two-thirds of the ceiling area. Land can be resumed from all categories of tenants, but the right of resumption is to be exercised within six months of the appointed date. In the case of defence personnel and disabled landowners, the time limit for resumption will expire after six months from the date of discharge from service or cessation of disability. Where the landowner does not bring the land under personal cultivation within a period of one year from the date of resumption or if he ceases to cultivate it personally for a continuous period of one year within a period of six years, the dispossessed tenant will, on application, be restored to possession.

###### Telengana Area of Andhra Pradesh

A landholder was entitled to resume from protected tenants (tenants with 6 years’ possession on specified dates or tenants whose landlords held more than 3 family holdings) lands up to 3 family holdings. He was not, however, entitled to resume more than a family holding unless the income by the cultivation of such land was the main source of his income. A family holding varied between 4 to 60 acres. The resumption was further subject to the condition that a protected

![image 505](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile505.png)

![image 506](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile506.png)

###### Appendix 231



tenant would retain an area equal to a basic holding (i.e. one-third of the family holding) or half his land whichever is less. An owner holding a basic holding or less was, however, entitled to resume the entire area.

A landlord was to reserve the land he wanted to resume for personal cultivation before 12 September, 1957 and apply for resumption before 4 February, 1959. Thus, now all the lands held by protected tenants are non-resumable. Defence personnel can resume within one year of termination of service and disabled persons within one year of cessation of disability.

If a landlord, after terminating the tenancy did not cultivate the land personally within one year of resumption or after having commenced such cultivation discontinued the same within ten years, the tenants were entitled to restoration.

The above provisions do not apply to ordinary tenants. Tenants admitted within three years of the commencement of the Hyderabad Tenancy Act, 1950 were given a no-renewable term of ten years. Tenants admitted after three years have a renewable term of five years unless the landlord requires the land for personal cultivation at the end of a term.

###### Assam

Intermediaries and occupancy raiyats can resume land without any limit from under-raiyats who have not acquired rights of occupancy subject to the condition that 3-1/3 acres of land is left with each underraiyat until he has been allotted alternative land of equivalent value in the locality. The defence personnel who resume tenanted land are not required to leave any minimum area with the under-tenants. The right of resumption is continuing. If the resumed land is not brought under personal cultivation within one year of resumption or the landowner sublets it, the under-raiyat is entitled to restoration of possession.

###### Bihar

Under the Bihar Tenancy Act 1885, the right of occupancy accrucs to under-raiyats after twelve years of continuous possession. Nonoccupancy under-raiyats holding land on written leases are tenants-atwill and are liable to ejectment on the expiry of the term of lease, while

![image 507](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile507.png)

![image 508](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile508.png)

###### 232 Land Reforms in India



those holding land on oral leases are not liable to ejectment except on grounds of non-payment of rent or improper use of land. As most of the leases are oral and informal, all under-raiyats do not, in practice enjoy any security of tenure.

The Bihar Ceiling Act allows the landowners who hold land in excess of the ceiling area to resume tenanted land up to the ceiling limit. The landowner is allowed to resume all land held by the underraiyats in excess of five acres and if the under-raiyat holds less than ten acres, the landowner is entitled to resume half the area subject, however, to the condition that a minimum area of one acre will be left with the under-raiyat including the area owned by him, if any. As regards under-raiyats holding land from persons who are not subject to ceiling, the provisions of the Bihar Tenancy Act of 1885 are applicable. The Act was amended in 1970 to provide that under-tenants of landowners who own less than five acres of irrigated or less than ten acres of unirrigated land would not acquire occupancy rights in the lands they cultivate. Thus, small landowners owning less than ten acres of unirrigated land or five acres of irrigated land enjoy a continuing right of resumption.

###### Gujarat

In the former Bombay area of Gujarat permanent tenants got complete security of tenure. Landowners were permitted to resume half of the tenanted land from all tenants other than permanent tenants. On resuming the tenanted land, landowners were required to leave a minimum of half the leased area with the tenant. The right of resumption has ended. Defence personnel and disabled landowners, however, have a continuing right of resumption. In the case of defence personnel, the application for resumption is to be made within two years of discharge from service and in the case of disabled landowners within one year of the cessation of disability. Defence personnel are not required to leave half the leased land with tenants.

In the Kutch area of Gujarat there is a further provision that a small holder can resume the entire tenanted land without leaving any minimum area with the tenants.

In the Saurashtra area sub-leasing of agricultural land was prohibited in 1953. The existing tenants were given no rights.

![image 509](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile509.png)

![image 510](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile510.png)

###### Appendix 233



###### Himachal Pradesh

Under the Himachal Pradesh Big Landed Estates and Land Reforms Act, 1953, security of tenure was conferred on all categories of tenants and sub-tenants. A limited right of resumption was allowed to landowners subject to the stipulation that no tenant could be evicted from more than one-fourth of the area leased to him. After the merger of some former Punjab areas in Himachal Pradesh, the Himachal Pradesh Tenancy and Land Reforms Act, 1972, was enacted. It is applicable to the entire State. According to the provisions of that Act a landowner having under his personal cultivation an area of land not exceeding 1\ acres of irrigated or 3 acres of unirrigated land can resume land up to half the area leased to a tenant provided that the total area under his personal cultivation after resumption will not exceed 1\ acres of irrigated or 3 acres of unirrigated land.

In the case of defence personnel the limit of resumption is 5 acres while for disabled landowners it is the same as for others.

The most progressive feature of the Himachal law is that the tenant acquires ownership rights in the first 1\ acres of irrigated or 3 acres of unirrigated land without being required to file any petition or make any payment.

If the resumed land is not brought under personal cultivation within one year after taking possession, it will vest in the State Government and will be disposed of in the prescribed manner, preference being given to the tenant from whom the land was resumed.

###### Jammu & Kashmir

Under the Kashmir Agrarian Reforms Act, 1972, a landowner whose monthly income, including that of the other members of his family, does not exceed Rs. 500 and who was on 1 September, 1971 an inhabitant of Jammu & Kashmir may resume for personal cultivation land not exceeding 3 standard acres provided that the total extent of land held by him after resumption, including orchards, does not exceed 3 standard acres. No land can be resumed from occupancy tenants. A landowner can resume only land leased out to non-occupancy tenants and even in such cases a minimum of 2 standard acres must be left with the tenant. A person serving in the armed forces on any date between

![image 511](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile511.png)

![image 512](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile512.png)

###### 234 Land Reforms in India



1 April, 1965 and 1 September, 1971, or on his death in military operation during this period his widow and/or dependents can resume land up to the ceiling area, including any other land already held. If the resumed land is not brought under personal cultivation within 8 months of entry into possession it will vest in the State and will be disposed of in the prescribed manner.

The right of resumption was to be exercised within 180 days after the appointed date under the Jammu & Kashmir Agrarian Reforms Act, 1972.

###### Karnataka

Landholders other than companies, religious and charitable institutions, whose income by the cultivation of land is the principal source for their maintenance and who had not exercised a right of resumption under the earlier law were allowed to resume land up to 3 family holdings or half the leased area, whichever is less. Landowners were not, however, allowed to resume any land from permanent tenants. They could resume only land leased out to protected tenants or ordinary tenants. On resumption, protected tenants were to be left with one basic holding or one-half of the leased land, whichever was more, and the ordinary tenants were to be left with one-half of the leased land. The right of resumption has since expired. The Karnataka Land Reforms Act was comprehensively amended in 1973. Under the 1973 Act the resumption of land comprised in a tenancy was barred except by soldiers and seamen who must give notice of resumption within one year of release from service. The resumed land is not transferable within fifteen years of restoration. Furthermore, the resumed land will vest in the State if it is not cultivated personally or is put to nonagricultural use.

###### Kerala

A small holder i.e. a landlord who did not have interest in land exceeding eight standard acres (or ten ordinary acres in extent) was allowed to resume half the area leased to a tenant subject to a maximum of 2 standard acres. No resumption was, however, permitted from tenants who had already acquired fixity of tenure immediately before 21st January, 1961 under any law then in force. Where the

![image 513](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile513.png)

![image 514](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile514.png)

###### Appendix 235



tenants held land exceeding the ceiling area, the small landholder could resume the excess land up to five standard acres including any other land already in his possession. No resumption could, however, be made from tenants belonging to Schedule Castes and Scheduled Tribes or homestead tenants. If the resumed land was not brought under personal cultivation within three years of resumption, the evicted tenant could apply for restoration. The right of resumption has ended in all cases.

###### Madhya Pradesh

A landholder could resume land from his tenants on ground of personal cultivation subject to the condition that (i) the total area of the land which the landholder could resume, including the land already under his personal cultivation, did not exceed 25 acres of unirrigated land or equivalent area; and (ii) the tenant was left with 10 acres of unirrigated land if the land had been under the possession of the tenant for a period of less than five years and 25 acres of unirrigated land if it had been in his possession for a period of more than five years (1 acre of irrigated land was treated as equal to 2 acres of unirrigated land). The right of resumption expired on 1 October, 1960. If the landlord failed to cultivate personally the resumed land during the agricultural year next following the date of resumption, the tenant was entitled to restoration.

###### Maharashtra

Bombay Area: The provisions are similar to those in the former Bombay area of Gujarat.

Vidarbha Region: All landowners are permitted to resume land up to three family holdings (i.e. from 21 to 120 acres) subject to the following conditions:

- (a) Where the total holding of a landlord was equal to or less than one-third of a family holding the landlord could resume for personal cultivation the entire land leased by him:
- (b) Where the total holding of a landlord exceeded one-third of a family holding but did no exceed a family holding, the landlord could resume one-third of a family holding or half of the land leased by him, whichever was more;


![image 515](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile515.png)

![image 516](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile516.png)

###### 236 Land Reforms in India



- (c) Where the total holding of a landlord exceeded a family holding, he could resume half the area leased by him;
- (d) The income from the cultivation of the land of which he was entitled to take possession was the principal source of income for his maintenance. This condition did not, however, apply to a landlord whose total holding did not exceed a family holding and whose principal occupation was agriculture;
- (e) A landholder holding land up to a family holding could resume the entire holding if the tenancy was created on or after 1 April, 1957 and was terminable but for the Vidarbha Ordinance of 1957.


[The right of resumption has ended in all cases.] Defence personnel are allowed to resume tenanted land within two yean of discharge from service and upon such resumption no minimum area is to be left with tenants. Landowners suffering from a specified disability are permitted to apply for resumption within one year of the cessation of disability.

If the landowner failed to cultivate the resumed land within one year of resumption or ceased to cultivate it during the next 12 years, it would be restored to the. evicted tenant.

Marathwada Area: The provisions applicable to Telengana area were in force in the Marathwada area with some minor modifications inasmuch as the provisions relating to protected tenants have been extended to ordinary tenants also.

###### Manipur

Tenants including share-croppers have security of tenure subject to the owner’s right to resume land for personal cultivation. The provisions for resumption follow the recommendations in the Second Five Year Plan. The limit on resumption is 25 acres i.e. the ceiling area. A person who owns a basic holding or less can resume the entire area owned by him. A person who owns more than a basic holding but not exceeding a family holding, may resume one-half of the leased area or an area as will make up a basic holding under his personal cultivation, whichever is greater. A person who owns more than a family holding and is cultivating less than a family holding, may resume half the area leased to a tenant so as to make up a family holding under his personal

![image 517](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile517.png)

![image 518](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile518.png)

###### Appendix 237



cultivation; provided the tenant is left with no less than a basic holding. A landowner will, however, have a prior right to make up at least a basic holding. If the landowner is cultivating more than a family holding, he can resume only such land as is held by a tenant in excess of a family holding. A basic holding is equal to 2.5 acres and a family holding, 7.5 acres.

In determining whether a person holds a basic holding or a family holding or more, any transfer made after 6 March, 1956 will be disregarded.

A further provision has been made that no tenant shall be evicted from the minimum area of 1.25 acres unless alternative land has been provided to him.

A landlord is required to apply for reservation of land for personal cultivation within one year of the commencement of the Act and to apply for the possession of the resumable land within five years thereof. If he fails to personally cultivate the resumed land within one year of its resumption or ceases to cultivate it during the next four years, the tenant will be entitled to restoration. The right to resume tenanted land was terminated in 1975.

###### Orissa

Landowners were allowed to resume land up to the ceiling limit but half the tenanted land was to be left with each tenant. The application for resumption was to be submitted within three months of the commencement of the Act. Defence personnel and disabled landowners, however, enjoy a continuing right of resumption.

###### Punjab and Haryana

Landowners owning land up to the ceiling limit enjoy a continuing right of resuming land up to the ceiling area. A tenant is, however, entitled to continue holding land up to 5 standard acres till alternative land is allotted to him by Government. There is no special provision for defence personnel or disabled landowners. Tenants of defence personnel are, however, given priority in the matter of allotment of Government land so that defence personnel would be able to resume all their land expeditiously.

![image 519](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile519.png)

![image 520](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile520.png)

###### 238 Land Reforms in India



###### Rajasthan

Landowners were allowed to resume land for personal cultivation up to the ceiling limit from tenants of Khudkast and sub-tenants provided that the tenant was left with a minimum area which could provide him a net income of Rs. 1200 per year (in terms of acreage the minimum area varied from 15.6 acres to 125 acres). The right of resumption expired in 1958. There are no special provisions regarding defence personnel, disabled landowners or small holders.

###### Tamil Nadu

Landowners who own less than 13 J acres of wet land or 40 acres of dry land and are not assessed to sales tax, professional tax or income tax were allowed to resume tenanted land. They were allowed to resume half the leased land subject to a maximum of 5 acres of wet land or 15 acres of dry land. Defence personnel could resume the entire land they had leased out at the time of joining armed forces. The landlord’s right of resumption has ended with effect from 5 July, 1973.

###### Tripura

The provisions in Tripura follow the provisions in the Manipur law except that there is no provision for a minimum area of 1.25 acres being left with the tenant. A basic holding in Tripura is equal to 2 standard acres. In determining whether a person owns a basic holding or a family holding, the date from which the transfers are to be disregarded is 10 August, 1957.

###### Uttar Pradesh

In Uttar Pradesh all tenants and sub-tenants were brought into direct relationship with the State. Resumption of tenanted land by landowners on grounds of personal cultivation was not permitted. Leases in future are not permitted except by defence personnel or landowners suffering from a specified disability. It has, however, been stipulated in the law that “any arrangement whereby a person is entitled to a right merely to a share in the produce grown on the land in consideration of such person assisting or participating with tenure holder in the actual performance of agricultural operations is not a

![image 521](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile521.png)

![image 522](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile522.png)

###### Appendix 239



‘lease*. Thus, share-croppers holding land as ‘Sajhis’ are not treated as tenants. Therefore, all landowners in effect enjoy a continuing right of resumption in respect of land cultivated through Sajhis.

###### West Bengal

As in Uttar Pradesh, in West Bengal also on the abolition of intermediary interests, all raiyats and under-raiyats were brought into direct relationship with the State. But share-croppers (bargadars) were not treated as under-raiyats. A landowner who owns less than 7 \ acres of land enjoys a continuing right of resumption in respect of land leased out to bargadars. When a landowner resumes tenanted land from a bargadar a minimum area of one hectare is to be left with the bargadar. There is no special provision regarding defence personnel or disabled landowners. The amendment of 1977 stipulates that on resumption by the landowner, the bargadar shall be left with an aggregate area of not less than one hectare.

![image 523](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile523.png)

![image 524](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile524.png)

Appendix I1I.2

##### Definition of personal cultivation in the various State laws

###### Andhra Pradesh

Andhra Area

'Personal cultivation’ means cultivation of land by a person on his own account:

- (i) by his own labour or by the labour of any member of his family; or
- (ii) by servants on wages payable in cash or in kind or both but not in crop share, or by hired labour, under his personal supervision or under the personal supervision of any member of his family. (Sec. 2(gg) Andhra Pradesh (Andhra Area) Tenancy Act, 1956).


Telengana Area To cultivate personally' means to cultivate on one's own account:

- (i) by one’s own labour, or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or
- (iii) by servants on wages payable in cash or kind, but not in crop share, or by hired labour, under one’s personal supervision or the personal supervision of any member of one’s family.


Explanation:- In the case of an undivided Hindu family, land shall be deemed to be cultivated personally, If it is cultivated by any member of such family.

(Sec. 2(g) Hyderabad Tenancy and Agricultural Lands Act, 1950).

###### Assam

“Personal cultivation” means cultivation by the person himself, or

![image 525](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile525.png)

![image 526](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile526.png)

###### Appendix 241



by a member of his family or by hired labourers on fixed remuneration payable in cash or kind but not in crop share, under personal supervision of the person himself or any member of his family, provided it is accompanied by the bearing of risks of cultivation by the owner and by residence in the village in which the land is situated or nearby village or town within a distance of 5 miles during the greater part of the agricultural season.

Provided that in the case of a person who is a widow or a minor, or is subject to any physical or mental disability or is a member of the defence forces of the Indian Union or is a student below the age of 21 years of an educational institution recognized by the State Government, the land shall be deemed to be under personal cultivation even in the absence of such personal supervision. (Sec.3(10) of Assam Temporarily Settled Areas) Tenancy Act, 1971).

###### Bihar

“Personal cultivation” with its grammatical variations means cultivation by the raiyat himself or by members of his family or by servants or hired labourers on fixed wages payable in cash or kind but not in crop share under personal supervision of any member of his family during main agricultural operations.

(Sec.2(i) of Bihar Land Reforms (Fixation of Ceiling Area and Acquisition of Surplus Land Act, 1961).

Gujarat

Former Bombay Area

‘To cultivate personally” means to cultivate land on one’s own account:

- (i) by one’s own labour, or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or
- (iii) under personal supervision of oneself or any member of one’s family by hired labour or by servants on wages payable in cash or kind, but not in crop share, being land, the entire area of which: (a) is situated within the limits of a single village, or


![image 527](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile527.png)

![image 528](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile528.png)

###### 242 Land Reforms in India



- (b) is so situated that no piece of land is separated from any other by a distance of more than five miles; or
- (c) forms one compact block. Explanation I. A widow or a minor or a person who is subject to


mental or physical disability, or a serving member of the armed forces shall be deemed to cultivate land personally if such land is cultivated by servants, or by hired labourers or through tenants.

Explanation n. In the case of a joint family, the land shall be deemed to have been cultivated personally, if it is cultivated by any member of such family; and in the case of a family other than a joint family, a person, other than the husband, or as the case may be, other than the wife of the person concerned or any of his lineal descendants dependent on him, shall not be deemed to be a member of the family.

Explanation III. The expression “personal supervision” means giving from time to time instructions or directions to the labourers or servants in regard to the cultivation of land, and exercising control in respect thereof, during the entire process of cultivation, or according to the circumstances, during a substantial part of the entire process of cultivation by the person concerned residing during the major part of the agricultural season in the village in which the land is situated or at a place in another village situated at a distance not exceeding 15 km. from the land.

Provided that, for the purpose of this explanation, it shall not be necessary for a person to reside in such village or place if a certificate is granted by the Collector to such person that owing to the smallness of his holding, limited income from agriculture or any other reason as may be prescribed it is not possible for him to so reside in such village or place, without detriment to his means of livelihood, and such certificate is in force. (Sec.2(5) of the Bombay Tenancy and Agricultural Lands Act 1949— as extended to Bombay area of Gujarat).

###### Saurashtra Area

“Cultivate personally” or any cognate expression means to cultivate on one’s own account:

- (i) by one’s own labour; or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or


![image 529](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile529.png)

![image 530](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile530.png)

###### Appendix 243



- (iii) by servants on wages payable in cash or kind, but not in a share of the crops or by hired labour under one’s personal supervision or supervision of any member of one’s family.


(The Saurashtra Prohibition of Leases of Agricultural Lands Act, 1953).

###### Kutch Area

‘To cultivate personally” means to cultivate on one’s own account:

- (i) by one’s own labour; or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or under the personal supervision of oneself or of any member of one’s family by hired labour or by servants on wages payable in cash or kind, but not in crop share.


Explanation I. A widow or a minor, or a person who is subject to mental or physical disability or a serving member of the armed forces shall be deemed to cultivate land personally if it is cultivated by his or her servants, or by hired labour;

Explanation n. In the case of a joint family, the land shall be deemed to have been cultivated personally if it is cultivated by any member of such family.

(The Bombay Tenancy and Agricultural Lands (Vidarbha Region and Kutch Area) Act, 1958.)

Haryana

Former Punjab Area

‘Self cultivation’ means cultivation by a landowner either personally or through his wife or children, or through his brothers or collateral in the first degree or uncles and nephews whether maternal or paternal or under his supervision.

(The Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act, 1953 and Rules framed thereunder).

###### Pepsu Area

The expression ‘to cultivate personally* with its grammatical variations and cognate expressions means to cultivate on one’s own account:

![image 531](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile531.png)

- (iii) by servants or hired labour. (The Pepsu Tenancy and Agricultural Lands Act, 1955 and Rules


framed thereunder).

###### Himachal Pradesh

To cultivate personally means to cultivate:

- (i) on one’s own account;
- (ii) by one’s own labour;
- (iii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or
- (iv) under personal supervision of oneself or any member of one’s family or by servants on wages payable in cash.


- Explanation I. A widow or a minor, or any unmarried girl or a person who is subject to mental or physical disability or a serving member of the armed forces shall be deemed to cultivate personally if such land is cultivated through tenants.
- Explanation II. In the case of a joint family, the land shall be deemed to have been cultivated personally if such land is cultivated by any member of such family.


(Sec.2(4) of Himachal Pradesh Tenancy and Land Reforms Act, 1972.)

###### Jammu & Kashmir

“Personal cultivation by a person means cultivation -

- (a) by the person himself; or
- (b) by any member of his family; or
- (c) by a khana-nishin daughter, or khana-damad or parent, or a major

- (i) son;
- (ii) adopted son;
- (iii) pisarparmardah; or
- (iv) unmarried adopted daughter, of the person, or

Provided that such servant or hired labourer or agent or guardian does not bear the cost or risk of cultivation nor receives wages or remuneration as a share of crop.

Explanation. Personal cultivation shall not cease to be so merely because of engagement of hired labour to help in agricultural operations provided that the labour is paid wages in cash or kind but not in crop share. (Sec.2(7) of the Jammu & Kashmir Agrarian Reforms Act, 1972).

###### Karnataka

To cultivate personally means to cultivate land on one’s own account -

- (i) by one’s own labour; or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of one’s family; or
- (iii) by hired labour or by servants on wages payable in cash or kind, but not in crop share, under the personal supervision of oneself or by a member of one’s family.


- Explanation I. In the case of an educational religious or charitable institution or society or trust, of a public nature capable of holding property, formed for an educational religious or charitable purpose, the land shall be deemed to be cultivated personally, if such land is cultivated by hired labour or by servants under the personal supervision of an employee or agent of such institution or society or trust.
- Explanation II. In the case of a joint family, the land shall be deemed to be cultivated personally, if it is cultivated by any member of such family.


(The Mysore Land Reforms Act, 1961). Kerala

(The Kerala Land Reforms Act, 1953).

###### Madhya Pradesh

“To cultivate personally” means to cultivate land on one’s own account:

###### Manipur

Personal cultivation means cultivation by a person on his own account:

- (i) by his own labour; or
- (ii) by the labour of any member of his family; or
- (iii) by servants or by hired labour on wages payable in cash or in kind, but not as a share of the produce under his personal supervision or under the personal supervision of any member of his family.


Explanation I. Land shall not be deemed to be cultivated under the personal supervision of a person unless such person or member resides in the village in which the land is situated or in a nearby village within a distance to be prescribed, during the major part of the agricultural season.

(Sec.2(r) and 120 of Manipur land Revenue and Land Reforms Act, 1960).

Appendix ¡113

##### Provision in the State law« regarding surrender of tenancy rights

###### Andhra Pradesh (Tetansapa area)

A tenant may surrender his rights in land provided that such surrender is made in writing and is admitted by him before and to the satisfaction of the Tahsildar. There is no restriction on the extent of surrendered land which the landlord may occupy. The law is silent as to what will happen if the tenant does not report to the Tahsildar his intention to surrender.

Andhra area of Andhra Pradesh

A cultivating tenant may terminate his tenancy and surrender his holding 91 the end of any agricultural year after giving to the landlord and the Special Officer at least three months notice expiring with the end of such agricultural year. The surrender shall be effective only if it is accepted by the Special Officer that it is voluntary and genuine.

(Sec. 14 Andhra Pradesh Andhra area) Tenancy Act, 1956).

###### Assam

No surrender shall be valid: (i) if it is not approved by the Deputy Commissioner; (ii) if the tenant does not give at least three months notice in writing to the landlord; and

(iii) if it is done without the consent and approval of the encumbrance or under-tenant or tenant when there is an encumbrance or an under-tenant or tenants as the case may be:

Provided that the landlord shall not be eligible to resume the land for personal cultivation. The Deputy Commissioner may, when he

![image 545](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile545.png)

![image 546](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile546.png)

###### Appendix 251

permits a surrender place a landless agriculturist in the holding who shall, thereafter, become a non-occupancy tenant of the landlord (sec.63. The Assam Temporarily settled Areas Tenancy Act, 1971).

###### ft&ar

In Bihar, if any sub-lessee wishes to surrender the land, he has to submit an application to the collector who shall, if satisfied about its voluntariness, accord permission thereto and register the same. The collector shall then, in consultation with the landlord sublet the land on behalf of the landlord for the remainder of the term of the original lease. The provision for consultation with the landlord practically amounts to giving the landlord the right to change his tenants. Further, the applicability of this provision is restricted to sub-lessees admitted after the commencement of the Ceiling Act (19th April, 1962) and the landlord can take possession of the entire land. In the case of tenants holding land from before the commencement of ceiling legislation, surrenders are not regulated even to this limited extent. Further, if the sub-lessee does not report to the collector his intention to surrender, the latter has no jurisdiction. There is no provision for suo moto action by the collector.

###### Giyarat

No tenant can surrender his right in land in favour of the landlord. The intending tenant has to intimate his intention to surrender to the landlord and the collector. The land is to vest in the State and is to be settled with another person entitled to allotment of land as a tenant The landlord gets compensation for the land equal to the purchase price payable by the tenant. There is no provision for suo moto action by the revenue authorities nor is there any penalty provided for nonobservance of the provision relating to surrenders.

###### Himachal Pradesh

No tenant can relinquish his tenancy in favour of a landowner. Where a tenant wants to surrender his tenancy land, the same shall be in favour of the State Government who shall have the right to induct any suitable tenant or landless agricultural labourer in the prescribed manner.

![image 547](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile547.png)

![image 548](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile548.png)

252 Land Reforms in India

###### Jammu & Kashmir

A tenant may surrender his tenancy by giving oral or written notice to the landlord. He may instead of or in addition to giving notice to the landlord apply to a Revenue Officer for causing the notice to be served on the landlord informing him of his intention to surrender the land. The landowner will be entitled to take possession of the entire surrendered area. Where a tenant was unlawfully dispossessed after 9 December, 1955 he could apply for his restoration within six months of his unlawful dispossession. If on enquiry the Revenue Officer finds that the tenant was illegally dispossessed, he may restore him to possession. During the course of enquiry if the Revenue Officer finds that the surrender was not made in writing and was not certified by a Revenue Officer, the defence of the landlord that the possession of the land was voluntarily surrendered by the tenant shall not be entertained. There is no provision for suo moto action by the Revenue authorities.

###### Karnataka

Letting out of land is permitted only in case of landowners who are soldiers or seamen. A tenant of such a landowner cannot surrender land except in favour of the State Government: Provided that any such surrender shall not be effective unless made in writing and the tenant has admitted the same before the Tahsildar and the same has been registered in the office of the Tahsildar. The State Government is authorized to lease the surrendered land to any person if possession thereof is not claimed by the soldier or seaman for personal cultivation. The new lessee will pay rent directly to the landlord.

###### Kerala

A tenant can surrender his rights only in favour of the Government. Even such, a surrender has to be in writing and to be admitted by the tenant before the land tribunal and it is to be registered in the office of the land tribunal. The Government is to pay fair rent to the landlord till a new tenant is settled on the land. The surrendered land is to be let out by the Government to another person entitled to distribution of land, who will pay fair rent to the landlord directly. If a landlord enters into possession of any land surrendered by the tenant, he shall be punishable with imprisonment which may extend to one year or with fine which may extend to Rs. 2000 or with both.

![image 549](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile549.png)

![image 550](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile550.png)

Appendix 253

###### Madhya Pradesh

No surrender by occupancy tenant is to be valid unless it is effected under a registered deed. The bhumiswami will be entitled to take possession of the surrendered land only to the extent of his right of resumption and the excess land is to vest in the State for which the bhumiswami will be paid compensation. No penalty has been provided in case of non-observance of the conditions for surrender, nor is there any provision for suo moto action by the revenue authorities for restoration of the tenant in case of an invalid surrender.

###### Maharashtra

A tenant may surrender his rights in land provided the surrender is made in writing and admitted before the Mamlatdar. The landlord can take possession of the entire surrendered land subject only to the ceiling limit. (No penalty has been provided for in case of nonobservance of the conditions for surrender, nor is there any provision for suo motu action by the revenue authorities for restoration of the tenant in case of an invalid surrender).

###### Manipur

No tenant can surrender his land and no landowner can enter upon the surrendered land without the previous permission in writing of the competent authority. Such permission will be granted if the competent authority is satisfied that the surrender is bona fide and does not exceed in extent the resumable limit of the landowner. Where the permission to surrender is refused and the tenant gives a declaration in writing relinquishing his rights in land to the competent authority, the latter has no jurisdiction as there is no provision for suo moto action by the revenue authorities for restoration of the tenant in case of an invalid surrender.

###### Orissa

No surrender to the landlord or abandonment of any holding or any part thereof by a raiyat or a tenant shall be valid unless such surrender or abandonment has been previously approved by the Revenue Officer.

Any raiyat or tenant desiring to surrender or abandon his holding or any part thereof may furnish information thereof in writing to the

![image 551](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile551.png)

![image 552](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile552.png)

###### 254 Land Rtforms in India

Revenue Officer, who may, after holding such enquiry as may be prescribed, either approve or disapprove the proposed surrender or abandonment.

Where the surrender or abandonment of any holding or pan thereof is approved by the Revenue Officer, the holding or part thereof shall be settled by the Government with any other person in accordance with specified priorities.

Where any raiyat or tenant surrenders or abandons his holding or part thereof without the previous approval of the Revenue Officer and the landlord enters into possession, the latter shall be liable to pay a penalty of an amount not exceeding Rs. 200 per acre of the land so surrendered or abandoned for each year or any part thereof during which the possession is continued. (Sec. 22A. Orissa Land Reforms Act).

###### Rajasthan

A tenant can surrender his tenancy by giving a registered notice to the landowner. The surrender is to be accompanied with a writing attested by the Tahsildar or the Chairman of a Municipal Board.

###### Tripura

Same as in Manipur.

###### West Bengal

When a bargardar surrenders his right in land, the landowner may give information in writing of such fact to the prescribed authority, th e prescribed authority will give notice to the bargardar and after hearing both the parties determine whether the surrender was bona fide. Where the surrender was not bona fide the prescribed authority may restore the bargardar to cultivation. Even where the surrender was bona fide the landowner cannot bring the surrendered land under personal cultivation, but he may with the permission of the prescribed authority get the land cultivated by another bargardar entitled to distribution of land under the Land Reforms Act. Where a person resumes cultivation of surrendered land, he is liable to be punished with imprisonment up to six months or with a fine up to Rs. 1000 or with both.

Haryana, Punjab, Tamil Nadu and Uttar Pradesh Surrenders are not regulated in these States.

![image 553](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile553.png)

![image 554](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile554.png)

##### Provisions In the tenancy laws of various States for conferment of ownership rights on tenants

Àndhtn Pnutaà (îeleh#âna Aft*)

They are given the right W puítháse ownership Hghts, so however that the landlord is left with at least two family holdings ahd the land in the ownership of the protected tehaht does not exceed one family holding inclusive of the land he tnaÿ àlready hâve iri his ownership, trt areas to be notified by the Government, ownership rights are automatically conferred on the protected teñáhts ih respect of the land they áre entitled to purchase, th e purchase price payable by the tenant is fixed at 12 times the land revenue and it is payable ih lump sum or ih 16 iristalments.
