Hie cultivât!tig tenant has only the first option to purchase the tenanted land When the landowner intends to sell it There is no provision for conferment of ownership rights on tenants.

A sbñiti

All occupancy tenants, hoh-occupancy tenants and Under-tenants can acquire ownership rights in respect of noh-resumable área held by them under their personal cultivation. Land owned by persons suffering from specified disabilities and persons in the defence services are excepted. The law provides for acquisition of ownership at the initiative of the tenant. Alternatively* at any time ftom the commencement Of thé Act the State may notify such transfer of ownership rights at its own initiative subject to the conditions as laid

![image 555](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile555.png)

![image 556](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile556.png)

256 Land Reforms in India

down in the law for purchase by the tenant. The purchase price is fixed at 50 times the land revenue and is payable in 5 instalments.

###### Bihar

Under-raiyats of raiyats holding land in excess of the ceiling area can acquire occupancy rights in respect of non-resumable land. Such a right is in no way different from the right of a tenant to be allotted that part of a landowner’s surplus land which has been under the tenant’s cultivating possession. In reality, the great majority of tenants who are mostly share-croppers do not enjoy even security of tenure, and the question of their acquiring ownership rights does not arise at all.

###### Gujarat (Bombay area) and Maharashtra (Bombay area)

All permanent and non-permanent tenants who cultivated land personally were deemed to have ownership rights with effect from 1 April, 1957 in respect of non-resumable area. The purchase price is fixed at 20 to 200 times the assessment and can be paid in lump sum or in 16 instalments. Certificate of purchase is given after the whole amount is paid. Certain conditions are specified in the law that may render infructuous the purchase deemed to have taken place on 1 April, 1957.

###### Gujarat (Kutch area) and Maharashtra (Vidarbha area)

In the Kutch area of Gujarat and the Vidarbha area of Maharashtra, the provisions are similar to those in Bombay area except that they became effective from 1 April, 1960.

###### Gujarat (Saurashtra Area)

In the Saurashtra area of Gujarat, the tenants are entitled to acquire occupancy rights on payment of a price subject to the Girasdar’s right to allotment of certain specified areas of lands. There is, however, no provision for conferment of ownership rights on tenants.

###### Maharashtra (Marathwada Region)

In the Marathwada region of maharashtra, the provisions are similar to those for the Andhra Pradesh (Telangana area). The purchase is deemed to have become effective from 1 April, 1958.

![image 557](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile557.png)

![image 558](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile558.png)

###### Appendix 257



###### Himachal Pradesh

Occupancy tenants have been conferred ownership rights and the purchase price payable by them is 48 times the land revenue. Subject to a minimal right of resumption for small landowners, so defined in the law, ownership rights vest in non-occupancy tenants also and the price payable is 96 times the land revenue. The tenant, however, acquires ownership rights in the first 1 ^ acres of irrigated land or 3 acres of unirrigated land out of the non-resumable area without having to make any payment.

###### Jammu & Kashmir

The landlord’s rights vested in the State from 1 September, 1971 and the State Government conferred ownership rights on the tenants personally cultivating the land. Only small landlords with a monthly income of less than Rs. 500 and not cultivating land personally on 1 September, 1971 may resume within six months 3 standard acres for personal cultivation. However, defence personnel or the dependents of deceased defence personnel owning land on 1 September, 1971 are allowed to resume up to the ceiling area. The right of resumption is subject to the tenant’s right to hold 2 standard acres including orchards and all lands owned or held by him from before. Payment for ownership right is to be made to the State Government as per rules under the Act.

###### Karnataka

The law provided that all land held by tenants on 1 March, 1974 (the date on which the amending Act came into force) would stand transferred to the State. Tenants were entitled to ownership as noted below:

1) Every person who was a permanent tenant, protected tenant or other tenant would with effect from 1 March/1973 be entitled to be registered as an occupant in respect of lands he held and cultivated personally. In no event, however, the area, together with the land he holds as an owner would exceed the ceiling area.

![image 559](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile559.png)

![image 560](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile560.png)

258 Land Reforms at frwha

- 2) The tenant was entitled to choose the area and the location of the land in respect of which he wished to be registered as the occupant.
- 3) Every tenant entitled to be registered as occupant Was to apply to the Tribunal for occupancy rights on or before 31 December, 1974. The dale was, however, extended fh*n time to time op to 31 December, I97S.
- 4) A tenant who was entitled to be registered as an occupant was required to pay to the State Government a premium equal to 15-20 times the rent The premium is payable either in tump sum or in 20 instalments.


###### Kerala

All land held by such cultivating tenants who are entitled to fixity of tenure and who have not received certificates of purchase kinder Section 59 shall vest in the State free from ail encumbrances from the notified date. The Government may, at any time after the vesting, assign the rights, title and interest in respect of those lands to the cultivating tenant who will be bound to accept such assignments. The State pays compensation to the erstwhile landowners and the amount is recovered from the cultivating tenant The compensation payable is 16 times the fair rent.

###### Madhya Pradesh

The landowners were allowed one year’s time from the commencement of the Madhya Pradesh Land Revenue Code, 1959 to resume land not exceeding a specified measure for personal cultivation subject to the condition that a specified area is left with the tenant The tenant acquires ownership rights over the non-resumable area at the end of one year from the date of commencement of the Code. The tenant is required to deposit with the Revenue Officer the price payable for the land and the Revenue Officer shall pay the amount to the landowners. The purchase price is fixed at 15 titties the land revenue and is payable in 5 installments.

###### Manipur

Tenants in occupation of land at the commencement of the

![image 561](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile561.png)

![image 562](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile562.png)

###### Appendix 259



Amending Act of 1975 (except tenants under those landowners having a continuing right to lease out their land) were given ownership right to the land. The landowners were entitled to compensation amounting to 30 times the land revenue. The tenants were required to pay the amount in 20 installments together with interest at 4 per cent per annum.

###### Orissa

The landlord was given the right of resumption to the extent of one-half of the lands held by each tenant. Either the landlord or the tenant could apply for the determination of the resumable and nortresumable land. The tenant could acquire raiyati rights in the nonresumable land on payment of compensation at the rate of Rs. BOO per standard acre to be paid in five equal instalments.

###### Punjab and Haryana

The tenants of landlords who own land ih excess of the permissible ceiling area can acquire ownership rights on land which is outside the landlord’s permissible area, tn the iPunjab region, however, there is a further stipulation that tenants of non-resumable area can acquire ownership, provided they have been in continuous possession of the tenanted land for six years. Compensation payable is not to exceed Rs. 200 or 90 times the land revenue in the P6PSU area. In the t^njab area, the compensation payable by the tenants is to be fixed at 3/4th of 10 years’ average market value of similar lands.

###### kajftsthan

Ownership rights were conferred in respect of non-resumable land on tenants and sub-tenants who held land at the commencement of the Act of 1955. The compensation payable to the landholder was 15 times the rertt for un-irrigated lands and 20 times the rent for irrigated lands and was payable in annual installments not exceeding ten. Tenants admitted after the commencement of the Act cannot enjoy security of tenure nor can they acquire ownership rights as the landowner is entitled to take back the land after the expiry of the lease.

###### Tamtl Nadu

There is no provision for conferring ownership rights on tenants.

![image 563](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile563.png)

![image 564](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile564.png)

###### 260 Land Reforms in India



###### Tripura

According to the Tripura Land Revenue and Land Reform Act, 1960 as amended in 1974, tenants were conferred security of tenure subject to the landlord's limited right of resumption and were also given the right to acquire ownership of the non-resumable land. All recorded under-raiyats have been granted ownership rights. However, these provisions have been of no avail to unrecorded under-raiyats.

###### Uttar Pradesh

After the abolition of intermediaries there are only two tenures, namely, Bhumidars and Sirdars and one sub-tenure namely Asamis. Bhumidars and Sirdars hold land directly under the Government. Bhumidars have permanent, heritable and transferable rights. Sirdars have permanent and heritable but not transferable rights. Sirdars can acquire Bhumidari rights on payment of a sum equal to 10 times the hereditary rates. Asamis are under-tenants who hold land under disabled Bhumidars or Sirdars or are tenants of Gaon Sabhas. Their rights are not transferable nor can they acquire Sirdari or Bhumidari rights. “Sajhis" (share-croppers) are not treated as tenants and, therefore, there is no question of their being allowed to acquire rights of ownership.

###### West Bengal

On the abolition of intermediary interests all categories of tenants other than bargardars (share-croppers) were brought directly under the Government. Though limited security of tenure has been conferred on the bargardars, they cannot acquire ownership rights.

![image 565](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile565.png)

![image 566](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile566.png)

Appendix III. 5

##### Provisions in the State laws regarding prohibition of, or restrictions on, leasing out of land

Andhra Pradesh

Telangana Area

The leasing out of land by large landholders is prohibited. Small holders owning below three family holdings are, however, allowed to lease out land. Minors, females, persons incapable of cultivating the land by reason of any physical or mental infirmity, and persons working in the Defence services and persons prevented by any sufficient reason from cultivating the land are also allowed to lease out land. The lease in such cases shall be for such periods as the Collector may fix. Landholders owning land up to three family holdings may lease out their land for a period of five years and must, subject to a right of resumption, renew it for further periods of five years. The right of resumption is limited to one family holding for each adult member in the landholder’s family. As the family holding can be a considerable area, the resumption can result in total or substantial dispossession of the tenant who is not guaranteed a minimum area. Thus in reality there is no effective restraint on the leasing out of land nor is the tenant accorded real security of tenure.

###### Andhra Area

There is no restriction on leasing. The tenancy is subject to the landlord’s right of resumption which might extend up to two-thirds of the ceiling area as defined in the land ceiling law. The tenant has, however, to be left with an area not less than one-half of that held by

![image 567](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile567.png)

![image 568](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile568.png)

###### 262 Land Reforms in India



him prior to resumption. While the right of resumption has ended in the case of leases subsisting at the commencement of the Amending Act of 1974, it continues in respect of future leases.

###### Assam

There are no restrictions on leasing out of land. The land owners have a continuing right of resumption if the land is required bona fide by the landlord for his persona) cultivation.

###### Bihar

Under the Bihar law, leasing out is prohibited except in the case of persons under disability (minors, widows, unmarried, divorced or separated women, persons suffering from mental or physical disability, persons in defence services and public servants in receipt of a substantive salary not exceeding Rs. 250 per month). Tenants of persons under disability can be evicted at the end of the lease period and no rights accrue to them.

Though the ceiling law prohibited leasing, under the Bihar Tenancy Act persons owning less than 5 acres of irrigated land or 10 acres of un-irrigated land are allowed to lease out land and such land owners enjoy a continuing right of resumption.

###### Saurashtra Area

Future tenancies are prohibited except for landowners serving in the armed forces or those who are widows, minors or persons suffering from mental or physical disability. Unauthorized leasing makes the lessor liable to a fine ranging from six to twenty times the assessment and the lessee to summary eviction. In addition, the tenant is liable to pay a fine up to Rs. 1000 because under the law, taking land on lease amounts to abetment of the offence. The provision has led to the concealment of tenancy as neither the landowner nor the tenant is interested in bringing the tenancy to light.

###### Haryana

There is no ban on leasing out of land and the tenants do not acquire any rights in the land.

![image 569](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile569.png)

![image 570](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile570.png)

Appendix 263

###### Himachal Pradesh

Leasing is prohibited except by a landowner who is

- i) a minor, or unmarried woman or a woman divorced or separated from her husband or
- ii) permanently incapable of cultivating the land by reason of any physical or mental infirmity, or
- iii) a serving member of the armed forces or
- iv) temporarily prevented by some sufficient reason from cultivating the land. AU landowners other than permanently disabled ones must make an application for resumption within one year of the cessation of the disability failing which the lessee would acquire proprietary rights.


###### Jammu & Kashmir

The law prohibits leasing out of land and continuation of tenancies after 1 May, 1973.

###### Karnataka

Future leasing, except by defence personnel, is prohibited.

###### Kerala

Future leasing is prohibited and no exemption is given to defence personnel or to any person suffering from any disability.

###### Maharashtra

As in Gujarat, in Maharashtra also, there is no ban on the creation of tenancies, but the tenant acquires the right to purchase the leased land within one year of the commencement of the tenancy. Persons serving in the armed forces or suffering from specified disabilities may lease out the land. These landlords may resume land within one year of the cessation of the disability.

###### Manipur

Leasing is prohibited except in the case of defence personnel and persons suffering from specified disabilities. The right to lease ceases with the cessation of the disability, but resumption cannot extend

![image 571](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile571.png)

![image 572](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile572.png)

###### 264 Land Reforms in India



beyond one-half of the leased land nor can it be such as to leave the tenant with less than one hectare of land.

###### Orissa

Leasing is prohibited except in the case of Privileged Raiyats, Defence personnel, small holders and persons suffering from specified disabilities. Privileged Raiyats are cooperative societies, Lord Jagannath, public trusts and public financial institutions. The small holders are those owning less than three standard acres of land

###### Punjab

In Punjab also, as in Haryana, there is no ban on leasing out of land and the tenants do not acquire any rights in the land.

###### Rajasthan

Future leasing is not prohibited. There are, however, some restrictions on leasing. Where a “Khatedar” tenant leases out his land, the term of the lease should not exceed five years at a time and there should be an interval of at least two years between two leases. A “Ghair-Khatedar” tenant can lease out his land for only one year. These restrictions do not apply to persons suffering from special disabilities.

###### Tamil Nadu

The Tamil Nadu law allows future leasing, but the law stipulates that every tenancy agreement must be in writing and shall be executed in triplicate. The document should show the details of the land and the terms of the tenancy. A copy of the agreement is required to be deposited in the Taluk office. It is left to the parties to decide the duration of the tenancy.

###### Tripura

The Tripura Land Revenue and Reforms Act of 1960 laid down that every lease should be for a maximum period of 5 years renewable for a further period of 5 years subject to the landlord’s right of resumptions for personal cultivation. The amending Act of 1974 provided that every under-raiyat admitted after the commencement of the Principal Act shall be deemed to hold the land under a lease in

![image 573](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile573.png)

![image 574](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile574.png)

Appendix 265

perpetuity and is not liable to ejectment except on the ground of nonpayment of rent, misuse of the land or sub-letting.

###### Uttar Pradesh

Future leasing is prohibited except by defence personnel or persons suffering from specified disabilities.

###### West Bengal

There is no restriction on leasing. By an amendment of 1977, there is a presumption in law that a person lawfully cultivating the land of another person is a bargadar in respect of such land. The barga right is heritable and can be terminated only on certain specified grounds. On resumption, the bargadar has to be left with an aggregate area of not less than one hectare, nor should the land held by the landowner for personal cultivation exceed three hectares.

Appendix IV. 1

##### Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

P. S. Appu

##### Ministry of Agriculture, New Delhi, 1972 [Extracts]

Chapter 3

##### The Case for Low Ceilings

As we have seen, the Five Year Plans advocated a ceiling on agricultural property solely on considerations of social justice. An attempt will be made in the following paragraphs to delineate the social and economic justification for fixing the ceiling at a low but viable level.

The Directive Principles of State Policy enshrined in the Indian Constitution enjoin that “the State shall strive to promote the welfare of the people by securing and protecting as effectively as it may a social order in which justice, social, economic and political, shall inform all the institutions of national life**. Articles 39 and 41 go on to say, among other things, that the State shall, in particular, direct its policy towards securing that the citizens have the right to adequate means of livelihood, that the ownership and control of the material resources of the community are so distributed as best to subserve the common good, that the operation of the economic system does not result in the concentration of wealth and means of production to the common detriment and that the State shall, within the limits of its economic capacity and development, make effective provision for securing the

![image 579](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile579.png)

![image 580](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile580.png)

###### 268 Land Reforms in India



right to work. It is suggested that the fixing of a ceiling on agricultural holdings at low level will subserve all these objectives indicated in the Constitution.

In this country a simple and effective means for ensuring a measure of social and economic justice will be radical redistribution of land. Agriculture has had and, will continue to have for years to come, a dominant position in the Indian economy. It contributes nearly 50 per cent of the national income and over 70 per cent of the population depends upon agriculture and allied occupations for subsistence. The country’s industrial base being relatively small, even if the future programmes of industrialization meet with a great measure of success, for years to come the bulk of India's population which is increasing at the very high annual rate of 2.5 per cent will have to depend upon agriculture for its livelihood. In such a situation social and economic justice calls for a more equitable distribution of the available agricultural land.

Another important point is that a disproportionately large share of the benefits accruing from the heavy investments made by the society during the last two decades in irrigation, rural electrification, community development, road building, agricultural extension, etc. has gone to the richer farmers. This is inevitably so because all these measures have led to a marked increase in agricultural productivity and, naturally, those with more land have derived a larger share of the increased prosperity. This process has also led to greater concentration of wealth in the hands of the rural rich in direct contravention of the Directive Principles of State Policy. In Indian conditions it is only the ownership of land which can provide a minimum of social security and raise the social status of the under- privileged. The following statement will show how unequally land is distributed in this country:

Perceptive observers of the Indian situation consider the extremely inegalitarian nature of the Indian society as the biggest obstacle to the successful functioning of democratic institutions in this country. The failure of Gram Panchayats, Cooperative Societies and the Community Development Programme is directly attributed to the inegalitarian character of the society. It was only natural that the Gram Panchayats dominated by the rural elite failed to elicit the warm support of the under-privileged. The cooperative societies, controlled as they are in

![image 581](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile581.png)

![image 582](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile582.png)

###### Appendix 269



TABLE (3.2)1

Cumulative per centage dlstributioa of number of boldines and area of land under them by size of bolding: 8tfa and 17th rounds of NSS (reference period: 8th round-major crop season 1953-54,17th round-1961-62)

Sl.No. Holding size (acres) Household ownership holding 8th round 17th round number area number area 1 2 3 4 5 6

- 1. 0.00 23.09 11.68
- 2. below 1.00 47.26 1.37 44.21 1.59
- 3. below 2.50 61.24 6.23 60.06 7.59
- 4. below 5.00 74.73 16.32 75.22 19.99
- 5. below 7.50 82.55 26.28 83.51 31.56
- 6. below 10.00 87.23 34.72 88.08 40.53
- 7. below 15.00 92.28 47.50 93.17 54.50
- 8. below 20.00 94.94 57.08 95.64 64.16
- 9. below 25.00 96.40 63.83 97.15 7176
- 10. below 30.00 97.40 69.55 98.01 77.09
- 11. below 50.00 99.06 82.46 99.40 88.87
- 12. all sizes 100.00 100.00 100.00 100.00


most parts of the country by the rich farmers, seldom cater to the needs of the weaker sections of the population. The benefits of the Community Development Programme as of all other development efforts in general, have accrued to the richer sections of the society leaving the poor untouched. If the glaring inequalities in the rural society are done away with, all these institutions would have a better chance of success. Effective public participation in democratic institutions at the grass roots is possible only among equals or near equals. Hence, if our efforts at rural uplift are to have a fair chance of success, the present structure of the rural society should yield place to a less inegalitarian one. And, obviously, the main instrument for such a transformation of the rural society is the implementation of radical land reform the main planks of which should be ownership rights to share

![image 583](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile583.png)

![image 584](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile584.png)

###### 270 Land Reforms in India



croppers and occupants of homestead lands and a radical redistribution of land These measures will lift those at the bottom of the social ladder, namely, the share-croppers and the landless labourers, to a higher status. It is realized that such a policy will meet with the opposition of the rural elite.

There is a point of view that the fixing of a ceiling on agricultural holdings at low levels and the redistribution of surplus land in countries of heavy population pressure and inadequate avenues of productive employment like India is likely to lead to an increase in overall agricultural production and fuller utilization of the available man-power. The explanation for both these results is that the owners of big holdings generally depend on wage labour and, therefore, they will employ labour only up to the point where the increase in output resulting from the employment of the last unit of labour is at least slightly above the wage level. No such consideration exists in the case of smaller holdings which are generally operated by family labour. There being no alternative sources of employment, family labour will continue to be applied far beyond the point where output per unit of labour is equal to the wage level. In fact, as long as there is any hope of increased production additional family labour will continue to be employed. Thus the smaller holdings will be cultivated more intensively leading to enhanced overall production. Simultaneously there is also fuller utilization of the available man power. It is, however, conceded that in spite of the favourable effect on gross production and utilization of labour, if an imputed value is given to family labour in terms of the ruling wage rate, most of the operations on the small holdings are unremunerative. However, as the growth of alternative employment is not likely to be very significant it is better that family labour is continued to be employed as long as there is a resulting increase in production even though the increment is not commensurate with the imputed value of the labour.

The experience of Japan after the drastic land reform in that country confirms this view. R.P. Dore in his work, ‘Land Reform in Japan’ says “there has been an increase in productivity per acre. This has been most apparent in the case of the main crop, rice. The bumper harvest of 1953 produced a yield of no less than 80 million Koku (one Koku”.=150 kg.) compared with the previous record of 71 million

![image 585](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile585.png)

![image 586](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile586.png)

###### Appendix 271



Koku”. According to Dore one among the several factors contributing to this result is “increased intensity of labour input due to post-war inflation of the agricultural population. Estimates of the concealed unemployment in Japanese villages vary from 1 to 3 or 4 million workers who could easily be spared. These supernumerary workers are under and not un-employed They are all members of some farm family, and, for families which have such a bigger than necessary labour force, laws of diminishing returns have no meaning. Even if it does bring only an infinitesimal increment to the crop one might as well give the rice field an extra weeding as sit around drinking t e a . 2

Even in Japan where a higher level of technology is applied the small farm is not profitable in the strict sense of the term. “The family farm is today a going concern because family workers are ready to accept a reward for their labour below the wage rates which have to be paid to hired workers. Any purely economic calculation which took into account the interest to be paid on investment in land and the cost of hired labour would almost certainly show a loss.” 3

A number of farm management studies conducted in India under the auspices of the Directorate of Economics and Statistics of the Ministry of Food and Agriculture since 1954-55 also show that gross output per acre was higher in the smaller farms.4

A.K. Sen has summarised the results observed in the Farm Management Studies as follows:

- (i) “When family labour employed in agriculture is given imputed value in terms of the ruling wage rate, much of Indian agriculture seems un-remunerative;
- (ii) By and large, the ‘profitability* of agriculture increases with the size of the holding, 'profitability* being measured by the surplus (or deficit) of out-put over costs including the imputed value of labour;
- (iii) By and large, productivity per acre decreases with the size of the holding”.5


In actual practice also it has been observed that smaller holdings under owner cultivation absorb relatively larger number of units of labour. Crops on small holdings usually get a great deal of tender care and constant attention. The writer has personally observed that in some parts of the country in the context of the sharp rise in agricultural

![image 587](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile587.png)

![image 588](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile588.png)

###### 272 Land Reforms in India



wages, some farmers depending on wage labour have given up weeding paddy fields. No such development has taken place on the small farms of owner cultivators. Well-to-do farmers in Kerala have shown a tendency to give up the cultivation of paddy which calls for labourintensive operations and convert their paddy lands into coconut plantations. Apart form the higher cash income the main attraction is that a coconut plantation requires the application of fewer units of labour compared to paddy land of equal area. Cultivation of crops like tobacco, chillies, vegetables etc. which call for intensive application of labour, is usually undertaken by small holders employing family labour. It is seldom that owners of large holdings take up such cultivation. Thus there is considerable force in the argument that a radical redistribution of land leading to a substantial increase in the area under owner-cultivation will have beneficial results on the utilization of labour in agriculture.

A strong argument in favour of drastic redistribution of land is that it is bound to enhance the purchasing power of the rural under-privileged. The resulting increase in the effective demand for manufactured goods needed by the common man will hasten the tempo of the country’s industrial development and enlarge the scope for non-farm employment.

Another argument is that such redistribution of land will engender a better sense of responsibility among the new assignees of land, and this will, in its turn, enhance productivity. Often it has been urged as an argument against radical redistribution of land that it will lead to an increase in consumption of food-grains. For the bulk of the new land will go to landless labourers and small holders who are likely to consume almost all they produce. The advocates of lower ceiling would utilise this also as an argument in favour of radical redistribution of land. It is suggested that in this country such an improvement in levels of consumption of the weaker sections of the community is to be welcomed because it will improve their health and vitality and enhance their productive efficiency. The result will be an eventual improvement in overall production. Thus there is enough social and economic justification for fixing ceiling on agricultural holdings at a low but viable level in this country.

![image 589](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile589.png)

![image 590](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile590.png)

###### Appendix 273

- 3. Ibid., p. 300.

- 5. A.K.Sen, An Aspect of Indian Agriculture, Economic Weekly, Annual Number, 1962. 243.


![image 591](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile591.png)

![image 592](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile592.png)

###### Chapter 4

### The Case Against Low Ceilings

In stating the case against low ceilings, we shall first deal with the points generally made by the opponents of ceiling legislation. Then we shall consider the views put forward by Gunnar Myrdal in Asian Drama and by V.M. Dandekar and Nilkantha Rath in Poverty in India, two works which contain a dispassionate study of the question.

The most important objection to the imposition of ceiling on agricultural property and the consequent redistribution of land is that it will inevitably result in a large increase in the number of uneconomic holdings. The Five Year Plans themselves having recognised the preponderance of uneconomic holdings as the gravest malady in Indian agriculture, it will be unwise to take a step that will add to the number of tiny farms.

In the context of the actual conditions under which farming is done in India there is not much basis for the apprehension that the imposition of ceiling on agricultural property will lead to the creation of new uneconomic holdings. As pointed out by Myrdal:

“In much of the discussion of alternative patterns of agrarian reorganisation, land redistribution is ruled out at an early stage on the grounds that it would simply create small uneconomic holdings and sacrifice the efficiency of the present larger units of cultivation. These fears are, in fact, vastly exaggerated. It must be borne in mind that there is an enormous difference between the units of ownership and the units of cultivation in South Asian agriculture. The ‘farm’ in South Asia bears no resemblance to farming units found in North America or Europe. The South Asian farm is not an economic unit composed of a house for the operator, a garden, and a collection of buildings for storing crops and sheltering animals and machinery. The farming unit is simply a piece of land or, more often, a number of scattered small

![image 593](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile593.png)

![image 594](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile594.png)

###### Appendix 275



strips, barren of fixed investment other than perhaps a well or, at times, some terracing and drainage or irrigation channels. The agricultural land lies on all sides of the hamlets and there are in India no farms in the sense of stable and compact agricultural properties. There are only fragments of land and each farmer cultivates as his “holding” a number of these fragments which lie scattered in the fields surrounding the village, intermixed with strips cultivated by other farmers. In some districts persons may customarily farm a long way from the village, even amongst land predominantly worked by persons from another village. There are no farm houses or farm buildings, the peasants living together in the village”.1

Another point, also made by Myrdal, is that the entire holding is seldom cultivated by the owner himself. Informal share-cropping arrangements are prevalent on a fairly large scale. The extent of such informal tenancy varies from region to region. Wolf Ladcjinsky estimated that in the package districts of Tanjore and West Godavari more than 50 per cent of the land had been leased out to tenants.2 After going through the available evidence on the subject A.M. Khusro has estimated that over 10 per cent of the cultivated area in the country is under oral and un-recorded tenancies and 12.5 per cent is under open tenancy.3 It can reasonably be assumed that about a quarter of the total cultivated area is under either open or concealed tenancy. If this is the case, the break-up of the large holdings which are even now partly cultivated by tenants, will not lead to much change in the existing situation. In the words of Myrdal “when the work on these lands is done by share-croppers or other tenants and by landless labourers, the transfer of title to the tiller would disturb very little the established pattern of operation. Some adjustments would, of course, be required, particularly when it came to giving land to landless labourers who now work for others, but the reorganisation called for is by no means as sweeping as has often been alleged”. 4

As a corollary to the argument that the imposition of low ceilings will lead to a large increase in the number of uneconomic holdings it is also pointed out that such a step will further reduce the scope for economies of scale in Indian agriculture. The larger holdings are in a better position to utilise economically work animals, farm machinery, irrigation facilities etc. In answer it may be pointed out that even now

![image 595](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile595.png)

![image 596](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile596.png)

###### 276 Land Reforms in India



there is a great deal of sharing of tractors and other farm machinery and even of work animals. Furthermore, a drastic redistribution of land will create a more favourable climate for the successful functioning of service cooperatives, and once such a development takes place even the small farms will be in a position to enjoy the benefits of economies of scale. To quote Myrdal “In fact a radical redistribution might improve the prospects for service cooperation—first, because the inequalities that have frustrated past experiments in cooperation would be minimized; secondly, because the newly landed might genuinely require the services of implements and work animals that could most easily be made available on a joint basis”.5

It is also contended that the beneficiaries of redistribution will be mostly landless labourers who have no experience of cultivation and, therefore, the inevitable result will be less efficient utilization of land. There is not much force in this argument either. Even now the agricultural operations on the big holdings are actually conducted by landless agricultural workers and small holders, either as wage earners or as share-croppers. For generations these people have been working on land and they have adequate experience of cultivation. It does not stand to reason that the handing over of the land to them will turn out to be a great calamity. There is, however, considerable force in the argument that landless labourers show less resilience and a tendency to part with the land. Special efforts will be necessary to ensure that this does not happen.

Another important point against the redistribution of land is that the new assignees are likely to be men without adequate capital for investment and that they will find it difficult even to procure the funds necessary for carrying on cultivation. In the result the land will not be properly utilised and production will fall. There is, undoubtedly, some force in this line of argument. This need not, however, be an insurmountable difficulty. It is the business of the cooperative institutions, commercial banks and ultimately of Government, to provide the necessary credit facilities. Once credit facilities are made available to assignees of new land, they would not face any special hardship. Furthermore, as pointed out by Myrdal, most of the needed capital investment being highly labour consuming, the new assignees should be able to provide it without any difficulty. He has also pointed

![image 597](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile597.png)

![image 598](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile598.png)

###### Appendix 277



out that the deficiency in implements and work animals can be overcome through pooling of resources. There is sound basis for the hope expressed by Myrdal that all forms of cooperative effort have a better chance of success in the egalitarian social and economic structure likely to emerge after radical redistribution of land.6

Another argument against the imposition of a ceiling is that it will adversely affect capital formation in the economy. Theoretically there is some basis for this argument. The relevance of the argument in Indian conditions is, however, somewhat doubtful. In many parts of India, the land owning classes have shown a marked inclination to spend their surplus funds on weddings, funerals, litigation etc., and whatever is left is usually utilised for the purchase of more land. It cannot, however, be denied that some progressive cultivators are now showing a tendency to invest in agricultural machinery and farm improvements. This welcome development is likely to be affected adversely by the further lowering of ceilings.

Then there is the argument that it is unfair to impose a ceiling on agricultural property as long as there is no ceiling on urban property or on income from other sources, particularly business and industry. In reply, it may be pointed out that one has to start somewhere, and if a beginning is to be made it should be with agriculture which has a dominant position in the Indian economy inasmuch as over 50 per cent of the national income is derived from this sector and over 70 per cent of the population depends upon it for its subsistence. In due course steps can be taken for removing glaring inequalities in the other sectors.

Then it is argued that piecemeal reform spread over two decades and constant talk about lowering of ceiling have scared progressive farmers. As a result of the uncertainty prevailing in the country they are not inclined to make the necessary investments and whole-heartedly strive for increased production. This is, no doubt, a valid point. It would have been desirable if all the measures of land reform had been conceived of at one time and implemented simultaneously. The frequent amendment of the law and the periodical revision of policy have naturally had a deleterious effect on agricultural production. It is, therefore, necessary that all aspects of the matter are now examined and a final decision taken which should not be revised for at least a decade.

![image 599](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile599.png)

![image 600](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile600.png)

###### 278 Land Reforms in India



Finally, it is argued that whatever justification existed for the imposition of low ceilings and the redistribution of land some years ago, the situation has materially changed since the recent technological break-through in Indian agriculture, popularly styled as the Green Revolution. Some years ago the level of technology was the same in the big holdings and in the small holdings. But now the bigger holdings have, by and large, embraced the new technology, and, this has resulted in a phenomena] increase in production in some parts of the country. In such a situation the break-up of the bigger holdings will be a retrograde step. It has to be conceded that this argument is not without force. In answer, it may, however, be pointed out that the adoption of modem technology is not limited by the size of the farm. It is possible to employ modem technology even on small holdings with equally satisfactory results as in the bigger holdings provided the necessary inputs are available. If Government, Commercial Banks and Cooperative Institutions play their role effectively it should be possible for the small farmers also to adopt modem technology. It has also to be remembered that with the adoption of the new technology net return per acre has shown a steep increase. In such a situation disparities in income will increase greatly in the absence of redistribution of land. Thus the opposite view is that the Green Revolution has actually brought to the fore the extreme urgency of imposing lower ceilings.

After examining the case for a radical redistribution of land from a sympathetic angle Myrdal has rejected it for practical reasons.7 According to Myrdal one serious objection is that such a redistribution of land might reduce the volume of marketable foodstuffs. Myrdal himself has suggested that this difficulty could be met by a compulsory delivery system. His objection is that such a move might frustrate many of the favourable psychological effects sought through radical land redistribution. The experience of the food grains levy operations in the country does not give enough basis for hoping that a compulsory delivery system can be effectively enforced.

Another practical objection pointed out by Myrdal is that “the titles transferred to the actual tillers might easily be eroded by the same forces that have undercut peasant proprietorship in the past. Many of the new and old owners of farms would start out in debt to money-lenders. And once a title was conferred on share-croppers and

![image 601](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile601.png)

![image 602](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile602.png)

###### Appendix 279



landless labourers, their credit standing would improve and they would be tempted to borrow more. A process would be started that in the end would separate them from their holdings”.8 This difficulty can be met by providing effective legal safeguards against the transfer of the newly assigned land and also by providing adequate credit facilities. But Myrdal’s fear is that “legislation designed to block future sub-divisions or fragmentation that prohibit the sale of land might collapse under pressures from the increase in the labour force in agriculture. An increase in the proportion of the landless in the agrarian population may be unavoidable.”9 For these reasons Myrdal concludes that radical redistribution does not even in theory represent a wholly favourable alternative to the status quo. And lastly, he doubts whether such a scheme would be acceptable politically. “Measures that would deprive the upper strata in the villages of land and power and would genuinely confer dignity of status on the under-privileged and the landless, are among the last that those in power would find acceptable”.10 Past experience shows that this apprehension is not unrealistic.

As “Poverty in India”11 contains the most recent and comprehensive study of the question of fixing lower ceilings, we shall be quoting from this work extensively. Having considered the proposal for a drastic reduction in ceilings at some length, Dandekar rejects it as impractical. After a detailed study of the pattern of land holdings in different States he has classified the States into two broad categories-4the high density’ and ‘the low density*. In the first group fall Kerala, Tamil Nadu, Assam, West Bengal, Bihar, Orissa, Uttar Pradesh, Jammu & Kashmir and the Union Territories. The second group consists of Andhra Pradesh, Mysore, Punjab, Gujarat, Madhya Pradesh, Maharashtra and Rajasthan. He has worked out the levels at which ceilings will have to be imposed if 1/2 to 1 acre is to be distributed to the landless households in the high density States and 2 \ to 5 acres in the low density States.

According to Dandekar if half an acre of land is to be given to every landless household and to every household with less than half acre, the ceiling will have to be 7.5 acres in Kerala and Assam, 10 acres in Tamil Nadu and West Bengal and 15 acres in Jammu & Kashmir and the Union Territories. To provide one acre to the landless households

![image 603](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile603.png)

![image 604](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile604.png)

###### 280 Land Reforms in India



and to those households which have less than one acre, the ceiling will have to be 12.5 acres in Bihar, 15 acres in Orissa and Uttar Pradesh and 25 acres in Punjab. In Andhra Pradesh, Mysore, Gujarat and Maharashtra if a minimum of 2.5 acres is to be given to all with no land or with land below that level, the ceiling will have to be 15 acres in Andhra Pradesh, 25 acres in Mysore and Gujarat and 30 acres in Madhya Pradesh and Maharashtra. In Rajasthan with the ceiling at 50 acres it will be possible to assure a minimum of 5 acres to all rural households. If such a redistribution is effected about 35 to 55 per cent of rural households in the different States will have only the minimum area i.e. half an acre in Kerala, Tamil Nadu, Assam, West Bengal and Jammu & Kashmir, one acre in Bihar, Orissa,Uttar Pradesh and Punjab 2.5 acres in Andhra Pradesh, Mysore, Gujarat, Madhya Pradesh and Maharashtra and 5 acres in Rajasthan. The result of such a redistribution is described in the following words:

“It will thus be seen that once it is agreed that the landless households should receive priority in any redistribution of land, little land remains for giving to the small cultivators and bringing them up to a viable size. As we have seen, on the basis of some land to every rural household, even with very low ceilings, no more than half an acre can be given to each rural household in Kerala, Tamil Nadu, Assam, West Bengal, Jammu & Kashmir and the Union Territories, no more than an acre each can be given in Bihar, Orissa, Uttar Pradesh and Punjab; no more than 2.5 acres can be given in Andhra Pradesh, Mysore, Gujarat, Madhya Pradesh and Maharashtra and even in Rajasthan, only 5 acres can be assured to each rural household. Consequently, existing smaller holdings cannot be lifted above these levels. Therefore, the redistribution cannot reduce the number of such uneconomic holdings. Indeed, it lifts the landless into this category and vastly increases the number of uneconomic non-viable holdings’*.12

Thus the main objection is that such redistribution will lead to a vast increase in the number of uneconomic, non-viable holdings and these holdings will be somewhat worse off than the existing uneconomic holdings.

“The new uneconomic holdings which may be created will be worse still because the surplus land which will be surrendered and redistributed will in general be of a much inferior quality. It will be

![image 605](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile605.png)

![image 606](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile606.png)

###### Appendix 281



worth nobody's while to cultivate such land in the small pieces into which it will be distributed. It will also be beyond the means of the new holders to develop these lands. No amount of credit, long-term or short-term, from the cooperatives or the nationalised banks, can make these uneconomic holdings economically viable. Most probably, the small uneconomic holders, old and new, will sell their land and turn landless. The entire operation of redistribution may thus be nullified. If this is prevented by law, the small uneconomic holders will lease out their land to bigger cultivators and tenancy will emerge in a new and reverse form. If it has not been easy to enforce tenancy legislation on the bigger owners, it will be impossible to do so in respect of the innumerable small owners. If this is attempted, the small uneconomic holders will mortgage their lands but they will not cultivate them because it will not be worth-while. A patently uneconomic proposition cannot be sustained by law. It is not entirely for doctrine that the communist countries, after redistribution of land, have immediately proceeded to collectivize them in large co-operatives”.13

The following is Dandekar’s conclusion: “However simple it may appear, it is futile to try to resolve the problem of rural poverty, in an overpopulated land, by redistribution of land which is in short supply. Ceiling on holdings was accepted in principle, though generally not in practice, at the beginning of the decade when, because of the operation of the tenancy laws, tenancy had become increasingly difficult and owner-cultivation had not yet become profitable. Thanks to the technological advances in agriculture made during the decade, owner cultivation in sizeable farms has become distinctly profitable. The entire course of future agricultural development now depends upon this impetus. Its essence is the opportunity to grow. Therefore, further lowering of the ceiling will not be politically accepted and, if imposed, will be sabotaged in more than one way. Such a course also does not appear to be desirable from the standpoint of agricultural development. As mentioned above, much of the surplus land to be surrendered will be of inferior quality. Its distribution in small pieces to the landless does no good either to the landless or to the land. There is a better chance of such inferior land being developed and brought to. profitable productivity if allowed to remain in the large profitable farms which will have surplus to invest.

![image 607](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile607.png)

![image 608](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile608.png)

###### 282 Land Reforms in India



“Hence the legitimate policy to pursue in future should be to enforce the accepted ceilings more rationally and genuinely, namely, by imposing them on family holdings and not on individual holdings. At any rate, future acquisitions beyond such ceiling limits applied to family holdings should be prevented. But any drastic lowering of the ceilings and redistributing the surplus land to the landless worker will serve no useful purpose. Firstly, it does not meet the problem of rural poverty. Secondly, it is not a feasible solution in the sense of one which can be maintained in the face of economic forces operating in an economy with private ownership of the means of production. Thirdly, it nullifies the major impetus which the recent technology advances have given to agricultural development in the country. As we have mentioned, the essence of this impetus is opportunities for farm businesses to grow. The new technological advances have made owner-cultivation in sizeable farms a distinctly profitable proposition. This must be regarded a socially desirable development. Such farms may be regulated by registering them as farm businesses and bringing them under suitable labour and taxation laws. But subject to such regulation and within the limits of accepted ceilings they must be allowed to grow. They will grow by absorbing small, uneconomic holdings and by mechanisation of their operations. This should be recognised as legitimate and desirable because it will lead to an organization of agriculture into not only viable but profitable units with capacity for capital accumulation and development. Undoubtedly, this will increase the number of landless and, though some of them will find more regular and better-paid employment in agriculture, on balance, the employment in agriculture will decline. Certainly something will have to be done to these people who will be thrown out of land and also out of agricultural wage employment. It is important to recognize that their number will grow rather than believe that they can all be settled on land”.M

Dandekar has given some cogent reasons for rejecting the case for further lowering of ceilings. It is true that most of the land likely to become surplus on the imposition of the ceiling law will be of inferior quality and the new assignees will find it extremely difficult to bring such land under cultivation. However, if adequate credit facilities are

![image 609](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile609.png)

![image 610](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile610.png)

###### Appendix 283



provided and if the Government and the Agro-Industries Corporation make necessary arrangements for loaning tractors wherever necessary, the new assignees may be able to bring the land under cultivation. Furthermore, if the ceilings are lowered and efficiently implemented, the chances are that land of somewhat better quality which is already under cultivation would become available as surplus.

There is no doubt at all that the recent technological break-through has made the imposition of lower ceilings much more difficult. Any move to reduce the ceiling will be stoutly opposed by the rural elite. It is also true that imposition of lower ceiling and redistribution of land will not solve the problem of rural poverty. All the same it will be of some help. There is no reason why any measure should be rejected just because it is not a complete solution.

One of the objections raised by Dandekar is that such redistribution nullifies the major impetus which the recent technological advances have given to agricultural development in the country. In answer it may be pointed out that it is precisely because of these technological advances that it has become possible to impose lower ceilings. The new technology is not related to size and if the necessary facilities are extended, it can be efficiently employed even on small farms.

Dandekar has suggested that the big holdings should be allowed to grow by absorbing small uneconomic holdings. Such a course would have been perfectly legitimate if the aim of the economic policy were the organization of agriculture on capitalist lines.

Notes

- 1. Gunnar Myrdal, Asian Drama, Vol. II pp. 1370-71
- 2. Wolf Ladejinsky-A Study of Tenancy Conditions in Package Districts, Government of India, Planning Commission, 1965.
- 3. A.M. Khusro in his forthcoming book ‘The Economics of Land Reforms and Farm Size in India”.
- 4. Myrdal, Asian Drama, Vol.II-p. 1371

- 12. Ibid p. 120

- 14. Ibid p. 121-122


![image 613](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile613.png)

![image 614](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile614.png)

###### Chapter 5

### Policy Alternatives

We have seen the arguments for and against lowering the ceiling on agricultural holdings. Now we shall proceed to examine the possible policy alternatives within the framework of the Indian Constitution.

The first alternative is to fix the ceiling at a very low level say, at about one family holding of irrigated land, take over all the land above the ceiling without paying compensation and distribute it among landless labourers and small holders owning land below a minimum limit to be fixed taking into account the density of population and the availability of surplus land in the State. There are good reasons, social as well as economic, for advocating such a radical policy. It is in consonance with the letter and the spirit of the Indian Constitution, and, hopefully, it may turn out to be the chief instrument for the creation of an egalitarian social order. And, such a measure will be no more revolutionary than what Japan adopted during the American occupation. But then, the India of today is not the same as Japan immediately after the Second World War. It is extremely doubtful whether such a thorough-going reform would have been found acceptable even in the months immediately following independence. Many changes have taken place since the dawn of freedom, changes that have made the adoption of any radical measure much more difficult. During the last two decades, mainly as a result of land reforms and the modernization of agriculture, a new class of substantial landowners has come to the fore. The rich benefits of the sizable Plan investments and of the technological break through in agriculture have largely accrued to this class. It is the backbone of almost all political parties that matter, and, naturally, it wields considerable sway over all organs of democratic Government from the Village Panchayat to the Council of Ministers. The political power-structure being what it is,

![image 615](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile615.png)

![image 616](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile616.png)

###### 286 Land Reforms in India



there is no basis at all for hoping that such a radical measure will be adopted. We may, therefore, reject it as impractical. One cannot but agree with Myrdal’s following assessment:

‘The political consequences of the post-war trend of events are far-reaching. The evidence suggests that the opportune moment for a radical reshaping of the agrarian structure has passed. Sweeping changes might perhaps have been accomplished in the revolutionary environment of the immediate post-war and post-Independence years. But if consent for a fundamental change in property and tenancy rights might have been won then, it is not possible now. The piecemeal reforms that have been accomplished have bolstered the political, social and economic position of the rural upper strata on which the present Governments depend for crucial support. Not only has the political influence of this group increased, but its interest in the perpetuation of the status quo has been enhanced. Its stake in the existing order is, of course, shared by the middle and upper strata of the urban population, whose members often own land. In combination, these forces exert a strong pressure for conservatism in regard to agrarian structure, however radical the tone of policy resolutions and certain laws, and however radical the behaviour of some Governments towards industry, particularly when foreign owned.”1

Similar views have been expressed by Dandekar, “ceiling on holdings was accepted in principle, though generally not in practice, at the beginning of the decade when, because of the operation of the tenancy laws, tenancy had become extremely difficult and owner-cultivation had not yet become profitable. Thanks to the technological advances in agriculture made during the decade, owner-cultivation in sizeable farms has become distinctly profitable. The entire course of future agricultural development now depends upon this impetus. Its essence is the opportunity to grow. Therefore, further lowering of the ceilings will not be politically accepted and, if imposed, will be sabotaged in more than one way.”2

The second alternative is to repeal the existing ceiling laws and follow a policy openly favouring the development of capitalist agriculture. There will be no legal limit at all on the area of land one can hold. Now that investments in agriculture have become profitable, the result of such a policy will be that the rich farmers will buy up most

![image 617](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile617.png)

![image 618](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile618.png)

###### Appendix 287

of the lands of the small holders. Not only the rich farmers, but moneyed men from other walks of life too will begin making investments in land. There are good prospects too of a number of joint stock companies being formed. The beneficial results will include a reduction in the number of uneconomic holdings and a substantial increase in the availability of funds for investment in agriculture. And it should be remembered that the new technology absorbs a great deal of capital. Such a development will lead to the rapid modernisation of Indian agriculture. It may be pointed out that the Administrative Reforms Commission's Panel on Agriculture has recommended the adoption of such a policy. This is the suggestion of the Panel:

“Our Policies should be primarily production oriented. Even ceiling on land holdings should be considered on the same basis. Holdings which are contributing to high levels of production should not be disturbed on this reasoning.

Land Ceilings Acts should be repealed, and replaced by a provision for graduated taxes for the extent of land over and above the minimum prescribed.”3

The implementation of such a policy will pose no difficulty at all. All that is necessary is simply to repeal the existing laws. The social cost of such a policy is, however, likely to be heavy. The distribution of income will become more unequal and there will be greater concentration of wealth. Small holders will progressively be pushed into the ranks of the landless and the social cleavages will become more sharply defined. As large scale capitalist fanning is bound to promote mechanisation including the use of combine harvesters, the employment potential of agriculture may fall further leading to acute unemployment in rural areas. The pursuit of such a policy will unleash forces that may ultimately put an end to our experiment in parliamentary democracy.

The third alternative will be to make it clear that no further lowering of ceilings will be attempted. The emphasis will be on the effective implementation of the laws already enacted. The social objective of removing inequalities in income would be pursued by progressive taxation of agriculture and other ancillary measures. Though there are some further refinements, this is in essence the line of policy suggested by Dandekar and Myrdal. Dandekar’s conclusion is:

![image 619](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile619.png)

![image 620](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile620.png)

###### 288 Land Reforms in India



“Hence the legitimate policy to pursue in future should be to enforce the accepted ceilings more rationally and genuinely, namely by imposing them on family holdings and not on individual holdings. At any rate future acquisitions beyond such ceiling limits applied to family holdings should be prevented/"4 With all the uncertainties about ceilings having been removed, Indian agriculture will develop on genuinely capitalistic lines. The holdings of the better off fanners will grow within the accepted ceilings by absorbing small economic holdings and by mechanisation of their operations. Dandekar, no doubt, realises that “this will increase the number of landless and, though some of them will find more regular and better-paid employment in agriculture, on balance the employment in agriculture will decline”. According to Dandekar, the problem of rural unemployment is to be met by launching a massive rural works programme involving an annua] outlay of over Rs. 800 crores. Additional resources of the order of over Rs. 600 crores will be raised annually by heavier taxation on the upper strata of the rural population. Though one may have reservations about these figures, most people will certainly agree about the crucial role of a massive rural works programme. But the problem oT rural unemployment is so vast and intractable that the rural works programme alone may not offer a complete solution. As a supplementary measure, there should also be a more even distribution of land which is expected to improve the employment potential. Furthermore, past experience with taxation of agriculture gives no room for hoping that such a heavy dose of taxation as recommended by Dandekar would be imposed on the rural sector. To launch a massive programme of rural works without counter-veiling taxation would be to invite certain disaster. For the inflationary impact may have a crippling effect and throw the economy out of gear. What Dandekar has suggested is a package deal. If a Government chooses to accept the proposal, the chances are that the part dealing with ceiling on holdings may be accepted and implemented, while the other part dealing with massive rural works programme and heavy additional taxation will remain largely unimplemented.

Myrdal’s solution is somewhat different in details. Having rejected radical land redistribution and radical land consolidation Myrdal recommends:

![image 621](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile621.png)

![image 622](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile622.png)

Appendix 289

“As neither the political will nor the administrative resources for a radical, or for that matter, any fairly effective land reform are present, it may be preferable to make a deliberate policy choice in favour of capitalist farming by allowing and encouraging the progressive cultivator to reap the full rewards of his enterprise and labour, while approaching the fundamental issues of equality and institutional reform from a different angle and by different policy means.”5

The policy recommended by Myrdal should be distinguished from one of laissez faire. It will not “tolerate passive and parasitic land ownership on the part of persons who sap the surplus of the agricultural sector but contribute nothing to its productive performance/*6 Heavy taxation will be imposed on nonparticipating landowners and laws will be enacted prohibiting the future transfer of titles to non-farming non-residents.

With a view to improving the economic and social status of the landless labourers Myrdal recommends that “High priority in this scheme should be given to a programme to give a small plot of landand with it dignity and a fresh outlook on the life as well as a minor independent source of income—to the members of the landless lower strata. Even in the most densely populated countries of the region it would be possible to give the landless at least small plots on acreages that are now uncultivated waste. In some cases land is available for the landless in the vicinity of existing holdings. The existing pattern of cultivated holdings need not be seriously distributed at all. In the most crowded districts some reallocation of population would be necessary.

“A modest redistribution scheme drafted along these lines, far less radical than the land reforms generally discussed and to an extent legislated, would have two important things to recommend it: first, it would offer a minimal form of social security to the dispossessed in the agrarian structure by allowing them to produce a modest income in kind; secondly, and more important, it would attack the institutional and attitudinal problem of status and dignity at its roots. It would be essential in such a scheme of limited redistribution, however, that inalienable and unrestricted right to own and use the land pass into the hands of the landless as individuals.”7 Myrdal, no doubt, had India also in view while making the above suggestions. It is, however, extremely doubtful whether in this country, particularly in the area of high density

![image 623](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile623.png)

![image 624](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile624.png)

###### 290 Land Reforms in India



of population, it will be possible to accomplish the objective of giving a small plot of land to every landless family without reducing the ceiling further. Wasteland suitable for cultivation may not always be available in the same or neighboring villages. The studies made by Dandekar show what drastic reductions in ceiling will be necessary to make even small areas of land available to landless labourers.8 Hence some reduction in ceilings appears unavoidable even to achieve the modest goal suggested by Myrdal.

The fourth alternative, which is the one that this paper recommends, is to amend the ceiling laws of the other States keeping the latest Kerala law as the model, enforce the new law efficiently and distribute the surplus land on the lines indicated by Myrdal. The policy suggested is somewhat more ambitious than the third alternative, but the sights are kept far lower than in the first. It is not being suggested that the Kerala law should be extended to all the other States in exactly the same form. Conditions vary between States and, therefore, some modifications will be necessary. The level of ceiling for each State will have to be fixed taking into account the man land ratio, productivity of land, kinds of crops grown etc. The matter will be examined in greater detail in the next chapter. The broad suggestion is that for States with very high density of population the ceiling for a family of five should be fixed at 10 to 15 standard acres. In the other States it may be fixed at 15 to 20 standard acres. Land should be classified on the basis of availability of water, productivity, soil classification, crops grown etc. and conversion ratios should be prescribed for different classes of land. Families with more than 5 members may be allowed additional land subject to an overall ceiling at twice the ceiling fixed for a family of five.

The area of land that will be declared surplus in each big holding will have to be worked out on the basis of the number of members in the family, the area of different classes of land in the holding and the conversion ratio provided in the law. Without making such detailed calculations it will not be possible to indicate the additional land that is likely to become surplus in the different States. It may, however, be assumed that if the ceiling laws are so amended and efficiently implemented, enough land will become available in every State so that small plots of land can be allotted to a substantial number of landless labourers.

![image 625](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile625.png)

![image 626](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile626.png)

###### Appendix 291

What has been proposed is not a very radical measure. West Bengal has already reduced the ceiling to the Kerala level. The suggestion is that the other States should also be persuaded to adopt similar legislation.

It is conceded that adoption of such legislation and its implementation will not by itself solve all the problems of the weaker sections of the rural society. Such a programme of redistribution of land should be accompanied by effective measures for the protection of share-croppers and homestead tenants and the launching of a well-conceived rural works programme. The aim of the rural works programme should not be merely to provide avenues of employment. The execution of the schemes should lead of the creation of valuable capital assets and the returns should be commensurate with the expenditure. Furthermore, the funds necessary for financing the programme should be found by imposing additional taxation on the prosperous sections of the community.

These measures will lead to a substantial improvement in the social and economic status of the rural underprivileged and the fuller utilization of the factors of production, and, incidentally, will also prevent the extreme concentration of wealth in the agricultural sector.

Undoubtedly it will not be easy for the State Governments to adopt such a policy. The upper strata of the rural elite will resist it tooth and nail. But then the opposition will not be as strong as in the case of the first alternative. As the ceiling will be kept at a higher level than that contemplated in the first alternative, only a small section of the well-to-do farmers will be adversely affected. Even so, the influence of this small section should not be under-estimated. Their number may be small, but they are the people who wield authority at all levels. They have also waxed strong on the rich fruits of the green revolution. They will stiffly resist any attempt to lower the existing ceilings. In the event of the law being amended, they will also do everything in their power—they can certainly do a great deal—to prevent its efficient implementation. In spite of these difficulties, if the necessary political will is forthcoming, it should be possible to enact and implement the proposed legislation.

The fifth, and undoubtedly, the simplest course will be to continue the present policy. It is not necessary to dilate on this. Myrdal thinks

![image 627](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile627.png)

![image 628](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile628.png)

###### 292 Land Reforms in India



that this is the most probable development in all South Asian Countries. His description of the present policy and its outcome is vivid and succinct:

“Under the banner of ‘social and economic revolution, land, reforms and other institutional changes of a radical character will be talked about and, occasionally, enacted into law in some form. Very little reform will actually be accomplished and even greater inequality may result from both Government policies and developments beyond governmental control, such as the increase in labour force. A state of insecurity about the future of land ownership will be maintained. That itself will tend to suppress improvements in production. The trend would thus seem to be towards capitalist agriculture though its advance is hampered in various ways and denied in the official ideology. That this process is allowed to take place in a non-planned manner, and very often in blunt contradiction of commonly acccpted programme means also that its pace is slower than it would be if Governments had accepted the need for a capitalist agriculture.”9

Notes

- 1. Myrdal, Asian Drama, Vol.II p. 1367.
- 2. V.M. Dandekar and Nilkantha Rath, "Poverty in India”, Economic and Political Weekly, January 9,1971.
- 3. Administrative Reforms Commission, Report of the Study Team on Agricultural Administration. Vol.I. p. 36.
- 4. V.M. Dandekar and Nilkantha Rath, “Poverty in India”, Economic and Political Weekly, January 9,1971.
- 5. Myrdal, Asian Drama, Vol.II p. 1380.
- 6. Ibid p. 1380.
- 7. Ibid p. 1382.
- 8. Op. cit.
- 9. Myrdal, Asian Drama p. 1384.


![image 629](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile629.png)

![image 630](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile630.png)

###### Chapter 6

### Level of Ceiling

As we have seen, the First Five Year Plan, the Planning Commission’s Panel on Land Reforms and the Second Five Year Plan recommended that the ceiling should be fixed in terms of a ‘family holding’. The first plan defined “family holding” as an area of land which under the existing local conditions and the level of technology was equal either to a plough unit of work or to a work unit for a family of average size, working with such assistance as was customary in agricultural operations. In other words, a family holding should comprise enough agricultural land to keep a pair of bullocks and the family labour fully employed. In this sense a family holding is the same as an economic holding under conditions of traditional agriculture.

It was recognised that the family holding would not be the same for different parts of the country and that variations would have to be made taking into account soil and climatic conditions, irrigation, crops grown etc.

Considering that mechanization has touched only a fringe of Indian agriculture, the concept of economic holding as indicated in the First and the Second Five Year Plans appears to be still valid and adequate. After analysing a large number of farm management studies conducted in different regions of the country A.M. Khusro has worked out the plough unit and the work unit of land for different areas.1 His conclusion is that for the country as a whole the farm should be 7.5 to 10 acres in area to fully utilise a pair of bullocks. According to him the plough unit for the different States is as shown:2

![image 631](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile631.png)

![image 632](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile632.png)

###### 294 Land Reforms in India

bullocks is underemployed

Andhra 10 to 15 acres 10 acres Bombay lOto 15 acres 10 acres Uttar Pradesh lOto 15 acres 10 acres West Bengal 7.5 to 10 acres 7.5 acres Punjab 5 (or 7.5) to 10 acres 5 acres (or 7.5) acres Tamil Nadu Approximate All India
