Thus after an analysis of the data available Khusro has come to the conclusion that the approximate All-India average of plough unit and work unit will be between 7.5 to 10 acres.

![image 633](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile633.png)

![image 634](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile634.png)

###### Appendix 295

The farm management studies covered all kinds of land, irrigated as well as unirrigated. If irrigated land only is taken into account the work unit and plough unit will be somewhat lower than 7.5 acres. It may, perhaps be assumed that with assured irrigation or under conditions of adequate, well-distributed rainfall, a holding of even a somewhat smaller area will provide full employment to a pair of bullocks and the workers in a family. As was pointed out in Chapter 1, the Second Plan had rejected as impractical the suggestion to relate the concept of family holding to any particular level of income. Taking into account the cogent reasons given in the Second Plan it does not appear either necessary or feasible to reconsider the issue.

The Agrarian Reforms Committee of the Congress, the Planning Commission’s Panels on Land Reform and the Second Plan suggested the fixing of the ceiling for a normal family at three family holdings.

In the context of these facts it appears reasonable to fix the ceiling for a family of five members at about 15 acres of irrigated land. As soil and climatic conditions and crops grown vary from State to State it will be advantageous to suggest a small range within which the ceiling should be fixed instead of indicating a precise figure for the whole country. Some adjustments will also have to be made for the pressure of population on the arable land. It was on the basis of all these considerations that we suggested in the last chapter that the ceiling for a family of five members should be fixed at 10-15 standard acres in States with a high density of population and at 15-20 standard acres in the other States. The concept of a standard acre is well known and has been adopted in several State legislations. As early as 1954 the Government of India had suggested that an average acre of irrigated land or other land of good quality in a State as a whole or in a region thereof, might constitute a “standard acre” and other classes of land given corresponding lower or higher values. This appears to be a satisfactory method of classifying land.

As we have seen, the Second Five Year Plan was in favour of the ceiling being fixed on the family basis. It was, however, left to the State Governments to decide whether the law should provide for an individual ceiling or a family ceiling. As pointed out in Chapter 2, Gujarat, Kerala, Mysore, Rajasthan, Tamil Nadu and West Bengal have fixed the ceiling on the family basis. In all the other States except Bihar

![image 635](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile635.png)

![image 636](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile636.png)

###### 296 Land Reforms in India



and Madhya Pradesh it has been fixed for individual land-holders with some States making an allowance for the size of the landholders’ family. The extreme case is that of Bihar and Madhya Pradesh where the ceiling is for each member of the family. There are good reasons for fixing the ceiling on the family basis. As pointed out in the Second Plan the fixing of the ceiling on family basis would reduce the scope for the evasion of the law by effecting malafide transfers. For transfers are usually made in favour of the members of the family. When the ceiling is for the whole family no useful purpose will be served by such transfers. Another point in favour of a family ceiling is that even where several members of the family are recorded as owners of land, usually all their lands constitute a single operational holding. Investment decisions are made jointly or by the head of the family, work-animals and implements are jointly owned, funds come from the same source and all the available members of the family either actually work or take part in supervising cultivation. Hence it is reasonable to apply the ceiling to the entire land held by the members of the family, the term “family” being defined so as to include husband, wife and dependent children.

Following the latest laws of Kerala and West Bengal some allowance may be made for the size of the family. Where there is only one member in the family the ceiling may be kept at one half of the area prescribed for a normal family. Where the number of members is between 2 and 5, the ceiling may be kept at 10-15 standard acres in States of high density and at 15-20 acres in other States. Where the number of members in the family exceeds 5, some additional land may be allowed. In Kerala the ceiling for a family of 5 is 10 standard acres, and for every member in excess of 5 one additional standard acre is allowed subject to an overall ceiling of 20 acres for a family. According to the latest West Bengal law, the ceiling for a family of 5 is 5 standard hectares, and where the number of members in the family exceeds five, additional land at the rate of 0.50 standard hectare is allowed, subject to an overall ceiling of 7 standard hectares for a family.

The Second Plan had recommended that additional land might be allowed for larger families but there should be an overall ceiling at twice the ceiling area. This is somewhat more liberal than the provisions made in the Kerala and West Bengal laws. It is suggested

![image 637](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile637.png)

![image 638](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile638.png)

Appendix 297

that the area allowed to a large family should in no case exceed twice the ceiling area for a normal family.

In Chapter 2, a reference was made to the provisions in the various States regarding exemption from the operation of the ceiling law. These exemptions have played a significant role in rendering the ceiling laws ineffective and drastically reducing the area of land liable to be declared as surplus. There is a need for reviewing the provisions in the various State laws and following a more rigid policy. Broadly stated our suggestion is that the exemption in favour of tea, coffee and rubber plantations may continue. Commercial plantations have the characteristics of modem industry and in their case industrial and agricultural work are closely integrated. It is neither feasible nor desirable from the point of view of the national economy to break them up. If it is felt that they should not be left in private hands, the only alternative is to nationalise them.

Cooperative farming societies may be allowed to hold land subject to the stipulation that the share of each individual member shall be within the ceiling limit and that where the members own other land also, in computing the ceiling area for them their share in the cooperative society will also be taken into account.

An enabling provision may be included in the law empowering the State Government by notification in the official Gazette to permit religious, charitable and educational institutions and industrial undertakings to hold so much land in excess of the ceiling as is considered necessary for the due performance of their obligations. The contemplated exemption will enable these bodies to hold the land on which buildings stand, playgrounds, land needed for future expansion etc. They should not be allowed to hold land in excess of the ceiling for purposes of cultivation. Thus there will be no exemption for sugarcane farms of sugar factories.

Several States have exempted orchards from the operation of the ceiling law. Orchards can be distinguished from commercial plantations in as much as the former do not have the characteristics of modem industry. Moreover, even orchards with a small area can be managed efficiently and economically. The application of the ceiling law to orchards may, however, lead to some undesirable consequences.

![image 639](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile639.png)

![image 640](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile640.png)

###### 298 Land Reforms in India



If the surplus land is distributed to landless labourers, there is the danger of the fruit trees being neglected or cut down. The alternative of the orchards being managed by the Government does not appear to be a feasible proposition in the existing circumstances. It is, therefore, suggested that following the latest West Bengal law, it may be laid down that where a family owns land comprised in orchards, whether or not in addition to other land, the ceiling area in relation to such family may be increased by 5 acres or the actual area of the land comprised in the orchards, whichever is less. As families will have the option to keep the entire land admissible to them in orchards, the preservation of the bulk of the orchards can be ensured. The other exemptions in the existing ceiling laws may be withdrawn.

The fourth alternative policy discussed in the previous chapter contemplates the amendment of the ceiling laws on the above lines.

###### Notes

- 1. A.M. Khusro in his forthcoming book. The Economics of Land Reform and Farm Size in India.
- 2. Ibid.


![image 641](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile641.png)

![image 642](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile642.png)

###### Chapter 7

### Compensation

Regarding the payment of compensation the broad principle laid down in the Second Plan was that the scheme should be self- financing i.e. the aggregate payment on account of compensation and interest to the owners should be recovered from the assignees of surplus land in easy instalments in such a manner that there would be no additional financial burden on the State Governments. Keeping this principle in view it was suggested that it would be convenient for the State Governments to issue compensatory bonds redeemable over the period of, say, 20 years. It was, however, left to the State Governments to decide whether they would fix the compensation either as specified amounts related to different classes of land or as a multiple of land revenue or in such other manner as might be considered feasible. As far as the purchase price to be recovered from the assignees was concerned, the suggestion was that it should be in instalments spread over a number of years and that the burden on them should not exceed the level of fair rent recommended in the Plan i.e. one-fourth to onefifth of the gross produce.

Different principles have been followed in the ceiling laws of the different States in the matter of payment of compensation. The amount payable to land-owners for surplus land is fixed as a multiple of land revenue in Assam, Madhya Pradesh, Uttar Pradesh, Gujarat & Maharashtra, while it has been fixed as a multiple of rent in Rajasthan and the old Pepsu region of the Punjab. In Mysore and Tamil Nadu the amount of compensation is a multiple of the net annual income while in Andhra Pradesh it has been fixed as a multiple of the gross income. In Orissa and under the old Kerala law compensation was based on market value. The Bihar law specifies the amount of compensation payable for different classes of land. The Kerala law was amended

![image 643](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile643.png)

![image 644](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile644.png)

###### 300 Land Reforms in India



recently and now the compensation payable for different classes of land in different areas has been specified in the law itself.

The ceiling laws of the different States invariably provide for payment in instalments spread over a period or partly in cash and partly in bonds. The period of maturity of the bonds varies from 10 to 30 years and the rate of interest is between 2 \ to 4^ per cent. The wide variations in the State laws have naturally led to gross differences in the amount of compensation payable for similar land in different States. In Mysore where the compensation is fixed at 10 times the average net annual income or in Tamil Nadu where it is fixed at 9 to 12 times the net income, the land-owner is in a much happier position than in Assam and Madhya Pradesh where the compensation is fixed in multiples of land revenues varying from 20 to 50.

States, where the amount of compensation is fixed as a multiple of net or gross income or on the basis of market prices, have been facing a great deal of difficulty in working out the amount of compensation payable. Kerala, which had earlier, fixed compensation on the basis of market value, found that it was an impossible task to work out the compensation for the numerous plots of land involved and, therefore, was obliged to amend the law.

What would then be a simple and reasonable basis for working out compensation? To start with, it may be pointed out that there is a point of view that constitutionally it is not obligatory to pay compensation for land vesting in the State Government on the implementation of the ceiling law, such a law being a measure of agrarian reform covered by Article 31-A(lXa) of the Constitution. The guarantees given in Articles 14, 19 and 31 will not apply. It is argued that a ceiling law can be defended successfully even if it does not contain any provision for payment of compensation. This view finds support in the Supreme Court’s decision in Shankar Rao and others vs. State of Mysore (AIR 1969 SC 453). A recent Full Bench decision of the Kerala High Court also upholds this view (O.P. Numbers 209 Series of 1970 decided on August 14, 1970). In that case the Kerala High Court observed as follows:

“There are a number of decisions of the Supreme Court that lay down what seems to be clear from the language of Article 31-A itself

![image 645](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile645.png)

![image 646](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile646.png)

Appendix 301

that a law for acquisition falling within its scope cannot be assailed on the ground that it enables acquisition without payment of any compensation at all. That being so, there can be no complaint that the compensation payable is a mere make-believe”.

Even though constitutionally it may not be obligatory to pay compensation, the question arises whether compensation should in fact be paid and, if so, what would be a reasonable level. In view of the fact that for the abolition of intermediary interests a huge amount of compensation exceeding Rs. 600 crores had been paid and that under the constitution as it stands at present, in all cases except those covered by Article 31-A, payment of adequate compensation is obligatory, it may not, perhaps, be expedient to make a drastic change in policy and do away with compensation altogether.

The Planning Commission’s first Panel on Land Reforms had recommended that compensation should be fixed at about 25 per cent of the market value. This recommendation was not, however, included in the Second Plan document. The Panel on Land Reforms which reported on the eve of the Third Plan, was in favour of compensation being fixed at 40-50 per cent of the market value. While fixing compensation it is necessary to bear in mind its impact on the State finances and the capacity of the assignees of new land to pay the purchase price. Keeping all these factors in view and also considering that constitutionally it is not obligatory to pay compensation, it appears reasonable if compensation is fixed at a level not exceeding 25 per cent of the market value. Our suggestion is that for the first Rs. 20,000 compensation may be fixed at 25 per cent of the market value, for the next Rs. 20,000 at 20 per cent, for the next Rs. 40,000 at 15 per cent and for amounts beyond that at 10 per cent of the market value.

We do not, however, recommend that in the ceiling law the amount of compensation should be indicated as a per centage of the market value. The determination of market value for numerous plots of land will lead to serious practical difficulties and will also result in too heavy a burden on the administration. It is, therefore, suggested that the amount of compensation payable should be fixed as a multiple of land revenue or specific amounts should be indicated for different classes of land. While doing so it should, however, be ensured that the compensation payable does not generally exceed the proportion to

![image 647](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile647.png)

![image 648](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile648.png)

###### 302 Land Reforms in India



market value indicated in the preceding paragraph. The compensation may be paid in 20 annual instalments or the first Rs. 2,000 may be paid in cash and the balance in 20-year bonds carrying interest at 3 per cent.

Regarding the purchase price to be paid by the assignees of new land, it may be laid down that the instalment together with land revenue should not exceed 20 per cent of the gross produce. In this case also the amount to be realized should be fixed as a multiple of land revenue or specified amounts may be indicated for different classes of land. In view of the fact that most of the assignees of new land will be landless labourers, many of them may have difficulty in paying even the easy instalments suggested above. In some regions it may become necessary to allot land at concessional rates. It may not always be possible to ensure that the payments lo be made by the assignees would be adequate to cover the payment of compensation to land-owners. Fart of the burden may have to be borne by the State exchequer. This additional burden can be met by imposing a surcharge on agricultural income tax or on land revenue on bigger land owners paying land revenue over a certain minimum. After the payment of purchase price is completed the assignee should be required to pay only the normal land revenue.

![image 649](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile649.png)

![image 650](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile650.png)

Chapter 8

### Re-Distribution

While drawing up any scheme for the redistribution of land the following aspects of the matter need consideration.

- (1) The categories of people to whom allotment should be made and their inter se priority;
- (2) Whether allotment should be made to individuals or to cooperative farming societies of beneficiaries;
- (3) The area of land that should be allotted to each person;
- (4) The terms and conditions on which the allotment should be made; and
- (5) Supporting measures for ensuring the success of the scheme. Regarding the categories of persons to whom the surplus land


should be allotted the recommendation in the Second Five-Year Plan was: “In the settlement of lands acquired in consequence of the application of the ceiling, tenants displaced as a result of resumption of land for personal cultivation, farmers with uneconomic holdings and landless workers should receive preference".1

It was not indicated what the order of priority among these categories should be, nor was the term uneconomic holding defined. The State Governments have followed different practices in the matter of allotment of land. In some State laws the order of priority has been indicated. The order, however, is not the same in the different States. In Assam, Gujarat, Maharashtra and Mysore the highest priority has been given to tenants rendered landless consequent upon the resumption of their land by the landowners for personal cultivation. Madhya Pradesh, however, gives the highest priority to joint farming societies of agricultural labourers and landless persons. Allotment to individual agricultural labourers and landless persons is given priority in this State over allotment to displaced tenants. In Gujarat small holders are given a

![image 651](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile651.png)

![image 652](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile652.png)

304 Land Reforms in India

higher priority than landless persons and agricultural labourers, while in Maharashtra the small holders are placed after agricultural labourers and landless persons. However, in this State ex-servicemen are given a higher priority than agricultural labourers. In Punjab, preference is given to all ejected tenants or those liable to be ejected and agricultural workers come after small holders. In West Bengal there is a simple provision under which allotment is made only to persons who own no land or less than 2 acres and intend to bring the allotted land under personal cultivation. The latest Kerala Law provides for allotment of 87 \ per cent of the surplus land to landless agricultural labourers with the further stipulation that one half of that shall be assigned to landless agricultural labourers belonging to Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes residing in the same village or adjacent villages. The remaining 12^ per ccnt shall be assigned to small holders and other landowners who are not entitled to resume any land and who do not possess more than one acre of land.

There is justification in giving the highest priority to dispossessed tenants who have become landless. Where they are left with some land above a minimum of one or two acres, they should be treated on par with other small holders. The real question for consideration is whether the available land should be distributed to small holders with a view to increase the size of their holdings and make as many holdings as possible economic or whether the available land should be distributed to landless persons. It can be argued that the small holders have already some experience of cultivation and that from the point of view of promoting agricultural production it will be desirable to allot all the available land to them so as to raise as many holdings as possible to a reasonable size. The minimum size of an economic holding will depend upon soil and crop conditions, irrigation etc. Even so, it may perhaps be assumed that all holdings below 5 acres in any area are uneconomical. Seventy-Five per cent of the holdings in this country are below 5 acres in area and they account for only about 20 per cent of the cultivated area. Even with a scheme of redistribution more radical than what we have proposed, it will not be possible to allot additional land so as to raise a substantial number of these holdings to the economic level. Furthermore, the result of such a policy will be to help those who are

![image 653](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile653.png)

![image 654](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile654.png)

Appendix 305

already better off than the rural under-privileged. As has already been pointed out, in the Indian conditions the highest priority has to be given to a scheme to improve the social and economic condition of the landless. It is, therefore, suggested that all the available land should be distributed to landless agricultural labourers. Those who own land less than a low minimum, say one or two acres, which may be fixed taking into account the relevant local conditions and who have no other means of livelihood may be treated on par with the landless.

The next question is whether allotment should be made to cooperative societies of landless persons or to individuals. The Second Plan recommended that as far as possible land should be settled on cooperative lines. The experiment in co-operative farming has not been a success so far. A further difficulty is that the surplus land may be in small bits here and there and it may not be suitable for cooperative farming. It is suggested that where land is available in sizable blocks and landless labourers are willing to form cooperative farming societies and there is reasonable chance of the societies functioning satisfactorily, a settlement may be made with cooperative societies. But such cases will be very few indeed. Normally the allotment should be made to individuals.

Then the question arises as to what area should be allotted to each individual. Should land be allotted in such a manner as to constitute economic holdings or should small plots of land be allotted? In this matter also the States have followed different practices. While in some States allotment is made in blocks of 3 to 5 acres, in Kerala there is a limit of one acre and in West Bengal of 2 acres on new allotment. Considering that the main aim of the proposed redistribution is to raise the social and economic status of the landless labourers and that the available land will fall far short of the requirements, it is suggested that in areas of high density allotment may be made in blocks of one acre or of even lesser area Where the pressure on land is not great, blocks of 2 to 3 acres may be allotted.

The next question is concerned with the terms and conditions on which the allotment should be made. A reference has already been made to the recommendation in the Second Plan that the payment should be in easy instalments so that the burden on the assignee would not exceed the quantum of fair rent recommended in the Plan. In West

![image 655](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile655.png)

![image 656](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile656.png)

###### 306 Land Reforms in India

Bengal surplus land is allotted free to persons owning no land or less than 2 acres. In all the other States the allottees are required to make payment in instalments. Even if compensation is worked out as suggested in this paper and provision is made for payment of purchase price by the assignees in easy instalments spread over a period of 20 years, it is doubtful whether they will be in a position to make the payments regularly. There is the real danger of their being driven to seek the help of moneylenders to whom the land may be sold in due course. Bearing this in mind it is proposed that only about half the burden should fall on the assignee. The other half should be borne by the Government. This being a genuine welfare measure, it will be reasonable to include the expenditure in the State Plans. As suggested in the previous chapter the expenditure can be met by imposing a surcharge on agricultural income tax or land revenue.

Lastly, there is the question of taking necessary supporting measures to ensure the success of the scheme. The first measure proposed is that the surplus land should be allotted on the condition that the assignee will not have the right to sell or mortgage his land to any one other than a cooperative institution, a commercial bank or the Government for a period of 20 years. Such a measure is necessary to protect the assignees of new land from money-lenders and substantial landowners. But that alone will not, however, solve the problem. The assignees of new land will require short and medium term credit for starting agricultural operations. A number of avenues are available for extending credit to them. They can get short term crop loans form cooperative societies, the amount of loan depending upon the kind of crop grown and the area cultivated. Subject to a ceiling of ten times the share capital, a cultivator can get on an average about Rs. 300/- per acre from the cooperative society. He can also get medium term loans for purchase of bullocks, sinking of well, land reclamation etc. The loan can go up to 50 to 60 per cent of the value of the developed land. But in the conditions prevailing in almost all the States it is extremely doubtful if he will actually get the necessary cooperative credit. For most of the cooperative societies are dominated by well-to-do farmers and they are not likely to look kindly at the applications of the new assignees of land for loans. As most of the villages are located faraway from towns where bank branches have been opened, it is also doubtful

![image 657](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile657.png)

![image 658](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile658.png)

###### Appendix 307

whether these people can get necessary bank credit Special efforts will be necessary to extend adequate credit to them through the cooperative societies and commercial banks. Where these efforts fail Government should step in and resort to direct financing. Unless adequate arrangements are made for the provision of credit, the redistribution of land will not produce the desired results.

It is conceded that such a scheme of redistribution will not solve the problems of all the landless labourers. In combination with the other measures referred to earlier, it can, however, lead to a substantial improvement in the situation.

Note

1. Planning Commission, Second Five Year Plan, pp. 1975-453 PG/72.

![image 659](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile659.png)

![image 660](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile660.png)

Chapter 9

### Conclusion

As wc have seen, the imposition of ceiling on agricultural holdings in India is a case of inchoate policy, imperfect legislation and inefficient implementation. In the context of the socio-economic conditions obtaining in this country there is a strong case for a radical redistribution of land. Hie salient features of such a schemc would be the fixing of family ceiling at the level of one family holding, acquiring the surplus land without payment of compensation and distributing it among landless labourers and small holders owning less than a low minimum area of land. Given the prevailing political power structure, such a policy cannot be considered to be practicable. In the circumstances what is feasible is the amendment of the ceiling laws of the other States on the lines of the latest laws of Kerala and West Bengal, and serious efforts being made to implement the laws efficiently. The essential ingredients of the proposed policy are the lowering of the existing ceilings to the level of 10-15 standard acres in the States, enforcing the ceiling on family basis, doing away with most of the exemptions, fixing compensation at levels varying between 25 to 10 per cent of the market value of the land, distributing the surplus land in small blocks to the land-less and small holders owning less than a low minimum area of land and taking all necessary supporting measures to enable the new assignees to cultivate the land allotted to them.

To achieve the objective of ensuring a modicum of the bare necessities of life to the lower strata of the population and doing away with the glaring disparities and injustices in the Indian society such a programme of redistribution of land should be adopted in combination with a series of other measures. It is not possible to spell out all the

![image 661](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile661.png)

![image 662](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile662.png)

Appendix 309

necessary measures here. In passing it may be pointed out that in the agricultural sector itself equally important measures like protection of homestead tenants and share-croppers, a massive rural works programme backed by the mobilization of adequate additional resources and the channeling of a substantial portion of institutional credit to small farmers will be necessary. It is neither fair, nor politically feasible, to adopt in isolation progressive measures in one sector only, leaving the other sectors of the economy untouched. The array of policies for the other sectors should conceivably include a levelling down of disparities in income and wealth by effective taxation or other feasible measures, curbing conspicuous consumption and consciously developing a climate of austerity in all fields of activity, particularly in the matter of public expenditure. Undertaken as an integral part of such a comprehensive programme it will be less difficult to carry out the proposed redistribution of land.

There is little doubt that the proposed policy would be fiercely opposed by the elite, both rural and urban. Past experience of land reform legislation in general, and of ceiling laws in particular, affords little basis for optimism. In spite of the delineation of national policy in successive five year plans, periodic resolutions at political conventions and the frequent exhortations of the Central Government, tenants, particularly share-croppers, do not even now enjoy security of tenure in some parts of the country. We have seen how half-hearted was the enactment and implementation of ceiling laws.

Such being the actual state of affairs there exists only a slender chance of all the States accepting the proposed policy and implementing it efficiently. Even with the best will in the world, implementation will not be easy. There are formidable difficulties. Land records will have to be patiendy constructed in several States. Proforma transfers will have to be detected and nullified. To prevent evasion, local committees on which the landless labourers are adequately represented will have to be associated with the implementation of the law. Their social and economic status being what it is, it will not be easy to ensure that they are not bullied by influential landowners. The administrative machinery should be geared up by constant supervision and deterrent action should be taken

![image 663](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile663.png)

![image 664](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile664.png)

###### 310 Land Reforms in India

promptly against government servants who are found to have acted in collusion with persons trying to evade the law. All these difficulties can be overcome if at the political level the policy is accepted without any mental reservations whatsoever, sincere and dynamic ministers of high political stature head the State Revenue Departments and hand-picked civil servants arc entrusted with its implementation.

![image 665](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile665.png)

![image 666](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile666.png)

Appendix V-l

##### Report of The Task Force On Agrarian Relations Planning Commission, March 1973

Two Decades of Land Reform—a Brief Review

1.1 Introduction

Indian economic planning has laid considerable emphasis on land reform right from the First Five Year Plan. As set forth in the national plans, the objectives of the land policy are, firstly, to remove such motivational and other impediments to increase in agricultural production as arise from the agrarian structure inherited from the past, and secondly, to eliminate all elements of exploitation and social injustice within the agrarian system so as to ensure equality of status and opportunity to all sections of the population. These objectives were sought to be achieved by abolishing all intermediary interests between the State and the tiller of the soil, regulating rent, conferring on tenants security of tenure, and, eventually, ownership rights, imposing ceiling on agricultural holdings, distributing surplus land among the landless and the small holders, and bringing about the consolidation of holdings.

A state wise review of the progress of land reform may be seen in Appendix.I. A brief overall review of the programme is given in the following paragraphs.

###### \2Abolition of Intermediary Tenures

Immediately after Independence high priority was given to the abolition of intermediary tenures. Accordingly, every State promoted its own legislation for the abolition of intermediary tenures on payment of compensation, and by 1954, necessary legislation was adopted in all the States. The implementation of the enacted laws has since been practically completed. Only a few minor intermediary tenures still

![image 671](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile671.png)

![image 672](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile672.png)

###### 314 Land Reforms in India



remain to be abolished and efforts are afoot to put an end to them also. As a result of the abolition of intermediary tenures about 20 million

tenants arc estimated to have come into direct contact with the State.

Large areas of privately owned forest land, grazing land and culturable waste land vested in the State. Implementation of these reforms imposed a heavy administrative burden; financial burden was also heavy on some of the State Governments. Compensation payable to the ex-intermediaries has been computed at Rs. 600 crores out of which about Rs. 275 crores has been paid in cash and bonds.

###### 13 Reform of Tenancy

There had been leasing of land on a large scale even in areas where intermediary tenures did not obtain and subleasing where such tenures existed. The prevailing rates of rent were high and the tenants enjoyed little security of tenure. Three important guidelines were laid down in the Five-Year Plans for the reform of Tenancy. Firstly, rent should not exceed ^ to \ of the gross produce; secondly, the tenants should be accorded permanent rights in the land they cultivate subject to a limited right of resumption to be granted to landowners ; and, thirdly, in respect of non-resumable land, landlord-tenant relationship should be ended by conferring ownership rights on tenants. All States have enacted legislation to regulate the rent payable by cultivating tenants. Maximum rates of rent have been fixed at levels not exceeding those suggested in the Five-Year Plan in all States except Punjab, Haryana, Jammu & Kashmir, Tamil Nadu and the Andhra area of Andhra Pradesh. In Punjab and Haryana fair rent is 33^ per cent of the gross produce, in Tamil Nadu 33 J per cent to 40 per cent of the gross produce. The Andhra Pradesh State Assembly recently passed a Bill for reducing fair rent to the level of 30 per cent of the produce for irrigated land and 25 per cent for dry land. In Jammu and Kashmir, for tenants of landowners holding above 12\ acres of land fair rent is 25 per cent of the gross produce for wet land, and 33 \ per cent for dry land. However, for tenants of landowners who own less than 12\ acres of land fair rent is 50 per cent of the gross produce. Several States have

![image 673](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile673.png)

![image 674](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile674.png)

###### Appendix 315



enacted legislation for conferring security of tenure on tenants. Under the existing law the position of tenants, and particularly of share-croppers, continues to be insecure in Bihar, Tamil Nadu, the Andhra area of Andhra Pradesh, the Saurashtra area of Gujarat, Punjab and Haryana. Provisions have been made in several States to enable tenants to acquire ownership rights. Necessary legislation for this purpose is still to be enacted in Andhra Pradesh, Assam, Bihar, Haryana, Jammu & Kashmir, Punjab and Tamil Nadu. Thus, in several States in the matter of tenancy reform, legislation falls far short of the accepted policy. And what is even worse, the implementation of the enacted laws has been half- hearted, halting and unsatisfactory in large parts of the country. The legal protection granted to tenants has often been ineffective. In accordance with the policy laid down in the Second Five-Year Plan the laws enacted by several States provided for the resumption within certain limits of tenanted lands by land-owners for personal cultivation. The term ‘personal cultivation’ was wide enough to cover all cases of cultivation under the landowner’s own supervision or the supervision of a member of his family. The provision for resumption of tenanted land by land owners resulted in the ejectments of many tenants. Then there were large scale ejectment through the device of “voluntary surrenders”. In many cases, tenancies have been pushed underground and converted into ‘Naukamameh*. Even where the law provides for security of tenure, tenants have, in practice, found it extremely difficult to successfully claim tenancy rights because most of the leases, particularly crop-sharing arrangements, are oral and informal. Where tenancy is insecure, legal provisions regarding fair rent are useless and no tenant dares initiate action to get a fair rent fixed. This is so because the tenant who has the audacity to pray for fixation of fair rent faces the risk of certain ejectment. Thus the objective of ensuring fair rent and security of tenure still remains unattained in large parts of the country.

###### 1.4 Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings

Laws imposing ceiling on agricultural holdings have been enacted in all the States except the former Punjab areas of Haryana and Punjab, where the State Government has powers to settle tenants on lands held by owners in excess of the permissible limit. However, as a result of

![image 675](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile675.png)

![image 676](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile676.png)

###### 316 Land Reforms in India



the high level of ceiling, large numbers of exemptions from the law, malafide transfers and partitions, and poor implementation the results achieved have been meagre. So far only about one million hectares of land has been declared surplus. There are wide variations between different States with regard to the level of ceiling, unit of application, exemptions etc. In August 1971, the Central Land Reform Committee made certain recommendations with a view to bring about a measure of uniformity in the ceiling laws of the different States. In the Chief Ministers’ Conference on Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings held on 23rd July, 1972, various aspects of the ceiling laws were discussed and certain conclusions were reached with regard to the level of ceiling, unit of application, exemptions, compensation, distribution of surplus land etc. It was agreed that the amended ceiling laws should be enacted by 31st December, 1972 in the light of the conclusions of the Chief Ministers’ Conference and that all the amended laws should be included in the Ninth Schedule of the Constitution.

- 1.5 Distribution of Land Though the imposition of ceiling has not yielded much land for

redistribution, sizeable areas of culturable waste lands got vested in the State on the abolition of intermediary interests. Such lands always belonged to Government in ryotwari areas. About 5.9 million hectares of waste land and 0.5 million hectares of surplus land have so far been distributed to landless agricultural workers and small holders. (Appendices - II and III).

- 1.6 Consolidation of Holdings In India most of the agricultural holdings are not only small but


they are also widely scattered. Hence emphasis was laid on consolidation of holdings in the Five Year Plans. No law for effecting consolidation of holdings has yet been passed in the Andhra area of Andhra Pradesh, Kerala, Orissa, and Tamil Nadu. The laws of Gujarat, Madhya Pradesh and West Bengal provide for consolidation on a voluntary basis. The other States have passed necessary legislation for compulsory consolidation. By 1972 about 32.6 million hectares of land had been consolidated (Appendix-IV). The progress has been uneven in the different States. The work has been completed in Punjab and

![image 677](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile677.png)

![image 678](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile678.png)

###### Appendix 317



Haryana. Uttar Pradesh has made good progress and some headway was been made in Maharashtra and Himachal Pradesh. Not much has been done in the remaining States.

A major weakness of the programme was that consolidation was done without taking effective steps to ensure security of tenure to tenants, particularly share-croppers. As a result consolidation of holdings has led to large scale ejectment of insecure tenants. For one thing, when holdings were fragmented even a resident landowner found it difficult to cultivate personally all the plots of land constituting his fragmented holding and, therefore, perforce he had to lease out some portions of his holdings to share-croppers. After holdings were consolidated, the land-owner found it both feasible and profitable to personally cultivate the entire area and, therefore, he spared no efforts in getting rid of the share-croppers. Another aspect was that the relationship of share-cropping tenancy subsisted in respect of a specific plot of land easily identified in the field. Once consolidation was effected, the identity of the particular plot that the share-cropper had been cultivating was lost and he could be automatically ousted from the land. Thus consolidation of holdings has often turned out to be the coup de grace for the share-croppers who lead a precarious existence.

- 1.7 An Overall Assessment of Indian Land Reform A broad assessment of the programme of land reform adopted


since Independence is that the laws for the abolition of intermediary tenures have been implemented fairly efficiently, while in the fields of tenancy reform and ceiling on holdings, legislation has fallen short of the proclaimed policy and implementation of the enacted laws has been tardy and inefficient. With the abolition of intermediary interests the ownership of land became more broad-based and the erst-while superior tenants acquired a higher social status. It should not, however, be over-looked that as a result of the tenancy laws enacted in the decades prior to Independence the superior tenants had already been enjoying security of tenure and fixity of rent. It is a moot point whether the abolition of intermediary interests conferred any new economic benefits on the tenants. The programmes which could have led to a radical change in the agrarian structure and the elimination of some of the elements of exploitation in the agrarian system and ushered in a

![image 679](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile679.png)

![image 680](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile680.png)

###### 318 Land Reforms in India



measure of distributive justice were those of tenancy reform, ceiling on agricultural holdings and distribution of land to the landless and small-holders. As already pointed out these programmes cannot be said to have succeeded. Highly exploitative tenancy in the form of crop-sharing still prevails in large parts of the country. Such tenancy arrangements have not only resulted in the perpetuation of social and economic injustice but have also become insurmountable hurdles in the path of the spread of modem technology and improved agricultural practices. Thus the overall assessment has to be that programmes of land reform adopted since Independence have failed to bring about the required changes in the agrarian structure.

- 2.1 Lack of Political Will Enactment of progressive measures of land reform and their


cfficicnt implementation call for hard political decisions and effective political support, direction and control. In the context of the socio-economic conditions prevailing in the rural areas of the country, no tangible progress can be expected in the field of land reform in the absence of the requisite political will. The sad truth is that this crucial factor has been wanting. The lack of political will is amply demonstrated by the large gaps between policy and legislation and between law and its implementation. In no sphere of public activity in our country since Independence has the hiatus between precept and practice, between policy-pronouncements and actual execution, been as great as in the domain of land reform. With resolute and unambiguous political will all the other shortcomings and difficulties could have been overcome; in the absence of such will even minor obstacles became formidable road blocks in the path of Indian land reform. Considering the character of the political power structure obtaining in the country it was only natural that the required political will was not forthcoming.

- 22 Absence of Pressure From Below Land reform in India has essentially been a gift from a benign


government. The beneficiaries of land reform, particularly share-croppers and agricultural labourers, are weighed down by crippling social and economic disabilities. Except in a few scattered and localised pockets, practically all over the country the poor peasants

![image 681](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile681.png)

![image 682](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile682.png)

###### Appendix 319



and agricultural workers are passive, unorganised and inarticulate. TTie basic difficulty in our situation arises from the fact that the beneficiaries of land reform do not constitute a homogeneous social or economic group. The peasants may be divided into various groups on the basis of the size of the holding, security or insecurity of tenure, mode of payment of rent and the degree of their economic dependence on the landlords. These groups are not mutually exclusive. Many small peasants lease out a part of their land while some enlarge the size of their operational holdings by leasing in land from others. A number of small peasants engage agricultural workers for certain operations, while these peasants themselves earn wages as agricultural workers during some periods of the year. All these groups not only overlap. Often their economic interests are also in conflict. The mixed economic status of the different groups and their conflicting economic interests have rendered organized collective action very difficult. And finally, the multi-caste composition of the peasantry effectively rules out the possibility of the development of a common out-look and collective will. In the circumstances it is small wonder that there has been no insistent pressure from below, a pre-requisite for the effective implementation of land reform laws.

- 23 Administrative Organisation—Inadequate Policy Instrument In all the States the responsibility for the implementation of


measures of land reform rests with the revenue administration. Implementation of land reform is only one among its many functions. Traditionally, high priority is given to maintenance of public order, collection of land revenue and other regulatory functions. Land reform does not, therefore, get the undivided attention it needs. Even it if had not been overburdened with multiple duties, it is very doubtful if the administrative oganisation would have proved to be equal to the task of efficiently implementing the land reform laws. The attitude of the bureaucracy towards the implementation of land reform is generally luke-warm, and often apathetic. This is, of course, inevitable because, as in the case of the men who wield political power, those in the higher echelons of the administration also are substantial landowners themselves or they have close links with big landowners. The village functionaries like Patwaries, Karmacharies, Kamams, Shanbogs,

![image 683](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile683.png)

![image 684](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile684.png)

###### 320 Land Reforms in India



Talatis etc. are invariably petty landowners. Thsy are also under the sway of big land-owners. No state has taken necessary steps to forge a

-suitable administrative organization and keep it in proper trim by systematic in-service training and periodic orientation courses. Nor has any conscious effort been made to post able and dedicated men with faith in land reform to key positions in the administrative set-up. As a matter of fact there have been cases where administrators who tried to implement land reform laws honestly and efficiently were hastily transferred elsewhere. As a result, practically in every State, the administrative organization has proved to be an inadequate instrument for the speedy and efficient implementation of land reforms.

- 2.4 Legal Hurdles In a society in which the entire weight of Civil and Criminal laws,

judicial pronouncements and precedents, administrative tradition and practice is thrown on the side of the existing social order based on the inviolability of private property, an isolated law aiming at the restructuring of property rehtion in the rural areas has hardly any chance of success. And whatever little chance of success was there completely evaporated because of the loop-holes in the laws and protracted litigation. The land reform laws were defective in many ways; some loop-holes were deliberately built in, while others were the result of poor drafting. The formidable combination of tenancious landowners and ingenious lawyers found a godsend in a legal system which puts great store by procedural formalities. All possible avenues of appeal and revision would be exhausted before a single step could be taken to implement a law. Practically in every state protracted litigation has delayed and often frustrated, the implementation of land reform laws. Provisions in certain land reform laws for ousting the jurisdiction of Civil Courts, have no doubt, helped. But the writ jurisdiction of the High Courts continues to afford to the landowners an avenue for obstructing the speedy implementation of measures of land reforms.

- 2.5 Absence of Correct Up-Dated Land Records An important factor that has militated against the effective


implementation of land reform is the absence of correct, up-to-date record of rights. A programme that aims at the redistribution of income

![image 685](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile685.png)

![image 686](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile686.png)

###### Appendix 321



and wealth in the rural areas cannot succeed unless the beneficiaries can produce evidence of their rights. No amount of legislation will help the tenant in the court unless he can prove that he is in fact a tenant. This he can do only if there is a reliable and up-dated record of tenancies with presumptive evidentiary value. The position regarding record of tenancies, particularly in the matter of entries relating to rights of share-croppers, is not satisfactory anywhere in the country, and no record exists in some areas. The main reasons for this unsatisfactory state of affairs are the following:

- i) Some areas have never been cadastrally surveyed.
- ii) In some areas, where cadastral surveys have been out-of-date for a long time, no re-surveys have taken place.
- iii) In some cases no machinery of any kind existed for maintaining village records.
- iv) In some areas (such as the Zamindari areas) the machinery which kept records was private.
- v) Even where records are kept by Government revenue officials there is no uniform system.
- vi) Even official records may not be correct. The errors would be of two kinds with reference to their origin.


- (a) Bonaf.de errors arising out of lack of knowledge either of facts or of law on the part of the recorder.
- (b) Deliberate wrong recording from malafide motives.


The problem is largely administrative. The absence of up-to-date land records is a serious obstacle in the implementation of land reforms.

- 2.6 Some Weak Spots in the Programme The Indian land reform programme has suffered from a number of


weaknesses some of which are inherent in our federal democratic polity, while some could have been avoided. Under the Constitution the subject of land reform is in the State List and, therefore, the responsibility for the enactment of necessary legislation and its implementation rests with the State Governments. The regional tenurial variations and diversity in socio-economic and political conditions have brought in a measure of complexity and variety in the land reform laws

![image 687](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile687.png)

![image 688](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile688.png)

###### 322 Land Reforms in India



of the various States. The enacted laws have been implemented at varying levels of efficiency.

The programmes of land reform have been viewed so far in isolation from the mainstream of economic development. The main ingredients of the programme like abolition of intermediary tenures, tenancy reform and ceiling on agricultural holdings were treated as disjointed programmes and sought to be implemented as such. Consolidation of holdings was conceived of and undertaken without providing for village roads, irrigation and drainage channels, land shaping, soil conservation etc. An integrated approach would have yielded much better results.

Lack of financial support has plagued Indian land reform right from the beginning. No separate allocation of funds was made in the Five-Year Plans to finance land reform. Many States declined to include even expenditure on such essential items like preparation of record of rights (which would have qualified for plan assistance) in their plan-budgets. The State Plans which are nothing but aggregates of expenditure programmes hardly make any reference to land reform. Whatever funds were needed for financing land reform had to be provided in the non-plan budgets. The prevailing view both at the Centre and in the States being that all nor.-plan expenditure was evil and hence should be kept to the minimum, the programme of land reform did not get the necessary budget support in any of the States.

Against this background, it is hardly surprising that funds were not available to extend supporting services to beneficiaries of land reform. An agrarian reform programme can succeed only if it is accompanied by adequate arrangements for the supply of inputs, particularly credit on easy terms for the development and cultivation of land. Dominated as they are by well-to-do farmers, the Co-operative societies render little assistance to poor peasants and assignees of new land. Nor can such people hope to derive much benefit from the commercial banks. In the absence of adequate supporting facilities, the assignees have not been able to put to proper use the land allotted to them.

- 2.7 Deficiencies in Reporting System and Evaluation Even after two decades of land reform it is difficult to get reliable


and up-to-date data on various aspects of land reform. The reporting

![image 689](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile689.png)

![image 690](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile690.png)

###### Appendix 323



system is weak and irregular and the deficiency is rooted in the weakness of the Revenue administration and the absence of updated land records. There has been no systematic review of progress at periodic intervals. Not much attention has been paid to comprehensive concurrent evaluation. In the absence of concurrent evaluation, it has not been possible to identify obstacles and take timely remedial measures.

###### Some Policy-Issues

- 3.1 Broad Objectives In our country land is the chief source of gross national product,


employment, economic status and social prestige. The broad objectives of land policy should, therefore, be to increase agricultural productivity, promote fuller utilisation of available manpower and ensure distributive justice. Since a direct attack on poverty is the main goal of the Fifth Five Year Plan, the clear aim of land-policy should be to confer economic benefits on the rural poor who are below the poverty line as defined in the “Approach Paper”. The rural poor consist mainly of landless labour and small peasants. These two categories overlap since small peasants also work as agricultural labourers. All the programmes of land reform, particularly distribution of surplus and waste-land, tenancy reform and protection of homestead tenancies would greatly benefit these people. Hence in the field of land reform high priority should be accorded to the removal of gaps between policy and legislation and between law and its implementation. It is also necessary to settle certain policy issues and adopt a more comprehensive programme.

- 3.2 Impact of Public Investments and Modern Technology on the Agrarian Structure


Since Independence considerable public investments have been made in irrigation, rural electrification, community development, road building, agricultural extension etc. The investment in irrigation alone has been to the order of over Rs. 2,000 crores and this investment is estimated to have created an additional irrigation potential of about

![image 691](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile691.png)

![image 692](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile692.png)

###### 324 Land Reforms in India



10 million hectares. The benefits of these public investments have largely accrued to the bigger landowners who are not required to pay any betterment levy or even reasonable irrigation rates. The benefits of the recent break-through in agricultural production based on the adoption of modem technology have also gone mainly to the well-to-do farmers. One of the spectacular results of the heavy public investments in the agricultural sector and the spread of modem technology has been a widening of the disparities in wealth and income in rural areas. On top of this the unplanned spread of mechanisation threatens not only to accentuate the inequalities but also to reduce employment opportunities. In the result new social tensions are developing in the countryside. The policies for the future should be planned keeping these facts in view.

- 3.3 Special Measures for Command Areas of Major Irrigation Projects


Though successive Five-Year Plans recommended betterment levy on land irrigated by public irrigation projects, no State Government has so far made any serious efforts in this direction. With a view to recover part of the capital cost from beneficiaries and also with the objective of reducing disparities in wealth and income, betterment levy should be imposed on all beneficiaries. It is suggested that instead of being collected in cash the beneficiaries may be required to part with a portion of the land that has benefited from the project. The Raj Committee (Report of the Committee on Taxation of Agricultural Wealth and Income) has expressed the view that the imposition of betterment levy is not likely to be effective and that it is worth exploring the scope for setting up a Land Commission (as has been done in the United Kingdom recently) with powers to acquire, develop and resell land and associated property. Such a Commission may be useful for urban development and for promoting and financing certain types of local rural development as pointed out by the Raj Committee itself. It is, however, extremely doubtful whether it will be administratively feasible for a Commission to handle work relating to the acquisition and resale of agricultural property spread throughout the country. What we advocate is a simple provision under which the beneficiaries would be required to part with a portion of the land

![image 693](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile693.png)

![image 694](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile694.png)

###### Appendix 325



benefitted by the project. Persons owning land below a certain minimum, say 6 acres, may be exempted from the levy. On those who own over the prescribe minimum, a betterment levy at a progressive rate should be imposed. The enforcement of such a levy will make some irrigated land available for distribution. It will also ensure that part of the unearned increments that accrue to land-owners are mopped up for redistribution. In the command areas of large irrigation projects, particularly in the Eastern States, large areas of land remain uncultivated during the rabi and summer seasons because the land-owners find it difficult to personally cultivate their large holdings and the share-croppers consider it unattractive under the prevailing conditions of rack-renting to raise a second or third crop which calls for larger investments in inputs. Thus with a view to ensure the better utilization of the irrigation potential also, it is necessary to enforce efficiently the ceiling and tenancy laws within the command areas of large irrigation projects.

- 3.4 Distribution of Surplus Land So far about one million hectares of land has vested in the State

Governments on the enforcement of the ceiling laws and about half of it has been distributed. When the ceiling laws of all the States are amended in accordance with the latest guidelines more surplus land will become available. No accurate estimate is available of the area of additional surplus land that is likely to vest in the State Governments consequent upon further amendment of the ceiling laws. The State Governments have been requested to report their estimates. With regard to the question of distribution of surplus land it was agreed in the Chief Ministers’ Conference on Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings held on 23rd July, 1972 that while distributing surplus land priority should be given to the landless agricultural workers particularly those belonging to Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes. The State Governments should frame their rules with regard to the distribution of surplus land in the light of this policy decision.

- 3.5 Supporting Facilities A programme of large-scale distribution of land should go hand in


hand with an integrated programme of consolidation of holdings,

![image 695](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile695.png)

![image 696](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile696.png)

###### 326 Land Reforms in India



complementary public works and investment support. At present there is no effective arrangement for extending medium and short-term credit to assignees of new land. In the areas covered by S.F.D.A. and M.F.A.L. Schemes it may be possible to provide supporting facilities to assignees of new land and other beneficiaries of land reform by suitably redesigning these schemes. In other areas direct financing by the Government will be necessary.

- 3.6 Land to the Tiller It was pointed out in the Second Plan that the term ‘personal


cultivation’ may be said lo have three elements via., the risk of cultivation, personal supervision and personal labour. The Second Plan suggested that a person should be treated as cultivating the land personally only if he bears the entire risk of cultivation and himself supervises the agricultural operations or supervision is done by a •member of his family. The Plan also suggested that in order to ensure that such supervision is effective it should be accompanied by residence during the greater part of the agricultural season on the part of the owner or a member of his family in the village in which the land is situated or in a nearby village. As far as the existing holdings were concerned it was pointed out in the Plan that there might be difficulties in insisting upon an element of personal labour. It was, however, suggested that when a landowner was allowed to resume land for personal cultivation the question of providing for personal labour also might be considered. These suggestions made in the Plan have not been acted upon. The new Gujarat law stipulates that either the landowner or a member of his family must reside in the same village or a village within a distance of 30 kilometres.

An important question is whether the term ‘personal cultivation’ should be defined so as to make personal labour an essential element. If the slogan of “land to the tiller” is to be meaningful and honestly implemented, the land should certainly go to those who cultivate it, namely those who perform the various operations like ploughing, sowing, transplanting, harvesting etc. Conversely, persons who do not personally participate in these operations, should not be allowed to own land. We are, however, inclined to think that in the context of the social, economic and political conditions prevailing in the country such a law will not be enacted,and if enacted will not be implemented.

![image 697](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile697.png)

![image 698](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile698.png)

###### Appendix 327



What is feasible in our situation is to insist upon supervision either by the land-owner himself or a member of his family, the term family being defined as in the ceiling law. Such supervision would be possible only if the land owner or a member of his family resides in the same village or in an adjacent village. Furthermore, we would also suggest that in future transfer of agricultural land should be permitted only to persons who reside in the same village or an adjacent village. These provisions, if strictly enforced, will reduce the incidence of absentee ownership of land.

An allied question to be settled is whether or not tenancies should be permitted to be created in future. Past experience shows how difficult it is to regulate tenancies. The logic of development in the field of land reform lies in rights of ownership being conferred on all tenants. Leasing out should be permitted only in such rare cases like physical disability, service in the Defence forces etc. Even in such cases all contracts of tenancy should be in writing and should be for fixed periods and when the land-owner resumes the land a part of the tenanted land should be left with the tenant if he has no land of his own.

- 3.7 Special Problems of Tribal Areas The basic problem in the tribal areas are land alienation and

indebtedness. Many states have passed legislation for restoring to the tribals the land sold by them to non-tribals and for debt redemption. But the enforcement of these laws has been far from satisfactory. The loopholes in these laws should be plugged and steps should be taken to ensure effective enforcement of the enacted laws. Large parts of the tribal areas still remain to be cadastrally surveyed. It will be necessary to have a special programme of survey and settlement operations in tribal areas which should be undertaken as a Centrally-sponsored Plan scheme.

- 3.8 Financing of Land Reforms According to the present policy the only schemes of land reform


that qualify for inclusion in the Plan are those relating to consolidation of holdings and preparation of a record of rights. As pointed out earlier, several State Governments still provide for expenditure even on these

![image 699](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile699.png)

![image 700](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile700.png)

###### 328 Land Reforms in India



schemes in their Non-Plan budget. In the result lack of financial support has hampered the progress of land reform. Adequate financial provision should be made in the Fifth Five Year Plan for the preparation of record of rights, implementation of the ceiling and tenancy laws, investment support to assignees of new land and an integrated programme of consolidation of holdings. As far as preparation of record of rights is concerned the accent should shift from time-consuming survey and settlement operations to a simple programme of preparation of record of tenancies to be completed within a short period. Such a programme may be financed wholly by the Government of India. Unless up-to-date land-records are prepared it will not be possible to ensure the effective implementation of any measure of land reform or consolidate agricultural holdings. The cost on the other schemes, namely survey and settlement operations, implementation of ceiling and tenancy laws, consolidation of holdings and investment support to assignees of new land, may be shared by the Central and State Governments.

- 3.9 Broad Outline of A Comprehensive Programme The distribution of surplus and waste-land and the reform of


tenancy will benefit only a section of the rural poor. The bulk of them will derive tangible benefits only if employment opportunities are expanded to provide them with employment at reasonable wages. In order to provide additional employment, the programme of rural works which has already been taken up on a large scale, should be continued and even expanded and it should be dovetailed into a well-conceived programme of consolidation of holdings. Besides enlarging employment opportunities, necessary steps should also be taken to raise agricultural wages so that the agricultural workers get a fair share of the increased production. It is also necessary to devise appropriate measures of taxation with a view to garner a share of the increased prosperity in agriculture for the State Exchequer and also for controlling the spread of mechanisation of agriculture on the right lines. Besides adopting a suitable fiscal policy, other measures may also have to be devised. In this respect a selective approach should be adopted, the main consideration being that only such mechanisation should be permitted as would lead to increase in the utilization of labour. A

![image 701](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile701.png)

![image 702](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile702.png)

###### Appendix 329



package of policies designed on these lines efficiently implemented may stop the further widening of disparities in the rural sector.

- 4.1 Some Suggestions for Improving Implementation We have given considerable thought to the question of setting up a


suitable administrative organization for the implementation of land reform. We propose to make certain specific suggestions on the basis of our experience. It should, however, be clearly understood that the mere setting up of an efficient administrative machinery will not by itself lead to any substantial improvement unless the political and economic constraints operating against the programme are removed.

###### 42 Main Jobs in Implementing Ceiling and Tenancy Laws

In order to pin-point the failings of the administrative machinery in properly implementing land reform measures, it may be helpful to list the jobs involved. Let us consider two aspects of the programme, namely, (a) ceiling on holdings and distribution of surplus land and (b) security of tenure and prevention of rack-renting.

The jobs involved in implementing a ceiling law are: (a) identification of the persons or the families having land above the ceiling in the State; (b) locating and identifying all the plots of land in the possession of every such person/family both according to the record of rights and in reality. This will involve tracing benami or clandestine transfers of property made to friends, relatives or other persons, real or imaginary, through fictitious or collusive transactions; (c) initiating legal processes for vesting of all surplus land including land held benamix(d) taking over possession of land vested after completing the required quasi-judicial and administrative processes; (e) distribution of surplus land; (0 providing supporting services including credit to assignees of surplus land and taking effective action to prevent the transfer of newly assigned land to the old trinity of landlord-moneylender-trader or to the emerging class of progressive cultivators - the ‘nouveau riche’ of rural India.

Items of jobs necessary for ensuring security of tenure and prevention of rack-renting are the following: (a) recording the names of al persons who hold land on the basis of written or oral leases, including share-croppers, in the record of rights; (b) ensuring that not

![image 703](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile703.png)

![image 704](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile704.png)

###### 330 Land Reforms in India



more than the legally stipulated share of crop is taken from the share-croppers by the land-owners; (c) ensuring that no ejectment takes place either on the basis of “voluntary” surrender documents like “Fala Gutte Karar”, ,,Istafanama,\ Khetmazdur kabuliat” etc, or through other extra- legal or illegal methods; (d) ensuring inheritance to the heirs of the share-croppers on their death where law provides for it; (e) providing supporting services including credit to share- croppers to free them from the clutches of land-owners and money-lenders.

###### 43. Impediments of Effective Implementation

The most important administrative impediment to enforcement of both the ceiling and tenancy laws is the lack of a proper record of rights. In the ryotwari areas records of rights are prepared mainly for the purpose of assessment and collection of revenue. In the ex-zamindari areas such records were prepared to accord tenancy rights to the different grades of tenants and tenure-holders as they existed at that time. Hence the basic record of rights of the zamindari areas are, in many ways, much more informative for the purpose of implementation of land reforms than the purely assessment-orientd record of rights of the ryotwari areas. In both the cases, however, it is very difficult to find out from the record of rights itself how lands above the ceiling have been dispersed clandestinely to evade the expected ceiling law. As the ceiling laws were enacted after protracted deliberations, the landowners got plenty of time to anticipate the shape of things to come and to set about circumventing the prospective ceiling laws.

###### 4.4 Steps for Better Implementation of Celing Laws

Big landowners usually have lands in different parts of a district or even in different districts of the same State. Basic records being village-wise, it is difficult to find out from the records, as they are, the total area of land held by a landowner in a district or a State. The bigger the administrative unit for which information is proposed to be compiled, the greater is the difficulty. The only fool-proof method of compiling complete information about the land held by such big landowners is to prepare card indices of all the names appearing in the record of rights for any given geographical area and to consolidate these indices in alphabetical order. More sophisticated systems of card

![image 705](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile705.png)

![image 706](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile706.png)

###### Appendix 331



punching can also be tried for the same purpose. The first step is to produce a landowners’ ledger for the district showing the exact area of land held by every person in the district as noted in the record of rights. Thereafter, a ledger covering the entire State in respect of the bigger land owners should be prepared. These ledgers will not, naturally, reveal the area of land held in a clandestine manner by the landowners. Even so, this would be a good starting point for further enquiry.

Preparation of ledgers is, no doubt, a stupendous job. But then, the effort is quite rewarding and there is no other satisfactory method available to us. If it is not possible to prepare such a ledger for the whole State or even a whole district straightway, card indices may be prepared for selected areas known to have concentration of big land-owners. Intelligence should be collected locally in respect of big landowners and after identifying such landowners, genealogical trees should be prepared. The lands held by different members of such big families now and at the time of the last settlement may be compared to find out how the lands have been dispersed among the various members of the family. The process of finding out the exact area of land held by any particular big landowner involves tracing out benami transactions. Benami transfers are not transfers at all. Their real nature can only be ascertained by local enquiry. Whether or not the transfers are fake would be clear only if sufficient evidence regarding the effective possession of the land and the utilisation of the usufruct could be gathered by local enquiry. Land-owners often change the classification of land so as to bring it under exempted categories like, orchards, tank-fishery, sugarcane plantation, etc. They also resort to creation of religious and charitable trusts which are generally within the exempted category. If the ceiling law provides for annulling bogus religious/charitable Ousts, necessary action should be taken after holding detailed enquiries. Where any large scale change of classification has taken place spot resurvey and consequential correction of records of rights have to be undertaken. The land reform administrator is quite justified in looking with suspicion on all recent transfers made by the landowners. Some of the sham transfers are ratified through collusive suits filed in the Civil Courts. It is almost impossible under the existing law and procedure to annul the transfers ratified through such collusive suits. By this process large areas of

![image 707](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile707.png)
