![image 708](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile708.png)

###### 332 Land Reforms in India



surplus land arc being kept in the effective possession of the big landowners though nominally recorded in other person’s names. The land reform law should provide for the re-opening of such cases which should be disposed of by Land Reforms Tribunals to be manned by senior revenue officials and not by judicial officers.

- 4.5 Steps for Better Implementation of Tenancy Laws Problems that have to be faced in providing security of tenure to all

categories of tenants and preventing rack-renting are also rather intractable. Even where the administrative machinery was generally keen to record the names of share- croppers in the record of rights, the response from the beneficiaries was found to be inadequate because they were most reluctant to annoy the landowners on whom they were dependent in many ways. Mere recording of name in the record-of-right by itself, will not guarantee security of tenure. There is nothing to prevent a determined group of landowners from adopting “other methods” to throw out physically from the land the share-croppers who have the temerity to get themselves recorded as tenants. It has been found that very often the landowners short circuit the provisions of the tenancy law affording protection to share-croppers by having recourse to the preventive sections of the Code of Criminal Procedure or to litigation in Civil Courts. The basic problem is how to increase the bargaining and staying power of the share-croppers. Here peasants’ organizations, kisan sabhas and the like, have a very important and significant role to play. Experience shows that where the

share-croppers could build some such organisation with a modicum of

militancy, they could, for sometime, resist the onslaught of the landowners. But in most cases the resistance was short-lived because the share-croppers could not withstand for long the economic and social sanctions that were applied against them by the landowners.

- 4.6 Need for Organisation of Beneficiaries and Supporting Services In our view a certain degree of politicisation of the poor peasantry


on militant lines is a pre-requisite for any successful legislative-administrative action to confer rights and privileges on them. Otherwise, there is always the possibility of such conferment of rights not only becoming illusory, but also turning out to be positively

![image 709](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile709.png)

![image 710](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile710.png)

###### Appendix 333



harmful as the vast majority of the beneficiaries would"succumb to the pressure of the landowners and would relinquish their rights and privileges either “voluntarily” or through open and naked coercion. Even if a militant peasants’ organisation exists, tenancy reform will not succeed unless it is accompanied by massive economic support to the share-croppers through institutional sources. Experience in this respect has been extremely unfortunate. An analysis of the situation in West Bengal where share-cropping is prevalent on a very wide scale shows that out of an estimated number of 2.2 million share-croppers, only 0.8 million have been recorded. The nationalised banks, as far as figures could be collected, have financed only 111 pure share-croppers for one crop season in 1971-72. The existing financial institutions show a definite bias in favour of the viable and efficiently managed farm units. The basic reality of the situation today is that the share-croppers are very largely at the mercy of their landowners for their sheer existence. In such a state of utter dependency it is futile to expect any betterment of their conditions merely through legislative and administrative measures. The goodwill of the legislators and the sympathy of the administrators would not carry the share-croppers far without massive doses of financial support from institutional sources or directly from Government.

- 4.7 Outline of Proposed Administrative Set-Up An efficient administrative machinery operating at the village,


tehsil, district and State levels would be needed to attend to the above mentioned tasks. A question will naturally be asked whether the present district administration is adequately equipped for undertaking this job. A simple answer to this question might be that if we increase the manpower at the disposal of the Collector, he will do the job just as he is discharging so many other important functions. But this answer ignores the crucial point that land reform cannot, in fairness, be compared with the other development programmes like sinking of tubewells or digging minor irrigation canals or setting up primary schools. Measures of land reform which affect one way or the other almost the entire rural population of the country and which cannot but engender formidable forces of opposition, have to be carried out with the fanatical zeal of a single track mind. Hence, it is absolutely

![image 711](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile711.png)

![image 712](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile712.png)

334 Land Reforms in India

necessary to have an alternative administrative set-up exclusively to look after land reform. What should be the Collector’s role in land reform and what should be his relations with the new organisation are matters to be decided by each State in the light of the local conditions.

We would suggest that a land reform organisation parallel to the district revenue administration, manned by hand-picked administrators may be set up for a short period to perform the functions already enumerated. At the State level there should be a Land Reforms Commissioner with the rank of the First Member of the Board of Revenue functioning directly under the Minister of Land Reforms. At the district level there should be a District Land Officer with the same seniority as that of a District Collector. There should be officers of appropriate seniority at the lower levels also. As we have already pointed out, the village level functionaires like Patwaris, Kamams, Karamcharies etc. have been a serious impediment to the implementation of land reforms. We are, therefore, of the view that no staff below the rank of a Revenue Inspector or-Circle Inspector should be entrusted with the work of land reform at the lowest level. All the staff needed for the administration of land reforms may be drawn from the general pool of officers of the General Revenue Administration. The creation of a separate organisation charged with the exclusive responsibility of implementing land reform measures with direct accountability to its own hierarchy right up to the Cabinet Minister, is likely to give it the desired momentum and required orientation. Only well-paid staff who can reasonably be expected to be above temptations should be entrusted with land reform work. Adequate attention needs to be given to the in-service training of the field officers. Training should be imparted in regional centres through intensive field experience and inter-State visits.

- 4.8 Land Reforms Tribunals Another important suggestion that we wish to make is that the


judiciary should not be involved at any stage in the implementation of land reforms. The general pattern of Indian laws is to provide for judicial sciutiny of all executive action. This practicc should be done away with for various reasons. In the first place, civil litigation by its very nature is time consuming and dilatory. Secondly, the outcome of

![image 713](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile713.png)

![image 714](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile714.png)

###### Appendix 335



court action depends on the weight of evidence that is produced. The stronger party can always produce the required evidence through bait, bluff and other kindred means against which the weaker side has hardly any chance of success. Thirdly, the entire burden of the civil and criminal law, judicial pronouncements and precedents, being in favour of the protection of private property, it becomes rather difficult for the judiciary to take a different view on broad social issues. As a result an anti-status quo law does not always get the required support from the judiciary. Stages of appeal can be built into the administrative set-up itself. Appeals from the decisions of a lower ranking officer should be heard not by the immediate superior but by an officer superior to the next higher rank. The Land Reforms Commissioner will have the power of revising any order either on motion of the affected parties or on his own motion. We would also suggest the establishment of special itinerant courts which will bring justice to the door of the poor people. Such courts are likely to take decisions after the proper understanding of local conditions, rights and customs. The itinerant courts should be purely administrative tribunals.

The ousting of the jurisdiction of the Civil Courts will not be enough. Resourceful landowners have succeeded not only in delaying, but even in thwarting progressive measures of land reform by filing writ petitions in the High Courts and Supreme Court. We are, therefore, of the view that the Constitution should be amended to bar the Writ jurisdiction of the High Courts and the Supreme Courts so far as land reform laws are concerned.

Even after these steps are taken, it will still not be possible to ignore the hazards of litigation as far as the beneficiaries of land reform are concerned. We would, therefore, suggest that free legal assistance should be given to the beneficiaries of land reform particularly to share-croppers where their rights are endangered by litigation started by landowners.

Strategy for the Fifth Five-Year Plan

5.1 Some Distortions to be Set Right

As far as land reform is concerned, while some refinement in policy as suggested may be attempted, what is more important is to

![image 715](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile715.png)

![image 716](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile716.png)

###### 336 Land Reforms in India



remove the existing gap between acceptc olicy and legislation, and between the enacted laws and their implementation. One point needs special mention. Of late there has been a levival of public interest in land reform. Unfortunately, this renewed interest is largely confined to the ceiling laws. The equally important field of tenancy reform, particularly the question of protecting share-croppers and homestead tenants, has been neglected. This distortion has to be set right by according high priority to a programme of affording complete protection to share-croppers and home-stead tenants. The following guidelines may be given to the State Governments regarding land reforms:

###### 5.2 Loopholes in Existing Laws to be Plugged

The gaps and weaknesses in the existing tenancy laws as indicated in the Position Paper (vide Appendix) should be removed so as to ensure complete security of tenure and conferment of ownership rights on cultivating tenants and share-croppers according to a time-bound programme.

###### 5 3 Ceiling Laws to be Amended According to Guidelines

The ceiling laws should be amended according to the national guidelines based on the conclusions reached in the Chief Ministers’ Conference on Ceiling on Agricultural Holdings held on 23rd July, 1972. The amended ceiling laws should be included in the Ninth Schedule to the Constitution.

- 5.4 Distribution of Surplus Land The surplus land should be distributed expeditiously, say, within

12 months of the land vesting in Government. The distribution of surplus land should be accompanied by timely supply of inputs in adequate quantities and investment support. The SFDA and MFAL schemes may be redesigned in order to assist the assignees of new land and other beneficiaries of land reform. In areas not covered by such schemes, direct financing by Government will be necessary.

- 5.5 Record of Rights The highest priority should be given to a comprehensive


![image 717](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile717.png)

![image 718](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile718.png)

###### Appendix 337



programme of preparation and maintenance of record of tenancy. Where there is no statute for the preparation of such records, the necessary law should be enacted. It is necessary to design and execute a quick programme for the up-dating of record of rights, particularly the recording of the rights of share-croppers. The highest priority should be given to the removal of lacunae in the enacted tenancy laws and their strict enforcement. Consolidation should follow the conferment of security of tenure on tenants.

- 5.6 Special Attention to Tribal Areas The loopholes in the laws of land-alienation and debt redemption

as applicable to the tribals should be plugged and the administrative machinery should be strengthened for the enforcement of those laws. The cadastral survey of the tribal areas should be completed quickly and it should be treated as a Centrally sponsored scheme to be financed exclusively by the Government of India.

- 5.7 Consolidation of Holdings All tenants including share-croppers should be identified, their

rights should be recorded and permanent and heritable rights should be conferred on them before land consolidation operations are started.

While land consolidation is undertaken, the land of small- holders and the surplus and waste land available for distribution to new assignees should be consolidated in compact blocks. Such a step will facilitate the adoption of a policy of directing the flow of future public investments in irrigation and land development for the benefit of the under-privileged.

- 5.8 Publicity The beneficiaries and even Government servants charged with the

implementation of land reform are not always familiar with the provisions of the law. An intensive publicity campaign should be undertaken with a view to educating the public and Government servants.

- 5.9 Administrative Set-up A new administrative machinery should be set-up as suggested in


![image 719](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile719.png)

![image 720](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile720.png)

###### 338 Land Reforms in India



paras 4.7 and 4.8 and the personnel should be given necessary training to implement the laws efficiently. If the administration shows the same vigour, alertness and efficiency in the implementation of land reform laws as it usually evinces in the maintenance of law and order, a great deal can be achieved. This is possible if there is dynamic, firm and unambiguous political direction and the work is entrusted to handpicked officers.

- 5.10 Adequate Plan Allocation As proposed by us earlier in this report, adequate financial

provision should be made in the Fifth Five-Year Plan for financing land reforms.

- 5.11 Association of Beneficiaries With Implementation The existence of militant organisations of tenants and landless


labourers would have facilitated the better implementation of land reforms. Such organisations cannot, however, be built up simply by issuing executive instructions. In the absence of strong peasants’ organisations, the next best arrangement is to associate committees of beneficiaries, with the implementation of land reforms. We suggest that Committees of beneficiaries be established at the village and block levels to advise on the implementation of all measures of land reform and provision of supporting facilities to beneficiaries of land reform. All landless labourers, share-croppers and small holders of the village owning less than 2 acres of land may elect 5 to 7 members from among themselves to constitute the village Committee. Members of each Village Committee may elect one among themselves to the Block Committee.

![image 721](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile721.png)

![image 722](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile722.png)

Appendix V-2

##### Letter dated 31.12.1972 from Shri. P.S. Appu Commissioner, Kosi Division, to Shri P.K.J. Menon, Chief Secretary, Bihar

J.M. Lyngdoh joined as District Magistrate, Pumea in April, 1972. He has been running the district well. He is taking keen interest in the Rahi Drive, particularly the bamboo boring programme. The results achieved have been impressive. He has tried to exercise control over relief operations, which, quite naturally, has not made him exactly popular with some public men who have developed a morbid interest in the lavish spending of public funds in the name of scarcity relief.

- 2. What has impressed me most is the effective action he has taken to extend to the bataidars the protection to which they are entitled under the law. Ever since I took over as Commissioner, I have been impressing on all my Collectors that every effort should be made to uphold the rights of bataidars. Lyngdoh initiated necessary action in good time to maintain law and order during the harvesting of the paddy crop. On the eve of the harvest season, armed force was deployed at strategic places and clear instructions were given to the magistrates and police officers that besides maintaining order they should also uphold the tenancy law. In a couple of cases in which big landowners forcibly harvested the crops grown by bataidars deterrent action was taken against the aggressors. This had a very salutary effect and the harvest season passed off peacefully throughout the district. Thus this year the district administration of Pumea under Lyngdoh’s leadership succeeded, perhaps for the first time in recent years, in ensuring that those who sowed would also reap.
- 3. In all the measures that Lyngdoh initiated to protect bataidars, he received the loyal and wholehearted support of Tarkeshwar Prasad,


![image 723](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile723.png)

![image 724](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile724.png)

###### 340 Land Reforms in India



Subdivision^ Officer, Pumea Sadar. Quite naturally the powerful landowners of Pumea have been carrying on a whispering campaign against Lyngdoh and Tarkeshwar Prasad. They are probably trying their best to get both the officers transferred and have pliable men posted to Pumea. Tarkeshwar Prasad has already been promoted to the rank of Additional Collector and posted at Pumea as Deputy Director, Food. It is a matter of considerable importance that an upright and impartial officer of proven ability is posted as S.D.O., Pumea Sadar.

4. A couple of weeks ago I heard a rumour that Lyngdoh would be

transferred. I dismissed it as the wishful thinking of the landowning gentry. However, during the last two days I happened to hear from more than one source that he was likely to be transferred soon. People have even named his successor. It will, indeed, be a great pity if the vested interests are allowed to have their way. Lyngdoh’s premature transfer from Pumea will utterly demoralize upright officers who discharge their duties fearlessly in strict conformity with the law. His transfer at this juncture will also adversely affect the Rabi Campaign and the drive we are organizing for the collection of rent, loans and other government dues. I still hope the rumour is baseless. I am, however, bringing these facts to your notice so that Government may not be in the dark in case Lyngdoh’s transfer is under consideration.

Wishing you a happy new year,

###### Letter dated 20.1.1973 from Shri PJS. Appu, Commissioner, Kosi Division, to Shri P.K J . Menon, Chief Secretary, Bihar

I am to invite a reference to the correspondence resting with your D.O. letter no.5/CCS dated January 2, 1973 about the transfer of J.M. Lyngdoh from the district of Pumea. Frankly, that letter was a great disappointment to me. For a while I wondered whether I should again take up the matter with you. After pondering over all aspects of the question for a couple of days I came to the conclusion that there was no point in entering into further correspondence on the subject. For one thing, with a habit of mind formed in the course of over two decades in the Civil Service of the country I readily persuaded myself to believe

![image 725](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile725.png)

![image 726](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile726.png)

###### Appendix 341



that it was, after all, the government’s prerogative to post whomsoever they liked wherever they pleased. For another, knowing as I did the circumstances in which the transfer was decided upon and the character of the prevailing political power structure, I felt it would be entirely futile to take up the matter again. So I left it there.

- 2 .1 was at Pumea on the 12th and 13th of January and again I have

been camping here since the 17th to inspect the Collectorate and the Subdivisional courts. Quite a few interesting developments have come to my notice during my stay. I consider it my duty as Divisional Commissioner to apprise you of all these developments.

- 3. To put these developments in perspective, I shall first touch


upon the general background, then briefly deal with the developments in the recent past, and finally, give an account of the incidents which are primarily the subject matter of this letter. The district of Pumea has a long record of agrarian disputes. The salient features of the agrarian structure of the district are extreme concentration in the ownership of land, absentee land lordism with all its accompanying evils and widespread share-cropping characterized by insecurity of tenure, rack renting, and other outrageous iniquities. Before the taming of the Kosi, Pumea was subjected to the ravages of that river. Land was of little value and agricultural yields were among the lowest in the country. In the inhospitable and unhealthy climate of the region agricultural operations could be carried on only by indigent share-croppers. In most cases the share-croppers had also reclaimed the land. A substantial per centage of the share-croppers were members of the Scheduled Tribes, mainly Santhals and Oraons who had migrated decades ago from Santhal Parganas and Chhotanagpur. Thanks to the completion of the Kosi project and the introduction of modem technology, agricultural yields have gone up in recent years. Naturally, land values have also appreciated greatly, in some cases the range of appreciation being as much as from about Rs 50 to Rs 5,000 or so per acre. The period of spectacular improvement in agricultural productivity and land values was also marked by legislative and administrative efforts to protect the rights of share-croppers. These two developments prompted the land owners to do everything in their power to get rid of the share-croppers. Where the share-croppers were members of the Scheduled Castes, the land owners found it easy to achieve their objective as little resistance

![image 727](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile727.png)

![image 728](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile728.png)

###### 342 Land Reforms in India



was offered by the share-croppers. The tribals were, however, better organized and being a proud people they did not easily yield to threats. This led to frequent clashes between share-croppers and landowners. Some of these clashes were of a serious nature in which even fire arms were extensively used. The high water mark of the protracted, but unequal, strife between the land owning classes and tribal sharecroppers was reached in the ghastly incident that occurred in village Rupaspur on 22.11.1971 when 14 Santhals were brutally murdered in broad day light and the entire village was reduced to ashes. The incident was fresh in my mind when I took over as the Commissioner of Kosi Division because I happened to accompany the Union Minister of State for Home Affairs, Sri R.N. Mirdha who visited Pumea during the last week of December 1971 following a furore in Parliament. Immediately before assuming charge as Divisional Commissioner I had spent over two years as Land Reforms Commissioner in the Ministry of Agriculture and the Planning Commission. In that assignment 1 had occasion to familiarise myself with all aspects of land reform in different parts of the country. I had reached the conclusion that the strict enforcement of tenancy laws, particularly the protection of sharecroppers, was the most important aspect of land reform. Accordingly, when I took over as Commissioner I resolved in my mind that the entire administrative machinery should be geared up for the strict enforcement of the tenancy laws and the protection of sharecroppers. In the very first Divisional meeting that I convened on October 9, 1972 I impressed upon the District Magistrates that this matter should be accorded the highest priority and that every effort should be made to afford to share-croppers the protection that they are entitled to under the law of the land.

4. This was the background against which Lyngdoh initiated

effective action for upholding the rights of sharecroppers. He deputed magistrates with armed force in good time at all places where trouble was apprehended. Clear instructions were given to the magistrates and Police officers that their duty was to ensure that those who cultivated the land would harvest the crop and that the provisions of the Code of Criminal Procedure should not be wrongly and unjustly used for harassing poor share-croppers. These unambiguous instructions and the deployment of magistrates and armed force at strategic places played a

![image 729](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile729.png)

![image 730](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile730.png)

###### Appendix 343



significant role in affording protection to share-croppers during the recent harvesting season. Another equally important factor which led to the harvest season passing off peacefully was the prompt and effective action taken in a couple of cases in which big landowners were involved. The first occurrence took place in village Sorkahi of P.S. Barhara Kothi on 20.11.72. The land belonged to one of the most influential landlords of Pumea. The allegation was that the landlord’s men, numbering over 300, variously armed, forcibly harvested the standing paddy crop which had been grown by share-croppers. When the aggrieved share-croppers reached the Police Station to lodge First Information Report, as chance would have it, Tarkeshwar Prasad, Subdivisional Officer, Pumea Sadar and the Dy. Superintendent of Police, Dhamdaha were present there. The S.D.O. and the D S.P. immediately proceeded to the place of occurrence. Their enquiry revealed that after thorough preparation the landlords’ men had forcibly harvested and removed standing paddy crop from four plots of land which had been cultivated by share-croppers. As is well known, this landlord owned several thousand acres of land and anticipating the enactment and enforcement of the Ceiling Law, he had effected benami transactions in favour of his relations, dependents and others. The land involved in the dispute is also held benami in this manner. The S.D.O’s enquiry revealed that several share-croppers who had cultivated the land in question had not succumbed to the pressure tactics employed by the landlord. The enquiry also revealed that the forcible harvesting of the paddy crop was done at the instance of the landlord. A case was instituted against the landlord’s men under section 395 of the Indian Panel Code. A proceeding under section 107 CrPC was also instituted against the landlord and others. Furthermore, the landlord was directed to deposit his fire arms in the police station as harvesting was in full swing and there was the apprehension that fire arms might be misused. The action taken in this case had a very salutary effect throughout the district and the entire harvesting season passed off peacefully. This was probably the first time when effective action was taken against one of the big landlords of Pumea.

5.' There was yet another incident in which a powerful landlord of Pumea had occasion to feel annoyed with the local administration. This incident which occurred in village Dibra of P.S. Barhara Kothi was the

![image 731](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile731.png)

![image 732](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile732.png)

###### 344 Land Reforms in India



offshoot of keen rivalry between two factions of the village. One faction led by Moti Singh, the Mukhia of Dibra Gram Panchayat instigated some Santhals to institute a false case against the members of the other faction alleging that the houses of the Santhals had been burnt down and their property looted by the accused. On the basis of that information, case no. 11 (11) 72 of Barhara Kothi P.S. under section 395/436 I.P.C. was instituted. In this case also the S.D.O. and D.S.P. visited the spot. Their enquiry revealed that a false case had been instituted with the objective of wreaking vengeance against the enemies of Moti Singh . Accordingly, a case U/S 153A/436/120B was instituted against Moti Singh and others, numbered as case no. 13(11) 72 of Barhara Kothi P.S. The above-mentioned Moti Singh happens to be the protege of one of the big landowners of Pumea. When Moti Singh was arrested the Second Officer of Pumea Sadar, who was holding court on that day granted provisional bail even without waiting for the receipt of police papers. A perusal of the order sheet makes it clear that the magistrate acted in great haste. It is not improbable that in taking the decision the magistrate failed to insulate himself from the influence of some of the powerful land owners of Pumea. Subsequently the Subdivisional Magistrate looked into the matter and recorded a detailed order rescinding the order for grant of provisional bail. I have gone through the order passed by the Subdivisional Magistrate and I am satisfied that it is in conformity with the well established principles of law.

6. In the past the big landlords of Pumea invariably had cordial and

friendly relations with men in charge of the district administration. When they found that the District Magistrate and the S.D.O. were fearless men who would uphold the majesty of law even at the risk of incurring the wrath of powerful vested interests, the big landlords of Purnea came to the conclusion that they should bend all their energies towards getting these officers transferred and having pliable men posted in their place. Intensive lobbying started at Patna with results which are by now well known. The land owners did not rest there. The District Magistrate being a member of the Indian Administrative Service, they knew that not much could be done to him except getting him transferred. So they decided to go all out for the blood of the Subdivisional Officer, who, being a member of the State Civil Service,

![image 733](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile733.png)

![image 734](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile734.png)

###### Appendix 345



did not enjoy the same degree of security of service as the District Magistrate. While moving for the kill they found a handy weapon in Governments’ Anti- Corruption Department presided over by S H., an open and implacable enemy of all progressive measures of land reform. One Pandey who is reported to be an Additional S.P. of the Anti Corruption Department met the S.D.O. on 10.1.1973 and wanted to see the records of Barhara Kothi P.S. case nos. 9(11) 72, 11(11) 72 and 13(11) 72 and the record of the proceedings under section 107 CrPC started against influential landlords. These are the cases discussed in the preceding paragraphs. The S.D.O. in good faith showed him the records. The Additional S.P. is reported to have made some uncalled for comments on the manner in which these cases were proceeded with and bail petition was disposed of. It was none of his business to do so. When he wanted to take some extracts from the case records, the S.D.O. advised him to obtain the District Magistrate’s permission. Interestingly enough, the Additional S.P., never approached the District Magistrate. I do not know under whose authority this Additional S.P. started the enquiry. Bearing in mind the facts narrated in the following paragraph I am inclined to think that, in all probability, he was acting under the direction of the Chairman of the State Anti- Corruption Board. Be it as it may, it was wrong on the part of the Additional S.P. to have looked into the case records without the prior permission of the District Magistrate. And his subsequent conduct in not approaching the District Magistrate in accordance with the S.D.O’s suggestion appears to be suspicious. I would suggest that suitable action should be taken against him.

7. On the 17th January, S.H. visited Katihar and Pumea. Though a

government jeep had been made available to him, the Chairman of the Anti-Corruption Board travelled from Katihar to Pumea in the comfort of a limousine lent by a local contractor with an unsavoury reputation. After eating a sumptuous lunch at the residence of a big landlord S.H. met the local officers, exhorted them to eschew corruption and expatiated on the pressing need to cultivate the virtues of probity and rectitude. While at Pumea he enquired from the Sadar S.D.O. about the above- mentioned cases. He remarked that the law had been enforced too rigorously against the landlords. Later on he addressed a poorly attended public meeting where one of the speakers complained that the

![image 735](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile735.png)

![image 736](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile736.png)

###### 346 Land Reforms in India



district administration had been unduly harsh in its attitude towards the landowners. Had S.H. not been elevated to the high position of Chairman of the Anti-Corruption Board, his moral fibre and personal conduct would have been of little interest to the public. He could have been treated as one among the many saffron-clad sadhus more interested in matters temporal then spiritual. But now that he presides over the Anti-corruption machinery of government, his conduct is of vital interest to the public. Government should, therefore, look into the matter and take appropriate action.

8. My purpose in writing this long, and perhaps tedious, letter is

not to seek to persuade government to reconsider its decision to transfer Lyngdoh from Pumea. Lyngdoh himself does not look upon his impending transfer as any grave personal tragedy. His greatest reward lies in the high esteem and affection in which he is held by all but the high and the mighty and their minions. Deprived of the just and fair treatment they received at his hands, the hapless share-croppers of Pumea may find themselves again at the complete mercy of the predatory landlords. But then, that will be no new experience for them. 1 have little doubt that their future will continue to be bleak until they build up a militant organisation capable of successfully fighting for their rights. Administrative efforts, at their best, can only be palliatives.

[Some names have been with-held or changed to ensure anonymity.]

### Glossary

Illegal exactions from the tenant in addition to rent

Abwab :

Crop-sharing tenant. Head of the District Administration.

Bargadar, Bataidar :

Collector, Deputy : Commissioner

Crore : District : Intermediaries :

Ten million The principal administrative unit in India

Holders of proprietary interests that existed between the occupiers of land and the State. They were known as Zamindars, Jagirdars, Talukdars, Malguzars, Janmies etc.

Kanungo :

A functionary of Land Administration above the Village Accountant.

Khud-Kasht

Land cultivated by an ex-intermediary himself, usually through hired labour or tenaijts-at-will.

A loose term covering peasants as well as small and medium farmers.

Kisan :

One hundred thousand. National Sample Survey. The lowest unit of local administration in rural India covering one or more villages.

Lakh, Lac

N.S.S. Panchayat

![image 739](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile739.png)

![image 740](Land reforms in India_images/imageFile740.png)

###### 348 Land Reforms in India



Patwari

The designation of the Village Accountant in several states.

Raiyat

A tenant in erstwhile Zamindari areas and an occupier of land in the old Madras and Bombay Presidencies paying land revenue directly to government

Settlement

Baden Powell defined Settlement as the process by which the Government officials determined the amount of land revenue payable.

Land cultivated by the ex-intermediary through hired labour or tenants-at-will.

Sir :

Tahsil, Taluk

An administrative unit consisting of several villages.

Tahsildar

The officer in charge of a Tahsil/Taluk is called Tahsildar.

Village Accountant:

The lowest functionary of land administration variously designated as Patwari, Lekhpal, Talati, Village Officer etc.

Zamindar :

The word means ‘landholder’. In the literature on Indian Land Reform the term connotes the holder of an intermediate interest in land with the obligation to pay land revenue to the Government.

(The terms hectare and acre have been used in the study. 1 hectare = 2.47 acres)
